# For Freedom!

*From my conversations with Count Carlo di Rudio, accomplice of Felice Orsini*

**Cesare Crespi** (1913)

*Translated from the Italian*

---

## Preface

<!-- pages:7-8 -->

<!-- pages:7-8 -->

# Preface

THE PRESENT volume might make some contribution to History; but it is not history in the sense in which that word is today understood and written... Something less, for those who would demand the methodical concatenation of facts and the cold judgment upon men and events; something more, for those who, regarding events as a mere resultant, might seek to grasp “the soul of the times,” rummaging among the passions that stirred them.

The Count di Rudio, so far removed, in time and in space, from the events he narrates, frequently protests that the miseries of politics no longer move him... Doubtless he deceives himself. Like a Minos who

“Judges and sentences according to how he coils”

he might have, in the estimation of many of my countrymen, a value altogether relative; but he acquires a great one as a “human document,” and it is precisely as such that he is especially to be considered.

Why did I resolve to write of him?

Consider: In this farthest West, in this land of vulgar commercialism... to find an exemplar of that epoch, now long swept away by the centuries, in which the third Italy had its gestation; to find him pulsing with intense life, still distilling a potent poetry, still capable of indignation and of tears... How could I not have been tempted, and all but mastered, by him? I wrote because my existence was thereby intensified by edifying and pleasurable sensations.

And I did not cast the manuscript to the bottom of a trunk, specially if not exclusively, out of regard for the Count di Rudio, whom I know to be either calumniated or forgotten. In the great outpouring of men and events that sprang from the European revolution which preceded my times, he possessed virtues that predestined him to be swept away. The triumph of political men is almost always a consequence of their most wretched qualities. It neither dazzles nor moves me. My sympathies are for those who manfully succumb.

I sensed that this book would see the light when di Rudio would be no more. That no doubt whatsoever might arise as to its authenticity, not only did I procure the approval, in writing, of every single chapter, but I also requested and obtained that every page liable to presumable controversy should be authenticated by di Rudio with his own signature.

I might have softened certain jarring notes, rendered the more strident by so much lofty and virile language; might have erased certain heterogeneous imprints that insinuated themselves into the volume through the influence of those authors whom di Rudio most cherished... I did not. To adulterate the word issued from lips silenced for ever may be a venial sin for a Minister of Public Instruction; for a simple mortal it is cowardice.

I have nothing further to premise. *Salute!*

Cesare Crespi.


### Chapter One

<!-- pages:8-14 -->

<!-- pages:8-14 -->

<!-- pages:8-14 -->



LOS ANGELES, a splendid summer morning. I had arrived there but a few hours since, on a train that had put me in mind of those which, from every part of France, converge upon the shrine at Lourdes.

For the metropolis of Southern California, situated near the ocean, between the desert and the orange groves, is a great centre of attraction for all those who have the misfortune to live beyond the Rockies: where, in summer, the sun prostrates men and herds alike, like withered grass; where the frost of the long winter makes the earth rigid and impenetrable even to the blows of the pickaxe; where cyclones sweep through the immense valleys, uprooting trees and shattering houses.

The wretched souls in whose hollow chests consumption gnaws find in the "City of Angels" a sky of deep blue and a balsamic air that give them illusion and hope.

How many of them I had encountered in the streets, in those few hours! They pass quietly, light as beings come from the realm of shadows, and they smile at children and smile at flowers. In their optimism, which here becomes extraordinarily tenacious, they accept as indisputable truth the merciful falsehood that their loved ones murmur in their ear at each fit of coughing.

They go and sit in the shade of the orange trees in blossom, and sometimes, whilst they cling, lost in reverie, to the sweetnesses of life; whilst the waxen corollas unconsciously exhale above their heads their invisible, most piercing fragrance — the waxen flesh unconsciously exhales its invisible, ultimate breath. The breeze blows in from the sea, and mingling that breath with that perfume, bears them away across the green countryside, away across the arid desert, away into the infinite mystery!

But I had not come to Los Angeles to prolong an agony, and when, after a long walk, I judged the opportune moment to have arrived, I halted before one of those little wooden houses which cannot be conceived of, in California, without associating with them the idea of a flora altogether special, altogether domestic — the flora that embraces and all but possesses the house.

A rubber hose coiled in spirals upon the green grass was driving from its metal tip, into the air, a fan of the finest droplets, which the sun was painting with the colours of the rainbow. A hummingbird, with its reflections of emerald, was attacking with swift and persistent flight a scarlet flower.

Beyond the climbing plants, within the walls of that little house, caught up in the social whirlwind, a man had sought refuge — a man who represented the last, glorious remnants of an epoch. Through the deep peace adorned with greenery, I could feel the suppressed longing of conspiracies, the fever of battles; and in that picturesque corner of the far West, I suddenly rediscovered the emotions of my youth, invoked in vain for so many years. Then I breathed it in, warm, warm, like a breath from the Homeland, and the fibres of my heart exhaled a word of thanks.

But a subtle thought crept in upon me. Three-quarters of a century; an unceasing succession of struggles, of dangers, of sufferings — what could have survived? Was I to regard that asylum as a temple enclosing a living flame, or as the final resting-place of a long pilgrimage, the sorrowful threshold of a sepulchre? Resolutely — Before raising my hand to the electric button, a figure took shape in the half-shadow of the corridor, beyond the glass door.

I found myself face to face with a stout lady, with a slight tendency toward portliness. Her hair was of a fairness that was fading into white and already showed signs of yielding; but the face was still fresh and rosy, and beneath the prominent forehead a pair of blue eyes shone, gentle and assured. The mouth and chin revealed a creature accustomed to confronting obstacles resolutely.

She asked me sternly what I wished.

"Forgive me — Major di Rudio?" And I handed her my visiting card.

Her expression brightened. "Ah! Ah! That name is familiar to us. The Major will be pleased to see you. But he is still abed!"

I thought: "The infirmities of old age — the threshold of the sepulchre!" And I was about to tell her I would return; but she forestalled me. "Come in! Come in, come in! This is precisely the hour at which the Major rises; he is methodical — he cannot be long. Truly? From San Francisco? Well then, a cup of coffee can do no harm. Quite so! I am the Major's wife!"

— Signora Elisa!

And I extended my right hand with the familiarity of one who, though never having laid eyes upon a person, holds them dear because they have long occupied one's thoughts. Then a whim took me, and as I followed her along the corridor I asked her why my name was familiar to her.

"What shall I say? The Major reads whatever you write, and grows animated and bangs his fist on the table. Please sit down. The water is boiling and in four seconds —"

She left me alone. I was in the dining room. The leafy branches trembling before the single wide window tempered its light. A green tint pervaded the room, and the china on the sideboard reflected it; on the table there glowed, in a vessel of blue glass, a bunch of amaryllis.

The dining room gave onto a study, from which it was distinguished rather than divided by a simple cornice. Through the opening, wide as almost the entire wall, I could make out in the background another broad, solitary window, likewise encumbered by an arabesque of trembling branches. In the air, the same green tint, restful and tranquil. I gradually came to discern a number of plain and massive pieces of furniture in the Mission style. I thought that among that artful antiquity I might have found a place for myself as well; the more so as a bookcase crammed with volumes had instantly aroused in me the old instinct of the library mouse. I found myself with my hands behind my back before those volumes, intent on running over the gilt lettering impressed upon their covers of brown leather. History, philosophy — works stamped with the spirit of freedom of thought.

I looked about me. From one wall there smiled upon me the pensive countenance of a man known to the People and to its tyrants in every part of the civilised world: Giuseppe Mazzini. From the adjoining wall hung a picture bearing the likeness of a martyr less universally known, but of no less great a spirit: Fortunato Calvi. And I exclaimed: The house reveals the man!

"Beg your pardon?" — It was Signora Elisa, just then returned. She had thought I was speaking to her. She had spread a napkin, and in setting the place another lady was lending her a hand — a lady who struck me by her extreme pallor, her very dark eyes, and her slender, slight figure.

"My daughter — Italia Adair," said the Major's wife, and the young woman confined herself to a nod.

— Your only daughter?

— Not at all! We have two others: Roma Scott and America Vickery. I am expecting them today, and you shall meet them. For I hope you will be one of our company today. I have a son as well, the firstborn, born in England and set upon his first steps — you are not unaware of this, I trust — in tragic moments. But he is far away, in Arizona. A mania for the unknown has seized him somewhat, and he spends his life in the saddle, in solitude, exploring.

The refreshment was ready. The gracious lady had added to the coffee some toast and a dish of fresh figs. I protested. I was not accustomed to touching food before midday, and such kindness was superfluous. And she insisted. The eggs had been taken that very morning from the henhouse — she kept many hens; her particular care. As for the figs, Italia had plucked them with her own hands, at that very moment. They were ripe and delicious.

She begged my pardon and went off about her affairs. I remained alone with the other lady. I was sipping my coffee and felt somewhat ill at ease. That air of an invalid — perhaps my conversation would fall upon her inopportunely. I made use of the brief moments when she was occupied in arranging some knick-knack to observe her closely, without betraying myself, without committing any rudeness. She had a mass of brilliantly black hair, perhaps too voluminous for her graceful head; fine eyebrows, magnificently arched; an aristocratic profile; an ethereal little figure. But I was not long in noticing a certain energy of movement that contrasted, in a strange fashion, with her appearance of an invalid.

I resolved to bring all my resources as a reporter wizened in the trade into play. She, little by little, grew animated, and from her lips came forth ideas such as I had not looked for.

I learnt that she had been brought to the last extremity by appendicitis, and that the doctors had saved her by resorting to the knife — a few hours' delay would have proved fatal to her. Children? One only! In homage to Uncle Sam, the eternal and glorious Uncle Sam, she had given him the name of Samuel. She wished to guide him toward a military career. At West Point, where she hoped to have him admitted, he would have the advantage of one of the finest educations in the world. She had spent a great part of her youth with her father, at the Regiment. Beautiful, superb, the military life! Discipline, servitude? You jest! Freedom boundless as the prairies over which one ranged, as the desert! Papa belonged to the Seventh Cavalry. "When I think —

"When the dust, sparkling with arms, announced them from afar, on either side there arose cries to shame a horde of Bedouins. What moments, what embraces! On their faces, together with the joy of homecoming, the serene pride of having repressed barbarism, of having snatched our bold pioneers from torture and destruction. On ours, together with the joy of seeing them again, the serene pride of being the daughters and wives of those strong men. Wherever the horses of Uncle Sam advance, they leave behind them the way smoothed for honest toil and civil life!" — she added — "when I think of the life of the Regiment, I feel a thrill of energy stir within me. Not a moment of idleness, not a moment of tedium! True, there was no lack of days of painful apprehension. The regiment was often called upon to drive back into their reservation the Indians who were committing some raid or other. It would remain absent three, four, eight months. But when the dispatch-rider, white with dust, burst into the encampment and announced its return at the top of his voice, joy was in the air and stole into one's whole being. In an instant, life redoubled in intensity. The ladies hastened to communicate the good news to one another; the children doubled their leapings on the grass; the rose-bushes were stripped bare; an endless coming and going of orderlies as we set everything in order, everything afresh. And from that noisy bustle we launched ourselves at full gallop, away as if in a race — ten, fifteen miles — to be the first to salute the phalanx of the returning men.

"You are mistaken, you are mistaken! Few bonds are so cordial as those that bind soldiers and officers together. The roughness is entirely apparent. When a subordinate receives a dressing-down, he looks his superior in the eye and can always read there a phrase very different from the one his lips have uttered. Severity is reserved exclusively for him who shows signs of being a coward. But have you ever heard tell of cowards in Uncle Sam's army?

"Intemperate? I will tell you; there are birds whose natural state is flight. To set foot on the ground and never rise on the wing again is one and the same thing for them, and what is worse, they walk with an awkwardness to move one to pity. The natural state of the Anglo-American race is movement, incessant, intense labour. The exuberance of life that does not take on arduous and glorious enterprises vanishes in the taverns and the gutter. And the American never does things by halves — not even evil ones. But at the arising of a difficulty, at the mere idea of danger, in the hour of trial — what a transformation! Where find a body of men of greater dash, quicker at expedients, more spontaneous in sacrifice, more steely in temper? And what enemy, equal in numbers, has ever known how to withstand them?"

I listened, full of wonder and almost disconcerted. That harmonious voice, rendered the more caressing by a residue of weakness, expressed ideas which I had up to that point believed to be the exclusive province of virile minds, of people born to face the dangers of battle, whole and happy only amid wrath and blood. But if strength bows to grace and sometimes adores it, why should grace not admire strength?

She bent her slender little figure over the table and set her white hand to work arranging the bunch of amaryllis that glowed in the blue vase. Then, in answer to a fresh question of mine:

"No, the Indian raids are by now, save for the rarest of exceptions, a memory of the past. Uncle Sam now keeps arms and spirits burnished by means of exercises and frequent transfer orders. One never leaves a post where one has lived happily without a sense of sadness; but it is a sadness that vanishes, in a short while, amid the wholesome emotions of travel. For the ladies, travel is a festivity and, I venture to say, a renewal. On the last one in which I took part, we covered, on horseback, more than seven hundred miles through a marvellous country. It is a most singular sensation. One's being, having forgotten its own individuality, feels itself transformed and almost a part of the elements. Strength, infinite motion — we are those things. One goes on and on, in an atmosphere of sunshine, and we ran with the wind."

She fell silent, lowered her eyes, then resumed. "No, true life is not in the great cities. The great city makes us small. An elegant prison, visits of convention, a childish prattle of gowns and bonnets. If this is civilisation — I hate it."

I was no longer surprised. The impetuous nature, the need for action in the grandfather had passed to the father, and from the father into that slight lady, so ethereal that, as she walked, she seemed barely to graze the carpet. What would she have become in one of those enclosed environments in which virtue bursts its banks and rushes headlong to victory or to martyrdom?

The mother returned. She had grasped the thread of the conversation and added much of her own to it. She concluded the series of vivid recollections with this melancholy lament: "Happy days, happy days — set, alas. For us they will return no more!"

Among the episodes she recounted was the following: Captain di Rudio (he was a captain then) showed in the execution of orders a meticulous precision. On a certain occasion, so as not to delay the departure by a few seconds, he cut clean away with his sabre the sleeves of a magnificent gown that were protruding from a trunk. For several years Signora Elisa had kept them, as mute accusers of an unheard-of crime.

The good lady, dissembling, ended by leading me out into the courtyard. She walked scattering handfuls of grain to a flock of clamouring poultry, and I followed her step by step, attending now and then to a learned lesson in poultry-keeping. My readers, I imagine, take no interest in hens and cocks that are not roasted with a carving-fork in their flesh. But an incident which I shall relate in due course obliges me to bring those poor feathered bipeds into this opening passage as well — pure-bred birds, moreover.

We arrived at a sheltered corner, in the lee of which a white hen was brooding in her basket. And the lady said: "Today or tomorrow the brood will emerge from the shell. What maternal love beneath that fistful of feathers! Is it not strange that the great affections of which we are so proud should be enclosed and potent even in hearts so very small? I am fond of the animals that have no speech."

At this point she set down the grain vessel and walked quickly away.

Planted upon the landing, standing out against the dark background of the kitchen, framed almost by the doorposts, I descried an old man dressed in a grey suit, a tuft of white hair upon his brow, pointed moustaches, the goatee of other countries and other times. The sun, beating full upon his face, threw his angular features into strong relief, as though carved in bronze. He was beating the floor nervously with his heel, while his deep-set but very lively eyes were scanning beyond the bushes, beyond the plants that cast their shade over the yard.

There exist in California isolated groves of ancient vegetation which botanists call *sequoie*, but the people call simply "big trees." They rise straight, without boughs, like the immense columns of a temple raised, in the night of the ages, by some race of giants. The tallest among the pinnacles of the Lombard cathedral would seem, by comparison, a slender reed. They were already resisting the wind when the Almighty was creating, according to the Scriptures, the Sun and the Stars. The prehistoric man who danced his bestial round, devouring his fellow man, has vanished; the patriarchs who, wandering through the solitudes, traced the first paths for young humanity, have vanished; the Brahmins passed the lit torch of civilisation to the Egyptian — and vanished; the Egyptians transmitted it to the Greeks — and vanished; the Greeks to the Romans — and vanished. What remains of the mighty Roman Empire? Race has pressed upon race; States have hurled States into nothingness, through such long vicissitudes that men have lost even the memory of their own origin — and those trees stand.

It is said that with invisible eyes they behold the short-lived pygmies who flock to their feet from every part of the world and babble out their wonder there. If that is so — what disdain in their austere rigidity!

But the lofty crowns, adorned with an evergreen tuft, quiver in the wind of the Sierra and, perhaps, murmur ancient stories to the sky.

I was thinking of those trees, as Count Carlo di Rudio and the companion of so many of his vicissitudes, standing together upon the landing, heedless of my presence, pressed upon each other's cheeks the morning kiss.


### Chapter Two

<!-- pages:14-23 -->



Half an hour of discussion in the study, beneath the likenesses of Mazzini and Fortunato Calvi, who seemed to be listening to us. And I was already almost disposed to renounce my idea, when, to forestall future regrets, I said with some vehemence: "But do you not see, Major, that this is a duty to be discharged? The affair had a historical motive and to History it belongs. Why not clarify its circumstances and its intentions? Why permit inexactitude and falsehood to overlay the truth? If you persist in your silence, the word will remain unchallenged with your political adversaries, and you will make yourself a willing accomplice in their mendacity. It would be a grave wrong toward the martyrs whose memory you are bound to defend; toward your children, to whom you must leave, in all its entirety, the good name you received from your father."

The Major passed a hand over his brow and ground his teeth. It was his manner of manifesting strong emotions. All at once he fixed his eyes upon my face and began:

I was born in Belluno on the 26th of August, 1832, son of Count Ercole and Countess Elisabetta de Domini.

My family has old roots in that uttermost extremity of Italy. Angelo Biave wrote of it — and wrote, God rest his soul! — in Latin hexameters. A great-uncle of mine took the trouble of rendering them into the vernacular, thereby fashioning an instrument of torture for his great-nephews. For every escapade of ours, if punishment came it was mild enough for the times, yet terribly tedious: to commit to memory a translated passage from Biave. Papa presumed, in this manner, to retemper you in the glory of your forebears. I can still recall the opening:

*"I gird myself for an arduous undertaking; I tell of ancient personages, descended from illustrious lineage, who gave to their Peoples the most just of laws.*

*"In the ninth century, the Emperor Otto the Great ordained that the celebrated Nosadana family should govern Belluno and endow it with a wise order of Laws. Just recompense accorded by Caesar to virtue.*

*"For a long time the Nosadano ruled the city which our ancestors, with good reason, called Belluno; for therein Bellona proves herself and the citizens are valiant in arms, undaunted before peril.*

*"The Nosaidana family boasts personages of high renown in war and in counsel. It changed its name and was called Rudia."*

The poet then proceeds to recount the deeds of a goodly company of Nicolai, Ercoli, and Eustachi — some of them *podestà* [magistrates] of the native city; others celebrated for books dripping with learning; still others distinguished as advocates of the common people, or as commanders of the Venetian infantry, or for hoary heads rendered venerable by holy works and by the mitre...

But let us leave the forebears to sleep in peace, and speak of my father; from whom I believe I inherited something more than blood and name. An anecdote to make my meaning plain.

At the opening of 1848, when the gaze and the hopes of Italian patriots were converging upon Rome and popular songs repeated the praises of Mastai-Ferretti, the citizens of Belluno organised a demonstration in homage to the "liberating Pontiff." They filed through the streets bearing in triumph the bust of Pius IX.

My father sat at a table in a café. He sees, he trembles, and planting himself boldly before the demonstrators, he cries out to them: "O deluded souls, you carry in triumph the greatest enemy of our Fatherland." And without adding another word, with a powerful blow of his stick, he sends the idol of the multitude to pieces.

Intense was the excitement that followed the momentary stupefaction, and my father owed it to his deeply rooted popularity that he did not live, there and then, his last quarter of an hour.

Pius IX, repudiating the holy crusade for the independence of his Country and fornicating with the foreigner, presently took it upon himself to reinforce that popularity and to demonstrate the perspicacity of the old and proud patrician.

A man of such a temper was bound to prove odious to Austria, gloomy and suspicious as she was. The fact remains that he never obtained permission to practise at the bar, despite his considerable grounding in the legal sciences. He consoled himself with the sweetnesses of fatherhood and had fourteen children. I was the fifth-born.

They called me the *"Moretto"* — the dark one — from the colour of my skin. I was the favourite, and I do not prefix "modesty aside," for I believe I was so more on account of my mischief than of my virtues. My maternal grandfather, Fortunato de Domini, Colonel of the Austrian infantry, blinded by intense affection, fancied he could discern in me the stuff from which something might be made; and in order to remove me from the influence of the liberal ideas that had seeped into the family, he wished to keep me constantly by his side. First in the barracks at Belluno, an old building which had once resounded, in its better days, with the psalmody of friars; then in the fortress of Palmanova, to which he had been transferred. The good man died before perceiving that liberal ideas were more dextrous than his vigilance, stronger than the walls of any stronghold.

The simple and rough education he had imparted to me was continued thereafter at the Military Academy of San Luca in Milan, which was at that time destined for the cadets of the Lombardo-Venetian Kingdom. And I should have remained there, with my brother Achille, until the completion of my studies, had there not suddenly arisen to overwhelm me the fierce tempest that, having gathered in the North, crossed the Alps and swept through and bloodied the whole of Italy.

Here, at the risk of recounting things too well known to some, I think it useful to summarise the Italian question as it presented itself in that momentous epoch. Memory is more fleeting than one supposes among the fervent Latin race, and the evoking of the ills that afflicted us will better explain why those who desired a United and Independent Italy could not and should not have remained in doubt before the greater or lesser correctness of the means employed.

Milan was the first centre where our fury broke forth. No plan long prepared, no understanding among the citizens. The news of the insurrection in Vienna had disposed men's minds to attempt something new; a yellow and black banner unfurled, at an ill-chosen moment, from her windowsill by a woman of easy virtue, kindled — as if stirred by destiny — the flame that refines men and transforms them into heroes.

He who had long prepared the material to feed the insurrection was the aggressor himself; it was the folly of the wise Metternich.

That dismal incarnation of underhand arts already regarded himself as the most powerful intellect that had appeared on the world stage in centuries, even before the day when, taking advantage of the embarrassment of Napoleon the Great, he had managed to leap at his throat and hasten his ruin. One can well imagine him after that!

Napoleon, from his great height, had treated peoples as a flock of sheep; why should he not have done likewise?

And so the one and the other, by excess of confidence in their own powers, fell, by different roads, into the same fatal error.

Napoleon I, by the very nature of his genius, having subjugated a People, redoubled its activity and fomented its ambition. Not for nothing does one trample underfoot the prejudice of birth and reserve the highest offices of State for positive merit. A son of the Revolution, he had usurped its lightnings; yet only through it, and upon the ruins of the past, had he been able to raise himself a giant.

Wherever his eagles appeared, there blew a potent spirit of modernity; wherever he passed with his victorious armies, he spread, of necessity, the ferment of the new ideas that smoothed the way to the future. And in every other respect, his entire life was a wholesome lesson: "one must become strong and be bold in order to overcome the strong."

Thus in the very yoke he imposed lay the germ of liberty and redemption; the very good that accompanied his evil works was destined — strange to say! — to undermine his power and precipitate him into the abyss of Leipzig.

Metternich was too keen an observer not to perceive that point of weakness. In his titanic enterprise of driving the human flock back toward the past, he took every care to be a tyrant — a dark tyrant and nothing else.

In 1814, when the power of Napoleon, though declining rapidly, still appeared menacing and the aid of the peoples seemed indispensable to forestall surprises, Metternich and the other European diplomatists who took their cue from him made the most lavish promises of liberty to the Italians. It sufficed, to obtain it, that they should show themselves worthy of it by helping the Holy Alliance to break the bloody yoke of the French adventurer; by rallying round a banner that signified the happiness, the regeneration, of their Country.

Once the Napoleonic peril had vanished, those deluded souls, divided in the choice of a prince, yet showed themselves agreed in openly demanding their independence.

The replies that came, at long intervals, from the mouth of Metternich mark the various phases through which the nefarious work of subjugation passed, and would suffice, of themselves, to arouse hatred in the heart of any people that has feeling.

— The affairs of the Italians will be settled in Vienna.

— Austria will never consent to relinquish its rights of conquest.

— Italy is a geographical expression.

Unfortunately, Metternich was able to give force to his own pronouncements not only through diplomatic intrigues and through the arts of the Church, but also — and above all — through the armies of the Holy Alliance.

The debt of gratitude toward the peoples being treated as non-existent, there remained to Austria that toward the princes. And perhaps, striking a line through even this last, she would have appropriated half the Peninsula for herself, had not the jealousies of Prussia and of Czar Alexander I arisen, most opportunely, to play, to however limited a degree, the game of the petty tyrants.

Compelled to divide Italy into morsels to amortise that debt, Metternich took care to create about himself a cluster of satellites that would gravitate of necessity toward the great centre of Vienna. A partial exception he proposed to make for Murat, for whom he reserved the kingdom of Naples. The wife of Murat, Napoleon's sister, had delighted him with kisses worth a kingdom... but the impetuous French general's untimely adoption of the role of champion of the Italians deprived Metternich of the opportunity to accomplish the only phosphorescent design of his entire political life.

The kingdom of Naples fell to Ferdinando I, who had, moreover, prepared his way to it by lavishing nine million francs in the antechambers of the Congress of Vienna. It was an Austrian army that conducted him triumphantly to his capital.

Rome and the Legations fell to Pius VII, the restorer of the power of the Jesuits; the future compiler of the Bull *"Ecclesiam Jesu Christo"* which excommunicated all those faithful who, knowing an affiliate of the Carboneria, did not degrade themselves by denouncing him — by playing the spy.

The Grand Duchy of Tuscany and the principality of Piombino fell to Ferdinando III, an Austrian archduke; the Duchy of Modena to Francesco IV, surnamed by his subjects the "executioner." Maria Luisa, daughter of the Emperor of Austria and wife of Napoleon I, had to content herself with ruling at Parma; whilst the other Maria Luisa, Infanta of Spain, was placed over the principality of Lucca.

Genoa, despite its protests and the eloquence of the Marchese Brignole, who sought to invoke its long centuries of liberty and its past greatness, was incorporated into Piedmont; upon whose throne, returning from his refuge in Sardinia, Vittorio Emanuele I took his seat.

Austria retained under its direct dominion Lombardy, Venezia, the Tyrol, Istria, and Dalmatia.

Having provided for securing Austrian preponderance by surrounding the empire with weak States, Metternich set himself to augment its power ever further by spreading a close-woven network of secret treaties. And by imposing, along with the obligation to adopt reactionary measures, the further obligation of reciprocal aid in the event of unrest, he reserved to himself, in substance, the right to intervene with arms in the affairs of each separate State.

The sovereigns of Italy, so haughty and cruel toward their subjects, had, in order to reign, accepted the part of Austria's hired assassins. Austria, having had its direct dominion over the entire Peninsula taken from it by the jealousy of its faithful allies, had turned the difficulty and had succeeded in securing for itself an indirect dominion no less substantial. And the chains that weighed upon the wrists of the august Latin Mother were none the lighter nor the looser for it.

By virtue of those Treaties, every Italian movement, even peaceful, aimed at provoking liberal reforms, was smothered in blood by the nefarious work of the army in white uniforms with pomaded moustaches; and not even Piedmont, whose Kings, not lacking in courage, had already given some fine proof of manly independence, was spared by the barbaric deluge.

In the great historical periods, glad or sorrowful, there unfold incidents that epitomise them and may serve art in figuring them forth. The symbol of Italy in those times was as though embodied and enacted in the torture practised under the diabolical inspiration of the Bourbon of Naples. An imprudent word, a suspicion, the hatred of a delator — the more ready for calumny in that the accused and the accuser were never confronted with one another — and an innocent man was torn from his family, stripped to the waist, bound astride a donkey, driven through the streets of the city.

A company of Austrian soldiers preceded him to the sound of a drum, another followed behind; at his side was the gaoler wielding a scourge armed with nails, and at every step came blows that penetrated into the flesh and tore it away in shreds.

The torture was suspended when the wretch, scarlet with blood, bowed his pale face and seemed about to breathe his last... Suspended, but to greater torment. It recommenced as soon as a groan, a convulsive movement of the muscles, revealed the revival of sensibility.

The passers-by did not react; they covered their faces and fled in horror... At the end of the long Calvary, the gibbet! It was the agony of Christ renewed daily by the work of most Christian Kings allied together — under the auspices of the Pontiff — in holy alliance!

More refined, but no less inexorable, was the oppression weighing upon those Italians subject to the direct dominion of the Empire. The air, impregnated with falsehoods, had begun to envelop tender childhood, which was taught, as its highest duty, as a religious obligation, servile submission to the tyrant; it penetrated the lungs of the university student, upon whom the institution was compelled to furnish the State not so much with learned men as with faithful subjects; it hung, subtle and dissolving, over every phase of social life. Free, the cherished subjects of His Majesty were, to move from house to house, from place to place; to attend to their affairs upon whose profits the Exchequer extended its rapacious claws... serfs in all besides; men resigned or swollen with rage... citizens never!

Not for this did the repression of refractory spirits proceed without due respect for the forms prescribed by strict legality — an easy task where the Laws are promulgated by despotism, without the collaboration and assent of the governed. And with generous Italians, lawfully struck down, the fortress of the Spielberg in Moravia was peopled — that fortress which, for the groans suppressed by its walls, for the noble intellects that were withered and extinguished there, for the crimes against humanity that it concealed in the darkness of its underground chambers, shall rise horrible in the memory of men even when the last of the Habsburgs has been confounded, for centuries, with the dust.

The flood of informers, high and low, who inundated the Kingdom provided admirably for the supply of innocent flesh to that Austrian Bastille, to the pillory, and to the flogging reenacted in its old forms. Bearing no distinguishing marks of recognition; concealed beneath plausible occupations, they insinuated themselves into every family, drew up reports on the tendencies, the habits, the conversations of every individual — even on the style of hat habitually preferred; they violated the postal secret; they spun plots; they besmirched or sought to besmirch the reputation of the most prominent and authoritative men, whose word might have incited men's souls to manly vindication of their Rights.

The population of the Lombardo-Venetian Kingdom, emaciated and corroded by a monstrous fiscal exaction, monstrously applied, had to provide not only for the expenses of the local administration but to replenish the imperial coffers handsomely as well. So true is it that when you scrape the tyrant, the freebooter is laid bare!

And the freebooter cast aside the refined and reptilian means to lay hold, like the other oppressors of Italy, of those barbarously ferocious, the moment the Milanese, refusing to smoke and to wear cloth manufactured by the foreigner, attempted to injure him in his purse. The prison gates were thrown wide, and the dregs of society, mingled with the police, roamed the streets of the metropolis, smoking and compelling others to smoke. The bold men who refused to press between their lips the leaf impregnated with German spittle were mutilated or run through with steel; and neither sex nor age offered any defence.

Martial law became ordinary law; treason, punishable by harsh torments, was the wearing of cloth of certain colours, the singing of certain songs, the applauding of certain passages in this or that drama; the adorning of oneself with beard or hair cut in a given fashion; allowing oneself to be carried away by any act which the Authorities, entirely at their own discretion, deemed a demonstration of a subversive nature. "Three days of slaughter ensure thirty years of peace," said old Marshal Radetzky. And the pretext to perpetrate the slaughter was sought with the pertinacity of one who pushes the craving for dominion to the pitch of lust: of one who knows no other inspiration than the dark interests of the dynasty.

Such were the delights that the well-groomed and smiling Metternich dispensed, with a hand more or less open, to a large part of Europe. And the Peoples escaped him by virtue of a quality inherent in the very arts that held them in bondage. For those arts of necessity aroused the hatred which is a regenerating energy, which drives men toward the luminous affirmations of Right as powerfully as, or more powerfully than, love.

And so intensely did this energy stir the hearts of the Milanese that on the 18th of March, 1848, they found themselves united by a supreme intent; without distinction of class, without distinction of sex, without distinction of age... to kill or be killed; to conquer or to lie buried beneath the ruins of their own metropolis.

No exhortations were required, no watchword, no leaders. The hundred and sixty thousand inhabitants of the city became one terrible unity... the PEOPLE; even as the thousands of millions of grains of powder constitute, upon exploding, the terrible unity of the powder magazine.

Arms?

They were drawn from beneath the earth, from within the thickness of walls, from the hiding-places where bold men, yearning for the day of vengeance, had smuggled them away... old implements of every description, rusted; to which the armourers, emptying their shops, soon added a supply of hunting-pieces. Then came the bludgeons, the rapiers, the halberds once gripped by mercenary soldiers; the great swords that perhaps broke the impetus of Barbarossa in the fine days of the Lombard League... the past issuing from the half-light of silent Museums, hurling itself into the vortex of the uprising, bringing its own contribution to the redemption of a People.

That formidable tumult of bursting passions did not shatter itself against the walls of the Military Academy of San Luca; but, penetrating within, it aroused in our youthful souls a fever which, though suppressed, lost nothing of its intensity.

On the first day of the insurrection, the Officers spoke to us with contempt of a handful of brigands whom the Austrian force, and the good sense of the "respectable" population itself, had soon enough brought back to their duty. Then... a hard face and silence. But that silence was worth, concentrated, the speech of several volumes.

The energy that agitated the living and made them greater than themselves had passed all at once into things. In little more than a night, more than a thousand barricades sprang up and blocked the narrow streets of the city. The pavement had yielded its stones; from the palaces artistic furnishings came crashing down; the workman's house furnished its household goods, its mattresses, its pillows. Sanctified by the feverish hand of a people that willed it, even the humblest utensils had been transformed into a bulwark of liberty... shapeless bulwarks; their disorder proclaimed the fury of men's souls.

The combatant who, pale, tormenting his carbine, waits at his post in ambush, rediscovers emotions refined almost to their purest essence. It was among that furniture that fathers, chastising him, had taught him to mouth secretly the name of Italy. And now that name he would cry aloud at the appearing of the enemy as a supreme provocation, as a challenge to the death; now he would prove that the ancient valour may languish in Italian fibre, but perish never. Conqueror or conquered, he would have accomplished a work not without greatness.

The assault came, and at a hundred disparate points, simultaneously. But the Austrian found everywhere men whom desperation had taught the art of defence. The barricades creak and shift form under the fury of assailants and assailed, are drenched with the blood of one side and the other... blood... but they send back the imprecation of the barbarian who falls, the battle-cry of the citizen who triumphs.

While the patriots fight in the foremost ranks, the mothers, the sisters, the children share the care of the wounded and prepare fresh means of attack. The cobblestones and tiles heaped upon the rooftops are transformed into deadly projectiles; the web of barricades extends and draws close about the barracks, the isolated garrisons. Besieged by the oppressor, the Milanese thus become, in part, the besiegers. And under the leaden sky, more insistent, more resonant than the battle itself, spread the peals of bells rung in alarm. Across the broad plain they spread the certainty that Milan is not beaten, that she still hopes and fights.

On the morning of the fourth day, we knew that the garrisons within the ring of the Naviglio had surrendered; that Radetzky was compelled to confine himself to the outskirts of the city; that a Provisional Government had been constituted; that the insurgents, certain of victory and impatient to achieve it, had rejected the offer of an armistice; that the wind and the balloons — one of which had flown above our very Academy — had scattered through the countryside incitements to the uprising; that the cities of Lombardy and of the Veneto were in full revolt; that the Piedmontese army was already moving to lend its strength to the Italian cause; that armed bands were ranging through the countryside and making even the problem of retreat arduous for the old Marshal.

In the afternoon of that same day, a committee of citizens, preceded by a white flag, came to the College and demanded the return to their families of the Milanese cadets. Then discipline availed no longer to set a barrier to our enthusiasm. Those who depart and those who remain embrace each other as brothers, and not without tears. Brief and solemn promises were murmured which not a few of those smooth-cheeked youths were later to seal with their blood.

Not many hours passed before we were concentrated at the Monte Tabor, near Porta Romana. The order was to fall in behind the imperial army, which was filing along the bastions. Radetzky — the iron Radetzky — despite the resources of his own genius, found himself compelled to avert utter ruin by a retreat, dissembled but precipitous.

Five days had the clash of virtue against the barbaric oppressor lasted. The latter, maddened by his own unexpected impotence, massacred old men and children, buried prisoners alive; bound body to body and burned alive lovers and husbands; bloodied the Sforza castle by severing, for sport, the limbs and the breasts of the gentle and majestic Lombard women.

The Milanese people had, in conquering, inaugurated the period of history exuberant in enthusiasms and disillusionments, in victories and defeats, in tumults and silences, from which — as from a labouring womb — our Nation was pouring forth to new life, to the tremors of Liberty and of Glory!


### Chapter Three

<!-- pages:23-26 -->

<!-- pages:23-26 -->



On the very day of our flight, I had my first plunge into the life of intensity... and it was to prove decisive.

The Germans were filing along the bastions that run from Porta San Celso to Porta Romana, weary, dispirited, and sullen all at once. And since we were obliged to take our place at their rear, we stood our ground and watched that forced withdrawal of theirs. From the bastions, one commanded the whole city. Here and there, houses could be discerned in flames; amid the rooftops, the white cathedral towered aloft, and the Germans shook their fists at the tricolour that flew from its highest pinnacle. The bells went on ringing the tocsin.

Between the bastions and the dense mass of houses, there stretched at that time, still green, an expanse of kitchen-gardens... a strange quietude held sway there, amidst that great city swept and re-swept by the impetuous tide of passions. Solitary near the bastions stood the farmsteaders' cottage. A child, as serene as the strip of earth that had in all likelihood seen him born, came running out and, climbing the green slope, stopped in silent contemplation beneath the horse-chestnuts that overhang the walls. Then, as though drawn by the marvellous world of small things, he bent down, and his slight fingers played among the little stones that glittered at his feet.

The troops continued to file past, each Company preceded by three Croatians serving as outriders. One of these cast a sidelong glance at the child, lowered his rifle, and drawing it back for greater force, drove a thrust forward, as though it were a matter of running a wild creature onto a spit. The bayonet entered the flank and came out through the back of that innocent who, lifted bodily by the ferocious impetus, opened wide his mouth. But the cry, intercepted by death, never came.

The little one's mother was at a window of the solitary cottage and she saw it and screamed for him. She came running, clambering with effort up the green slope... heavy upon her was the belly, swollen anew and fecund. The wretched woman did not reach him in time to gather the tender remains in her arms. No sooner had she arrived than the tormentors of Italy drove in another bayonet thrust, and she fell backwards, a corpse with a corpse in her womb.

I was seized with fever, with delirium. On the point of levelling my rifle blindly at the murderers, I felt the iron hand of my brother Achille restraining me. He whispered a phrase in my ear which, though I could not make it out, I understood. Then I raised my face instinctively, as though awaiting vengeance from on high. The sky was leaden, motionless... God, if He exists, must have been absorbed in the affairs of some better world.

It was then that in my heart there began to take hold the conviction that the wretched have no salvation save in the awakening of their own rebellious energies. "To fools," I concluded, "the feeble prayer; to the strong who strongly will, action."

Our retreat from Milan to Verona was not of a nature to weaken that terrible impression. Radetzky had given orders to the garrisons posted here and there throughout the various parts of the Kingdom to contract upon the main body of the army. At the same time, in order to forestall the danger of being harassed by an enemy that might decimate his forces and slip through his fingers, elusive as a swarm of gnats, he commanded the evacuation of every town, large or small, lying in his line of march. At the slightest sign of resistance, cannonades and the horrors of sacking.

The various garrisons descended almost unmolested, arriving in time to replenish Radetzky's forces, which had been weakened by continual desertions. The confusion and panic among the Imperialists were such that, having barely reached Lodi, all discipline among them appeared ephemeral. Had Carlo Alberto, instead of wasting precious time in scheming to satisfy the demands of Diplomacy, crossed the Ticino like a thunderbolt, the retreat would have degenerated into a rout, and perhaps Austria, having lost the army of Italy, would never have risen again.

As for the population, unarmed, misled by uncertain and contradictory reports, it neither knew how nor could bring itself to attempt great deeds. Yet inveterate hatred and a virile spirit broke forth here and there. Melegnano, which had flattered itself that it might cut off the enemy's road, was swiftly reduced to submission by fifty pieces of artillery. I saw the Croatians sacking it, drunk and savage, flinging dishevelled women and defenceless children from the windows.

Not a day passed but I was obliged to witness scenes of ferocity and cowardice, often entirely unprovoked, and the suppressed rage tempered my patriotism all the more swiftly.

It found a first outburst at Desenzano. The garrison of Brescia, commanded by one De Domini, my maternal uncle, had effected its junction with Radetzky at that place. Learning of the presence of his two nephews, he wished to see them. He questioned us as to our political opinions. The uncle was a rabid *austriacante* — a zealous partisan of Austria — and I was well aware of it. I expressed myself in bold and unrestrained language.

The old soldier went pale and interrupted me. "These," he said, "are the accursed sentiments of your father. He has instilled them into your blood. Poor fool! In what do you trust? In what do you hope? Do you believe that four peasants armed with pitchfork and cudgel will suffice to frighten the most battle-hardened army in the world; to call into question the stability of the empire?" I contented myself with coolly alluding to Radetzky's retreat; to the revolution bursting forth from one end to the other of the usurped dominion.

"Ah, were you not my nephew..." he muttered between his teeth. "Thank your mother; she is what saves you! But so be it!... sooner or later you will leave your head in the hands of the headsman!"

And I, smiling: "I would sooner face the headsman a hundred times over than be a vile renegade, as you are!"

He departed fuming, and I never saw him again.

But those were mere words. Another terrible incident soon came to fill the measure to overflowing, to give the fateful turn to my existence.

The army had left Peschiera behind when informants arrived to report that the small town of Castelnuovo dared to barricade itself. The spirited inhabitants, reasoning — rightly — that Radetzky could not reach the strong shelter of Verona without passing through their locality, heeded the call of Carlo Alberto, who urged them to obstruct the retreat, and resolved to oppose their breasts to armed force, to offer themselves in holocaust to the Fatherland.

In those days of heroic enthusiasm, one did not deliberate overmuch upon the probability of success. The greater and more certain the sacrifice, the sharper the spur that drove one to embrace it.

The imperial artillery soon made short work of Italian patriotism. Castelnuovo, riddled with shot and in flames, was abandoned to the licence of the soldiery. We, cadets of the Academy, watched that carnage drawn up near the gate of the old walls, at one end of an elliptical piazza. And as the Croatians, in their blind fury, did not aim with any great precision, we had sought cover behind several abandoned carriages.

All at once, I saw a young girl burst from a burning house, her hair unbound, barefoot, scarcely covered by a simple shift... evidently someone taken ill, surprised by the fury of the fire and of men. And behold, from that same house, a filthy Croatian came rushing out, weapon in hand, in pursuit of her.

I thought: "If that cur catches her, he will cut her throat!" And following an irresistible impulse, I was already throwing myself forward to come to her aid, when another brutish soldier, forgetful that in his own country too there were mothers and sisters trembling with dread, levelled his carbine and fired. The young girl fell, and a scarlet streak ran across the white linen that enveloped her.

An imprecation, a leap, and I was at her side, trembling with pity and with rage. At the touch of my hands lifting her head, she opened her eyes; but no sooner had she glimpsed the hated uniform than she shuddered and turned her face away... that pallid face on which, standing out more sharply than the deep marks of the end that hung over her, there reigned an expression of utter revulsion.

"Do not be afraid. I am one of your own; I too am Italian!" and I touched her flesh to find the wound.

The unhappy girl gestured towards her heart, sighed, and her young limbs were overtaken by the ineffable lassitude of death. I remained to contemplate her. She was of a beauty rendered sublime in my eyes by the aureole of martyrdom. Then it was as though those lips spoke to me in a passionate and vibrant tongue. My emotion grew overpowering. I suffered as though I were receiving into my soul all the sorrows of the Fatherland, and a surge of new resolves broke loose suddenly from the depths. Nor ever, after that day, in moments of danger, did I invoke the sacred ideal of Liberty but that, to renew that surge, there reappeared before me, sharp and clear in my mind, the delicate features of that young girl driven into eternal silence by the lead of the oppressor — unconsciously great, tragic, and beautiful.

Night fell, and it was the last night for certain tormentors who had expected to savour much longer the juice of our grapes. Livid lips gave up no secrets. Nor was the matter examined too closely. To what end, in times of inextricable confusion? But I can speak now without fear and without bravado. It was that night that my hands and Achille's came to know their first blood. An act in defence of my resurgent race, an act of Justice!.. The pregnant Lombard mother and the young girl of Castelnuovo did not long remain unavenged!


### Chapter Four

<!-- pages:26-31 -->

<!-- pages:27-34 -->



Having found refuge and safety in Verona, the old Marshal offered the cadets entry, as non-commissioned officers, into the regular army, granting them the privilege of choosing their Regiment. No one accepted. We were interned as far as Gratz and incorporated into that Military Academy; until the Minister of War, acceding to our petition, permitted us to return to our homes.

September came. The valiant Piedmontese army, abandoned to its fate by the defection of the Italian princes, its impetus blunted by the mental infirmities of its own King, vanquished more by the deficiency of its own organisation than by Austrian valour, had been compelled to evacuate Venetia, the Duchies, and Lombardy; to fall back behind the line of the Ticino. Milan had fallen once more into the hands of the oppressors... The royal war being virtually ended, there continued, according to the expression then in vogue, that of the People.

Among the individuals unadorned by any official insignia who most distinguished themselves that year, for valour and for integrity of spirit, was a patriot and a very dear friend of my family: Pietro Fortunato Calvi...

With a handful of mountaineers and with arms almost primitive, he had defended the Cadore from the first days of May to the first days of June, inscribing upon the annals of our epopee the names of Ampezzo, of Termine, of Rivalgo, of Chiusa di Venas, of the Passo della Morte, of Selva, of the Rindimera, and the ill-fated Mauria.

In this astonishing campaign, which certain historians pass over with a rapidity even more astonishing, Pietro Fortunato Calvi repulsed the attacks — first separate, then combined — launched by the Austrians against the three natural gateways of the Cadore. When, at the third phase of the war, he laid down his arms, it was a necessity created by the complete exhaustion of provisions and of munitions. Nor in that age, so ready to attribute reverses to treachery, did a single voice arise to diminish the glory of the hero. He made his way, by unfrequented roads, to Venice and, wherever he appeared, was received not as one vanquished, but as a conqueror.

In his footsteps, by the road through Portogruaro, the only one that remained open, I too entered Venice, together with my brother Achille. Calvi admitted us at once into that legion of the Cacciatori delle Alpi, whose name became synonymous with heroism and with glory.

During the close of 1848 and the first months of the following year, I thus had the opportunity to witness a marvellous spectacle. In my long existence I have known no other more potent in exalting the human soul... A people that, by sheer force of will, retempers its own fibre; that purges, in privation, in the grip of hunger, in the horrors of pestilence, in the baptism of its own blood, the shame of centuries of wantonness, the cowardly resignation to servitude.

In destruction, the glimmers of life renewing itself, of greatness reaffirming itself.

In those months, beyond my share of the common sufferings, I endured a grief intimate and intense. My brother Achille had his saintly resolves, his fervent hopes, inexorably cut short by pestilence. I came to kiss his icy brow and it seemed to me that, at the touch of my lips, within the inert cadaver there stirred as it were a last remnant of affection. I believe that only from that moment did the young patriot wholly cease to tremble with life.

I myself took part in but a single engagement of importance: the sortie towards Mestre which furnished the city with forage and with meat. On that occasion I saw, for the first time, without speaking to him, a man who was destined to have a great influence upon my future: Felice Orsini.

Two grave infractions of discipline removed me prematurely from that theatre of sacrifice and of greatness. I had been assigned to the defence of the forts of Marghera, lunette number thirteen. A tedious duty; for Cavedalis, the Minister of War, under the pretext that munitions were lacking, had imposed upon us the order not to fire. We looked on with sullen idleness at the feverish labours of the Austrians who, concentrated at Mestre, were digging their parallels and preparing for the bombardment. Our inaction had emboldened them so greatly as to induce them, one day, to advance along the canal to within a rifle shot of the forts... and from that position they wounded in the arm the sentry of my lunette.

The Minister’s orders and the Minister himself vanished from my mind, not to say from the face of the globe... I snatched the linstock from the gunner’s hands and the shot was fired. Instantly, as though my fury had spread with the swiftness of electricity, all the cannon of the forts thundered... the few of the enemy who remained standing took to their heels.

Pietro Fortunato Calvi in person pleaded my cause before the Council of War. He adduced, in my defence, ignorance of the orders, my youth, love of Country, the irresistible impulse, and for the time being I was spared. But at the second infraction, political animosities rendered my position untenable.

The fatal dualism between republicans and monarchists, which in 1848–49 accounted for so great a part of our reverses, had of necessity its repercussion at Venice too. Republican at first, then annexed to monarchical Piedmont, the city had once more assumed republican governance when the royal war appeared irretrievably lost. The upper bourgeoisie, which even then arrogated to itself the exclusive possession of practical good sense, did not readily reconcile itself to the popular verdict, and several Ministers lent it their support.

Hence, hybrid measures that irritated the multitude and drove it frequently to assemble in the Piazza San Marco and to clamour.

In March of 1849 — the precise day I do not recall — the exasperation, having reached its height, seemed on the point of degenerating into open revolt. To forestall it, Cavedalis issued the order, to Pietro Fortunato Calvi, to disperse the crowd at all costs, and Calvi, having detached several patrols from his legion, laid the thankless duty upon the non-commissioned officers. However, at the moment of our departure, he called us aside and, certain of being understood, pronounced these exact words: “Remember that in Venice it is no longer the Croats who give the orders.”

I marched at the head of my patrol through a good stretch of the city without encountering any obstruction; but scarcely had I emerged into the Piazza San Marco than I found myself confronted by a tempestuous sea. Our green uniform braided in white and the drooping plume immediately attracted general attention, and a ringing voice cried out: “Look at them! They have come to shoot us down!”

I placed my *kepi* upon the point of my dagger and, waving it by way of salute, cried at the top of my voice: “We are here to defend the People, not to tyrannise over them!”

Farewell, repression! The crowd parted to give us free passage and, its sentiments transformed in an instant, roared from a thousand and a thousand throats: “Long live the Cacciatori delle Alpi!” Lost and as though engulfed by that stormy sea, we ended by becoming an integral part of it.

Fortunately, where our arms proved unavailing, Daniele Manin supplied what was needed with authority and with eloquence. I have still before my eyes his face, resembling, as one drop of water resembles another, that of Cavour, though his spirit was so wholly alien to every form of diplomatic duplicity. The thoughts he expressed I am able to repeat today still, almost in his very own words. He exhorted them not to aggravate the miseries of the Fatherland, which in its straits called for the concord of all its sons. To divide it, to diminish its strength, was a crime.

The urgent task, the supreme task, was that of vanquishing the foreigner, of breaking the chains being prepared for the Republic. There would be time afterwards to settle internal dissensions; when the glorious banner would once more wave over free lands and over free men. What might not then be achieved by a people conscious of its own rights, drunk with enthusiasm, strengthened by victory? What scheming, what blandishment would avail to wrest from it, even in part, the fruit of so many sacrifices? “Return to your homes, restore yourselves in the caresses of mother, of wife, of anxious children... poor great People, so harshly tried in the school of adversity! Bethink you that the cannon may, from one moment to the next, tear you from those caresses, summon your presence there where men fight, where men die. No Italian worthy of the name must refuse to present himself unsullied and without remorse to the supreme sacrifice!”

He fell silent, and that strong People and that good People dispersed, little by little. How much greater a glory would accrue even today from the wise and humane word, to those Governors who so lightly set it aside in favour of fratricidal arms!

The following day, Colonel Calvi sent for me. I found him more saddened than stern. “The Minister Cavedalis,” he said to me, “is beside himself with rage. Venice is full of informers, as in the days of the Signoria, and he has learned, chapter and verse, what... what has occurred. He demands the names of the non-commissioned officers who commanded the patrols; above all — you understand me? — the name of a beardless sergeant who uttered seditious phrases and whom he wishes to have punished. The escapade is provided for by military regulations...”

I set about piecing together a defence; but he interrupted me with a gesture. The superior officer would not and could not enter into that discussion; but the patriot extended his right hand to the patriot and gave me a clasp that spoke as much as an entire volume.

“It is the second of the kind,” he continued, “and there would be no remission. Sergeant di Rudio, you must leave Venice!.. You will find enemies to fight in every corner of Italy. Rome has need of defenders, and the Government of the Republic, at the solicitation of those Triumvirs, is sending thither the legionaries of the Unione, who came among us after the volte-face of Pope Pio. Venice has more need of bread than of soldiers, and Colonel Ferrara, who leads the legionaries, will set sail, precisely tomorrow, from Chioggia. Here is a letter for him... you will be well received. Go — I command it!”

I rendered the military salute and departed. The unforeseen awaited me. The Minister Cavedalis had already sent to Chioggia the order to arrest, and return to Venice, all soldiers who did not belong to the Unione, or who could not produce a pass signed by the commandant of the Piazza. I did not walk for long through the streets of Chioggia.

The barracks in which they confined me flanks a street that leads down to the waterfront. A single second sufficed to spring from the gateway and dart round the corner into that street; four squirrel-like bounds sufficed to traverse it and reach the shore. In the courtyard of the barracks there paced about, in mingled confusion, legionaries of every province and in every manner of uniform... at the wide-open gateway stood on guard a sentry, or rather a great lout who, to judge by appearances, seemed to be dreaming of the good soups of his village. Slowly, slowly, as though my sole concern were to amuse myself by watching the passers-by, I drew up alongside him. Then I flailed my arms and cried to him in a muffled voice: “Look! He’s escaping... escaping from that window!”

He looks to the left, I leap to the right, turn the corner, and am out of range. Oh the blue waters of the Adriatic, oh the brown *trabiccoli*, rocking gently on the waves, light as shells!.. the *trabiccoli* brushed by the wings of the gulls, resounding with labours and with songs! I had some forty of them before me, already crowded with legionaries, already manoeuvring for the imminent departure.

I conceal myself in a small boat which, driven by two sturdy oarsmen, cleaves the waves swift as an arrow. But as I clamber up the side of a vessel, an officer on the shore caught sight of the green uniform braided in white. In that critical moment, the shifting manoeuvres of the mobile flotilla proved providential to me. The officer had undoubtedly fixed his eye upon the *trabaccolo* in which I had sought refuge; but in the passage across he became confused and boarded, in his search, the vessel where I was not. He persisted in inspecting a second ship, then a third, and arrived at last upon mine... but by then I had contrived to put on a uniform of the Unione. I fell into rank with the other legionaries and he, passing within a hand’s breadth of my nose, scarcely deigned to glance at me.

Nevertheless I was not to set foot in Rome without first describing a wide circuit. Many combatants had, like myself, quitted Venice by stealth, not from cowardice, but because drawn, by various considerations, towards the heart of Italy. But upon reaching Ravenna, Colonel Ferrara, citing I know not what peremptory orders, refused to enlist us and, having organised us into a detachment, had us escorted as far as Bologna under strict surveillance, as though we were Austrian prisoners. Our ultimate destination: Venice. But I was conscious of having taken up arms to break chains, not to have them imposed upon myself... I resolved to face the vicissitudes of a new escape.

As the sole non-commissioned officer, I had been entrusted with the duty of drawing rations for the entire detachment... one fine morning I turned this to account by bidding farewell to the barracks and to San Petronio. The soldier Angeli, whom I had induced to follow me, put a few coppers at my disposal so that I might procure a disguise; but the Venetian coinage, unfortunately, was not current in Bologna, and an extreme expedient imposed itself: to strip off my green uniform braided in white, which I had put on again at Ravenna, and exchange it for a suit of fustian. Deep was the pang of bitterness this occasioned me; but in the end I consoled myself with the thought that the garb of the labourer of the fields — the honest and unfortunate *paria* of my Country — is also an honourable one. Besides, the heart that yearned for Liberty, the heart that wins battles, I carried always within my breast, unchanged.

At one o’clock that very night, we arrived, without mishap, at the walls of ancient Ferrara. The gates were shut; in the deep darkness, on the far bank of the Po, in the distance, shone the watch-fires of the Austrian encampment. With the exception of the fortress, the piazza was in our hands. I knock at the gate.

— Who goes there?

— Italians deserted from the Austrian encampment. We wish to fight for the cause of independence.

We are admitted, welcomed with open arms, escorted to general headquarters, plied with questions. Our intelligence, gleaned from the newspapers along the road, was found plausible... besides, we had passed ourselves off as common soldiers, and no one expected revelations of high strategy. The matter concluded with our being conducted as far as Cento, where Major Mambrini enrolled us in the Legione Garibaldi.

The fairest sun of Italy was shining when, from the heights of Civita Castellana, I saw Michelangelo’s dome stand forth in sharp relief against the blue. The weariness vanished as though by enchantment, and our arms stretched out towards the plain, towards that place where there throbbed the metropolis that had filled the world with its power and its wisdom. In those days, amid the vestiges of its ancient civilisation, amid the superb creations of the Middle Ages, the red shirt blazed as the symbol of the new idea.

It was the last days of April. On the 30th, the troops of the French invader broke their impetus against our breasts, and the hearts of Italians opened once more to hope!


### Chapter Five

<!-- pages:31-34 -->

<!-- pages:31-34 -->



THE SIEGE OF ROME is indissolubly linked to the name of a man fatal to Liberty: that of Luigi Napoleone. The Carbonari, who had received him into their bosom, regarded him with suspicion as early as 1831; for at that time the rumour had spread that he had made away with his brother (Napoleone Luigi), who, having been born four years before him, barred his path to sublime heights.

Our revolutionary movements opened, or seemed to open, new horizons for Italy at that time, and the two sons of Hortense, taking part in those movements, sought upon the Peninsula a field propitious to their ambition; a compensation, perhaps, as well, for the loss of the French throne, then warmed by Louis-Philippe. This train of thought lent credence to the dark rumour, which, moreover, could never be thoroughly investigated.

The majority of historians who treat of the period assert simply that Napoleone Luigi died of measles. Others go so far as to admit that the matter is not altogether certain; yet leave it vaguely to be supposed that, if a crime was perpetrated, it is to be attributed to the fanatics of some *Vendita* [lodge of the Carbonari].

Be that as it may, the distrust was tempered by time and by the prince's humanitarian postures. On the other hand, the subversives well understood the immense advantage they might derive from a man whose very name was an entire programme of war against the old order, and who could not have emerged from his semi-obscurity save on condition of assuming full responsibility for that programme.

His hopes of making his fortune in Italy having vanished, Luigi Napoleone turned his eyes toward France. He first attempted the *coup de main* at Strasbourg, which failed chiefly owing to his want of resolution; then that at Boulogne, which led him straight to the prison-cells of the fortress of Ham.

The subversives of France and Italy then schemed to oppose the violent suppression of that political entity. Princess Cristina Belgioioso Triulzio, intimate with the affairs of Young Italy, was induced to employ her own influence at the Court of Louis-Philippe, to obtain an interview with the prisoner.

It is known that the substance of that interview required no lengthy preamble. The gentlewoman offered the support of the liberals of France and Italy to effect an escape and to set the man afloat upon the stormy political sea. Once arrived at power, he would discharge the debt of gratitude by working toward the promulgation of democratic laws and championing our independence.

Luigi Napoleone's hesitancy led the illustrious Italian to grant him two months for reflection. In the meanwhile, so as not to be found unprepared for events, his friends would see to the organisation of an escape plan.

The two months elapsed and a second interview took place. The prisoner let her speak; then expressed his profound sympathy for our Nation; but concluded by declaring that, for the moment, he did not feel disposed to act — whether because he distrusted the comely and most cultivated gentlewoman, or because he hoped for assistance from parties whose ideas were more akin to his own instincts and his secret designs. The plan seemed abandoned when, like a thunderbolt, word spread through our circles that Napoleone, suddenly changing his mind, had given his consent; and, shortly after, with equal swiftness, came the news of his disguise and his flight, from France to Belgium, from Belgium to England.

In London, and precisely at the Brunswick Hotel, he met again with the Belgioioso and sealed, so to speak, the compact: "He would aid Italy, as soon as the affairs of France were settled."

Events rushed headlong. Louis-Philippe was driven from the throne, and Napoleone asked permission to return to his country. To overcome the parliamentary opposition, Louis Blanc arose and, with a single speech, altered the course of French history as much as ten battles might have done. Napoleone became a candidate for the National Assembly for the Department of the Seine, and set foot triumphantly upon French soil.

A sadly famous epoch! The parties contending for mastery of the Country flew at one another's throats and unleashed upon Paris the tempest of blood that preludes every great political transformation. Cavaignac emerged grimly from the carnage and aspired to the Presidency of the Republic, contested by the vanquished who, in desperation, set against him the candidacy of Ledru-Rollin. The resultant of those two currents, foreseen and facilitated by our secret associations, carried the victory to a third candidacy — that of Luigi Napoleone. On the 20th of December 1848 he became the Supreme Magistrate of the glorious Nation.

What was his first act of note?

That of repaying his friends by working, with arms and with treachery, to restore in the very heart of our Peninsula the most feeble, the most baleful, the most hated of Governments — the misrule of the Pope!

The diabolical design was so well dissembled as to induce the Italians to facilitate the landing of General Oudinot's troops, which appeared without warning in the waters of Civitavecchia. They received them with enthusiastic, fraternal demonstrations.

In France, suspicions arose more promptly. The fierce protests against the suspected annihilation of a sister Republic, to the advantage of the element hostile to all liberty, spread from the halls of Parliament down among the multitudes, assuming here and there the proportions of genuine insurrections. And perhaps popular energy would at last have imposed honest intentions, when word spread of the reverse sustained, on the 30th of April, by the arms of the invader.

A shudder of dark patriotism ran from one end of France to the other. "The prestige of the flag must be restored; national honour must be vindicated at whatever cost." As though the shame of a People derived not from the crime itself, but from its failure to be carried through! Luigi Napoleone thus found, unexpectedly, a willing accomplice in his grim designs — strange to say! — the most generous, the most loyal among the nations of the old Europe!

The French, the soldiery of Re Bomba, the Spaniards, the Austrians, and the underhanded arts of the priests! — how could Rome have escaped utter ruin?

One man alone, Giuseppe Mazzini, foresaw, in that tragic moment, that from death itself might, by virtue of heroism and of martyrdom, be renewed the right, might spring forth life. He strove to transfuse his faith into all who surrounded him, and the men of learning and the humble alike were as if transfigured by it. The "brigands" who defended Rome wrote, in characters of blood, the purest, the most fruitful of the pages of our modern history. The iron resolve that restored its glorious capital to our Fatherland is owed, in great measure, to their defeats!


### Chapter Six

<!-- pages:34-39 -->

<!-- pages:35-42 -->



TO INSINUATE my own personality among the great men who laboured in that titanic defence seems to me an act of irreverence. And I will say, moreover, that my spirit remains as though overwhelmed by the surging return of those powerful visions.

On the 30th of April, I too saw the backs of the invaders, and certainly, pressing the advantage of our victory, we would have driven the French into the sea, had not Giuseppe Mazzini deemed it expedient, for his diplomatic designs, to blunt our élan.
