On the 9th of May, I was fighting with Garibaldi at Palestrina against the Bourbon troops of General Lanza. That easy victory sufficed to check the advance of Re Bomba, who quitted the Alban Hills and hastily recrossed the frontier a few days later, when Garibaldi, preferring victory to the appearance of discipline, urged our vanguard to engage the battle of Velletri.

In that memorable day I had a part in an episode which I recall with pride. The lancers of Angelo Masina, overwhelmed by an entire enemy column, were falling back at breakneck gallop along the road to Valmontone. Giuseppe Garibaldi, no sooner catching sight of them, spurs his white horse and, reaching a short distance from their front, plants himself across the road to bar their way. Behind him, mounted on a horse black as ebony, stood the giant Aguyar, his face no less dark and gleaming. Heroism forgot that motion has its laws. The lancers made superhuman efforts to pull up short; but to no avail. In less time than it takes to tell, the road was blocked by a tangled mass of men and horses... Garibaldi and Aguyar were buried beneath it. The enemy cavalry came on.

I was fighting along the road, to the right, among the vineyards. We were a handful of beardless youths... But we waited for no orders, and crying: "Forward! forward!" we rushed resolutely to the rescue. The Bourbon troops, assailed in front and on the flank, broke into headlong flight, leaving in our hands a goodly number of prisoners.

Garibaldi recognised in our impetuosity his own deliverance. In the evening, when by order of General Rosselli our offensive was unhappily brought to a close, he came among us. That man, so terrible when the cannon thundered, pinched my cheek good-naturedly. Of this sort of codicil to the episode, I smile now; but then... I felt all the heroic qualities I supposed in myself evaporate. That gesture of paternal familiarity reduced me, with unspeakable force, to the humble proportions of a child.

At the end of May, I was returning to Rome, and we were already on the point of marching against the Austrians, when General Oudinot, adding treachery to treachery, prematurely broke the armistice concluded after his defeat on the 30th of April, and seized, by surprise, the Villa Pamfili. There was no longer any need to go to Ancona to fertilise, with our blood, the soil of Italy!

That day — the 3rd of June — witnessed such proofs of desperate valour as to astonish Europe and the world, no longer accustomed to behold, among the hills of Rome, Roman greatness. But the hydra of reaction claimed, that day, too grievous a holocaust of our finest. Would it had been the last!

The French aimed at subduing Rome by taking possession of the Janiculum, which dominates the entire city. To reach it, it was first indispensable to carry our advanced posts. Having taken Villa Pamfili, they moved, under cover of darkness, against Villa Corsini which, by reason of its elevated and strategic position, might be considered the key to Porta San Pancrazio. They took it by surprise; but by a furious bayonet assault we retook it.

Unhappily, owing both to the enormous disproportion of numbers and to various topographical reasons on which I think it superfluous to dwell, the assault on that villa was more than arduous for those advancing from the city; easy for the French entrenched beyond Villa Pamfili. Our magnanimous contempt for our own lives might lead us, and did repeatedly lead us, to become masters of it once more; but to hold it was for us a superhuman and impossible undertaking.

Nevertheless we displayed incredible aggressive tenacity from the earliest morning hours until late evening. That superb manifestation of the reawakened, virile Italian temper had, so far as I was concerned, an interlude. In one of our first reconquests, as I was passing, weapon in hand, through a passage encumbered with corpses, I caught sight, through the crack of a door, of the red trousers of a Frenchman... an officer who had persisted in the defence and had been unable to withdraw with his men. To escape the first fury of the assailants, he had concealed himself in a privy. Upon my calling on him to surrender, he handed over his sword without a word.

I was charged with escorting him to the Chiesa Nuova, where the captured officers were being temporarily confined.

The Romans had treated the prisoners taken on the 30th of April not as enemies but as guests. Those were the days when the rosy hope of brotherhood between the two Peoples still trembled in the air, and the hopes of inducing the French to protect Rome against the Austrian hordes had been renewed by the very assurances of the Triumvirs themselves. Carriage-rides to the monuments, refreshments, songs, dancing... then liberty. The promise not to again take up arms against the champions of our independence was neither given nor demanded.

But on the 3rd of June, the spirit of the Romans had terribly changed. Oudinot's perfidy had roused to the point of frenzy the indignation of that people so alien to all duplicity, so hostile to treachery. I perceived this the moment I entered by Porta San Pancrazio, and the officer, whom it was not always in my power to shield from rough insults, had still more occasion to be persuaded of it.

On reaching the Ponte di San Sisto, I found my way barred by some militiamen of the Guardia Nazionale who, refusing to hear any argument and following the impulse of the moment, demanded that I hand over the prisoner to them. They wished to shoot him dead and cast his body into the river.

I cried out to them: "Let those who thirst for French blood run outside the walls, and blessed be he whose hands prove themselves capable and strong. But here, no! While there is breath in my body, I will not permit the Italian name to be dishonoured by acts of cowardice."

The timely intervention of an officer calmed the agitated spirits and averted every evil.

We reached the Chiesa Nuova without further incident, and the Frenchman, grateful, recounted what had happened to the other prisoners. While not far off two peoples, made to understand and to love each other, were hurling themselves one against the other, thirsting for vengeance, in that relatively tranquil corner I heard the very invaders extolling our valour and the wish that, once rancour was dispelled, French blood and Italian blood might flow mingled together, but with fraternal solidarity, but for a common and holy vindication.

A prophetic wish!.. In those moments, however, I considered it more probable that I should be taken up alive into the glory of Heaven!

Retracing the narrow and steep way that leads up to Porta San Pancrazio, I was obliged to walk against the current... the current of the wounded, being carried or dragging themselves to the church of San Pietro in Montorio, which had been converted into a field hospital. Soldiers and civilians alike applauded them. From the top of the walls, a band scattered amidst the gunfire the notes of the Marseillaise, flinging back in the faces of the assailants their own hymn to Liberty. In the midst of so much blood, at such an extremity, Rome found the means to be ironic! I had left Villa Corsini in the hands of the Legionaries; I found it reoccupied by the French. Our Bersaglieri, commanded by Luciano Manara, were attempting to retake it. I watched them run up the slopes of the hill, leaving behind them ground covered with the slain and the dying. The fire cutting them down was so heavy that, at a short distance from the building, they were as though suddenly stopped in their tracks by the lead. But their breasts, swelling with love of country, had no room for faint-hearted sentiments. Exposed as they were, they knelt down and continued to discharge their rifles, presenting an undaunted front to the enemy and to fate. Surely they expected to be reinforced by our men massed at the Vascello and on the walls; surely they offered themselves, with serene spirit, to death so as to give those men time to come up; to renew, at their side, the attack.

What moments those were for us who watched their ranks grow thin; for us who suffered their wounds and were proud of their greatness! The awaited order never came. They fell back when the officers commanded it; when discipline forbade further sacrifice. But in passing out through the enclosure, which terminated toward Rome in an acute angle, they became the certain target of a converging and terrible fire, and then... O my Country, what carnage!

Thus the proud Lombard youth blotted out the shame of past civil discord, and saturating, with their own blood, the soil of the Gracchi, traced upon it, invisible to the eye yet blazing in the heart, the fateful words: "Italy united, Rome the capital of Italy!"

It was in that assault that Enrico Dandolo ceased to throb and to fight for his, for our Country. His death, raising to still greater heights the courage of his brother Emilio, drove him to attempt the assault of the villa, leading alone twenty of his men. That slender band, thinned along the way by enemy bullets, was almost lost among the rough ground and the orange trees of the slope. Thirteen of them reached the peristyle, discharged their weapons, and withdrew in the face of the inevitable. When they took shelter at the Vascello, they were reduced to six... not one of them unwounded.

Their enterprise is one that cold and calculating minds may well describe as mad... and let it be so! But it was the madness of natures almost superhuman who fought not to win. The defenders of Rome, it is well to make it clear, had set themselves the aim of giving the lie to the belief that Italians do not fight; of placing, between the Temporal Power and the longed-for third Italy, the impossibility of any ambiguity; of affirming, by dying heroically, our right to live. And we may now proclaim it aloud: they achieved their arduous end.

The turn of heroism and sacrifice fell, after Emilio Dandolo, upon Angelo Masina of Bologna. By now the upper floors of the villa, cannonaded from the bastions of Porta San Pancrazio, are half in ruins and in flames; but for that, several hundred unseen Frenchmen, invulnerable unless rooted out with cold steel, prove no less formidable to Masina's forty lancers.

They ride up the hill at a gallop, and reach the summit with nothing to stay them. Here, to the great astonishment of friends and foes alike, Masina, suddenly taking on the proportions of a figure conjured by the power of enchantment, launches himself on horseback up the steps, dragging behind him, up onto the terrace itself, that fistful of bold men.

And perhaps they would all have left their bones there, had we soldiers massed at the Vascello, exalted by that spectacle, not feared that our commanders, persisting in their error, would render even so much heroism fruitless. It was as though an electric spark, leaping from the heart of Angelo Masina, had in an instant kindled a thousand thousand hearts. We broke from cover and rushed to the assault spontaneously, without awaiting orders from any man.

A yet stranger circumstance! The populace, crowded upon the bastions as in a theatre, divined our intent, and, seized by our fever, poured forth, surging like an impetuous wave, into the walled lane; spilled in great confusion and as though impelled by its own momentum up the slopes of the hill, soaked with so much blood. They had no weapons, or weapons altogether inadequate; but they seemed persuaded that their very presence would give strength to our arms... They would have shown, if nothing else, that they knew how to face danger, that they knew how to die!

Such a breach of discipline, such utter want of every elementary prudence, produced an effect contrary to that which might logically have been expected. Not only was every Frenchman swept from Villa Corsini; but, pressing the victory home, our bayonets retook likewise the Convent of San Pancrazio, where the invader had entrenched his outposts since the morning. And a band of resolute men, veering to the right, succeeded in taking possession of Villa Valentini, from whose windows the French had been harassing us, a little earlier, even within the Vascello itself.

Never, as in that hour, did the fortunes of the day seem so propitious to the glory of Rome!.. When, from Villa Pamfili, Oudinot hurled his seasoned and rested Regiments one after the other to recapture the lost positions, numbers, discipline, and superior arms prevailed once more; but not without enormous sacrifices. Neither our men, exhausted by so many ordeals, nor the almost unarmed People, fled.

We fell back to the Casa Giacometti, to the Vascello, to the walls, but always with our faces turned toward the enemy, always fighting.

Giuseppe Garibaldi carried in his heart the certainty that, with the loss of Villa Corsini, the fate of the young Republic was sealed. The improvident General Rosselli had not taken the trouble, during the armistice, to fortify it, to render it, if not invulnerable, at least stronger. To crown the disaster, he — Garibaldi — received from Rome only in driblets and at long intervals the fresh contingents necessary to sustain the battle. Seeing defeat inevitable, he was unwilling to submit to it without pushing his tenacity to the uttermost extreme.

Night was already near when he, placing himself at the head of the Reggimento Unione and whatever remnants of other corps the French lead had spared, personally led yet another furious assault. The valour the same as ever... but catastrophe the result!

I had just fired my last cartridges when I saw pass beside me, supported by his comrades-in-arms, pale but smiling, Goffredo Mameli. They told me he had been wounded in the knee... Italy was about to lose her young Tyrtaeus!

Night fell... Despondency, recriminations!? Not a shadow of either. There persisted in us, after the defeat, the exaltation that had sustained us, that had multiplied our strength a hundredfold during the desperate and unequal struggle. And as for Garibaldi, we felt we loved him as though he had led us not to slaughter but to victory. Our spirit felt itself so little vanquished, that, scarcely emerged from that bath of blood, we conceived and attempted a deed which, in an army made homogeneous not by the overmastering desire to act but by discipline, would have earned us the stern sentence of a court-martial.

Masina, the heroic Bolognese who for one moment had seemed to friends and foes alike a knight descended among the combatants by the power of enchantment, had given proof of being all too mortal. Among us were those who declared they had seen him fall during our retreat, at a short distance from Villa Corsini. We looked one another in the face, and barely a word sufficed: "Let us go."

Shortly after, we slipped out stealthily from the Casa Giacometti. We were twelve in number. We crept along the Via della Nocetta, hugging the wall that encircles the villa. The cannon had opened a small breach in it... through that breach we entered the enclosure, and still creeping, climbed the slope. Not a suspicious sound!.. we search, we find. But the stout-hearted men who lay their hands upon those glorious remains forget their prudence and, thinking to escape the danger more quickly, raise their burden and descend toward the breach at a run. In the night there rang out with sinister resonance a volley of rifle-fire... three fall in a heap; the others scatter... I leap behind a hedge and throw myself to the ground. Close beside me, touching my elbow, lay the corpse of an enemy with his skull shattered.

I was the last to return to the Casa Giacometti... four of our men never returned at all. And as I was recounting to my comrades-in-arms by what means I had extricated myself, one of them cried out: "Jesus... what a stench!" The sleeve of my uniform was all plastered with French brains.

The 8th of June was a torrid day... I stood for a few seconds in silence, contemplating the sad transformation undergone, in so brief a time, by the sublime organ of human thought, beneath the kisses of the splendid Roman sun.


### Chapter Seven

<!-- pages:39-43 -->

<!-- pages:39-43 -->



I DO NOT intend to narrate the detailed history of a siege that extended beyond all expectation. With his original plan, General Oudinot aimed to take possession of the Gianicolo by direct assault. However, the fierce, unexpected resistance offered by the Casa Giacometti and above all that of the Vascello — which was reduced to a heap of ruins, yet which French arms never succeeded in conquering — had compelled Oudinot to abandon his first design. He turned the difficulty by extending his trenches toward the south and preparing a flanking attack. This prolonged the agony of the Republic by a month.

During that month I had, between one exchange of rifle-fire and the next, more than one occasion to listen to the inspired words of Ugo Bassi and to engage in vibrant conversation with Ciceruacchio. Poor martyrs of a cause more than holy, they return at times to quicken my solitude, and in those moments I hear their voices still and dare not let fall an eyelid, lest reality reassert itself and the vision fade. Precisely as a son who, having had the bier that contains his father's remains unsealed after many long years, and finding the body intact, fears that a mere breath might scatter those dear features and reduce them to a shapeless handful of ash.

Among my memories of that time I count also the sad and ineffable smile of Anita Garibaldi who, on the verge of bringing into the world the child of the hero, yet found the strength to share our labours and to gather into her frail breast the sorrows of a country that was not even her own.

And I had occasion to exchange a few words with the Princess Cristina Belgioioso, of whom I shall speak briefly. That accomplished gentlewoman, who by her very nature — elect yet morbidly sensitive — was profoundly seized by the passions of the age, had, after Charles Albert's abandonment of Milan, lost for a time all her faith in the Savoyard Monarchy and flung herself, with an impetuosity all her own, into the republican ranks. She had hurried to the defence of Rome and revealed a resourceful intelligence in the organisation of the hospitals, and an unflinching abnegation in the care of the wounded. And there her hand was sanctified by the touch of a great dying man: Goffredo Mameli.

I shall add, incidentally, that after the fall of Rome the Belgioioso undertook a journey to the Orient. But the slaughter of so many heroes could not but arouse her noble indignation against the man whose treachery had provoked it. She drew up and published, in language lofty yet burning, her act of accusation, in which allusion was made — all too plainly — to the dealings that had passed between the liberals and the prisoner of Ham; dealings which she promised to document.

Before she could, or resolved to do so, she was struck repeatedly by the knife of a Bergamask, her own servant. And there were those who discerned, in that attempted assassination, the influence of Louis Napoleon. It is true that the servant himself harboured a strong resentment toward the Princess, who had dismissed him; yet it was observed that the agent of a powerful man would certainly not have exposed himself to personally committing so execrable a crime. To inflame the servant's hatred and drive him to strike was an undertaking at once less compromising and more sure.

The Bergamask, already imprisoned, contrived to escape punishment by flight, and of him nothing more was ever heard — a thing most difficult, not to say impossible, without the assistance of powerful allies. Nor should it be left unmentioned that certain publications of the Princess's became, in a mysterious fashion, unobtainable.

I hasten toward the end. The French take possession, in the dead of night and almost by surprise, of the pontifical walls... our defence is reduced to the inner line of the old Aurelian walls, where stands that Villa Spada which will shortly witness Luciano Manara fall gasping. The Genoese Medici, half buried among the ruins of the Vascello, triumphs still over the numbers and the arrogance of the foreigner; Giuseppe Garibaldi has transferred his headquarters to the upper Villa Savonarelli, not far from the Porta San Pancrazio. The villa crumbles under the blows of the artillery posted at Villa Corsini; yet from it he can dominate the field of battle... The night of San Pietro arrived.

Notwithstanding the hardships and the fate that now bore down with terrible imminence, Michelangelo's dome was illuminated like day. The Florentines, besieged by the army of Charles V, had three centuries before illuminated that of Santa Maria del Fiore... History was binding the new heroism to the old. Toward midnight, lightning flashes, thunder rolls, hail crashes down, water runs in torrents through the streets, and no sooner has the fury of the elements spent some of its force than the French fury pours upon the city.

“Courage — it is the final trial!” We rush to the defence, drenched to the bone, firing when the lightning flashes. Darkness, confusion, curses, death-rattles... Amid that chaos,

Amid that chaos, I stumble and fall headlong.

Before I could rise again entirely, I was enclosed in a ring bristling with bayonets — and it was well for me that the order to give no quarter proved insufficient to extinguish French generosity.

Taken prisoner, I am marched back, from trench to trench, as far as San Paolo. I am in a courtyard among a crowd of fellow soldiers... some lie stretched out staring wide-eyed; some pace about like beasts behind bars... here and there bandaged arms, faces streaming with blood.

Thunder still grumbles in the distance; a few paces away, musket-fire and cannon; the air saturated with electricity and with rage... my nerves transmit the impulse to brain and heart. It was an instant!... in the wide-open doorway the sentry stood black against the dark. He fell beneath my dagger like an empty sack, without a groan, and I flee across the open country, lie flat, crawl, leap; I cross the trenches again one by one, undetected. From the last I catch sight of the breach and am already flattering myself that I may once more hurl myself into the thick of the fray, when a lightning flash reveals my red shirt. I am seized again, brought back to San Paolo, and there I find the Judas who denounces me as the killer of the sentry. By daylight, I had been brought before a court-martial.

I have many times reproached myself, and still reproach myself, for having retained in memory so many incidents of my life that are of no importance whatsoever, and for having forgotten the name of the French officer who came to my aid in so dire a strait.

He spoke for over half an hour. By what quirk of procedure I was removed from the room, I do not know. Yet from the adjoining chamber I heard the most impassioned passages of the defence.

He maintained, in substance, that since I had not yet given my parole, I must be regarded as a lawful combatant, with the full right and duty to kill. And pressing his argument, he said to my judges:

"Does not the honoured uniform that covers you impose upon you the obligation of silencing every resentment and of receiving into your breast, as into a temple, the sentiments of humanity and justice? The prisoner has shed blood. But what thought, other than cowardice, could have restrained his arm? Did not cannon thunder on every side? Was he not fighting for a cause that even his enemies must acknowledge to be lofty and legitimate? Was he not staking his life in the defence of the Fatherland and of Liberty?"

And he urged them to hypothetically reverse the roles: "Suppose Paris besieged by Roman arms; suppose that the beardless youth who awaits your verdict were of French blood and had slain, in the very same circumstances, an enemy sentry in order to rush to the last defence of our beloved France... which of you would not admire his ardour and his daring? Which of you would not feel proud to call him a fellow-citizen? Which of you would not have wished to follow his example? Which of you would refuse to embrace him with brotherly warmth and to honour him as a true hero?"

I was acquitted, and on the 1st of July, together with some three hundred prisoners of every uniform, I was dispatched to Civitavecchia. Having come to about twenty miles from Rome, I slackened my pace... no one pays me any heed, and I take advantage of the fact to slip behind a thicket, ready and disposed to justify my halt with evidence all too compelling. A few minutes later I am joined by another Garibaldino, a Venetian fisherman, who on his part is preparing to furnish the same proof... Soldiers and prisoners continue their march, they are distant... they disappear. Here we are free once more, and we congratulate each other in turn, clasping hands with great warmth.

We clamber over hedges, cross barren fields and pastures; we take shelter for the rest of the day in the hut of a shepherd who refreshes us with black bread and cheese. Well into the night, we set off again toward Rome. But we found her occupied by the French. Garibaldi had departed the previous night, from the Porta San Giovanni, with the nucleus of his men, to hasten to the defence of Venice... the last strip of Italy where the People, starving and bleeding, still stood upright and close-ranked about the banner of emancipation!

The self-styled procurator of the good Lord, who had left the Eternal City disguised as the lackey of a countess, was already making ready to re-enter it from Gaeta with theatrical pomp. The traces of the titanic struggle effaced, he would find "his" Rome resplendent in a magnificence wrought by the centuries... so that, the twin throne shored up once more, he would there conceal his own ignominy with consummate splendour.

And there were those who, on the eve of the catastrophe, proposed leaving not one stone upon another of all those marvels. With perhaps less practical intent, twenty years later the Paris Commune was seized by a similar fever of desperation. But Giuseppe Mazzini held sacred the manifestations of genius, and his authority prevailed.

To the Master's calm eloquence I had attempted, in my youthful presumption, to oppose the impetuous torrent of very different sentiments. "Genius is, in Italy, scattered broadcast along every road, and requires nothing to flower but the propitious occasion. That occasion would be offered by Liberty! The very hands roughened in the pulling down of temples would have raised them up again as splendidly, dedicating them no longer to falsehood, but to Truth. The multitude of the bigots, incapable of comprehending the arduous abstractions of philosophy, needed to be educated by the school of deeds. Let the idols they regard as possessed of supernatural powers be shattered in the public square. When, contrary to their expectation, they see the Sun continue its course from East to West; when they must persuade themselves that no legion of angels descends from heaven to arrest the arm of the supposed sacrilegists, they will likewise be persuaded that the demolishing hammer has broken nothing but wood and marble. Let the hydra return and find the hideous ruin that befits it. Catholicism, having survived so much light of science, is now drained of every regenerating idea, and to sustain itself requires outward magnificence. The eye of the common people halts at the surface, and its grandiose semblance suffices to make it venerable in their sight. Strip away the gilding... expose the woodworm... reveal the emptiness! Rome must belong to the Italians, or vanish from the stage of the world. Her ruins would attest to the peoples the desperation of a people that truly wills; they would be a monument more glorious than any basilica."

A train of thought that might appear inspired by barbaric impulse; but which I then believed was not wanting in grandeur.

I attempted to reach Garibaldi on the road to Venice; but the stratagems that eluded the three armies pursuing him led me astray as well, and near Tivoli I came within a hair's breadth of falling into the fraternal arms of the Spanish soldiers. I too ended by taking refuge in San Marino, where, at brief intervals, with uncertain and gloomy outlines, there reached me the horrible stories of the scattered legionaries — dispersed, shot like wild wolves... Ugo Bassi shot, Ciceruacchio shot, and his twelve-year-old son who clung to his arms. Such was, in my time, the penalty exacted for the crime of loving one's Fatherland. And amid so much horror there passed, sad and tender, the last moan of Anita.

It was at San Marino that I learnt of the fall of the Lion of Saint Mark. With the surrender of Venice, the first part of the great Italian epic came to its close. The bloodied arms were laid away in their hiding-places; anger was stifled in every breast. The picturesque peninsula continued to be the garden of Europe, and to warm themselves in its sun the pale races continued to descend from the North. But around our souls there gathered a frozen sadness that found a sympathetic echo among the honest, the wise, and the strong, wherever the work of civilisation burned bright — kindled, across the wide earth, by the great and generous Latin mother!


### Chapter Eight

<!-- pages:43-48 -->



I wandered, almost adrift, through various cities, until I took refuge in Genoa. The "Superba" had upon her hands some ten to twelve thousand political refugees. She provided for them with generous largesse; but it was like casting water upon sand.

The city, saturated with political passions, was disturbed by frequent tumults, and the exiles, made more restless by their sufferings, added no little fuel to the flame. A child of my times, far from considering myself above my surroundings, I did as the others did. By degrees I fell into great straits. My father, learning of this, wrote a warm letter of recommendation to Alessandro Lamarmora, and I found myself unexpectedly offered the rank of sub-lieutenant in the Bersaglieri. I refused! Why? I refused!

Why?

Sudden conversions were not yet in fashion in Italy, and I had no wish to inaugurate the series. A Mazzinian to the marrow, I would sooner have served Satan than sworn fealty to a Monarchy. Let it be added that the Bersaglieri were, at that time, terribly hated in Genoa — the fatal aftermath of the Piedmontese cannonades!

I continued to live by humble labours and expedients, until an unforeseen event procured me the relief… of changing the nature of my embarrassments. A celebrated republican had died. His funeral proved memorable both for the extraordinary concourse of the agitated multitude, and for that, no less extraordinary, of the armed force bent on keeping them within prescribed limits. At the cemetery, passions broke loose. Bitter speeches were delivered, with open allusions to the monarchical objection and to the hopes placed in the future. They aroused a storm of approval. All at once, the banner bearing the motto "God and the People," concealed upon the breast of some Mazzinian, was unfurled high above the swaying heads. From the Sun it received its vivid colours, from the sea-breeze its trembling; from the generous and the impatient, a long, thunderous salutation.

That harmless banner precipitated matters. At the sight of it, the Bersaglieri launched themselves forward, resolute as though bent upon avenging some outrage. Balilla, ever alive in Genoa, greeted them with a shower of stones and drove them to fury. Then the veins of the rioters stained the enclosure of the dead with crimson.

Shortly afterwards, arrests were made en masse, and, as is ever the way on such occasions, at random. I, who, rightly or wrongly, had been among the first to lay hands, did not wait upon the pleasure of the constables, and that very day, seizing on the fly one of those opportunities that seem to fall from heaven, I was sailing toward free America.

I never arrived there. A storm, which forced us to put in at Cartagena, once again altered the course of my existence. Before us lay Spain with her manifold attractions; beyond Spain, France — political energy, life! I took counsel with a certain Trolli, a Lombard patriot who had gone into exile from Genoa under the same pressure and with the same purpose as myself. We had but a few farthings in our pockets; yet we were possessed of nimble legs. We concluded, with one unanimous cry: "To the Pyrenees!"

Trolli belonged to a most excellent family of Varese, which, proposing to make of him something in the nature of a Cardinal, had sent him to the priesthood. When the revolution broke out, he cast the cassock to the nettles and seized the musket. An ardent republican, he brought to his convictions the exuberance altogether proper to a robust constitution and a flourishing youth — so much so as to declare that the most righteous shots he had fired had been directed not against the Germans, but against that poor "execrated Carignano," whom he accused, among other things, of having betrayed the Milanese by abandoning them, without striking a blow, to Austrian vengeance; which had not prevented him — he would add — from making off with the jewels donated by the Lombard women for the defence of their metropolis.

Our few farthings having vanished after a few days of forced marches, the problem of appetite became for us at once as arduous as it was imperious. But my Cardinal — still in the bud — had an inspiration. He said to me, in substance:

"Garibaldian tactics: are provisions lacking? Wrest them from the enemy! We need bread? The enemy will give us bread and something to eat with it. I studied for the priesthood; Latin is my strong suit. We come from Rome; we are two ravens plucked bare by the revolution. I shall serve Mass; I shall preach in broken Spanish, but larded with excellent Latin. The curias and the monasteries will fling wide their doors — of the refectory. A comedy with a well-spread table; actors of distinction on both sides, and… let our bellies rejoice!"

My disapproval was lukewarm. At the sight of the first bell-tower, Trolli put an abrupt end to it by acting.

By this device we made our way from Cartagena to Alicante, then to Valencia, then to Saragossa, then to Tarragona. Trolli gave proof of inexhaustible quickness of wit. The seeds sown in his soul by the Seminary had not fallen upon ungrateful ground. With a single glance he could distinguish the simple, good-faith priest from the shrewd one who, versed in the hidden purposes of the Church, treated his "mission" as a comfortable trade. His transformations verged upon the phenomenal. Humble, steeped in a seraphic candour with the former, he assumed with the latter an undercurrent of guile that asserted itself while scrupulously respecting appearances. If he encountered any suspicion, he would resort to Latin, using and abusing it without stint. Those poor prelates, in their relative ignorance, felt themselves vanquished, all but annihilated. Trolli would afterwards say to me, in private: "You see that the tongue of the dead works miracles in the mouth of the living!"

We did not always live in this manner. In Barcelona, where Trolli was expecting a remittance from home which unfortunately never came, we adopted the counsel of a certain Piedmontese, a lame fellow who went on crutches and made his living selling saints and madonnas.

And in the Café of the Liceo, the haunt of political refugees, making good use of our brazen faces, we planted ourselves in the middle of the room and struck up, in thirds, the hymn of Mameli. For months we had allowed no legitimate outlet to our rebel souls, and that plunge into clear water had the virtue of lending to our singing an impetus it did not owe to art.

There was applause. A gentleman with large grey-streaked moustaches asked us, in French, who we were.

— Refugees from Rome.

That was enough. He turned to his friends and harangued them aloud. He told them that we had been driven from our homeland for the crime of having loved and defended her. And having depicted, in vivid colours, the sad life of the exile, he added: "Who was the cause of these evils? Who has made the hearts of so many generous men to bleed? We, we French, who, being strong, felt no shame in assailing the weak; we who, having risen in defence of a great principle, straightway disowned it, turning our arms against a people who had risen courageously, as we had done, for the vindication of that same principle; we who, having barely won our liberty, abused it by strangling the liberty of others!" He concluded by recalling the inescapable obligation of every Frenchman to exert himself individually in alleviating the sorrows called forth by the Nation.

And, removing his hat, he made the round of the room, table by table, soliciting and gathering oblations.

We departed from Barcelona with twenty-five duros in our pockets. Having crossed the Pyrenees with that provision, we arrived safely at Perpignan.

Here I was compelled to acknowledge that, even outside the world of old-fashioned tales, a good action may find a greater reward than that which derives from the mere satisfaction of having performed it.

The remittance from Varese, which we fingered, counted, and recounted precisely at Perpignan, had allowed us to transform ourselves from hat to shoe and to enter, for a little diversion, a café.

The spacious room was thronged with military men talking loudly of affairs in Algeria. I was listening in silence, sipping a refreshment, when I felt myself fixed, as it were, by invisible eyes. I turned sharply. At a nearby table sat several officers, one of whom was indeed scrutinising me.

— I beg your pardon. By your appearance… you are not French.

— Italian, and not of the worst sort.

— Were you at Rome?

— In the time of your siege.

— And… do you remember the bridge of San Siro? Oh, I am not mistaken! Your appearance, your voice…

And he embraced me.

In the room… profound silence. I felt myself borne, with affectionate force, towards the group of officers.

— I wish to present you to my comrades-in-arms. The right hand of the French soldier always cordially grasps that of the valiant — all the more so if, on a certain day, without hating me, they found themselves face to face with me.

It was my prisoner from Villa Corsini, the officer for whom I had forestalled a mortal leap into the Tiber. That evening we dined merrily in quarters. And since in conversation we disclosed our intention of making our way into the territory of the Republic, it was desired that we, honoured as guests, should accompany the Regiment — which was the 10th Light Infantry — as far as Marseilles, whither it was bound to embark for Africa.

There followed a succession of dinners and receptions which continued as far as Montpellier. The mysterious policy which already pervaded the affairs of France had sent thither an unexpected counter-order. The officer of Villa Corsini gave me one last, warm embrace and pressed on with his men toward Paris; I continued, with Trolli, my journey on foot as far as Marseilles — our paths and our souls were to meet no more.

My sojourn in this last city is memorable to me, for it was there that I pronounced the inspired oath of Mazzini and affiliated myself with the Giovane Italia. At the same time I became a member of the Circolo del Paradiso, a French subversive association which, in many affairs, acted in concert with it. However, a month later… fresh troubles! Obedient to the reactionary wind blowing in secret from the capital, the Police surrounded the headquarters of the Circolo. All those found within it did not sleep, that night, in their own beds. The foreign members were peremptorily expelled from France. To Trolli and me, the Police delivered a passport, with a fixed itinerary, which was supposed to conduct us, stage by stage, as far as England.

At Dijon, we slipped, by the first train, through the meshes of the net. The authorities either took no thought for us, or could not recover our traces, and, undisturbed, we entered the great vortex of Paris.

It was June of 1851, and stormy days were being prepared for France; yet to the eye of the superficial observer nothing of this appeared. Louis Napoleon, having assassinated the Roman Republic, had contracted a taste for blood and was now girding himself for a vaster crime, if not a greater one — the assassination of the French Republic. His Presidency expired on the 4th of March 1852. As the fateful date drew nearer, his hopes, his fears, his hatreds, and his appetites were all sharpened. But in dissembling his workings, in dissipating mistrust, he reveals himself a master. His ideal — to aim at the heights of Napoleon I and to attain them by the arts of Julius Caesar. The careful study of the life of the great Roman had persuaded him that a new tyranny is not established when, having flung aside the mask, one proclaims it audaciously as such. The favour of the masses was indispensable to him. How was he to secure it? He had begun by causing himself to be thought solicitous for the disinherited classes, publishing on the subject of pauperism pages which did not appear hostile to the doctrines of the socialists. Openly accused of ambitious designs, he replied with his famous comparison between Napoleon and Washington, concluding therefrom that, being unable to possess the genius of the former, his sole ambition was to imitate the virtue of the latter. And he drove home the nail by affirming, on every fitting occasion, that he would consider as an enemy of his country whosoever should attempt to make himself great by violating the Laws.

Such prattle lulled not only the great multitude to sleep; it had its effect likewise upon the National Assembly. This body was composed of the most disparate elements, which, while extolling the excellence of the prevailing régime, were zealously devoted to quite other ideals. And since, in the political field also, men readily believe what they desire, the honourable hotchpotch was, by those vanities, easily brought to the persuasion that the President of the Republic, more than satisfied with his rapid career, had no thought of attempting any dangerous novelty. On the other hand, Legitimists, Orleanists, reactionaries, and radicals all expressed their prudence and exhausted their energy in watching one another, and took very little account of Louis Napoleon, who, despite the warnings of his dark past, was held to be a man too feeble and ineffectual to meditate either virtuously great enterprises or great crimes.

Amid the general foolishness, there was no lack of clear-sighted men who foresaw the danger; but being conscious of their own weakness, they prepared themselves for the surprises of the future by working silently, in the shadow.

Then, unexpectedly, the National Assembly, disowning its own origins, abolished universal suffrage. Louis Napoleon had found his way!

He could henceforth undermine the ground beneath his enemies' feet; plunge into a cowardly crime and find historians to exalt its virtue; enslave the multitude and, posing as its champion, secure at once its neutrality, and perhaps even its goodwill! He set in motion the sinister forces long held in readiness, and struck the Coup d'État!


### Chapter Nine

<!-- pages:48-52 -->

<!-- pages:49-54 -->



THANKS to our connections with the Circolo del Paradiso, we were, from the very moment of our arrival in Paris, fraternally welcomed by the Club of Batignolles. We were put to work making cartridges.

The rooms of the Club were permanently occupied by some forty revolutionaries... not a corner in which to lodge ourselves. But we ended by finding somewhere to lay our heads in the house of a Turinese hatter, residing in the faubourg Saint-Antoine, at the opposite extremity of the metropolis.

To rise before the Sun, to go about our rounds cheerfully, revisiting the vicissitudes of the past; to nourish ourselves with Spartan frugality; to submit ourselves to the life of the exploited, to comprehend their sorrows and their hopes, to share in them; to return love for love with a frank and open heart; to refine fraternally the capable but benighted intellect; to lend a hand in preparing its vindication... such was our life until the 2nd of December.

Quickening our step on the second of December 1851, as we made our way to our usual meeting-place, we had occasion to find ourselves greatly surprised. The Place de la Bastille was under military occupation; we halted. Infantry and cannon. The gunners with their match-cords lit, as though a battle were imminent. More soldiers we found drawn up along the boulevard. In the faubourg, beyond the barrière, a sudden coming and going of columns moving with grave deliberation..., the silence that presages a storm.

Hastening our step and working upon our conjectures, we soon arrived at the mouth of the Rue de la Paix. Here we stopped short.

Opposite the seat of the Club stood a hedge of Guards. We watched the brutally satisfied workers employed in the manufacture of cartridges emerge from the gateway two by two, four by four, brutally shoved along by the gendarmes.

The affiliated Clubs had to be warned. In retracing our route, we were compelled to satisfy ourselves that the soldiers were not posted merely for a show of force. Singly, but with an ill-omened frequency, shots rang out. We were making for the Club of Saint-Denis. Before reaching it, we very nearly stumbled over several corpses... workers in their blouses, unarmed, who had gone out without suspicion and been treacherously launched into eternity!

We found at the Club a number of citizens, nerved tight with uncertainty, pallid, incapable of resolution. I recounted what I had seen... and exhorted them not to let themselves be slaughtered like sheep, but to act. Immediately the furniture is piled up into the street; the paving-stones are prised up, men labour feverishly to raise a barricade. A red kerchief, tied to a stick, flutters above that improvised bulwark and is transformed into a banner of Liberty. But before our work was even finished, a battalion of chasseurs from Vincennes, wheeling round the corner at the end of the street, advanced upon us at a run.

We were sixteen Frenchmen and two Italians. Not a finger was moved until the assailants were within certain range... then we opened fire simultaneously. No sooner had the smoke thinned than we opened fire again, with a ragged fusillade.

The assault was attempted twice and twice repulsed. At the third attempt, the battalion, closing its ranks, hurled itself forward with a temper manifestly enraged and a resolution equal to the pitch of its fury. A third volley of lead was awaited... which never came, and they scaled the barricade with shouting, striking blindly with their bayonets. They found not a living creature before them!

The few had held out against the many because they were sustained by the hope that the Parisian people would come rushing up to reinforce the resistance, with that marvellous ardour which is so characteristic of them. The shops, the windows, the doors had remained closed; the street desperately deserted. Once the last cartridge was spent, we took counsel together. What would our sacrifice have availed? We decided to disperse. The red kerchief, still fluttering, had baffled the assassins. The officer commanding them, deeming himself mocked by it, avenged himself by seizing it with a kick... not the least, perhaps, of the superhuman merits which afterwards earned him the ribbon of the Legion of Honour!

But what precisely had occurred; what was being prepared in the other quarters of the city? By what arts was Louis Napoleon consummating the Coup d'État?

It was in the dead of night that he had begun his dark work. The "suspect" generals — those whom he had been unable or unwilling to corrupt; those who, enjoying some measure of popularity, might conceivably have placed themselves at the head of an insurrection — were arrested in their beds. Simultaneously, every printing-house in the metropolis was seized and placed under seal; a trusted guard was stationed at the foot of every bell-tower. The Parisian people had not yet awakened, and already they had lost every means of publishing any protest whatsoever, of sounding the call to arms; of sending forth, from on high, a resonant stroke of alarm. Nor was even this to suffice. Louis Napoleon set the compositors of the Imprimerie Nationale to work with a pistol at their throats, and compelled Paris, compelled France, to speak on his behalf, to lend a semblance of legality to his brigandage. From the earliest hours of the morning, the still-drowsy population of the city was reading on every wall three documents that have since remained celebrated: the Proclamation to the People, the Proclamation to the Army, the Decree by which the President of the Republic dissolved the National Assembly, restored universal suffrage, convoked the electoral assemblies, declared a state of siege, and dissolved the Council of State.

This last document bore, alongside the name of "Louis Napoleon Bonaparte," that of "de Morny," Minister of the Interior. Those two men, strangers before the Law, bound by blood through the mysterious embraces of adultery, had strengthened the ties of blood by making themselves brothers in crime as well.

That same day the militia cleared the chamber of the National Assembly at bayonet-point, and the Police undertook to disperse the Supreme Court, which had assembled to protest, in homage to the Constitution, against the new parricide. The prisons of Mazas were crammed with representatives of the People.

But those Deputies of the Left had remained at large who, through some misunderstanding, had attended the Assembly only in the smallest numbers. To them fell the arduous task of effacing the outrage and saving the Republic. Not a printing-press, not arms, not powder; a people stupefied, paralysed by a fatal bewilderment; a forest of bayonets on every side, an entire Army Corps master of the strategic points, a Police force sold to the malefactor. To escape being shot, they were obliged to disguise themselves and to change their meeting-place every day. They acted with manly courage, they knew also how to face martyrdom with serenity... a blaze of heroism! but they could not — they were unable to — overcome the greatest obstacle of all: their own prejudices!

The nightmare of legality dominated them; the spectre of the Constitution held their arms fast, and they deemed it greatness not to cast it off — in such moments as these!

They were seen confronting the soldiers there with speeches, soldiers drunk with brandy and athirst for blood; opposing to the lead... dissertations upon the duties of the good citizen. They put their faith in the insignia of their own dignity, like devotees who await portents from a relic; they advanced, with great conviction, the inviolability of their persons... Language fitting enough in the mouth of a despot; ridiculous in that of men who lacked the force to sustain their own pretension.

In brief: indomitable courage, sublime self-abnegation... senile childishness!

The arid political questions left the masses cold, for they felt instinctively, if not with full consciousness, their emptiness. People who toiled from morning to night for a meagre subsistence could not have been moved by anything short of immediate improvements in their material existence. Certainly universal suffrage interested, more or less, a good number of the non-intellectual as well, and the Assembly, or rather its remnants, hastened to cancel the error they had committed and to restore it. But Louis Napoleon had forestalled them... their act seemed a forced concession and was derided.

Victor Hugo, in whose vast mind the truth flashed for an instant, proposed to his colleagues the immediate abolition of the octroi and the tax on spirits. For some time the People had been clamouring against those fiscal measures. The great lyric poet obtained that a decree to that effect be drawn up. But it availed nothing. Once again the practical men proved themselves shorter-sighted than the poets. They chose to defer its publication until the morrow of a problematical victory, thereby spitting, virtually, upon the most efficacious means of securing it. "If the People do not understand," said they, "that we are risking our necks in defence of their rights; if they do not fight, they are unworthy of any concession, unworthy of liberty." Folly from an attitude lofty and disdainful, but folly none the less. Perhaps those men, who faced death serenely every day, every hour, were afraid of giving the appearance of evoking the spectre of the red republic.

Another great mind, reputed fanciful, who had emerged from prison by chance on temporary leave — he was languishing there for an offence against Louis Napoleon — found the means of injecting among those irresolute men a note of cold-blooded positiveness. I allude to Proudhon.

"You are the victims of your own illusions. The People have let themselves be hoodwinked. They will not move, or they will move to help Bonaparte. A piece of foolery — the restoration of universal suffrage — has hooked the fools, who are always the majority. The usurper passes for a socialist. He means to be Emperor and he will be. It is a crime of impudence, but impudence triumphs when backed by bayonets. He has on his side the cannon, popular prejudices, the errors and unpopularity of the Assembly. Your attempts are doomed to fail. You, Deputies of the Left, are honest men; he is a rogue, and that too is a great advantage for him. You have scruples; he has none. Desist from the struggle. Given the circumstances, much may perhaps be attempted; but nothing obtained. And you will do nothing save drag a thousand, two thousand, ten thousand generous souls down into your own ruin."

Prophetic words! But was it in human nature to receive them; to smooth thus the highway for the enemy?

The misfortune is that the most logical, the most certain, the most expeditious, the least cruel means of paralysing the parricide occurred to no one, or at the very least no one dared to speak of it. Louis Napoleon had meditated the Coup d'État; he was the pivot around which the foul conspiracy revolved. The pivot had to be struck; Louis Napoleon had to be removed. By what strange aberration was the blood of one man, who, by perjury, was enslaving the Fatherland, to weigh in the minds of those representatives of the People more heavily than the lives of thousands upon thousands of innocents?

Victor Hugo wrote, from exile, masterly pages upon the terrible power of the "octopus." He knew that the blade of the knife slides over the tentacles of the monster, which releases its prey and falls like a rag the moment the iron is turned upon the one truly vulnerable point — the mouth that creates the vacuum and lends strength to the tentacles. But had a voice arisen within the Assembly calling up a hero Gilliatt against the baleful "octopus" of the Empire, he — Victor Hugo himself — would have been the first to hurl the thunderbolts of his indignant eloquence to execrate "the shame of a common assassination." A great and ingenuous dreamer, having stirred the Parisian People to raise barricades, to fight and to kill, he carried his daring so far as to confront the enemy's lead unarmed; he carried his sentimentalism so far as to rebuke the friend who held out to him the weapon with which to defend himself and to strike. A puerile contradiction in a sublime intellect!

But the horror of blood was not to restrain "the man without scruples." The regiments and batteries posted in the faubourgs were, by his order, concentrated on the Boulevard Montmartre. To whet the martial enthusiasm, ten thousand francs' worth of brandy had been distributed to each brigade. The hordes of passive obedience do not attain to the higher reaches of heroism without the fumes of alcohol. As for those at the top, lavish assurances of more imposing shoulder-straps and bulging envelopes "to meet any contingency" had been circulated. And for fear this should not prove sufficient, the influence of several high-class prostitutes was also skilfully brought to bear... the prostitutes who afterwards became one of the most salient characteristics of the Second Empire. The soldiers remained for several hours, weapon in hand, in silence, forcibly immobile, like hounds straining at the leash. Suddenly, shot from no one knew where, fired by no one knew precisely whom, the report of a rifle rang out. It was a signal.

All, simultaneously, began to kill. It was not arms levelled against arms; it was the merciless hunting of the inoffensive citizen attending quietly to his own affairs; it was premeditated slaughter carried out to terrorise, for the lust of slaughter. Soon, added to the intoxication of the alcohol, came that of blood, and disorder and death spread outward from the boulevard to an entire district. Foot-soldiers, lancers, cuirassiers advance in close order, cutting down all those guilty of being found in the street. Nor is not being there a sufficient defence... they fire into the courtyards; through the gratings, down into the cellars; they pour volleys of lead through the windows. As the killing runs wild it becomes by degrees a monotonous business. How to enliven it? The human beasts make a game of it; wagers are laid, laughingly, on the certainty of the aim; the target for this infernal contest — the women and the children. Soldiers were seen inflaming the frenzy of assassination by torturing the dying; widening their wounds with the steel.

After the killing with small arms, the cannon. The slaughter lasted three hours... a holocaust of several thousand persons: Napoleon announced to Europe that he had... saved "the social order." Blessed, with cowardly gratitude, by Pius IX, he set forth from so glorious a beginning, dragging France after him, towards the dark abyss of Sedan!

But Louis Napoleon never dared to publish the revealing figures. A cowardly malefactor, having struck his victims to death, he turns livid at the sight of the enormous number of corpses and takes every care to spirit them away from his own eyes, no less than from those of others.

Night descended... The centre of Paris bristles with barricades; but the People are nearly annihilated by terror and do not stir. Defending them stand, weaponless and despairing of victory, a few manly consciences... an ephemeral obstacle to the tyrant's artillery.

Nor, with victory achieved, does the carnage-lust of the executioner abate. The prisons are bursting with the wretched. How to make room for the crowds that keep arriving? The answer that came from above is brief and terrible: "Kill!" In the night they are abruptly awakened and chosen at random. Neither their name is asked, nor whence they come, nor what station they occupy in the social order... what matters is to reach the fatally established number. Three hundred and thirty-seven are thus dragged at one time from the vaults of the Tuileries, marched between a triple line of soldiers to the Champ de Mars, struck down, buried that same night in the cemetery of Montmartre, with their heads protruding above the ground... a lugubrious expedient of identification!

The horrible tragedy is repeated for twelve consecutive nights, at the Champ de Mars, in the courtyard of the Prefecture, in the adjacent Rue de Jérusalem. The slaughter is perpetrated at the mouth of the sewer, so that the human blood, flowing into it, may leave scant traces and not reveal the enormity of the hecatomb.

Then came the persecutions sanctioned by Law. What Law? Louis Napoleon creates the Military Commissions and the Mixed Commissions. It will suffice to indicate the manner in which the former conduct their proceedings. They sit with closed doors. They receive from above a list of names, they run through it — the accused being absent — and against each name they place, without further inquiry, a mark which, according to the case, has the power to acquit, or to send a wretch to Lambesa or, worse yet, to the dry guillotine of Cayenne.

In a brief space of time, the Military Commissions and the Mixed Commissions plunge forty thousand families into mourning! By such deeds does one acquire the sceptre "by divine right!"


### Chapter Ten

<!-- pages:53-58 -->

<!-- pages:162-165 -->



THE barricade having been abandoned to its fate, Trolli and I made our way toward the “boulevard.” But scarcely had we crossed the Pont Saint Denis when we were forced to acknowledge that martial law was being rigorously enforced and that the surest means of courting death was to walk in company. We found corpses here and there. I still recall, with great vividness, that of a water-seller, lying face downward, the vessel upon his back, his arms outstretched, his head literally shattered to fragments... There appeared, in horrible confusion, blood, brains and slices of lemon!

We parted, with the understanding that we should not lose sight of one another, and that we would quit the city by the Barrière du Trône. But to pass it, guarded as it was by a sound body of Finance guards and soldiers, was rather a ticklish undertaking. Before reaching it, I bought a large loaf and tucked it under my arm. Trolli divined my purpose and did likewise. As workmen returning from their labours we were looked up and down from head to foot, but suffered no molestation.

And when we reached the open country, the bare and wintry fields, we came together again. Trolli asked me where we should go to lay our heads.

"Where shall we go? To the devil, but free! Where shall we lay our heads? And what does it matter to you where, so long as it be not within the lunette of the guillotine? Switzerland is far away. But we have known how to cross Spain — we shall cross France as well!"

We had covered half a dozen miles when we were overtaken by a traveller in a little carriage. Upon seeing us, he drew in the reins and invited us to climb up. I cast a searching glance at him. He was decently dressed and had the look of a good-natured, timid countryman. His nose and his eyes were exceedingly red, and not from the cold alone... he could not be a spy. And besides... he was alone, while we were two, and possessed of resolution. We accepted.

As soon as the horse had resumed his trot, the good man asked us where we were bound.

— To Troy.

— A long walk! I am not going so far; but I will shorten the road for you.

And he began to give an account of himself and his affairs. He had gone to Paris to sell vegetables, the produce of his own little kitchen-garden and his own labours. And he showed us his calloused hands. The years had been rather lean; but with a little thrift and keeping his eyes open he had never gone without his rabbit and his small wine. And he launched forth, spreading out the long litany of his amours with the gossips of the neighbourhood. Of Paris, of the coup d'état, of the horrors of that day — not a phrase, not a word; as though he had left behind him a city perfectly at peace and still. The wine he had swallowed made him talkative, but was not enough to draw him out of a prudent reserve.

At a certain point there escaped him some remark about certain speculations of his, and he concluded that if they prospered according to his hopes he would have enough to live comfortably upon until the "Lady without a nose" came to cut him down with her scythe. And with that allusion to Death he fell silent, as if absorbed in profound meditation. Ten minutes later he was snoring.

I thereupon took possession of the reins, and laid on the whip — trot, horse, trot. We passed broad stretches of land, we passed through coppices, we passed through villages... twilight came, night fell... the peasant was snoring still...

All of a sudden, in the still air, there rang out slowly the brazen toll of a bell. It was eleven o'clock. A little later the wheels of the carriage were grating over cobblestones. And then the horse planted his feet and, for all my whipping, would not budge a step forward or a step backward.

The strange thing was that this abrupt halt had the power of breaking the peasant's heavy slumber. "Sacré cœur!" he exclaimed, after peering into the darkness. "Where have you brought me? Twelve miles from home!"

He falls to blaming — mark the justice of the world! — the horse, who might well have remembered where his manger was. But he ends by consoling himself with the thought that we were not in a desert. In the village he had good friends. "Do you see that strip of light? The inn is still open and the host is an honest man. I shall stand you a drink!"

Half an hour later he was lying down in order to be off betimes. Trolli and I went out into the open air. Warmed within and without, we once more confronted the darkness of the countryside and those yet denser darknesses of the unknown.

Our subversive Society spread its net over the whole of France, and the threads of that net were knotted again at Troy, so that it was not difficult for us to make our contacts there and to obtain a place on the stage-coach as far as Langres, the locality where the meshes, drawing together, formed the next knot.

The horses were still stamping under the archway of the inn at Langres when I was already making my way in haste to the house of the Postal Officer. A letter of introduction, a few agreed signs, and he would procure us the means to continue our journey. I pulled the bell and there appeared at once upon the threshold a lady of uncertain years, but not without a certain charm.

— My husband is not at home, nor do I know when he will return.

— I am the bearer of a letter which I should like to deliver to him in person...

The lady's mouth contracted, as though she were holding a slice of lemon between tongue and palate. She became inquisitive: A letter? From whom? Give it here, give it! I will undertake to deliver it... into his own hands.

— I beg your pardon! I have my orders and...

Her face had grown pale. She insisted and stretched forth her right hand... her fingers trembled. So much the more did I consider prudence a duty. She went back indoors, slamming the door behind her.

That evening I was seated in the common-room of the inn, before the fireplace, conversing cheerfully with other guests, when, to delay the sweet preparation of the chyle, there appeared unannounced the shrewish creature with her countenance at once harsh and melancholy. She had two gendarmes at her back.

The purpose of the visit was made plain at once. The lady demanded the surrender of the mysterious letter. The gendarmes had come to impound it.

I rose, drew out my pocket-book calmly, and, removing the letter of introduction, called the attention of those present to the fact that it lay in an open envelope. Then, with a bound towards the fireplace, I cast it into the flames.

There followed some little confusion. The gendarmes, who considered "the dignity of the uniform" to have been affronted, wished to proceed forthwith; but the order they had received was to seize the document and not to arrest its bearer. "We shall go for more precise instructions." They left, without arresting me, the more readily since the bystanders had been unable to refrain from bursting into uproarious laughter. I was compelled to protest with all my energy. The very Laws of the Republic specified that only sealed letters were liable to seizure; I had therefore, and with perfect right, resisted an abuse of authority.

And indeed the two gentlemen had placed themselves on false ground. The Law was against those who were supposed to uphold it; and, followed by the lady, discomfited and well-nigh in tears, they departed, and I saw them no more.

Late that night, however, the Postal Officer appeared, and I had the key to the riddle. His wife, an honest woman and of great merit, had the weakness of being jealous to the point of madness. This passion of hers had of late been inflamed by the mysterious coming and going inherent in our political Society, of whose existence she was unaware. Having fixed in her head the notion that I was bringing her husband the secret missive of some rival, she had made this ill-judged attempt to intercept evidence of guilt which existed only in her imagination.

Once the Swiss frontier was crossed, all secret influence failed us, and from that point there began the truly arduous portion of our pilgrimage. Trolli had written to his relatives to send him money to Berne. We had to trudge all the way there: our purses desperately empty, in the heart of winter, with snow lying deep, barely clothed.

We arrived. But the frost of free Helvetia had nearly crystallised itself, like fringe, about our hearts. As at Barcelona, so now at Berne — there was no money!

Night fell inexorable. We lay down in the market-place, beneath the stall of a greengrocer. Despite the fast and the bitter cold, we fell into a profound sleep. Blessed is youth!

But at some point in the night I was startled awake by a peremptory voice cursing in German. A very simple thing had occurred. Trolli was endowed with legs that called to mind a trebuchet; as he slept, he had stretched them out as far as the pavement and was, quite involuntarily, tripping a constable who was passing by. He was dragged out of the hiding-place like a sack of rags and pelted with questions. But he did not understand a word of German and, by way of reply, merely stretched and yawned. The policeman ended by losing patience, and seizing him by the collar shoved him forward with curses and buffets. I heard the heavy double-step fade into the distance; then I drew my jacket closer about my chest and went back to sleep. What good would it have done to intervene?

At daybreak I strolled about the solid prison building, whistling a cabaletta well known to Trolli. I was answered by a brief whistle of recognition, and immediately, framed between the bars of a small window, appeared the face of my Cardinal.

Farewell discretion! I waved my cap furiously and called out my greeting with the liveliness of a boy escaping from the mephitic air of a schoolroom. Nor did Trolli respond with less enthusiasm. And there and then he let me know that, in deference to the Law against vagabondage, the Police would have him escorted to the Italian frontier. "Do you promise to follow after me?"

I had barely time to extend my hand in solemn pledge.

A warder, hearing the commotion, had seized Trolli by his long dangling legs, and the cardinal face vanished.

That day I wandered long about the market, and, snatching up a turnip here and there, found a way of quelling the cramps in my stomach. When night fell again I took shelter, like Diogenes, in a barrel. My sufferings were such that, by dawn, I had almost made up my mind to give myself up to the Police.

But from that very extremity chance offered me, in a most singular fashion, the thread that gave me the means of revival. I had sunk upon a bench in the Public Gardens and was gnawing at the stem of my pipe, to deceive myself... the supply of tobacco had long since been exhausted. The paths were deserted; snow covered the green expanses, and upon it a flock of sparrows was chirping and hopping. Despite the rigours of winter, they had breakfasted.

Vexed, I seized a small stone, and among those happy creatures there passed, like a thunderbolt, the characteristic protest of empty stomachs. One sparrow gave a cry, turned its little legs in the air, and lay stiff.

This was no moment for hesitation. In like circumstances, man eats man... I could well bring myself to devour a raw sparrow. I was plucking it when I felt a hand laid upon my shoulder.

— What are you doing, young man?

— Taking a snack.

— You must have the hunger of a hawk. In any case, praise Divine Providence, which has known how to provide for your fast.

Beside me sat a neat, respectable-looking elderly man; a Protestant minister, in all probability. I pointed out to him that the Lord God had provided for my needs through the slaughter of the innocent, and in a manner too derisory for me to feel my heart overflowing with gratitude.

He jingled some silver coins in his pocket. After a brief silence: "Will you sell me your pipe for six francs?"

I pocketed the money; the stranger pocketed the pipe. Then he began: "You see that our Heavenly Father..."

Imagine if I meant to waste my time in philosophising! I sprang up — one bound, four bounds — and gave myself no rest until I found myself seated where the sweet fragrance of roasting meat was drifting through the air.

With what remained, I was able to make my way to Lucerne, where the twofold problem of feeding myself and pressing on was to be solved for me by a man against whom I had fought — Pope Mastai Ferretti.

Switzerland is a marvellous country. United by its beauties, upon those slopes, among those valleys, peoples of diverse origin, tongue and character live together in peace... a miracle of Liberty, a glorious anticipation of a holy Utopia.

And yet those peoples have failed to show the elevation of spirit needed to assume the full weight of their historic mission. In centuries gone by the heart of Europe beat mightily among the Alps; but in vain! As from the pale mountain ridges the waters rush down to furrow the fertile plains and water and fecundate so great a part of the Continent, so from crags inaccessible to the conqueror should those fair and terrible phalanxes have descended, to accomplish a work of fraternity, to revive the hopes of the oppressed, to extend the dominion of Liberty.

They did descend; but as a devastating scourge... blind instruments of the wicked, flesh and souls put up for sale!

Selfishness is fatal to nations because, among other things, the tainted atmosphere of your neighbour, sooner or later, corrupts your own as well, imperils and diminishes your glory. Despotism, defended by Swiss mercenaries, confined within the narrow circle of the mountains the very men who had nurtured it; the Republic remained a puny thing and, in the universal estimation, became a synonym for venality. At the time when I was passing through it, the evil habit was not greatly in force, and already the Federal Government was branding as a criminal both the citizen who was not ashamed to prostitute himself to a foreign Government and the agent who hired him out and made himself his accomplice. Nevertheless Pius IX, who distrusted his own Catholics, maintained secret agents in the Lutheran Cantons, to scrape together, for a goodly sum of *baiocchi*, the handful of insects he intended, in his heart of hearts, to use to alter the course of the stars, to annihilate the hopes of the new Italy.

One such agent espied me on the shore of the lake, absorbed in my twofold problem. Attracted perhaps by my soldierly bearing, he opened conversation in the holy name of God and concluded it by formally proposing that I should shoulder a musket in defence of the pontifical throne and the altar.

My first impulse was to hurl him headfirst into the lake. But a sudden thought restrained me. The Garibaldian tactic!... when you have no ammunition, take it from the enemy. The funds of the Holy See employed to repatriate an adversary of the old order!... my twofold problem more than resolved! And, incidentally, I might do good service to my country's cause as well, by unmasking the intrigue and clipping the nails of the unctuous agent!

I smiled and, passing myself off as a Swiss from the Canton of Ticino, accepted.

I was conducted through winding lanes to a house of mean appearance; I fixed its number well in my memory, mounted a dirty little staircase, entered a room of amphibious character — something between a study and a bedchamber. The contract was duly drawn up and signed, and a few hours later, from the stern of the steamboat as it put rapidly out from shore, I was bidding farewell with broad gestures to my recruiter.

Arrived at Flüelen, I presented myself at the Commissioner's office and narrated, from beginning to end, what had befallen me. I was brought back to Lucerne for the identification of the individual. I retraced the winding lanes, found the house of the less than modest appearance, remounted the dirty staircase. The blackbird was in his cage — indeed in bed; the evidence palpable, the Law precise. He was arrested.

While he was dressing, the Federal Delegate drew out a purse and attempted to count out for me some sum of money or other. A flush of colour rose to my face and I burst out: "Italians are ever ready to confront with a tranquil mind any sacrifice, even that of assuming the appearance of a villain. For when the good of the Fatherland is at stake and the lion's leap is not possible, then let the cunning of the fox be welcomed and blessed. But learn this, and let it be your guide: if ever you find one who, having made the sacrifice, stretches out his hand and demands payment, say freely that he was not born beyond your Alps... or, if he was born there, hold it for certain that he is of bastard race, unworthy of the name he flaunts!" I was escorted back to Flüelen, and then, alone, set out for Goeschenen, whence, facing the cold and the storm, I reached the summit of the Saint Gotthard. From up there, something passed across my face like a foretaste of southern warmth; an air saturate with trembling and with sobbing, with hatred and with love.

And I pressed forward almost at a run, with a heart full to bursting — whether with joy or with pity I could not myself have said. At Varese I was received by Trolli as a dear departed soul miraculously returned to life. And in the hospitality of that family I found ample recompense for my hardships. But in Lombardy the Austrian reaction was raging; and the sight of that hateful uniform rekindled the fever in me... and at that age, with my temperament, it is too arduous a task to put a curb upon one's impetuosity. Friends, privy to what was afoot, opportunely breathed a word of counsel. I must not compromise my hosts, and so I had myself escorted to the frontier.

Having taken leave of my escort and remaining alone, I turned to contemplate the beautiful and unhappy Land which, for the second time, I was compelled to leave. In the majesty of the place and the silence, I passed back in memory over all that had been... the desperate charge of the few, armed with nothing but their right, against the serried masses hideous with bayonets; the gaze of the innocent turned heavenward in vain; the silent tears, the streams of blood. And in the exaltation of that moment my thoughts flew from the foot of the Alps, on and on, to the farthest extremity of Sicily. Everywhere hearts that throbbed, hands that clasped one another in the dark, that in the dark sharpened their blades. Then the great soul of that gentle, vigorous, and unhappy race seemed to permeate me and make my own soul greater. I stretched wide my arms as though I expected to draw into them, lovingly, every one — every one of my companions in bondage. I renewed the inspired oath of Giuseppe Mazzini, and with tears upon my face turned once more upon the road of exile.


### Chapter Eleven

<!-- pages:58-62 -->



IN APRIL of 1852 I was once again in Genoa, working for a certain Ferrari, a merchant tailor, when I was informed that Don Bastiano Barozzi, parish priest of San Pietro in Campo, was living as a refugee in Turin.
