Barozzi, during the siege of Venice, had served as chaplain to a Legion composed almost exclusively of students from Padua. He was one of those priests in whom the cassock did not stifle the love of the Fatherland and who, to passive obedience, preferred to follow the impulses of their own conscience. Italy boasts a good number of such men, and the Church of Rome might have drawn great prestige from them, had she not preferred, in order to please the powerful, to hear their confessions and even lend a hand in their torment.

Deeply versed in ancient history, Don Bastiano judged men through the prism of the Roman world; to the level of Attilio Regolo, of the Scipios, of the Gracchi he would have wished to raise our entire race. During my school holidays, while walking with me along the Ardo and without seeming to make anything of it, he would steep my mind in heroic episodes. I believe that I owe to him, in no small part, my devotion to Liberty.

The priests of murky waters, who dared not wage open war upon him for his patriotic principles, dragged in the Decretals and took exception to his moustache and his goatee, which smacked of the modernist. He stubbornly refused to have them shaved off, and for it he lost the right to say Mass. In his straitened circumstances, he was aided by my father, and that alone had been sufficient for Don Bastiano to cherish towards him a gratitude that bordered on veneration.

It was to this man that I resolved to turn, in the hope of re-entering, with his help, the vortex of action, and thinking that two words spoken face to face are worth more than four long letters, I set off for Turin… on foot, just so as not to lose the habit.

My first care, upon arriving there, was to make my way to the Caffè Nazionale, the favoured haunt of political refugees. Before I reached it, however, I caught sight of a gentleman standing planted before the playbill of a theatre, his face so close to the print that he seemed to be reading it with the tip of his nose. Don Bastiano Barozzi's extreme short-sightedness was known to me, and that single clue was enough to reveal him.

Great was his astonishment at finding me; boundless his joy. He could not contain it and, there and then, without the least regard for the passers-by, pressed two resounding kisses upon my cheeks: "Here... this on my account; this on your father's account!" He then asked me what lucky wind had brought me to Turin. And I:

"Don Bastiano, you know that the di Rudios are not cowards. What is being contemplated, what is being prepared? I have come so as not to grow rusty, and I am counting upon you."

He lowered his eyes and did not answer. But, after a brief silence, as though concluding his own thoughts: "Fortunato Calvi is here. In a few leaps, we can be at his house. Come!"

Fortunato Calvi, the glorious organiser of the Cacciatori delle Alpi, my Colonel, the intimate friend of my family, received me with folded arms, without moving a step, with an expression that was almost one of disdain. The flood of affection that was on the point of bursting forth remained painfully compressed in my breast. I did not even dare to provoke an explanation. But the explanation came of its own accord.

Calvi had been informed by a person "worthy of belief" that I, having left Venice upon his orders, instead of proceeding to Chioggia, had gone over, with arms and baggage, to the Austrian. He even cited the name of one who had sworn to have seen me, behind the trenches, firing upon my own countrymen.

Calumny! — what man has not been, to some degree or another, its victim? Little by little, the breast hardens to its blows so that in time they no longer avail to stir any storm within me. I know bold men who, by their very bearing, almost provoke it of set purpose, for the acrid pleasure of showing themselves superior. But that was the first time I had found myself face to face with it, and its bite was extremely cruel to me.

Nevertheless, I found it easy to demonstrate its absurdity.

Fortunato Calvi, who had no less a desire to find me innocent than I had to prove it to him, smilingly extended his right hand, which I clasped with feverish emotion. While Don Bastiano, mute witness to that scene and a man of exceptionally sensitive feeling, turned upon his heel to conceal a tear.

The consequences of that conversation were, as far as I was concerned, of no slight moment. Giuseppe Mazzini, Luigi Kossuth and Ledru Rollin had openly founded, in London, a European Revolutionary Committee, to which innumerable branches were soon attached. Each People that, having vanquished that nefarious apathy — the cryptogam of souls — understood how regeneration springs from agitation and sacrifice, organised its own. Italy was a hive of them, and by force of circumstance the Committee of Turin held predominance over all the others. Upon it fell, above all, the task of inciting the spirit of rebellion that snaked through the Peninsula and of co-ordinating its movements. Its head, in 1852, was Count Grillanzoni; but the command of eventual military operations fell to Fortunato Calvi.

From Calvi and Count Grillanzoni I received the commission of traversing, as special envoy, the principal cities of Lombardy-Venetia; first to distribute subversive manifestos and bonds of the Mazzinian national loan; then, on a subsequent journey, to inspect the various garrisons and report precise particulars of their strength, their nationality, and their temper.

The system adopted was simple. A passport was procured for me in the name of Carlo Moretti, a silk merchant. It sufficed for me to write: In such-and-such a place I have sold two, three, four bales of white, or green, or yellow silk. The number of bales corresponded to the number of Regiments; the colour indicated their uniform and arm of service. A studied allusion to the particular quality of the merchandise, or to the character of the customer, sufficed to convey whatever other circumstances I deemed it necessary to bring to notice.

I recall the admonition Calvi gave me on the very day of my departure. I was concealing in the stem of my pipe the list of the various Sub-Committees, and I was smiling. Calvi suspected that smile to be the fruit of levity and said sternly: "Do not delude yourself, young man! A slight imprudence, a simple fortuitous incident, and you would be hanged!" I did not fall into the gallows' hands, but I passed within very close reach of them more than once.

My first halt was at Stradella, where I shook hands with Nino Bixio — who had survived the wound sustained at Villa Corsini — and with that Winkler who had been, at Venice, the commander of a Hungarian legion, and who was to close, a few years later, his heroic existence fighting on the banks of the Volturno. Contact with men of such mettle was a source of pleasant excitement for me. But having crossed, with the help of a smuggler, the blue Ticino and ventured but a little way into the domains of Austria, it seemed as if an iron hand were closing around my throat. The oppressor had, after 1849, sharpened his sluggish wits to transform those lands into a pit of hell.

As a general rule, Austria regarded the Provinces subdued by her own arms, and the others reconquered by the allied arms of Russia, as a den of brigands. To vent upon them a barbaric hatred, to gratify the most atrocious vengeances — this appeared, in courtly spheres, the height of political wisdom. Persuaded that he was carrying out a mission entrusted to him by God, Francesco Giuseppe did not even consider himself bound by his own word or by the capitulations he had stipulated. General Klapka, the heroic defender of Komorn, had obtained excellent terms of surrender for the garrison. Every one of them was violated. A code of honour exists even among thieves; but under the pretext of reasons of State, none such existed for Francesco Giuseppe.

Of the thirty-four Hungarian generals — chosen minds, heroic fibres — who had repeatedly compelled the Imperial troops to show their backs, three were condemned to perpetual imprisonment, twelve were with Christian charity shot or hanged. And the carnage would have been greater, had the others not saved themselves by flight and exile. For several months a swarm of executioners, clad — as though in crowning mockery — in the colour of hope, busied themselves in Hungary heaping up the corpses of patriots. All this now seems blotted from the memory of those who shout in the streets their cheers for the contemptible old man. And yet one never ceases to curse the generous souls who, having taken into their own hearts the anguish of the victims, hazard their lives to administer to despots a measure of their own medicine.

In Italy, it is true, Francesco Giuseppe had his talons blunted by Vittorio Emanuele II who, having ascended the throne amid the glacial distrust of his subjects, knew how to bow to the terrible necessities pressing upon him without compromising the future. He refused to sign the peace treaty with Austria unless she undertook the obligation of granting full amnesty to the hundred thousand Italians who, from Lombardy-Venetia, had taken refuge in Piedmont, whether to make war upon her or to escape her persecutions.

Vittorio Emanuele II disposed, even in defeat, of a force not to be lightly reckoned with, and the Emperor kept the pact. Nevertheless, his "pardon" was tacitly conditional upon complete submission to the Austrian yoke. Were pretexts ever lacking? Sequestrations, arbitrary impositions, chains and the gallows were not doled out sparingly by the paternal solicitude of Francesco Giuseppe, and the rod was applied to the backs even of those, men or women, who, without rebelling, gave expression to manly sentiments.

Poor young Italian women, your souls open to every fair and gentle thing; and you mothers, aristocratic or of the people, who were made to pass, with commanded slowness, between two files of hirelings bringing down their cudgels with full force upon your bare limbs — lend me, you, the voice sufficient for a worthy apotheosis of the apostolic beast!

And I name Francesco Giuseppe deliberately, and not Austria; because when the sum of power is concentrated in one man, upon his head, by equal right of justice, must likewise be concentrated the sum of hatred and of infamy.

Mingled among the Italians, I encountered in my wanderings a good number of Hungarian officers, degraded because they had loved their Fatherland and defended it. Harassed with ferocious persistence, they nonetheless had the courage to rebel against the odious commands issued to the detriment of a People bound to them by common misfortune. And there were those among them who, despairing of better times, put an end, by suicide, to a life that had become unendurable.

Fortunately, as though to confound the oppressors, Nature has so disposed her affairs that death itself is the great sower of life, and tyranny itself the great fecundator of every liberty!

Despite the defeats, and the blood drawn from it, and the persecutions, life pulsed in our Fatherland — underground, but intense. Italians who were organising, who were conspiring, or who betrayed the feverish impatience to do so, I found in abundance in every centre. The logical conclusion drawn from the dreadful situation was that one must begin again from the beginning, do better, and conquer at any cost.


### Chapter Twelve

<!-- pages:62-66 -->

<!-- pages:168-172 -->



Of my excursion, which extended, on two separate occasions, as far as the remote Udine, I shall recount in brief some few episodes.

Having baffled the police at Milan, at Como, at Bergamo, at Brescia, I was drawing near to Mantova with a great confidence of spirit... I was reassured by the certainty of not being known there by a living soul. But at the very moment I was passing through the customs-post, violent as a battering-ram's blow, I felt my full name hurled squarely at my chest.

In that instant I believed myself discovered and lost. But the man who stood before me wore a smiling countenance, and his right hand, extended, invited mine. It was none other than the chief of the guards himself. Where I had made his acquaintance I can no longer recall... he was a patriot deep in secret affairs.

When he learned that I was not in Mantova for a change of air, his face darkened. The very day before, the police had carried out a number of arrests, the reason for which remained unknown... he glimpsed a connection between those unexpected severities and my arrival. He advised me to take refuge immediately in his house and remain shut within it. He would endeavour to sound out the ground, and in the evening, over a bite to eat, we should deliberate on what was to be done.

Provoked by the conspiracy in which the gentle figure of the priest Tazzoli plays a leading part, there was beginning at that very moment, in Mantova, the series of refined tortures that drove to an inhuman death the little band of the martyrs of Belfiore. But, returning late in the evening, my host could bring me nothing save vague tidings, and with them a breath of the terror that hung over the city. He had not encountered a single brother who felt secure of being able to pass the night in his own bed.

I lay down in the attic as best I could, without undressing... ready to attempt, at the slightest alarm, a flight across the rooftops.

Early the following morning, after a long conversation with my host, I learned that among the garrison there was an officer who had been educated at the Military Academy of Milan and had been a fellow-pupil of mine: the bonds of earliest youth do not grow worn with the passing of years. Half an hour later I was in the Castello, exchanging a fraternal embrace, or something very like it. I was under the protection of the double-headed eagle.

To explain my presence, I gave it out that I was bound for Belluno to attend upon my father, weighed down by years... I had made a detour to Mantova expressly to see my old friend again; to evoke, in his company, the age of happy carelessness. As by tacit understanding, we each took care not to touch upon subjects of a political nature; yet in the half-festivity that was organised in my honour, I never lost sight of the purpose of my journey, and gleaned, in snatches and by degrees, all the information I was seeking. Two hours before sunset, the officer himself courteously offered to accompany me, by carriage, beyond the city walls. And under his protection I passed through the customs-post without hindrance, receiving a military salute.

However, having escaped one danger that was in all likelihood imaginary, I was compelled to face one all too real at Verona.

I had taken lodgings at the "Scudo di Francia," and scarcely had I shut myself into my room than I made, by instinct, a small inspection of it. It was situated on the third floor; the single window gave onto a courtyard. Opposite me lay the rear wall of another hotel, the "Regina d'Inghilterra." Craning my face downward, I made out a broad penthouse roof that sloped toward the centre of the courtyard. A smell of the stable rose up to that height; I heard the stamping of a horse.

I sat down at the little table beside the lit fireplace and began to write my report on Mantova. All at once, measured footsteps resounded in the corridor. I blew out the candle and cast my letter into the fire, where it turned to ash and flew up through the flue.

There came a knock at the door and an obsequious voice, that of a servant: "Signor Moretti, if you please!" After a brief silence, another voice, gruff and imperious: "In the name of the Law, open up!"

I had already swung myself over the parapet and was clinging there with arms fully extended, so as to diminish the distance between myself and the penthouse roof. The gendarmes began to beat the door with the butts of their rifles... I loosened my grip and let myself drop, close against the wall. The shock was severe, but the damage fell to the roof-tiles. Along them I slid, and reaching the edge, availed myself of a projecting beam and of such gymnastics as my aching limbs permitted, in order to place the gutter between myself and the constables. Crouching beneath it, I heard the door of my room give way with a crash... then a hasty trampling... then silence... then a ringing voice: "Sergeant, I'll wager he jumped from the window!" And the sergeant, who knew better: "Fool! One doesn't jump from a third floor!"

I doubt they even leaned over the sill. The courtyard was deserted. From the half-open stable door a strip of light was cast. It revealed to me a dung-heap. I leapt into it...

A little later I walked resolutely into the kitchen of the "Regina d'Inghilterra:" scullions and servants, busy about their work, did not so much as raise their heads. A cook, his great paunch wrapped in a white apron, a white cap on his head, was blowing on his ladle at that very moment... He stared at me in stupefaction; but he had not yet recovered from his surprise when I was already making for the door of the dining room. The waiters took me in all probability for some scullion's friend. In a few seconds I found myself in the open air.

Where to pass the night? The police, rendered more suspicious still by my disappearance, would not have permitted me to find rest in any hotel. I wandered adrift until I found myself in front of the Post. Several stage-coaches stood there in a row with their doors flung wide... into one of them I threw myself at full length and crouched there until the approach of dawn. More than once, between sleep and waking, I heard the night-watch pass beneath my feet. Had I snored, in all probability I should not be here to tell that adventure of mine.

A yet more devilish peril I brought upon myself, through excess of zeal, on my return to Milan. On the advice of a certain Valentini, a student of chemistry, I had lodged, during my stays in the Lombard capital, at the home of his sweetheart; a woman who lived from the proceeds of a little dairy-shop she kept, and by taking in boarders. Several finance guards attended her table, as did a Hungarian sergeant, a relentless tormentor of the pianoforte. I felt as though I were entrenched within the very enemy's camp.

The dairywoman, a fleshy creature with a face like a fresh *panettone*, was of the most ardently Italian sympathies, so much so that on my previous visit I had entrusted to her, for destruction, a bundle of revolutionary pamphlets that had become superfluous. Now she informed me that she had preserved it intact, at the bottom of a chest of drawers. I rejoiced at the news... before returning to Piedmont I might, with a good posting of bills, put the finishing touch to my work.

I spoke of it to the Revolutionary Committee, which, exasperated in those days by the wretched news arrived from Mantova, approved my resolution. And among those patriots I found one who, of his own accord, offered to lend me his assistance... a stocky workman, with a broad scar upon his face. He told me not his name; he was an upholsterer, like the poor Antonio Sciesa.

A cold, rainy night, the streets deserted. We began at the locality known as the Carrobbio; I on one pavement, he on the other... we pasted up our proclamation with those small wafers which the Milanese call *obiadìtt*. We were heading toward the Duomo, and already I could see the façade of the magnificent temple gleaming white before me, when from the far side of the square, and precisely beneath the Coperto dei Figini, there rang out in the silence that terrible warning: "Run, run!"

I saw the stocky figure of the upholsterer flee at full speed toward the Piazza Mercanti, and a patrol of policemen, bursting forth from some dark alleyway, gave chase with shouts. Had it not been for his fraternal solicitude, I might perhaps have slipped away without attracting notice; but by launching that cry of his, he had put the constables on the alert as well; and a goodly number of them advanced toward me at a run.

There were no alternatives. I flung down the pamphlets and fled headlong through a labyrinth of narrow streets, until, breathless, I found myself confronted with the line of the city walls rearing up before me. I climbed the ramp on all fours; but with my pursuers at my heels and a customs-post, with its respective guard-house, to right and to left, I might consider myself trapped. Momentary shelter I found behind one of those great horse-chestnut trees, and for an instant I flattered myself that, uncertain of the direction I had taken, the wretches would turn back, crestfallen, upon their steps; or that, having gained the rampart, they would all veer to the right, leaving me free to steal away silently to the left. But they were not so simple-minded.

Having reached the top, they divided into two groups which, turning their backs to one another, advanced simultaneously on either side, peering with caution behind every tree. As at Verona, I found myself in the terrible alternative of submitting, sooner or later, to a small jump from which, by virtue of the noose, there would be no recovery, or of braving the immediate dangers of a great jump, which still left me some measure of a chance. I resolved upon the great jump.

How long I lay stunned down there at the bottom, in truth I never knew. Fortunately I had fallen upon soft ground, and perhaps it was the rain, which fell as though poured over me bodily, that served to bring me back to my feet. I set out, painfully and soaked to the bone, toward Pavia.

In what condition did I arrive there? It is not difficult to imagine. Three days under covers and I was once again in fine fettle. The brother of the Giovine Italia who had tended me with such care was desirous that I should remain longer; but the land of salvation was too near for me not to feel the fever to set foot on it once more. He accompanied me as far as the bridge that crosses the Ticino and joins the Lombard bank to the Piedmontese, and there we parted.

And then — two, three, a crowd of wild-eyed people came running noisily to the mouth of the bridge. From the phrases they exchanged at full voice, I understood that an unknown man, some smuggler or some hot-head pursued by a patrol, had thrown himself from the parapet. They had struck him mid-flight with a gunshot in the back, and were now endeavouring to fish him out.

I skirted a wide berth, and, having found a way to get myself ferried across by passing myself off as a deserter, I arrived, as midnight was approaching, at Stradella. The Casa Winkler was still lit. I entered and found before me a group of patriots who received me as though I had fallen from the clouds. A secret message, arrived from Pavia, had brought the news that I had been discovered, pursued, and mortally wounded while seeking escape by leaping into the Ticino. Fortunato Calvi had already been informed of it by telegraph.

I departed immediately and found Calvi still in bed. Barely recovered from his surprise, his first thought was for don Bastiano Barozzi, with whom he had already arranged to meet at nine o'clock at the Caffè del Pantheon. The good priest entered it pale and almost beside himself. His short-sightedness prevented him from seeing me, and Calvi went to meet him. Catching sight of Calvi, he exclaimed: "Ah, what a calamity! What will my benefactor say when he learns that I repaid him by driving his son to ruin?"

— And what would you say, if Carlo were to walk in here, sound and smiling?

The face of Barozzi underwent convulsive movements. All at once, the knot that was strangling him loosened, and he burst into a flood of tears.

Then the atheist threw himself upon the neck of the priest. Which tends to demonstrate that the hypothesis of God is not necessary to men in order that they may love one another as brothers!


### Chapter Thirteen

<!-- pages:66-74 -->



I KNOW of no Italian insurrectionary movement that brought upon Giuseppe Mazzini so many calumnies, so much bitterness, so many desertions as that of 1853.

The so-called practical men, with that short-sighted practicality which consists in leaving the world to run undisturbed even to its own ruin, maintained that a People recently bled white and broken could not respond with spirit to a call to arms; could not willingly submit to new sacrifices. And they pronounced, with great solemnity, that revolutions come to pass when they ripen of themselves, not when one wills it so.

A singular maxim which, stripped to its core, would lead to this other: "To win one's independence, to vindicate one's rights, nothing is better than to recline in one's armchair, leaving to the Sun the care of ripening events, as it ripens gourds."

The movements organised by Giuseppe Mazzini were never, in truth, either revolution or war; but they indubitably prepared the way for both and rendered them necessary.

Moreover, those of February 1853 were not initiated by the great agitator, but by precisely that People which, were one to trust the serious-minded men, would have had no thought save to make itself comfortable among accomplished facts and its own misery.

And Mazzini himself has said as much, and has written it, and has proved it. Yet even today there is no lack of supermen, the lustre and ornament of our Italy, who, unable to refute the declarations of that great man who never lied, skip over them with masterly ease and continue to extol the wonders of the armchair, persisting in the old calumnies.

The *Fratellanza*, a secret society bent upon insurrection, was already strongly organised in Milan three years after every hope for Italy had seemed to wither. Composed of several thousand workmen, it was subdivided into various sections distinguished by the letters of the alphabet. Its existence was utterly unknown to the Austrian Police, to Mazzini, to the intellectuals, to the aristocratic class, who, for all that, shared with it a common hatred of the foreign yoke.

Its principal organiser, the dyer Assi, entered into communication with Mazzini when he judged that the *Fratellanza* had sufficient strength to act. Assi requested arms, money, and leaders capable of bringing a plan of attack to a successful issue.

Mazzini received the news as proof that the propagation of his ideas had brought forth good seed even among the common multitude; but he did not place full credence in Assi's assurances regarding the numerical strength and the seriousness of purpose of the new organisation, until Major Eugenio Brizzi of Jesi and other informants, sent by him secretly to Milan, had confirmed them in every particular.

Nevertheless, he was urging patience when, in December 1852, Austria, giving fresh vent to her ferocity, crowned the ramparts of Belfiore with gibbets and, having chosen a victim from every rank of citizens, hanged them there.

In the face of this grim provocation, it seemed to the workmen of the *Fratellanza* a crime to deliberate any longer. They notified Mazzini that they had resolved to act, even without his aid and without his consent. Mazzini's inescapable duty then became that of seconding the inevitable, of endeavouring to ensure that this generous initiative should not remain isolated and fruitless.

In the last days of December 1852, charged with a mission received directly from Count Grillanzoni, I departed for Milan, and at midnight of the day on which I arrived, after a brief wait beneath the clock in the Piazza Mercanti, I delivered a packet of instructions to a man who, emerging from the shadow, had given me the agreed signal.

The definitive plan of the insurrection thus reached the hands of Major Brizzi himself, who was charged with overseeing its execution. The plan was, in its principal lines, the work of Pietro Fortunato Calvi. Its fundamental basis was the principle that weapons must be taken from the enemy by surprise, after his forces had been thrown into disorder by the killing of his officers.

By a privilege of regulation, these officers slept, as a general rule, not in barracks but in private lodgings. Note had been taken of their addresses, and their customary meeting-places were, of course, known as well.

The Austrian military establishment was, among other positions, quartered within the walls of Milan at the Palace of the General Command, at that of the Grand Guard, and at the Castello.

The first was guarded — rather than defended — by a picket of twenty-five soldiers. Their weapons stood ranged upon the gun-rack, beneath the passageway of the gatehouse, before which a vigilant sentinel paced unceasingly. The other soldiers whiled away the hours stretching their limbs in the courtyard or smoking in the barrack-rooms. At five in the afternoon, the Governor, the Generals, and the General Staff dined in that palace.

To reduce the sentinel to helplessness by a sudden assault would not have been a particularly difficult surprise. As a small cord draws a rope and the rope hauls the great cable, that first slight success would have put into the hands of the insurgents some thirty rifles, the disarmed picket, and the chiefs of the garrison.

Something similar, but with more powerful means, was to be attempted at the Grand Guard, which housed some hundred of the ruffians; but above all at the Castello, which contained in its armoury twelve thousand muskets. The two armourers who inspected them daily were men of ours. They undertook to leave the doors ajar, so that the time lost in breaking them down should not rob us of a precious moment.

In the premises of the Grand Guard, the insurgents were to entrench themselves and establish their own headquarters, to give a centre to the revolution. And from that centre, clad in the uniforms stripped from the dead and the prisoners, small patrols were to fan out who, beating the drum-roll, would have induced officers to emerge from their dwellings. On the threshold they would find those who would undertake to seal their lips — and for ever.

The sixth of February was chosen for the strike upon the enemy, a day of carnival festivity and of pay for the soldiers. Their quarters would thereby be left almost deserted. The surprise would thus have a far greater probability of success; and our men would have the greater ease to pour into the cafés and taverns, and to renew, though for a more sublime purpose, the blood-tide of the Vespers.

To smooth the path to victory, Giuseppe Mazzini had solicited and obtained from Kossuth, who was on the point of departing for the United States of America, a revolutionary proclamation addressed to his compatriots quartered principally in the barracks of San Francesco; and pledges had already been received that, at the first sign of a serious insurrection, the ill-subdued sons of valiant Hungary would have made common cause with their oppressed Italian brothers.

As the crowning touch of the enterprise — the barricades! The contractor for the city's lighting, in order to protect those who would raise them, had pledged to leave Milan in darkness. And to defend them, Giuseppe Mazzini had caused certain projectiles of a new invention to be smuggled into the city, to be hurled upon the assailants in the manner of bombs.

One must add that the students of Pavia, secretly consulted, had pledged to hasten to the aid of the capital; the agreed signal, a blaze upon the spire of the Cathedral. Acerbi was to have commanded them — and they asked nothing more than weapons. Mazzini had provided these by way of the Po.

Nor was the preparation of simultaneous movements beyond the walls limited to this. In Piedmont, the throng of political exiles kept watch, impatient for action. It was necessary to open them a broad and easy road to descend, in organised formation, upon the offensive. Giuseppe Mazzini entrusted this charge to Fortunato Calvi, who conceived the bold plan of seizing Lake Maggiore, a design I shall explain shortly.

And Aurelio Saffi, after having knocked at the doors of the wealthier patriots — on which occasion Agostino Depretis, the future Prime Minister, contributed twenty-five thousand lire — went to Bologna, defying death, and was fomenting, with fair hopes of success, the insurrection of that city and of the Romagna.

To put it briefly, Giuseppe Mazzini had secured the co-operation of the centres which might have interposed a barrier between the Milan garrison and the various Austrian nuclei scattered about the Peninsula. Fortunato Calvi confided in me later, at Zurich, that he had cherished the hope of turning, within a fortnight, with the revolutionary forces, the Austrian army of Italy, and of cutting it off at the Alps from its base of replenishment. It sufficed, to give spirit and time to Lombardy to rise, that Milan might hold firm and fight in her streets for at least a few days. And Italy would have won her independence by her own strength, as Greece had won hers, as the United States had won theirs, avoiding the fatal aftermath left by hybrid alliances, murky intrigues, and compromises that have clouded her greatness and whose effects, unhappily, will continue to be felt for a long time yet.

But here I do not argue, I narrate.

The workmen of the *Fratellanza*, with the exception of the leaders, were ignorant of the particulars of the insurrectionary plan; but they knew that on the sixth of February the blow would be struck. They had unearthed some hundred pistols and, on Mazzini's advice, had set themselves to fashioning daggers and knives. Even the rivets from the boats of their Naviglio were prised out and fixed upon shafts — everything serves the desperation of a People!

And I, seized by the general fever, was already weighing, in order to forestall them, all the foreseeable adverse contingencies of the assault upon the Grand Guard, to which Brizzi had appointed me, when I received from Count Grillanzoni the order to proceed immediately to Genoa and to await his instructions there.

These instructions were: to meet with a certain affiliated member, to have counted out to me, in Mazzini's name, four thousand lire in gold; to carry them to Sander and Radonitz, who were awaiting me at Locarno. To follow, on the journey, the road along Lake Maggiore and to watch whether anything unusual might be observed there indicating that Calvi's plans had been discovered, or that, by obstructing them, their modification had become necessary. To meet, on my return from Locarno, with Calvi himself, who would be found at Intra, and to report to him punctually all that I had seen and heard.

Austria maintained on the Verbano a flotilla of gunboats which ordinarily lay at anchor opposite Luino. Assigned to the surveillance of the shores and to the passenger service was the steamship *Radetzky*, which plied the lake daily and always had on board a company of soldiers. These, during the crossing, remained below decks, leaving their rifles ranged near the hatchway under the watch of a sentinel. With the sentinel overpowered, it seemed more than probable that one might seize the weapons and shut the other soldiers below in the hold. Sander and Radonitz, formerly officers in the Austrian navy and subsequently passed into the service of the Venetian Republic, would have taken command of the steamship and disposed of it as of an Italian vessel.

Pietro Fortunato Calvi had determined that the *Radetzky*, putting in at Luino well into the evening, should continue to fly the Austrian flag, with myself and a handful of our men on board, clad in the hated uniform.

It was necessary to approach as friends the gunboats unprepared for action; necessary to seize them by surprise. Then, having towed them out into open water, to turn their murderous muzzles upon the small garrison. Pietro Fortunato Calvi, having organised a sound body of patriots, would have assaulted Luino from the landward side. With the Italian flag raised over that strategic point, masters of the lake, the munitions, arms, and armed men coming from Piedmont would have found a comparatively open road to the very walls of Milan.

When I arrived at Arona there was still some hours to go before the departure of the *Radetzky*. I spent them walking along the shore of the lake. I recall that the magnificence of the landscape, all green and Alpine despite the winter, made upon my spirit an impression of such sweetness that, for a moment, it all but unmanned me. And I marvelled that men, being able to enjoy in fraternal ease so much of paradise, should spoil it for themselves by clutching at one another perpetually, as though nothing could take root in their breasts save base and ferocious passions.

But no sooner had the bell of the *Radetzky* announced that departure was imminent than my rebellious spirit reasserted itself. I thought: "Beautiful is our Fatherland. For that very reason they put the knife to our throats and attempt to make it their own. Therefore, all the more holy, as it is the more fierce, the rage that drives us to defend it. Let them recross the Alps and we shall be brothers again."

The steamship stood out into the open water. I lit my pipe and, walking with slow steps, this way and that, I endeavoured to assume the air of one who contemplates, in a kind of rapture, the landscape gliding past. But my mind was fixed upon my secret purpose. I saw the carbines ranged near the hatchway; I scrutinised — weighed, almost — the sentinel who, with German rigidity, with the regularity of a pendulum, trod back and forth across the planking; I heard chaotic voices rising from the depths of the vessel and understood them — they spoke, with coarse candour, of haunches and rumps, punctuating their discourse with coarse laughter. Nor did there emerge at any point during the entire voyage, from that dark recess, the pomaded moustache of a *kaiserlich* officer. Faithful to their orders, those geometric heads!

Our coup de main seemed to me so certain of success that more than once I bitterly regretted being alone and unprepared to attempt it.

But at Locarno I found spirits less willing than my own. Sander and Radonitz received me, not without a certain stiffness, at the house of one Olivieri, a dealer in sundry goods and also in arms. They took and counted carefully the gold which I drew from my money-belt. But when it came to settling the preliminaries of the action, the buts and the ifs grew thick and solid as a wall.

A few days before — I was told — two Hungarians with whom secret dealings had been in train had been thrown into prison; two of our men. "The Authorities kept them in complete isolation, and nothing had transpired regarding the grounds of their arrest. Why such precautions, such mystery? There was cause for alarm at far less than this."

Radonitz was firmly persuaded that the Police had caught some scent of what was being plotted. To attempt new ventures would be to walk headlong into the net. He urged that courage must not be confounded with rashness. It was a matter of the lives, sacred to him, of so many patriots. What would be lost by delay; by ascertaining, with due caution, the temper of an enemy so powerful and so cruel? Wisdom lay not in haste, but in acting in such manner that victory should prove certain and all but inevitable.

To all this I replied that if the arrest of some brother were enough to deflect Italians from their purposes, it would be as well to renounce action for ever. Since '49, not a day had passed without the Austrian baton being at work, without some chain being riveted afresh. The Police could not have suspected a movement as vast as ours without taking extraordinary precautions, which could not have escaped the notice of our brothers scattered everywhere. No alarm had come from any quarter whatsoever. I myself had traversed the lake from end to end, aboard an Austrian vessel, with my eye alert, and had noticed nothing — nothing at all — out of the ordinary. Moreover, the chances of discovery were in proportion to the time spent in organising the conspiracy and to the number of those who took part in it. All the more urgent, therefore, appeared the necessity for swift and almost lightning-like action. Fortunato Calvi must already have departed from Turin; he would be at Intra on the morrow. To him alone belonged the decision as to whether delay was more or less opportune. Our inaction would have robbed him of the possibility of choice and would have amounted to desertion. At all events, I had come to Locarno not to debate but to act. My orders were to report at Intra all that I had seen and heard. I would set out again alone, though it cost me my life.

Present while I spoke was Olivieri's wife. She left without a word and returned shortly after, bearing a packet of revolutionary proclamations and a bundle of daggers. She helped me, with her own hands, to conceal both upon my person, and all argument was thus cut short. On the following morning I embarked on the *Arno*, a Piedmontese steamship, and descended swiftly along the Verbano. Sander and Radonitz had remained ashore!

What a profound change in those few hours! The overcast sky heralded the fall of snow; the lake grey, and almost ashen. From the Alps there blew an icy wind that seemed to have swept away all festive life from the shores and the hillsides.

I found aboard the *Arno* muffled-up passengers with an air of self-absorbed preoccupation; common faces betraying the anxieties of commerce. My attention came at last to fix itself upon the captain, a bony man with a phenomenally large head. He was absorbed in the reading of a newspaper and cast only an occasional glance at the course. Who was he? What sentiments animated him? Could the name and the sufferings of the Fatherland find a place in his heart?

I answered myself with yes, and committed a grave error. He had suddenly risen and, as if following the impulse of a fleeting thought, walked with hurried steps to another part of the deck. He had left his newspaper on a seat. I drew forth a revolutionary proclamation, concealed it within the folds of that newspaper, and returned at a run to my place.

I did not wait long! A face painfully surprised, a knitting of brows, a swaying of that heavy head. He fixed me with a pair of eyes I have never since forgotten. Perhaps they were set in the face of the most immaculate Aristides that Piedmont had ever produced; but to me they seemed steeped, and all but drunk, with idiotic commiseration and bestial menace. I dissembled by whistling a cabaletta by Verdi into the wind that roared about me.

I am at Intra. Among the knot of curious idlers gathered to see the steamship arrive, I catch — as one might snatch something from the air — a well-known signal. I followed at a distance the person who had given it and was led to a solitary villa. The unknown brother remained outside and I went in. I found myself face to face with Fortunato Calvi.

What a flood of feeling, and how many things I should have wished to say to him; how many others, I imagine, he would have wished to confide to me! But we had to content ourselves with clasping each other's hand at length. For a moment, neither he nor I could speak a word. When I at last managed to unlock my lips, I said: "Are the sentinels at their posts?"

Having received the assurance that they were, I drew from my person the proclamations and every one of the daggers save one, and laid them on a table. I continued:

— All here and in the neighbourhood. They await the arms and the signal.

— I have had news of him from Milan. (1) It was an imprudence; but his shrewdness stood him in good stead. He is now at Lugano. Let the arms do their duty; the head watches and will do its own.

I then gave him a full account of my journey, and concealed nothing of the strange hesitation of Sander and Radonitz, who had failed to appear at the rendezvous. I had not yet finished when a window opened and in the aperture appeared the face of the same affiliated member who had guided me thither. He said, in an agitated voice: "Run, run! The carabinieri are at the gate. They are looking for you!"

In a single bound I was in the garden, which I crossed at a run, taking care that the view of the gentlemen at the gate should remain intercepted by the house. I climbed a tree and vaulted the enclosing wall. Four strides and I was at the lake-shore. On the shingle, chained to a landing-stage, several small boats lay drawn up in a row. One alone, propelled by two oarsmen, was gliding along close to the shore, as though seeking a place to run in and ground. Before the oarsmen, stupefied, had time to open their mouths, I had leapt aboard and was finding my balance amid that small shell. "Twenty francs for you! Quickly, to Locarno!" They understood and bent to the oars.

And then, followed by a crowd of children, two gendarmes arrived gesticulating on the shore. One jumped onto the shingle and busied himself, but in vain, with the boats. They were locked with padlocks, I believe. The other, flailing his arms like a Dutch windmill, shouted: "Stop, stop!" I judged it prudent not to entrust my salvation to another's compassion and, grasping my double-barrelled pistol: "Either to Locarno, or to the next world!"

From the shore came laughter and applause. The two carabinieri scanned the surface of the lake — no hope of assistance from that quarter. They vented their fury by shaking their fists at the horizon.

(1) That Mazzini was in Milan in 1853 was never stated either by him or by others, so far as I am aware. But the Count di Rudio insists that Fortunato Calvi's assertion admits of no ambiguity.

C. C.


### Chapter Fourteen

<!-- pages:74-76 -->



THE EXCITEMENT of the moment did not prevent me from reflecting upon the strangeness of my situation. I, a patriot, and no mean one (if the intensity of one's feelings counts for anything), was being hunted out, almost as an enemy, from the one strip of free Italian soil, and I was feverishly longing to cross the median line of the lake in order to return, as the saying goes, to the shadow of the yellow and black — the very object of our execration become all at once the symbol of my salvation. I reached it, and breathed more freely. But it was a temporary salvation, and fate decreed that I could not attain the Swiss frontier without grievous hardship.

The weather, which had been threatening since morning, turned for the worse, and from the Alps, towards which we had set our prow, there came, more than wind, a full tempest. The waters, which presaged it, took on a formidable rolling, and our frail craft was tossing about wildly. I soon perceived that we were making no headway, and, seizing a spare pair of oars, I made myself a third in that desperate struggle between human will and the tyranny of the storm. But deeming it imprudent to turn my back upon the two boatmen, I was compelled to resign myself to keeping my face turned towards the Alps. And then there fell, slanting across us, a frozen sleet — nay, a whirlwind of tiny needles of ice that grazed the skin, that tormented my eyes, that took away my breath. And the waves, foaming at their crests, curled into shavings by the wind, threatened to capsize the little vessel. There was no doubting it: the elements had declared for Austria, and more than once I thought that I had escaped so many dangers only to end miserably in that pond of shad.

Numbness of the limbs came upon us, and then the night. It was both necessity and salvation to abandon Locarno and make for the nearest republican land, Brissago. We arrived there with ice-crusted moustaches and broken bones. Nor was it given to me to show my deliverers the full measure of my gratitude; for one of them, the very same to whom I counted out the agreed price and a comparatively generous gratuity, clapped a rough hand upon my shoulder and said: "Whatever comes of it, we served you because that double-mouthed implement of yours admitted of no argument. Go about your business; we shall look after our own. Good night and good fortune!"

Half an hour, and I was between the sheets. Scarce had I stretched myself out than Sander and Radonitz, the captain of the *Arno* and Fortunato Calvi, the lake and all Italy vanished into the dark.

The following morning, I found my anxieties waiting for me upon the pillow. After long reflection, I persuaded myself that my failure had been entirely personal. Pietro Fortunato Calvi, remaining free, had certainly known how to organise everything, even without my assistance. It was now the 4th of February — therefore the useful time had not yet passed — an initiative delayed, not lost.

I resolved to make my way on foot to Locarno, and without further ado I set out. The wind had dropped, but the snow was falling in broad flakes — the mountain and the valley were white with it. In that fresh snow my legs sank to the calf, and beneath my boots there formed a tenacious clog that robbed me, treacherously, of both my footing and my strength. But when I arrived before the house of the Olivieris and saw, through the gratings, a light filtering through, and deduced therefrom that people were still waking, still at work, my weariness vanished as if by enchantment.

The agreed signal opened the door to me. I was led into a room where, seated at a small table, their faces lit by the oil lamp, sat Sanders and Radonitz. Standing with arms folded, I found a third person: Pietro Fortunato Calvi.

Greetings and congratulations! I cut them short to demand news of the situation: "What have you arranged? What hopes remain to us?"

Sanders and Radonitz shrugged their shoulders. They were waiting for the Police to withdraw its ambushes and fall asleep again. My mishap, as related by Calvi, had not only confirmed them in their intention to let matters drag, but had further persuaded them that the Austro-Hungarian authorities, grown suspicious, had incited those of the Republic. "We are no longer safe here!" said Radonitz. "By the by, how are you placed for funds?"

And without awaiting my reply, he reached out his hand towards certain rolls of money which, in my first anxiety, I had not even noticed.

It was as though a veil had fallen from my eyes. I thought: "Late — yet in time!" And turning to Radonitz, who had unrolled one of the packets: "Since you consider our enterprise as having failed, restore the sum I brought you. It was destined to purchase arms, and not for your own benefit. It was counted out to me in the name of Mazzini; to Mazzini I shall render it back."

The expression assumed at that moment by Radonitz is among my most diverting memories. He became haughty again: "I have given you a regular receipt; your responsibility is fully covered. Do not concern yourself with what does not regard you."

With toil and sweat, the men who groaned under the lash had scraped together that gold — bread taken from their own mouths, from the mouths of their children. I persisted. And Radonitz, pale as wax: "Young man, you are a subordinate here; take care not to forget it."

Sanders, who had remained seated with his elbows propped upon the table and his face resting on his fists, thought it opportune to intervene:

"How do you expect us to make our escape, to preserve our work for better times, if we lack the means of doing so?"

I replied: "I do not believe that the watch-chain and the trinkets hanging upon your breast and your gold watch are quite indispensable to you. You swore to give to the Fatherland your time and your blood and your soul and everything — would you now deny her four baubles?"

I turned my gaze to the man who had not yet spoken, who had remained standing in the shadow — to Fortunato Calvi. He gave me a brief nod of assent. Then in a single bound I was at the door; I shut it fast, and put the key in my pocket. Then, drawing out that double-barrelled implement which at Intra had already rendered me such signal service, I levelled it, crying out: "Enough of words! Either the money is restored, or no one leaves this room!"

I replaced the gold in my money-belt and pressed the hand of Fortunato Calvi. To the others I gave the military salute — not without a touch of irony. Then I departed.


### Chapter Fifteen

<!-- pages:76-79 -->

<!-- pages:77-80 -->



THUS on the fateful 6th of February I found myself neither at the assault on the Gran Guardia in Milan, nor aboard the Radetzky on Lake Maggiore.

Instead, early in the morning, I was climbing a steep path which, branching off from the Ceresio, a few steps from Lugano, led up to a villa gleaming white as the snow that surrounded it. Its green grilles were the sole contrasting note upon that vast whiteness. Having reached the esplanade facing the villa, I paused to contemplate it, and through my mind passed the words of Fortunato Calvi: "Let the arms do their duty, the mind keeps watch and will do its own!"

But if the climbing up there had been an easy enough undertaking, being presented to the man was not to prove so easy for me. The surroundings, outwardly solitary and tranquil, concealed hearts beating fast, and were as though saturated with a busy silence. I felt, beyond the walls, the suspicious eye that watched me, and in the sturdy manservant who approached me, stooping and low-voiced, I glimpsed the conspirator ready to draw himself up and, if need be, to attempt even a sudden stroke. Rather than answer his questions, I pronounced the password and extended my hand to him as to a brother. I added that I was the bearer of a letter of introduction to the Master, on behalf of Colonel Calvi.

But the Master, being most exceedingly occupied, had given positive orders not to be disturbed. And since I persisted and urged the urgency of the matter, the pretended manservant snatched the letter from my right hand, assuring me he would deliver it in person. It was a piece of luck that he invited me to enter.

I am in a ground-floor room, spacious and cold… on the mantelpiece were four pieces of firewood neatly arranged, but unlit. A writing-desk, a few bamboo chairs… bare walls. Here too the predominant note is whiteness. In the recess of the wide window, between two white curtains, hangs a cage of brass wire, wrapped in a rosy gauze… within, a canary was hopping about. I planted myself before it; but my thoughts turned at once elsewhere… to the little great man, toward whom there converged, from every quarter of Europe, so much impetus of hatred, so much impetus of love. I expected to see the stalwart "manservant" reappear at any moment to announce that I was expected, that I should be welcome. I heard the door creak on its hinges and turned. The man standing before me was slight, dressed in black, with a black kerchief at his throat. He had a high, smooth brow; pale cheeks, almost olive-tinged. I felt my soul as though enveloped in the warm and serene gaze of Giuseppe Mazzini.

Between his fingers he held the letter which had informed him of our failure, and he had hastened out to receive a fuller account of the particulars. He listened, with great composure, to my narration, and when, by way of conclusion, I drew out the money which I had wrested from avaricious selfishness, he took it and put it away, without counting it, in the drawer of the writing-desk. Not a word, not a flutter of an eyelid!… whether he deemed honesty unworthy of praise, or whether weighty thoughts prevented him from pausing over lesser matters.

He sat down and motioned me to do likewise. But I remained standing and apart, and while he meditated, I observed with wonder that slender, spare frame, the pallor of the face, the intellectual profile, so exquisitely delicate. The greying head seemed to me too heavy for the neck that bore it — an impression perhaps accentuated by his attitude; for the little great man was supporting his broad brow with lean fingers.

I felt perplexed. How had a man such as this been able to sustain so many hardships, so many intellectual struggles, such fury of battles? Whence came the strength that had roused so many from inertia, formed so many consciences, kindled so many fires, breathed new life into the Fatherland?

No sooner did he begin to speak than I was persuaded that a great heart has great impulses, even within a slender breast. One is commonly counselled: "Write as you speak." Giuseppe Mazzini, in grave moments, spoke instead as he wrote.

I summarise and piece together the various parts of his discourse:

"Nothing has been attempted… therefore nothing is lost! We must set ourselves to the work again… but not with such men as these. He who meditates an enterprise and trembles and cavils, labours for his own enemy and unknowingly digs the abyss that will swallow himself and the hopes of the Fatherland.

"Today's sun is destined to illuminate bold new deeds. A people that has never been sparing of its own blood will hurl itself, with the courage of desperation, unarmed against the powerful oppressor. The streets of its city, full of gaiety, will be transformed into a pit of groans and slaughter. If heroism avail not, if the brute force of lead and numbers triumph once more over right, Milanese youth will tread alone the road of exile, will climb alone the steps of the scaffold. But when the hatred of present oppression, the virile impulses, the disregard of suffering, shall have the power to ripen fate, we too shall gather the fruit of victory, we brothers of Italy. The Milanese rise and sacrifice themselves for a cause that is ours. And should we confine ourselves to the part of idle spectators? Shall we demonstrate to the enemy and to the world, through our selfishness, that our chains are well fitted to us; that the dignity of free men is unbecoming to our souls?

"Let Milan be certain that beyond her walls she has aroused sympathy of purpose and of passion… and not barren sympathy! In arduous trials, destinies hang by a thread; nor is succour limited to arms and munitions. Let our solidarity sustain the arm of the bold: let our ardour strengthen the sacred flame that drives them to action. It is imperative, it is imperative!"

He remained a moment in thought, and his lean fingers tormented his smooth brow, as though to wrest from it, by main force, an inspiration. "How to provide," he murmured, "in such straits of time?" Then suddenly, with great emphasis:

"Varese is the point! Access to reinforcements is easy there; the garrison small; the citizens patriotic and impetuous, the *Giovine Italia* well rooted, the assurances I have received serious and almost tangible. The thing is to cast a spark — *'Parva favilla gran fiamma seconda.'* A small spark kindles a great flame! A thorny undertaking, not a hopeless one. Let a man depart from Lugano this very day, and tomorrow he is on the spot; he meets with the leaders, distributes manifestos, spreads the news of the Milanese insurrection, revives men's spirits, calls them to arms, takes the enemy in detail, organises the barricades, sounds the tocsin, rouses the countryside.

"For this, a legion is not needed — one man suffices, but a man who has the cunning and the audacity of the feline, the sacred enthusiasm of heroes; a man who would prefer, if need be, to die with arms in hand rather than live a slave. Where is he to be found?"

Such words, spoken by such a man and at such a moment, could not glide over my soul without mark. My youthful exuberance overflowed and, grown bold, I dared to presume upon myself. I said:

"Master, I began to serve my Country when on these lips the first hairs had scarcely appeared. I put my life at hazard in Venice, in Rome. Emigrated to Paris, I did my utmost to oppose the fratricidal arms of Napoleon. And Fortunato Calvi can attest that, even recently, in Lombardy-Venetia, I more than once confronted, for the good cause, the danger of dying on the gallows. Assuredly, I have not accomplished great things, but—"

— "I understand, I understand! The spirit of sacrifice cannot be inexhaustible."

— "No, no, Master! Have you not taught us that right proceeds from a duty fulfilled? I have fulfilled mine, as best I knew. Now I claim the right to be deemed worthy of offering once more my arm and my blood for the benefit of my country."

He raised his head, and once again I felt myself as though enveloped in the warm and serene gaze of the Master. He said simply: "The enterprise of Varese is yours!"

I departed at dusk, escorted by several smugglers… the smugglers who gave Austria so much trouble and whom resurgent Italy, prodigal as she is of marble and bronze to the heroes… of the sixth day, appears to have wholly forgotten. We walked over crags and precipices in silence; I marvelling at their agility, they in all likelihood at mine. At daybreak we were at the frontier.

There stood in those days, near the point where we were to cross, a small cottage. At the top of its door was fixed a branch of pine. This signified, in the figurative language of those parts: "Here one drinks;" just as myrtle signified in ancient times "Here one makes love!" The leader of the party directed his steps toward it and all of us, one at a discreet distance from another, followed in his wake.

That innkeeper, positioned astride two states, sold wine and grappa in order to give himself… a social standing. His greater earnings derived from the secret understandings he had formed with the bold spirits who defrauded the customs. The smugglers descending into Lombardy and those who, from the Republic, penetrated into the Kingdom, made a halt at his cottage… to spy out the land, to receive orders, to deposit there merchandise and subversive literature.

Around the innkeeper gathered those who had escorted me. They fell to talking in low voices, in an agitated manner; I, prudently apart, was contemplating several pictures pasted upon the wall. Then we drank to one another's health and drank again. Grown impatient, I went out into the open air and cast an eager eye outward, far and wide across the plain below, in the direction of Varese.

But just as I was laying plans for days of endeavour, and in some small measure also of heroism, there arrived at full gallop a courier who was destined to thrust me back into grey life, among the resigned, among the unknown. I was so absorbed that I noticed nothing until I heard my name called. "Ah, it is you! I had already despaired of reaching you. I bring you a most urgent letter!" He rummaged in his greatcoat and handed it to me. As I broke the seal, my hand was trembling. I read:

*"Return immediately, your sacrifice would be in vain. In Milan all is lost."* "Mazzini."

Brief lines, immense calamity! To my eyes they seemed traced by a pen dipped in the blood of martyrs. I retraced my steps in haste and, before the setting of the sun, I was looking upon Giuseppe Mazzini once more.


### Chapter Sixteen

<!-- pages:79-83 -->

<!-- pages:79-82 -->



HERE is the painful page: The secret of the vast conspiracy, jealously guarded; the Austrian Authorities utterly unprepared; the distribution of soldiers’ pay already accomplished; the targeted positions stripped of their defenders; the garrison dispersed and carousing. Eugenio Brizzi himself, on the very eve of the meditated assault, penetrated into the inner courtyard of the Castello and conferred with the two armourers. He found them steadfast to their pledged word; nor had any coup de main ever set forth under fairer auspices.

But at the moment of action, Assi and several other chiefs, instead of mustering their squads, vanished. The few bold men stationed at their posts, seeing themselves too small in number, waited for a time, chafing with rage; then, suspecting treachery, or believing that everything had been deferred to another day, they dispersed.

Of Fortunato Calvi’s plan, only so much succeeded as suffices to show that it was not built upon clouds, and that it would have had a very different outcome had there not been in Milan certain individuals of great influence who, though they desired the expulsion of the foreigner, kept their eyes fixed — under the colour of prudence — upon the interests of their own caste.

The assault upon the Gran Guardia, led by a simple charcoal-burner, ended in success. The insurrection would have had its centre, and two howitzers to defend it. Yet the directing minds were conspicuous by their absence, and the popular impetus, impatient of delay, cast aside the instructions it had received. Instead of barricading themselves within the palace, those few brave men poured out tumultuously into the streets. They killed several officers, then fell back upon Porta Romana, where the innkeeper Giuseppe Monti organised a defence.

To suppress it, an entire battalion was despatched. And while a handful of common folk generously exposed their lives, in the hope of holding out until the middle and upper classes should resolve to descend armed into the revolutionary vortex… the wealthy, the right-thinking, closed their street-doors, closed their windows, and gave four-fifths of the capital the aspect of a deserted and silent city.

The insurrection failed because the Milanese intellectuals lacked the ardour, the heart, the spirit of sacrifice, the faith of the People. Already in the preliminary negotiations conducted between themselves and Giuseppe Mazzini, Emilio Visconti Venosta — the future Minister of the monarchy — and his kind had employed all the keenness of their wit in raising objections. Compelled by argument to yield one after another, they ended by declaring that, before resolving to lend their support, they intended to seek the opinion of a competent military man. And they turned to Giacomo Medici, who was then residing in Genova.

The hero of the Vascello, future general of Vittorio Emanuele II, entertained the selfsame thought that had informed the conduct of Giuseppe Mazzini: “To counsel against the rising; but to support it strenuously once it proved inevitable.”

This did not please them, and they did not follow him. I believe they had hoped to hear from Medici’s lips the very phrase that would lend respectability to their timid egotism. The conspiracy took shape and began its fateful course; yet they gave neither a soldo, nor a man, nor a musket. And they possessed all of these! — but with an expression already heralding the short-sighted and corrosive opportunism of times all too vivid in the memory of Italians, they declared “that they were reserving these for good use on the second day!”

The flight of Assi and the others, however blameworthy, had no other motive than these underhand equivocations. The Milanese working class was not yet accustomed to the present-day, subtle distinctions between proletariat and capitalism; it had not yet learned to stand erect upon its own spine. It had heard and read so many times that all Italians are brothers and, in its artless good faith, it held them as such. The first doubts are always the most bitter. Why should the *marsine* now refuse to make common cause with the *blouse*? Were not the sons of the People risking their lives for a holy common cause? Why should all the risks of initiative fall to them alone?

I came to know Assi in exile. He was expiating, in muffled anguish, his own error and those which were attributed to him unjustly. And he defended himself, saying: “They wished to drive us alone to the slaughter, so that they might disown us if we were beaten. Had victory smiled upon us — at no risk to themselves — they would have robbed us of its fruit!”

The bourgeois and aristocratic inaction of 1853 had recalled to mind that in 1848 the direction of public affairs had been usurped by those who had not made the revolution. Deplorable logic, that of Assi — but logic nonetheless!

Giuseppe Monti and his men fought on, from house to house, until not a scrap of powder remained to them. And so effective was their resistance that a full hundred and fifty of the enemy lay upon the cobblestones and ceased to lacerate Italy.

Then came the martyrdom! A military tribunal, hastily constituted, hastily condemned to the gallows Giuseppe Monti and fifteen other brave men. *(1)*

They faced their punishment with a serene spirit, teaching the slothful how to die, as they had already taught them how to rise in arms.

Then came the arts of Tartuffe. It was necessary to diminish the heroism of the good in order to give to their own desertion the character of an enlightened decision worthy of supermen. There is no lack of connoisseurs who would dismiss the verses of Dante as insipid, were they submitted to their judgement over an unknown signature. The plans of Fortunato Calvi, attributed to Mazzini — an intellect held to be refractory to all military discipline — were declared a foolishness more than puerile. And men were not ashamed, in order to prove it, to borrow with a smug air of triumph the Croatian phrases belched forth during the heroic hours spent at the tavern: “Had the Milanese killed our officers, we should have put the Milanese to the sword — men, women, and children alike!”

And the people of the Five Days, to please the Croatians and the Visconti Venosta, would have resigned themselves to being skewered like a dish of flaccid gnocchi!

Then came the infamy! — To vilify the honest; to reduce, if possible, the stature of the giant, so that the People, deceived and deluded, might resolve to gravitate toward the will-o’-the-wisps. The tactic is ancient.

And it was insinuated and written and given for certain that Giuseppe Mazzini was a spy in the pay of Austria. He provoked excesses, remaining himself prudently at a distance, in order to furnish the oppressor with the pretext for terrorising the timid by strangling the bold.

Then came the nauseating cynicism!

To stupefy a conscience revived at intervals by the tormenting thought of one’s own guilt; to attempt to deceive oneself and others by disowning even one’s own martyrs. To this they descended: they circulated a protest in which, having inverted all the parts, Giuseppe Monti and the other unfortunates were portrayed as assassins, and as martyrs the assassins righteously cut down in that desperate, glorious attempt to restore to us a Fatherland! Never had creatures born and raised beneath the fair sky of Italy prostrated themselves so low!

I repeat: The failure of the rising of the 6th of February 1853 is due principally to the fact that a handful of young men — wealthy, privileged, and influential — recoiled in horror before the possibility of a popular Government, and already preferred the not-making of Italy to making it otherwise than conservative and monarchical.

Yet those risings were not without fruit. They hardened Austria and, rendering her ever more blind to her true interests, drove her back toward reckless sequestrations, toward fresh persecutions; toward remedying our errors with the force of her own errors, greater and irreparable.

Giuseppe Monti and his companions in martyrdom thus indirectly rendered any accord between the oppressed and the oppressors impossible. The holy work of redemption hastened its course and became inevitable!

*(1) Giuseppe Monti, Siro Taddei, Alessandro Scannini, Bonaventura Brossini, Eligio Bigatti, Pietro Canevari, Cesare Facciola, Luigi Filsia, Camino Piana, Antonio Cavallotti, Gerolamo Saporiti, Pietro Colla, Angelo Benedetto Diotti, Alessandro Silva, Galimberti.*


### Chapter Seventeen

<!-- pages:83-88 -->



IN LOCARNO, where I had returned to deliver a missive from Mazzini to Calvi, I was arrested and released a few hours later, at liberty, on my word to present myself to the Federal Delegate. I was later tried for conspiracy against a friendly Power, expelled from Switzerland, and put to the alternative of choosing between America and England.

England was closer to the field of probable future enterprises and was preferred by me. I settled in London, where I found not a few of those compromised in the February uprisings, who had escaped Austrian vengeance through adventurous flights... among others, a Milanese Jean Valjean, accompanied out of the city walls in a coffin and with a solemn funeral.

Bound by our common ideal and common sufferings, we lived for some time in community, in the district called Baldwin's Garden, and precisely in the modest inn of the Milanese Tressoldi, a good-natured fellow who possessed, among his other virtues, that of turning a blind eye when we could not settle the bill.

Giuseppe Mazzini arrived in London in April. From him we learned that Pianciani, who was then dictating his "History of the Popes," tempered the fatigues of the intellect by modifying and re-modifying the garden of his villa... a probability of work for several of us. We were presented to his amanuensis, a certain Bardini from Padova, a law student, and he, in turn, presented us to Pianciani who, there and then, entrusted the material labour of those incessant modifications to the young Varisco, granting him ample faculty to avail himself of the work of as many of us as he deemed necessary. I too was immediately engaged, and thus, for some months, I drew my sustenance from the spade and the flowers.

That simple and laborious life was troubled by terrible images. Austria was growing ever more ferocious, and every newspaper, every letter brought us the painful certainty that the phalanx of the generous was being inexorably thinned.

I spoke of it on long walks with Giuseppe Mazzini, and I would fix my gaze upon that olive-hued, intellectual face, as if to say: "What is to be decided? Shall we let them be slaughtered like dogs?" The Master would quicken his pace. His expression was one of ineffable melancholy; but of the thoughts that swirled in his head... not a syllable!

One day in August, he sent for me with urgency. Why ever for! Certainly not to say "good day" or "good evening." So then?..

I planted the spade in the middle of a flowerbed: "Stay there! You are not the only noble instrument!" And off I ran.

But when I stood before the Master, I felt my arms fall to my sides, so sparing of words was his welcome. I was asked to carry a letter to Kossuth and to await the reply. Not another syllable!

Kossuth then held, near Regent's Circus, a sort of Court. At the door I found carriages and servants in livery; up the stairs, a coming and going of staid Englishmen in formal attire; Hungarians in their elegant, militaresque national costume; people with broad scars; uncertain figures, begging under the pretext of the Fatherland. Honest and dishonest, rich or poor, they rarely obtained access to the Dictator who, even beaten and in exile, exercised not only the prestige of his fame, but also that which comes from the habit of command.

While I awaited his pleasure in the antechamber, I tried to pass the time observing the paintings that hung from the walls: Kossuth in the guise of the simple citizen; Kossuth in hunting attire; Kossuth in a general's uniform — now in an armchair, now on horseback, now beside a shattered cannon; but always Kossuth, nothing but Kossuth!

Finally, from among so many simulacra, the great man appeared in the flesh. I must admit that art, in its intent to idealise him, had slandered him. The figure of the illustrious Magyar, more than affected heroism and slaughter, recalled paternal affections and the gentle emotions of the domestic hearth. It is the privilege of artists to alter, at their whim, the colours of History... for this reason Napoleon I is handed down to posterity crossing the Saint Bernard on a prancing colt, with manifest injustice to the poor donkey that lent its back to so great a weight.

The welcome reserved for me was not what the Count di Rudio had expected — nor the Italian patriot, nor the trusted envoy of Giuseppe Mazzini. Kossuth read the letter I handed him while standing on the threshold and, casting it aside, drew a memorandum from his pocket, wrote a few lines, and ordered me, curtly, curtly, to carry them to their destination. Not a word of greeting! And I, descending the stairs, asked myself whether the Cossacks of the Czar and the Croats of Jellacich, by lending their strength to Austria, had not, in substance, spared the brave Hungarians the vexations attendant upon a change of dynasty.

The few lines consigned to me by Kossuth were addressed to a high officer whose name I am now seeking in the recesses of memory. From him I received several rolls of sterling... "The same," he told me, "that we received from Mazzini for our participation in the movements of 1853. May they serve your great, unfortunate country!"

Kossuth, on the morrow of that failure, had publicly denied all complicity in those movements!

Upon my return, the Master opened his soul to me. He began by asking whether I felt disposed to place my life once more in jeopardy for the cause of the Fatherland and, having received the answer from my very bearing, he pointed me to a chair, and we conversed in low voices, the one opposite the other, for over an hour.

He explained to me that, on the counsel of Fortunato Calvi, the future insurrectionary attempts were to unfold in the region of the Alps. The reasons he adduced are the following: Our country is rich in latent forces, ready to burst forth; but whoever takes an initiative must know how to give assurance of serious resistance. The mountain is the natural bulwark of the weak and the oppressed. There it becomes possible for the few to hold their ground against the many; possible to escape pursuit; to descend with the swiftness of the eagle upon the plain; to surprise, to intercept. For the pitched battle, which was denied to us, it was necessary to substitute guerrilla warfare, which is necessarily the daughter of the mountains. If the banner of redemption could be kept upright and flying for three, for two months, there would not be a patriot without fever in his blood, not a corner of Italy where the flame of action would not blaze.

This last thought seemed to lead him into another order of reflection. A shadow spread across his face; then he said, almost in these very words: "I know! Cowardice, masked as prudence, accuses me of driving spirited youth to the gallows, of provoking desperate attempts that offer Austrian tyranny, and that which is home-bred, the opportunity to intensify their cruelties. But they do not reflect that the audacity and sacrifice of the few always end by shaking the torpid from their lethargy and driving the many to action. Nothing great without audacity! Napoleon III would in all probability never have smoothed his path to the Empire without the failures of Strasbourg and of Boulogne. No King of Italy will show spirit so great as to champion the redemption of our Fatherland, so long as Italians show themselves to be basking, in peace, in slavery. Would Carlo Alberto have crossed the Ticino were it not for the magnanimous self-abnegation of the Milanese! It is an imperative necessity, this keeping the great Italian question open and pressing. Europe cannot have peace if our agony endures. She will intervene, sooner or later, in our favour, and the sooner the more we show ourselves intolerant of the yoke and menacing. And what use are sound ideas, vigorous intentions, when want of initiative relegates them to the realm of mere abstractions! Action cleanses our centuries-old faults. Between us and a free Italy lies this baptism of blood. Should we hold back for the slanders of cowards!"

That very evening I procured a passport under an assumed name and set out for Switzerland. Fortunato Calvi was already awaiting me in Zurich. "The plan for the new movements is his," Mazzini had told me. "It falls to him to see to its execution. You will receive the necessary instructions from his own mouth."
