In Brussels, where I made a brief halt, I delivered a letter from the Master to dall'Ungaro, the Venetian Catullus. "Where is the Master, what is he doing?" he asked me, almost feverishly. And I, in his own verses:

*"Mazzini is in the place where men tremble*
*That the hour supreme may reach the traitor's ear;*
*Mazzini is in every place where men labour*
*To shed their blood for Italy entire!"*

Having arrived in Zurich, I wished, before going to Calvi, to have my hands free and hastened to pay a visit to de Boni, to whom I likewise delivered a missive; a third I carried to Caronti of Como. Giuseppe Mazzini had charged me to be very much on my guard with this man, of whom he was highly suspicious. He did, however, contribute sums of some considerable amount to the good cause, and this restrained Mazzini from being rid of him.

The brazenly inquisitive questions that Caronti directed at me came to me, already ill-disposed as I was, as a sharp irritant, and when I set foot once more beyond the threshold of his villa, I felt a relief as if I had suddenly been freed from a nightmare.

At last I could restore myself in the embrace of an authentic patriot, of an untarnished conscience... Fortunato Calvi! What joy in seeing him again, still handsome, still proud, still exhaling that indefinable sense of serenity which breathes forth from honest souls!

The substantial part of our colloquy had a beginning which I shall recount without false modesty.

"You are not," he said to me, "and you must not be, an instrument for blindly executing orders you do not comprehend. I shall confide to you the plan I have devised and which the European Revolutionary Committee has fully approved. Thus you will know how to parry, with unity of purpose, the inevitable counterblows. If we succeed in something concrete, you will be my aide-de-camp; if we do not succeed... we shall, in all probability, be made equal in our rewards... martyrdom!" My preliminary charge was to proceed into the valley of Treviso and make contact with certain smugglers, surnamed the Burrasca brothers, so as to induce them to carry a large consignment of munitions into the Agordino. I then had to cross the border, enter Belluno clandestinely, and make contact with Don Bastiano Barozzi, who had returned thither and who was expecting me. Barozzi knew of certain arms hidden in 1848 upon the return of the oppressors. In the Cadore, rebellious spirits abounded who had covered themselves with glory in its defence; the name and exhortation of Fortunato Calvi, who had been the guiding genius of that defence, would have rekindled in their breasts the slumbering enthusiasm. It was necessary to distribute among them the old arms. A band of exiles awaited the signal to descend from the Alps... Fortunato Calvi, leading them, would direct their fortunes.

The Austrian colossus was to be struck precisely at Belluno, and with a threefold surprise: at the Finance Office, at the Gendarmerie, and at the Barracks of the regular troops.

The Finance Office, situated near the old church of Santo Stefano, a solid building erected to serve as a convent, frames a vast courtyard with porticoes. Between one column and the next extends a parapet, so low that a man, creeping along it and on all fours, cannot be spotted by a sentry who, even while keeping watch, patrols the other parts of the arcade. The main gate had no doors and was left unguarded; the custodians of the building, a small body of militia quartered at the far end of the courtyard, to the right... three men in all and a corporal.

In the dead of night, a handful of our men in ambush near the gate; one bold spirit to creep, barefoot, along the parapet and take the sentry from behind... a hushed signal... men come running!... the three militiamen sleeping are rendered powerless. A bold enterprise, not an arduous one!

It was autumn, the season of tax collection; the coffers of the Administration, according to reliable calculations, were to contain a million and a half florins. To seize them... the sinews of war!

Simultaneously, another band of patriots was to conceal themselves near the Gendarmes' Barracks. They were to simulate a brawl and ring the bell of the Gendarmerie, as if to summon assistance. The guard who opens the door is to be dispatched. The lair is to be stormed, and the thirty manaclers sleeping there compelled to pass, by force, into the category of the manacled.

The garrison of Belluno at that time consisted of one hundred and fifty soldiers. The barracks were approached, to the right and to the left, by a circular ramping road — a *tourniquet*, as the engineers call it — flanked by trees and benches, a favourite resort of nursemaids and urchins. As a general rule, when the alarm sounded, the small garrison would emerge, in straggling fashion, from the main gate and draw up on the esplanade, awaiting orders. It was necessary to conceal a body of picked marksmen along the edges of the ramping road, to sound the alarm, and to shoot down as many soldiers as should come running.

The threefold surprise accomplished, the insurgents were to take possession of the civil and ecclesiastical authorities... hostages! Then we seize the means of transport, don the Austrian uniform, and proceed in military fashion to the fortress of Osoppo in Friuli, taking prisoners and compelling all whom we encounter on the road to follow us, so as to forestall any indiscretion.

At this point Fortunato Calvi, who had been speaking throughout in a low voice, almost as though meditating upon his own phrases, grew animated and said vibratingly: "We approach the fortress preceded by the Austrian flag and beating the drum, as friends. Once across the bridge, once the foot is set beneath the archway, the scene changes — the Latin valour bursts forth. On the ramparts, twenty-two of our own, we raise our flag, and woe betide whoever assails it! The hostages remain with us, with us the arms, the money, and the audacity. Osoppo becomes our base of operations, and it is an impregnable stronghold. Through the winter we organise the guerrilla warfare in the Alps, we hold in check the troops that would descend into the Peninsula. In the spring, Lombardy, Venetia, Hungary, France perhaps as well, second the movement... the oppressed and the oppressors measure their forces. The errors of the past are our instruction; the age-old vow is realised; Italy rises again, never to fall again."

I listened with bowed head, thinking what any other man would have thought: "To what extent could we count upon the ability of the European Revolutionary Committee to extend our initiative, to transform it into an international uprising?"

Could I hold back when an entity such as Fortunato Calvi was throwing himself entirely into the breach?

Three days, and I was on my way again.


### Chapter Eighteen

<!-- pages:88-92 -->

<!-- pages:88-92 -->



I touched Coirà, where from Clementi, who found himself at that time under trial as one implicated in the international risings, I received a letter for his younger brother. The latter would, in his turn, have seen to putting me in communication with the Burrasca brothers.

At San Maurizio, in the Engadine, at Muentzen, the point of contact between Switzerland and Württemberg and the Tyrol, at Salzburg, at Trento — no suspicious incident. At this last place I presented myself to the Austrian Authorities and signed, under the assumed name, my passport... they did not cast upon me so much as a single searching glance.

And from Trento, deviating from the direct route, I ascend again the valley furrowed by the torrent Trevis, meet with the younger Clementi; and that very same evening find myself face to face with the four smugglers.

Of them Clementi had told me that they were the despair of the man-hunters and the pride of the valley. Jealous of their own reputation, they left to lesser men the easy enterprises and made it a point of honour to bring to completion the most hazardous. Spirits intolerant of every restraint, they lent themselves nobly also to righting wrongs, to fighting for the liberty of others. They had already rendered services of no small account to the Italian cause.

I had no need of masterly phrases to bend them to my purpose, and when the eldest of the brothers, the captain of the small and courageous band, set down with energy the glass from which he had been sipping, stretched out his right hand and gripped mine, I understood that I had concluded, with that simple act, a pact for life and death, far more binding than any notarial document.

Early in the morning, having refused every guide, I set out on foot, by little-trodden paths which traced themselves, little by little, vividly in my memory before they spread out before my eyes. In the vast horizons, in the sudden precipices, in the solemn silences, in the alpine fragrances I recovered my boyhood and was so absorbed in it that the phantoms of the future grew pale and almost vanished away.

When, from the height, I descried my Belluno, the rain was falling in heavy drops; but serenity was in my heart. I stood, beneath my ample umbrella, seeking out among the indistinct lines the paternal house, and having found it, it seemed to me that the old papa and mamma and all my dear ones must feel me near and trembling, as I felt them.

I reached the bank of the Ardo at dusk; but the autumn rains had swollen it so that finding a ford proved impossible. It was a setback! The house of don Bastiano stood on the opposite side of the city. To reach it I now saw myself compelled to risk the high road, to cross the bridge, to traverse the whole length of Belluno. I waited patiently for the night, hidden in a thicket.

The clocks were striking ten when, with my umbrella lowered over my face, I entered among the dwellings. Few and hurrying were the wayfarers. I scrutinised them from afar, in the uncertain light of the lanterns... not a spy, not an acquaintance, not a living soul who showed any concern for me... I might have thought myself, in the eyes of my fellow-citizens, an invisible spectre. But when the houses thinned and I had left every lantern behind me, a sudden thought came to disturb me: “What if the Police, sharp-eyed and feline, had laid a trap for me in the very solitary dwelling of don Bastiano?”

I entered the little garden that surrounds it with such precautions that even the mastiffs took no notice. The ground floor... dark; all the shutters closed; those of the upper storey closed also... a single window yawned open upon a gleam of light that was lost among the boughs of a double row of trees. I strained my ear... the voice of the Ardo, a distant rustling of leaves, nothing more! Then I gathered up a small stone and cast it against the window, while with an instinctive movement I gripped the butt of a pistol.

And lo, darkening behind the panes, a woman’s figure... don Bastiano’s serving-maid, handsome and plump, with ample hips and impertinent bosom. She threw open the shutters and whispered into the night: “Is it you, Moretto?”

Don Bastiano received me with an embrace and with sobbing. His first words were: “Praised be heaven! I had already despaired of your safety.”

— If the cruelties of the oppressors can be called news! These past few days the unexpected arrests have followed one upon another. My very house is under constant surveillance. How could you have made your way here without mishap? I have a vague suspicion that you have dropped from the sky!

— Safe... and arrived but a few hours since. But who could vouch for the present? To sleep in one’s own bed has become an arduous problem in these times!

At his request, I related to him what I had seen and done in my rapid journey. And he:

“Providence protects you. So much the greater is your obligation not to abuse its protection. Listen, my son: That I love my country, you know. For her liberty I would give... everything save my soul. But one thing is to sacrifice what is our own, another is to imperil the safety of others, the lives of those we love. It is a tremendous responsibility for which I should have to render account, sooner or later, to One who counts the drops of blood uselessly shed and is not appeased by syllogisms. Heed me: These enterprises succeed... at times, if they burst forth suddenly, like a thunderbolt! But let the enemy, all-powerful as he is, but divine them... Carletto! Carletto! what avail the generous resolves, the daring of four, of thirty, of a hundred young men, against a colossus that holds all Europe in subjection? Would you lay your neck in the noose, thus light-heartedly, for the sterile glory that might come of it? And has not our Italy already too many and too many of her best to mourn and to pity? Reserve your energy for more propitious times. How it shall come about I do not know and could not tell you; but I believe that the Police have some inkling of our plots. They do not arrest at random. You are not safe here. O the times, the times! Friend meets friend and must grieve at it and must conjure him to depart. Austria makes us worse than barbarians!”

At that moment, outside a few paces away, a sharp crack rang out; the mastiffs howled... I blew out the candle.

Moments of terrible anxiety... for don Bastiano. I had gone, on tiptoe, to the door and, opening its peephole, had planted the barrels of my two pistols there. Then, thinking that to remain caged would cut off every avenue of escape, I went out and advanced... Great darkness, great silence. As a further precaution I made the circuit of the house... the wind, a shutter banging... that was all! But when I returned and relit the candle, I was struck by the deathly pallor of don Bastiano.

And I thought again of the fine walks taken, in his company, in autumn, along the Ardo, through the rich vineyards, when he, to educate me in virile sentiments, would recount to me the deeds of our forebears; when his priestly cassock disappeared from my eyes and I pictured him grave in breastplate and helm... so fierce were his words, so intense his enthusiasm!

Don Bastiano, accustomed to reading my thoughts since my earliest years, said: “Do not number me among the cowards. My character as a priest and these grey hairs lay duties upon me. But once the die is cast... upright and serene to the end!”

Early in the morning, while I, wearied from the long journey, still slept soundly, he went to inform papa of my arrival. I found the proud old man still vigorous, though upon his noble face I could discern wrinkles that were unknown to me. But our meeting was other than I had expected. It is probable that don Bastiano had persuaded him to throw into the balance the full weight of paternal authority, to dissuade me from so hazardous an enterprise. No embraces, no kisses; a powerful handshake, but a solitary one, and then at once, sharpened and grown almost sardonic, the same exhortations I had heard the evening before from the lips of the good priest. Our discussion grew so heated as to become most painful to me. I resolved to cut it short. “When the uniform and the bread of thieves grew odious to me,” I said, “I expected neither festivities nor flowers. Passive submission does not befit the spirit of a Rudio. And since you counsel it to me... well then, I shall continue to walk my own path, alone. From this moment I have no longer a father!” The poor old man remained a moment with a gesture suspended, as though he had been suddenly crystallised; then his eyelashes filled with tears. He flung himself forward with a sudden impulse and embraced me and held me close with a strength of which I should not have thought him capable. When he was able to speak: “It is you, it is you Carletto!... I recognise my own blood. Go! So long as my strength holds, I shall be with you!”

It was decided not to unearth the arms until the spirits of the bold men who were to handle them should be prepared and well disposed. Papa and don Bastiano undertook to approach them. In the meantime I should, by way of prudence, seek out a refuge less closely watched by the Police. In the environs of Belluno, towards the north, where the plain borders upon the heights, there stood an ancient chestnut tree, known, and almost celebrated, in the city and throughout the countryside for its extraordinary dimensions. It was agreed that I should repair, under cover of darkness, beneath its boughs. A trusted person would come, towards midnight, to indicate to me where I might lay my head. The recognition signal: three whistles, to which I was to respond with four.

By nine o’clock I was already stretched upon the grass, musing as I contemplated the moon through the dark foliage; then the moon set and the wind resumed its unbridled course. I rose to stretch my limbs and posted myself behind the enormous trunk.

From the tower of Belluno twelve strokes rang out upon the air, one hard upon another, and my eye, now grown accustomed to the darkness, ran searchingly among thickets and vineyards. Another long hour passed... I was seized by dark forebodings.

All at once, softly, timidly, from beyond a clump of bushes, the three whistles followed in succession... then a whisper... “On guard, Moretto! There are several of them!” I gazed intently and discerned, through the twigs, something... like the greatcoat of a spy. That I did not fire then and there was from fear of missing my target and finding myself defenceless before the enemy. I answered the signal and waited with both weapons levelled...

Oh what blood and what remorse does a moment’s hesitation murmur! A shadow advanced with arms outstretched... my mother!


### Chapter Nineteen

<!-- pages:92-98 -->



IN THE EFFICACY of any oratorical passage in stirring noble rebellious impulses, a mother's sobs reach the heart of a son. My mother's, in the silence of the night, resounded with terrible force.

I hastened to support her with one arm and placed my right hand over her lips as if to stifle them. "Calm yourself, calm yourself!" I wished to say, but the voice would not come and, overcome by a surge of tenderness, I too broke into sobs.

The first to utter a coherent syllable was my brother Giustiniano, who had remained to one side, almost unnoticed. He said: "Bitter news. Our friends, the best of them, have been arrested; don Bastiano has been arrested as well. Let us reckon the sum: poor Achille has left his bones at Venice; you are beyond doubt being sought by the Police."

And continuing, he recounted the particulars of the catastrophe. Don Bastiano had calmly held out his wrists for the manacles, "happy to contribute, through his own sacrifice, to completing the series of sufferings required by Providence for the redemption of his Country." But papa had wished to break his bamboo cane over the shoulders of the ruffians, and the caresses of his family had with difficulty managed to calm him.

I reflected for a moment and concluded: "For now the important thing is to bring mother home. We shall think of the rest afterwards."

But the poor woman, shattered by that terrible ordeal and rendered still more helpless by the darkness, stumbled at every step. I handed one of my pistols to Giustiniano and murmured in his ear: "Go ahead as scout. Think of whom you are defending... one does not tread upon the toes of the di Rudio with impunity. Do you take my meaning?"

I stooped and, before my mother had time to notice and protest, had lifted her in my arms and pressed forward, proud to carry the one who had once carried me in her womb and with far greater travail.

At the first uncertain light of the street-lamps, I set the saintly woman down and, embracing her, pressed a long kiss upon her brow. I did not foresee that it would be the last! "Until we meet again, mamma!" And I promised her that, however adversely fortune might show herself, I would always conduct myself in such a manner that, when we met again, or when thinking of her love, my conscience would not torment me with the sense of being unworthy of it.

In the spot where we had halted, Domenico was building a house. Those beams and bricks stacked along the road offered me an excellent screen. I told Giustiniano that I would await him at that place. He was to bring back my weapon. Should he discover any fresh news, he would inform me. My intentions? He would know them upon his return.

I did not wait long. Mother and Giustiniano had reached home without encountering a living soul. My sister Luisa had prepared a good cup of coffee for her; the poor woman appeared, if not resigned, at least calm.

"And now to our business!" added Giustiniano. "One does not tread upon the toes of the di Rudio with impunity... it is your maxim; but it is mine as well! Let us reckon the sum: poor Achille has left his bones at Venice; you are beyond doubt being sought by the Police, hunted like a malefactor through the streets of the night." This insinuated itself deep into my thoughts; my spirit at last came to mirror the picturesque majesty of the mountain.

I recognised Domenico's little cottage, leaning against the cliff, its dark doorway beneath the pergola, bare now of grapes and leaves — the pergola that stretched as far as the road and divided the little kitchen-garden plot into equal halves. From the foot of the old walnut tree whose trembling fronds tickled the roof of the dwelling, two hounds launched themselves forth barking; but they soon checked their ardour upon hearing themselves called by name. They followed in my footsteps, stretching out their muzzles and anxiously sniffing at my calves.

One, two, three blows of the knocker, at decent intervals... sleep clings tenaciously to the labourers of the mountain. A fourth, a fifth resounded in the void, and at last through the cracks of the old door, light filtered through; two heavy clogs advanced noisily.

"Open, Domenico. It is Carletto."

The door creaked, and I remained as if dazzled by the lantern which, with a jolt, was raised from the ground and sent a flash of light into my face.

"Carletto!.. taller, more proud-looking, more bronzed, with a beard... but Carletto! And I had taken you for the other end of the world! The old folk were right: only the mountains stay in their place. But how come you are here at this hour! Did you lose your way on the slope?"

I did not answer, but gesturing toward the right hand of the good old man, which gripped a billhook: "You meant to cut off my head!"

In the kitchen with its smoke-blackened walls, beneath the wide chimney-hood, a fire crackled up soon enough to have roasted a kid. We sat facing one another in the lateral niches carved into the thick wall, and while I laid out the situation plainly for him, he tormented the embers with the ends of a reed-handled bellows. When he heard that father was in chains, he drove that iron implement with a formidable thrust into the log, which sent up a shower of sparks. From the clenching of his jaws and the fury in his face, I would have said the blow was intentionally aimed at the head of some Croatian. He said:

"You did well to come up here. From our height, one commands the valley for more than five miles. They have barely set foot outside Belluno and we already know where they would like to cast their net. With five miles' advantage, there is no candle-chewing foreigner that can outrun Italian legs; leaving aside altogether that the mountain is scattered all about with our own people. And should the devil help them and they chance upon us unawares... there is a remedy for that too! Do you remember the old cavern, where I used to put you in punishment when you ruined my almond trees! A single leap from the house, the entrance narrow and so well-covered that you would not notice it even if you knocked your nose against it. In a desperate case, one man alone with an old musket, a rusted contraption, could make half Bohemia wish it had never been born. There is no denying it, you showed good sense in taking refuge up here."

He paused a moment as if suspended in thought, then added: "I am dithering and you need rest. I shall run and rouse the old woman. Half an hour more, half an hour less, what does it matter? She has to prepare the mash for the cow that is about to calf anyway. For this once, you must resign yourself to sleeping in our bed... we shall think later of making up something a bit more fit for a Christian."

And as I protested that I was broken to far harder fatigues; that I preferred to keep watch by the fire, he assumed the air of one touched on the quick and said: "And why not? In our bed... but have no fear!... We shall put on freshly-laundered sheets!"

That very day, in the afternoon, Giustiniano appeared up there. He had left the city on the pretext of going hunting for finches and goldfinches; he had with him the decoy-owl, the perch-pole, and the bird-lime. Nothing suspicious about his long circuit; but the news he brought was very gloomy. A raid by the police, a search in full form; mother in a terrible state of agitation... all the more so because Luisa was keeping a highly compromising letter. Fortunato Calvi had for Luisa a sincere admiration; a friendship that led him to confide to her, as to a sister, his most intimate thoughts. In those days he had sent his instructions to father, addressing them, the better to deceive the Police, to Luisa herself. They were written in veiled language, but not so veiled that they would fail to arouse the suspicions of one who already had some inkling of our plot. Moreover, yielding to the impulses of his heart, Calvi had, in a brief postscript, expressed the hope that, "the Fatherland once liberated, nothing more would stand in the way of his union with Teresa Duodo," a gentle young lady from Padova who adored him, and was adored by him in return.

Fortunately my sister, seizing the moment, swiftly thrust the letter into father's guitar — a great plucker of strings, father was! And the searchers, who had rummaged through every corner of the house and every nook, had taken no notice of that humble instrument, hanging upon the wall.

Already they were preparing to take their leave, already the two women had begun to breathe freely, when the inspector, who had beyond doubt received peremptory orders, placed Luisa under arrest... A long embrace... then mother had fallen back into the armchair, in the grip of a terrible nervous seizure.

On hearing that news, it seemed to me that the heavy air of the plain had climbed up to the mountain and was suffocating me. To what end linger any longer in the Cadore? I was held back by a chivalrous feeling that made me regard it as cowardice to seek my own safety while my loved ones were persecuted and exposed to every danger; I was held back also by the hope, however vague and fleeting, that Fortunato Calvi and Giuseppe Mazzini, having learned of the ill turn taken by their plans, would exert themselves to set them right and might have need of me. But events, in their headlong course, were soon enough to blow away even those scruples.

One morning I was walking a short distance from the farmstead and, by instinct, studying the terrain with a tactical eye, when, rounding a crag, I found myself face to face with an Austrian officer. I do not think he noticed my agitation, for he greeted me in a manner of faultless courtesy and told me he had set out from Belluno, with other officers, before sunrise. He had gone astray in pursuing a covey of partridges. He then asked me whether I had seen his hunting companions pass through those parts.

Having received a negative reply, he offered me a cigar and then, with a certain surprise: "You live in these solitary places? One needs a certain genius to avoid being bored by them!" Before I could answer, several rifle-shots echoed from crag to crag. He turned his thumb back and said: "Those are my friends!" He saluted and moved off with long strides.

A few hours later, Giustiniano arrived up there. I understood, from the mere sight of him, that some other bad news was pressing upon his heart. He told me that, early that morning, a clerk of the Police, in whom the trade had not deadened every honestly sensitive fibre, had knocked at our door. He confided to mother that positive word had reached his office that I was to be found in Belluno, or in the surrounding area, and for purposes that could not be welcome to the royal imperial authorities. These had issued a peremptory order to seize me, alive or dead. We were to govern ourselves accordingly and with dispatch. He had done his duty as a good Italian... he trusted that, once he was out of that office, we would know how to do ours, maintaining, even under oath, that we had never so much as laid eyes on him.

In my mind, the chance encounter with the Austrian officer and what Giustiniano had told me connected themselves spontaneously. If the Police want me alive or dead, they will have circulated my description to their direct and indirect ramifications. And if the officer, in explaining his absence to his fellow-soldiers, has spoken of me...

The thread was slender; but it would have been enough to braid a noose... my own!

I turned to Giustiniano: "Assure mother that tonight I shall not sleep in Domenico's house. By dawn tomorrow, I shall be far away."

The sun had barely set and I was already seeking refuge in the ancient cavern, past which I had never walked as a child without a feeling of profound apprehension. I entered it cautiously, without a light; then replaced the ivy that concealed its mouth. From that hiding-place, watching through the foliage, I found myself directly facing the window of Domenico's kitchen, which was unbarred and unshuttered; it lay open before my eyes as clearly as if I had been inside. Domenico was at that moment cutting slices of cheese... he was preparing my *viatico* — provisions for the pilgrimage of the morrow. Caterina sat in one of the chimney-niches telling the beads of her rosary.

All at once the dogs barked. This was not unusual. In the evening, foxes roam the mountain and the hounds, scenting them from afar, with their ceaseless circlings keep them in check. On this occasion, however, their barking, growing ever more furious, gave way to whimpering and — a circumstance in the highest degree suspicious — instead of coursing through the fields, they were falling back toward the house. They had before them, then, an adversary capable of commanding respect.

I strained my ears and was not long in detecting a measured footfall, a sharp clank of metal; then... between my eyes and the lit window, several phantoms filed past on tiptoe. Plumes and carbines erased from my mind even the last traces of doubt... the henchmen of Austria had surrounded the house.

Within, the two honest souls gave every appearance of being utterly unaware of the storm that had gathered about the old nest. All at once, the villains brought the butt of a rifle down on the frail door, which flew open with a great crash. I saw Domenico leap back and instinctively his right hand, armed with the large knife, drew back to gather the impetus needed to strike. But four levelled rifles drove away, with the fright of reality, that which his imagination had planted in him.

A lanky man, with waxed pointed moustaches and the face of a candle-chewing foreigner, made a gesture and two gendarmes threw themselves upon the bewildered man, manacled him, and bound him to a leg of the table.

Not a word had been spoken. The lanky man snatched the dark-lantern from a soldier, cast its light around, and catching sight of Caterina who, having let her rosary fall at her feet, had remained as if paralysed, said to her: "Get up and come with me!" She did not relax her rigidity and seemed not to understand, until the lanky man, seizing her by the shoulder, shook her, forced her to her feet and dealt her a powerful blow between the shoulder-blades. I could see Domenico grind his teeth distinctly.

The lanky man, a sergeant, disappeared through the opposite door, preceded by Caterina and followed by four of his men. I heard him give the order to put four ounces of lead in the stomach of anyone who attempted to flee; then a noise of heavy footsteps on the wooden staircase; then a dull shifting of furniture, a banging of drawers upon the flagstones. They searched every room, went even down to the cellar.

When the sergeant reappeared at the head of his men, he was wiping his pointed moustaches with the back of his hand... he had taken the trouble to make his search less tedious by drawing from some cask. But evidently the wine had not made his heart generous. He seized a chair, planted it near the prisoner, and straddled it resolutely.

He began to speak with a hypocritical calm and so quietly that only here and there his words reached me. I heard at intervals, in an Italian larded with Venetian dialect and encrusted with Croatian saltiness, these phrases all too full of meaning: "We haven't scrambled all the way up here for the pleasure of being made fools of. — The mouse is here and His Majesty the Emperor and King... — *el garon o un falò de la tua casa e di to straèsi* [the gallows or a bonfire of your house and your rags]. — The dungeon, the rod on... Short irons and the gallows!"

After this last phrase, he rested his fist on the chair-back, his chin in the hollow of his fist, and waited. Domenico did not move an eyelid.

The sergeant cried: "Come now! We must have it out!" And he cut through the air with one of those gestures of his that had the right to instil a burst of life into the ramrod-stiff automata standing at his back.

Domenico was untied and hauled, with his thumbs lashed behind his back, into the middle of the kitchen. Before one could say a word, a ruffian dropped a running noose around his neck; another ruffian threw the other end of the rope over a beam. Meanwhile the sergeant, having regained perfect calm, had been at pains to light a cigar and, worrying its tip between his fingers and blowing words and smoke in equal measure, said, almost syllable by syllable: "Either you sing... or I hang you up like a sausage!"

The mountain man turned pale. Nevertheless his face, plain if not coarse, appeared transfigured by a noble fieriness. He said: "You are mistaken. There is no one here. But you are the stronger... do as you please."

He had barely finished when two ruffians, having seized the end of the rope, drew it up slowly, slowly. The poor man's arms strove with a supreme effort to wrench free, his chest swelled horribly, his face turned livid. When his tongue was already protruding from his contorted mouth, they suddenly let him drop. His clogs struck the floor with violence and his knees buckled under him as though he were a rag puppet. The noose was immediately slackened.

A brief silence followed, during which Domenico's face became of a cadaverous pallor.

"What do you think of my medicine? Has it brought you to your senses? Are you now prepared to reveal the truth to me?"

Domenico gave an affirmative nod of his head.

"That is more like it!" continued the sergeant. "Give him a mouthful of water." And he blew a long, thin mouthful of smoke — it was his expeditious way of expressing his entire satisfaction.

But Domenico drew himself up and, pressing forward as far as the ruffians holding the rope permitted, snarled through clenched teeth: "At you, and at every carrion that has defiled my Country!" And he launched a broad spit upon his torturer's chest.

The latter gave a spasmodic start and a torrent of exotic blasphemies. But with a supreme effort he contained his fierce anger and dissimulated it with the slowness of his gestures and an icy tone. He said: "Hang me that toad!"

The end of the rope was drawn up slowly, slowly...

From my cavern I watched and deliberated. More than once I had tormented the butt of my pistol, but an inward voice, imperious, had cried to me: "Do not be rash!" One corpse, two, a resounding act of vengeance... to what purpose? I resolved to play the last card in desperation only when the game was irretrievably lost. My anguish at Domenico's torment was filled with apprehension. I knew the fibre of the proud old man and intuited that he would not betray me. But Caterina?.. the horrible spectacle, the certainty that a single word could avert that atrocious agony... How would her woman's heart withstand it — the heart of his companion?

Caterina ran frantically from one wall to the other; she invoked the Virgin and the saints, throwing her arms wide. And when, seized by a sudden fury, she made as if to fling herself, nails first, upon the impassive tormentors and the iron arm of a gendarme had thrust her back, nearly knocking her to the ground, she screamed and tore her hair out in handfuls. But the secret kept fast in the depths of her breast — the more the tempest of the soul rages, the more it clings to the flesh — and in the depths it remained.

Domenico was lowered for the second time. He was so utterly stunned that the renewed questions must have reached his brain too indistinctly for him to be able to reply. The sergeant called his ruffians to a brief council. They must have concluded that the mountain man knew nothing, that the royal and imperial Police had taken hold of the wrong end of the stick. In the still air, orders rang out; around the kitchen and the farmstead, one heard the measured footsteps of the "armed force" disposing itself for departure. A few minutes and all was silence...

Domenico turned his searching eyes about the room and his chest swelled with sighs. Then he raised himself, looked at his companion with a long, intent gaze and stroked her head. A little later, having taken up his knife again, he returned to cutting his cheese, as though nothing out of the ordinary had occurred. Caterina gathered up the rosary from beside the hearth and began telling it again... this time upon her knees.

I allowed as much time to pass as was needed to give me certainty that the accursed troop was truly making its way down the slope; then I stepped out from under the ivy and appeared upon the threshold. Caterina did not even turn. Domenico was content to open the front of his shirt and show me the ring the noose had carved into his neck. I clasped him in my arms and, seeking those livid furrows with my lips, kissed them again and again.

What I am about to recount I have drawn from the letters written to me by Giustiniano. They are impressed upon my memory in indelible characters.

In order to reassure mother, Giustiniano sketched the situation for her, tempering it with a good measure of optimism. Luisa would undergo a few interrogations, but would soon be released. Father was known as an old patriot, a man of unbending temper, and would not be able to extricate himself quite so easily. But how were they to condemn him without evidence? The judicial authorities were severe beyond measure; but they set great store by appearing beyond reproach, and would not dare commit an openly brazen iniquity. As for myself, he had personally accompanied me as far as the mines at Agordo. From that point, I had taken paths that only I and the chamois knew how to travel. I must by now have arrived beyond the frontier, and the Police believed me still in Belluno and were searching for me there. Before long, they would receive letters from me from Switzerland, or from some other country over which the black and yellow did not fly. Everything therefore depended on not losing heart, so as to preserve their health, so necessary in that storm.

But the assurance he was at pains to instil into mother's heart was far from dwelling in his own. The newspapers, subject to rigorous censorship, seemed unanimous in treating the latest conspiracy with the conspiracy of silence. But news knows how to travel a great distance even without the aid of newspapers. Now from the lips of trusted men he encountered along the way, now at the café, gathered about a table, between one remark about the fine weather and another about the price of farm produce, words that could not be spoken openly without danger would slip into his ear, and from them, as always in such cases, the cheerful assurances appeared barely sketched in, while the sinister forecasts were painted in heavy strokes.

One evening these seemed to have received a tragic confirmation from events. "Among the Alpine gorges, a patrol had barred the path of the fleeing patriot... the order to surrender disregarded... that noble heart shattered by Austrian lead... the series of generous impulses cut short for ever. That very night, the body would arrive in Belluno for the legal formalities."

Giustiniano hastened home to prevent the grim news from reaching mother through some careless mouth. He found her already retired to bed. Wake her? To what end? She would have more than enough time to weep. He paced the room back and forth until very late; then, overcome by fatigue, let himself fall into an armchair and fell asleep.

Through the half-closed shutters, the first light of dawn was barely peering in, when mother, impatient for news, brought him his customary cup of coffee. She looked at him and, divining the sleepless and anguished night, instead of waking him, went to the untouched bed, took a blanket from it and spread it over his knees and chest. Just then, the doorbell rang with great violence.

Giustiniano awoke with a start, looked, remembered, understood. He threw his arms about the poor woman's neck and said to her: "My poor mamma, be strong and prepare yourself for the worst."

They departed in a carriage, a gendarme on the box, an inspector at their side. No hysterical attacks, no sobbing, no silent tears. Giustiniano held tightly to the right hand of the grieving woman, who paid him no heed, who remained motionless, her gaze fixed upon some distant point visible to her alone.

She was conducted into a large, cold chamber. Detached from the opposite wall was a board, one end resting on a trestle; upon the board a sheet was laid; beneath the sheet, with a sinister uncertainty, the forms of a man in the rigidity of death were outlined. A soldier, rifle at the order, stood stiffly at his side. Another, in his shirtsleeves, cuffs rolled back, his left hand plunged deep into a large boot upon which his eyes were intently fixed, was furiously working the brush and polishing it. Four officers, arranged in a circle, were conferring in low voices. A glowing brazier adulterated the limpid morning light with its flickering.

At the mother's entrance, the officers broke off their conversation and turned their faces toward that severe apparition. She seemed not to hear; her whole spirit was concentrated in the eyes that blazed beneath the black veil.

The soldier in shirtsleeves blew warm breath onto the boot and drove the brush down with blows more furious than ever... perhaps at that moment he wished that the barracks and the Empire were concentrated in that muddy toecap. The soldier standing stiffly beside the corpse did not venture to draw breath.

One of the officers stepped forward, drew back the sheet, perhaps with a sudden gesture. Then a sob resounded in the sepulchral silence, one only!.. but it was enough to reveal the depth of a heart.

I had to exert myself to calm that youthful fever and with a voice in which the muffled threat of an entire people vibrated, I said: "No, this is not a small matter. I have no intention of leaving the neighbourhood of Belluno until I have seen clearly into the heart of these entanglements. And where find a more trustworthy person than yourself, to serve me as informant and, if need be, as intermediary?"

"Come then!" I added, to declare his triumph. She moved toward the exit. The soldier in shirtsleeves blew warm breath onto the boot and drove the brush down with blows more furious than ever... perhaps at that moment he wished that the barracks and the Empire were concentrated in that muddy toecap. The soldier standing stiffly beside the corpse did not venture to draw breath. The situation was uniform, awaiting a command that admits of no delay. "I choose Domenico's house as my refuge. That peasant has the right stuff in him and will not betray us. I expect you tomorrow. Come by a wide circuit and make certain no one follows you. I shall judge by your cunning whether you are worthy of greater enterprises."

And having pressed his hand, I turned my back and left the shadowy plain. In the fields, in the brisk open air, I rediscovered that purity of a glittering sky which lends strength in the face of fierce emotions. I had not set down any burden along the climb; yet my weariness had suddenly vanished. I mounted with great strides and with every step the serenity of the night pressed itself deep into my thoughts; my spirit at last came to mirror the picturesque majesty of the mountain.

I recognised Domenico's little cottage, leaning against the cliff, its dark doorway beneath the pergola, bare now of grapes and leaves — the pergola that stretched as far as the road and divided the little kitchen-garden plot into equal halves. From the foot of the old walnut tree whose trembling fronds tickled the roof of the dwelling, two hounds launched themselves forth barking; but they soon checked their ardour upon hearing themselves called by name. They followed in my footsteps, stretching out their muzzles and anxiously sniffing at my calves.

One, two, three blows of the knocker, at decent intervals... sleep clings tenaciously to the labourers of the mountain. A fourth, a fifth resounded in the void, and at last through the cracks of the old door, light filtered through; two heavy clogs advanced noisily.


### Chapter Twenty

<!-- pages:98-100 -->

<!-- pages:98-100 -->



WHAT I am about to narrate I gathered from the letters Giustiniano wrote to me. I have them impressed upon my memory in indelible characters. To hearten mother, Giustiniano would sketch the situation for her, tempering it with a good dose of optimism. Luisa would undergo some interrogations, but would soon be set at liberty. Father had the reputation of an old patriot, an irreducible man, and would not have been able to extricate himself so easily. But how could they condemn him without proof? The judicial authorities were rigorous beyond all telling; but they were keen to appear most upright and would not dare commit a manifest piece of knavery. As for me, he himself had accompanied me as far as the mines of Agordo. From that point, I had chosen paths that only I and the chamois knew how to travel. I must have already arrived beyond the frontier, yet the Police still believed me to be in Belluno and were searching for me there. Before long, they would receive letters from me from Switzerland, or from some other country where the yellow and black did not fly. Everything therefore depended on not losing heart, so as to preserve one’s health, so necessary in that storm.

But the confidence he laboured to instil in mother’s heart was far from dwelling in his own. The newspapers, subject to rigorous censorship, seemed agreed in treating the latest conspiracy with the conspiracy of silence. Yet news knows how to travel a great distance even without the aid of newspapers. Now from the lips of trusted friends he encountered along the way; now at the café, gathered about a table, between one remark about the fine weather and another about the price of agricultural produce, words that could not be openly spoken without danger would slip into his ear, and from them, as always in such cases, the hopeful assurances appeared but faintly delineated, while the sinister predictions were painted in bold strokes.

One evening, these seemed to have found, in the facts, a tragic fulfilment. “Among the gorges of the Alps, a patrol had barred the path of the fugitive patriot... the order to surrender set at naught... the noble heart shattered by Austrian lead... the series of generous impulses cut short for ever. That very night, the body would arrive in Belluno for the legal formalities.”

Giustiniano hastened home to prevent the sorrowful news from reaching mother through some incautious mouth. He found her abed. Wake her? To what purpose?

She would have more than enough time to weep. He paced the room back and forth until a very late hour; then, overcome by weariness, he let himself fall into an armchair and fell asleep.

Through the half-closed shutters, the first light of dawn was barely peering in, and already mother, impatient for news, was bringing him his customary cup of coffee. She saw him and, divining the sleepless and anguished night he had passed, instead of waking him, she went to the untouched bed, took a blanket from it, and spread it over his knees and chest. Just then, the bell rang furiously.

Giustiniano awoke with a start, saw, remembered, understood. He threw his arms about the poor woman’s neck and said to her:

“Mamma, my poor mamma, be strong and prepare yourself for the worst.”

They departed in a carriage, a gendarme on the box, an inspector at their side. No fits of hysteria, no sobbing, no silent tears. Giustiniano held fast to the right hand of the grieving woman, who paid him no heed, who remained motionless, her gaze fixed upon some distant point perceptible to her alone.

She was led into a large, cold room. From the opposite wall a plank projected, its far end resting upon a trestle; upon the plank a sheet was spread; beneath the sheet, with sinister uncertainty, the forms of a man in the rigidity of death were outlined. A soldier, rifle at the order, stood erect and stiff-chested beside it. Another, in a simple waistcoat, his sleeves rolled back, his left hand plunged deep into a large boot upon which his eyes were intently fixed, was furiously working the brush and polishing away at it. Four officers, arranged in a circle, were conferring in low voices. A blazing brazier adulterated, with its ruddy glare, the limpid light of morning.

At mother’s entrance, the officers broke off their conversation and turned their faces toward that severe apparition, which advanced to the foot of the corpse with a firm, almost resolute step.

Someone spoke. He alluded to the painful necessity imposed upon him by duty and lamented the untimely end of the misguided who lay hands upon the sacred majesty of the Law... She seemed not to hear; her whole soul was concentrated in her eyes, which blazed beneath the black veil.

There was one who drew back the sheet with a sudden gesture. A livid face appeared. Then a single sob resounded in the sepulchral silence — one only!... but it sufficed to resolve the tempest of a heart.

Mamma lifted her veil, gazed intently upon the officers, and in a voice in which throbbed the muffled menace of an entire People, said: “No, this poor wretch is not my son! Carletto lives!... he lives, and he will avenge the anguish I have suffered, and he will avenge his Country!”

In the general stupefaction, maternal majesty had its triumph. She moved toward the door with as firm a step as that with which she had come. The soldier in his waistcoat blew warm breath upon the boot and laid on with the brush more furiously than ever... perhaps at that moment he wished that the barracks and the Empire were concentrated in that muddy upper leather. The stiff-chested soldier beside the corpse did not stir an eyelid... an automaton in uniform, he awaited a command that did not come.

The four officers, having turned suddenly pale, had their thoughts perhaps dragged by force to a distant country, beyond the Alps, where other mothers were suffering.

Into the cold surroundings of the barracks, into breasts hardened by iron discipline, accustomed to the fierce emotions of arrogance and of blood, there evidently crept the gentle remembrances of childhood, and reawakened within them the stunned pity. For a moment, the despot’s minions became once more men of the People. The severe apparition — unmolested, admired — vanished!


### Chapter Twenty-One

<!-- pages:100-108 -->



From the heights overlooking the mines of Agordo, I cast a final glance at my brother, who was shrinking into the plain below. Then I resolutely took the path up into the mountains, beginning a flight that was to prove full of thrills and truly singular. Upon reaching the zone of perpetual snows, I slowed my pace, both from exhaustion and from the growing difficulties, but also because I felt hidden and made safe by the space and the solitude. I plunged my face deep into it all... a great many of what, on the ascent, had seemed to be heights, now appeared level with the plain. The hatred of the powerful, I thought, could not reach so far as to disturb that virgin silence.

But having escaped the snares of men, I nearly fell victim to those of nature. It had been forty-eight hours since I had closed my eyes, and I felt terribly predisposed to that morbid slumber which the frost and the altitude can render mortal. At a certain point, I was seized as by a vertigo of the sublime; my legs would no longer hold me, and I fell prostrate upon the snow.

Now see to what extent dream and reality can intertwine and become confused! I was asleep and incapable of moving a muscle... of that there was no doubt. And yet my mind, vigilant within my inert body, was conscious of that boundless whiteness, of that fearful solitude; I could feel my fragile heart beating, the icy wind blowing about me, and, amidst the grim images of life that crowded into my brain, the idea of my peril predominated, rigid, motionless as a boulder.

Suddenly, I saw myself distinctly, with the constables at my heels. I run, I run, I run: or rather, it is the mountain that comes rushing towards me. But the constables are equally swift, and swifter... I can already hear the crunching of the snow beneath their boots; already their foul mouths thunder the *Alt!*, and I run, run... In my head, several shots rang out.

A second later I was on my feet, awake, perfectly awake! The shots were still echoing from crag to crag. I scanned the valley and spied a line of soldiers running along the forest, rifle in hand. They halted and formed a circle around a human figure, prostrate and dark against the snow. I noted their agitated gestures; then I saw, or so it seemed to me, a soldier prod the flesh of the fallen man with his bayonet, as if searching for a remnant of life. A smuggler.

A smuggler — I thought — mistaken, in these days of fearful suspicion, for game of greater importance. It was he who, stiff and unconscious, would later cause my poor mother so much anguish.

I crossed the summit as quickly as I could. The sky remained splendid; the snow, very hard on the northern slope, no longer gave way beneath my feet. I received a more than honest welcome, and bread and milk, from a mountaineer; then, at Neumark, I handsomely tempered my cheese with an internal ablution of excellent beer. As dusk fell, I set foot in Bolzano.

If the crowd helps to conceal, I could not have arrived at a more opportune moment. For several days the life of the entire district had been flowing back to Bolzano, attracted by the annual fair. In those times and in those places of difficult communication, the fairs took on the boisterous character of a village feast-day. Haggling brokers and frowning farmers on the hunt for a florin; mountain women, beautiful with that rustic beauty which is born of the open air and the sun, having acquired their plenary indulgence, departed in large bands, singing in chorus. There was a reek of wine-casks issuing in puffs from the taverns; there were herds of heifers and oxen that solemnly forced their passage through the human herds; mountebanks who drew the crowd with the grimaces of their painted faces, to the beat of the bass drum; sacks piled high and ironmongery spread out upon the paving-stones, and baskets brimming with fruit, and tables of confectionery sheltered beneath enormous umbrellas, and a great crush and a humming of voices and a riot of colours... and high above, reconciling every base human dissonance, a mighty wave of bells ringing out in festivity.

Not without working my elbows, I reached the main square, all cluttered with stalls and vendors. At one corner of the square stood the inn of the "Queen of England." With one of its sides it flanked the principal street, and it was upon this side that the main entrance opened.

I entered, went up to the first floor, and found myself face to face with a *chellerina* with a rosy little face and a snow-white apron... only to be told that the rooms were all occupied, that I should have to content myself with sharing a bed with some stranger — and good luck finding a willing fellow!

"Very well! In any case, I can begin with a good supper."

I was shown into the dining hall. Instinctively, I sat at the first small table, to the left, near the entrance. I was alone. The ample room was dimly lit by a single lamp. By its reflection I recognised, on one wall, the effigy of Ferdinand — the Emperor who last remembered he was a despot in order to demand, on his deathbed, a plate of gnocchi with butter. I looked, but in vain, for that of Franz Joseph... perhaps he had not yet found means to penetrate so far.

But I found something worse. Near the door was pasted a large notice upon which the double-headed eagle appeared conspicuously. Amidst the dark and jumbled lines, one name stood out in larger letters, a name that was well known to me — that of... Carlo Camillo di Rudio. The devil, what is this? I sprang to my feet and read. Austria was placing a price of six thousand florins upon my head. To take me alive or dead... to denounce me! — a trifling labour, the bounden duty of an honest citizen for some great votary of Order and the Law. By what a slender thread my salvation hung!

I should lie if I said I supped with a tranquil spirit. If being in a crowded centre had seemed my salvation but a moment before, now I glimpsed in it the greatest of dangers. How to emerge from that vortex unobserved? Was it possible that among so many people there was not a living soul who knew me? who might suspect, by intuition, the truth?

As if in answer, on the stairs, amidst the varied tread of feet, voices rang out speaking in the Bellunese dialect — voices which, I would have sworn, were not unknown to me. I hid my face in my right hand and spied anxiously between my fingers. The door swung open; several strangers entered, and behind them, one after the other, four stout fellows... the smugglers of the Trevis valley, the Burrasca brothers.

They looked at me, they recognised me; but they feigned ignorance. They sat at the neighbouring table, and the eldest, who was beside me, touched my foot with his; then, without turning his face, pretending to speak to his brothers: "I am glad to see you. Given the rumours going about..."

At that moment the *chellerina* with the rosy little face and the snow-white apron entered. She lit another lamp... the letters on the notice with the double-headed eagle now loomed energetically, almost doubling their eloquence.

I said: "Saved!... only to leap from the frying-pan into the fire." And pointing to the notice: "What a fine stroke of luck for you!"

Four faces darkened. The eldest of the Burrasca, striking a fist in the air: "Morsels seasoned with shame do not pass down this throat!"

And another of the brothers, in a deep bass voice: "The blood of traitors does not flow in the veins of the Burrasca!"

They had the chance to prove it. A few minutes more, and the reek of the gendarmerie spread through the wide hall. One ruffian remained on the threshold and, leaning his back against the jamb, barred the door. Another ruffian came forward and asked me, in a brusque tone, for my passport. I drew it from my wallet and was unfolding it when, growing impatient, he snatched it from my hand. He glanced over it, drew nearer the lamp to examine it better, and ended by saying: "I do not understand Italian. You must come with me to the gendarmerie."

"Hold fast!" cried one of the Burrasca, who well understood the dangers of the fine proposition being made to me.

An instant... a butchery! I snatch the passport back from the importunate fellow and, before he can react, the smuggler's fist strikes him under the chin like a sledgehammer. He fell, struck the nape of his neck on the tiled floor, and moved no more.

Meanwhile, another smuggler, who had approached the threshold on the pretext of reading the terms of the reward, lands his blow fair upon the teeth of the ruffian blocking it... blood spurts from his mouth, from his nose; he staggers and goes down in a heap.

"Wings to your feet!" came the cry. But I needed no exhortation... I was already leaping down the steps four at a time, clutching a pistol — a providential inspiration! A third gendarme was on guard at the foot of the stairs. He turns, sees me, sees the weapon, and understands its tacit threat, reinforced by a forceful imprecation. He lets me pass without lifting a finger, and I back away with my pistol at the ready.

But in that constable's compliance there lurked a goodly measure of strategy... he had averted his own danger and had driven me into a greater trap. A fourth gendarme, having heard the commotion above, had run to summon the patrol. Both were advancing at a run as I turned the corner into the square, and it was a miracle I did not fall into their arms. Before me the patrol, behind me the gendarme from the landing, who had followed me and was now pointing me out, gesticulating furiously.

Night had fallen. The square, crowded with stalls and people, was lit by variegated lanterns, Venetian-style. With a sidelong movement I leapt into that thicket of a crowd. "Stop him, stop him! Catch him, catch him!" But in the confusion, no one thinks to act. As for the soldiers, compact by virtue of discipline yet hampered by their arms, they content themselves with pursuing me at a run along the sides of the square... I slip through and cut across it. Then, put on the alert that I had ducked into a certain alleyway, they divided into two squads; one flung itself at my heels, the other rounded the block and ran to the other end of the same alleyway, to bar my passage and take me between two fires.

They ended by finding themselves face to face.

From beneath the entryway of a house, with my eye to the keyhole, I observed their movements, noted their faces, heard their words. "*Der Teufel!* Has the earth swallowed him up?" They debated for a moment and concluded that I must be — the goodness of them! — a pickpocket. The Police had cast good nets throughout the town and, sooner or later, I should be caught in them. They re-formed their ranks and departed.

Without a special warrant, the constables could not, at that hour, violate a private domicile. I felt myself temporarily safe.

I climbed the stairs to the third landing... the last. There were four doors; two opposite, one to the right, the other to the left. I listened... absolute silence! Then I sat upon the steps, and with my elbows on my knees and my face in my palms, I began to ponder. Imperceptibly, my thoughts darkened and I fell asleep.

When I returned to the reality of things, the first light of dawn was showing through the skylight, and already, beyond the door on the left, voices could be heard alternating in brief and indistinct snatches of conversation, with the clatter of plates and crockery.

I rise, dust off my trousers, and am about to descend when the door opens and a woman of uncertain age, in scanty dress, catches sight of me and lets out a cry.

"What is it?" a gruff, masculine voice demands from within. Before I could open my mouth, I found before me a stocky man in his shirt-sleeves, an apron at his waist, an awl in his hand.

I urged them not to be alarmed. I was a cattle-dealer arrived from Switzerland the previous evening, at a late hour. No matter how many inns I had tried, I had not managed to find a bed, even offering to pay any price imaginable. And rather than spend the night in the open...

"By Christ!" said the man. "From the pavement to a landing... what difference does it make? Come in, you can warm yourself by drinking a cup of coffee in our company."

I offered to add a drop of spirits, and drew out my purse which, to the eye, seemed well furnished.

What followed was a three-cornered contest... the cobbler affecting to take offence that I should think him capable of demanding payment for a kindness; the woman drawing back, with gestures of false modesty, so as not to let my gold coin touch her forearm and hand; and I thrusting aside the man's protests with a succession of "But... But really... for shame!" and pressing the woman and doing her courteous violence. I ended by triumphing, and then added: "Indeed, to tell the truth, the cold has given me an appetite — such an appetite!... and in the town, these days, one eats... one eats like a dog! If you would be so kind as to buy a few mouthfuls... to your own taste. A light repast does no harm to friendship!"

When the woman had gone down a couple of flights, I raised my voice and called her back to commend to her the wondrous virtue of holding water in one's mouth. I was not without friends in Bolzano, and if it reached their ears that I had preferred sleeping on a landing to disturbing them...

It was an imprudence — ninety-nine times out of a hundred, the most effective means of making a woman chatter is to recommend she keep silent. But my good fortune would have it that, precisely from that crack, my salvation should spring.

No sooner had my hostess reached the street and the outer door was heard to slam shut, than the cobbler took me by the sleeve and, dragging me inside to the kitchen, fixed me with a long, wordless stare. Then, abruptly: "You have been spinning me tales. A cattle-dealer, you! You are a deserter!"

— Quiet! How did you come to know?

For all reply, he twisted his lips and tapped his right eye with his forefinger — the gesture which, among all known peoples, signifies: "I have too much wit to be taken in!"

With the conversation once placed on that footing, I found myself, as the saying goes, on home ground, nor did I lack arguments and precise particulars with which to enmesh my host ever more tightly in his own misapprehension. And as he listened, he demonstrated his growing confidence by winking at me and giving me certain little claps on the shoulder that were meant to convey all the warmth of a demonstration of brotherly affection — for the deserter enjoyed, in my day among those populations, the fame that rightly belongs to heroes, and not to lend him a hand savoured to them of cowardice.

Had my spirit not been terribly preoccupied, that would have been a day of rest. My host showed a rough but thoroughly proved cordiality. Only, too much of a good thing does harm, and when, solicitous for what was to come, he began to devise plans for my escape, he became unconsciously the cause of no small anxiety to me. In his enthusiasm, he ceased not from contriving projects, each more fantastic than the last, and on the pretext of knowing the country better and of being a shrewder judge of men's dispositions, he persisted in attempting to force my hand — I who, in that particular game, was risking my skin.

In the end I persuaded him to hire a carriage and drive me out of the town in time to coincide with the changing of the stage-coach bound for Marano, which took place several miles away, in open country, late at night.

"The Police have cast good nets throughout the town," the corporal of the patrol had said the evening before. But no sooner had Bolzano and its fair fallen into slumber than I slipped through mesh and mesh, almost triumphantly, reclining upon the cushions of my carriage.

An incident, not worth the telling, was at most the cause of a little apprehension for me at Marano. But at Salzburg, in the German Tyrol, I ran a danger so great that to have escaped it without excessive effort seems to me almost a dream.

The postilion had pulled up at the inn of the Posta, indicating thereby the place where I was to pass the night, which was already far advanced. I went up at once, to wash my hands and face, to the room assigned to me, and when I was called down for supper, I took the key from the lock but, inadvertently, did not shut the door. Having reached the foot of the stairs — the recounting of these trifles is necessary — I set my hat upon a small table and hung the key on its little hook.

In the dining hall I found myself alone; but the inevitable pair of gendarmes soon arrived... unarmed this time. They ordered a tankard, a pack of cards, and struck up a game of tarocco. When supper was done, I drew near to them as though interested in the play, and, to be brief, we ended by starting a game in three. We passed in this manner a good hour, drinking to each other's health and exchanging banter, until those endearments between the devil and the holy water were cut short by the hostess, who came to remind us that the Law is the Law, and that the prescribed hour of closing had already struck some minutes since.

I asked her: "At what hour does the stage depart?"

— At seven precisely! — In that case, be so good as to wake me at four. I must go up to the mountain to bargain for certain heifers, and should like to be back in time to continue my journey by the stage. I can sleep in peace!

— Between two pillows!

I parted from the gendarmes with great shaking of hands; but, scarcely arrived in the corridor, I took up again my dark thoughts and made my way upstairs without reclaiming the key. Go back down? The door stood ajar; I could fasten it from within with the bolt. I entered, threw myself upon the bed, and snuffed the candle.

It had been many nights since I had laid myself down at full length upon a soft mattress, with a good blanket over my legs. When I awoke, the sun, already high on the horizon, was beating full upon my face.

Confound the hostess! I came downstairs with my spirit in tumult, ears alert... the inn seemed deserted. Whereupon, concentrating all my rage into my knuckles, I knocked upon the small table so energetically that the candlesticks rattled.

And out from the kitchen bustled the old landlady, her face framed in her cap. She was neat, dressed in her Sunday best. On seeing me she stopped short, her mouth agape. She allowed herself to be assailed by a tempest of reproaches; then, with perfect composure: "Good heavens! Whose fault is it, pray? The key to your room was there on its hook, and from that I concluded you were already on your way to the mountain. If you do not believe me, ask your two friends of last evening. They came by at about four o'clock. They wished to speak with you." — "Forgive me, I am sorry. I told them you were already far away... on the mountain." — "They came to speak with me? Prepare a good breakfast for three. I shall go and find them."

And I departed with every appearance of a man appeased. But the hostess lost both her labour and her expense.

It was a Sunday in September. The village population had poured into the little church, which must have been packed to the doors, for a human swarm was pressing outside on the small churchyard. Crossing the square, I saw the plume of a gendarme's hat rising above those dark heads, and rejoiced at the sight. "There is one who will not arrest me!" And I added: "So true is it that even the Mass serves some purpose."

Once clear of the village, I left the main road and struck off through the fields along hedged lanes; then I took to the pine-wood, which served me as an admirable screen.

The nearest territory was Muntzen, a cluster of houses situated astride the frontier. It had, like Janus of ancient memory, two faces: one gloomy, the other smiling — the former Austrian, the latter Swiss and republican. I knew this, and I knew moreover that the frontier line divided, politically speaking, the village square and the inn, which thus found itself under two jurisdictions. Hanging on the wall of the common room was a cuckoo-clock. The gendarmes of the Empire could not pass beyond that clock without provoking an international incident. On this side, the regime of the cudgel, the manacles, the gallows; on the other, the regime of a free people, breath drawn in full lungs, salvation!

I crossed the frontier at a solitary point, entered the village, and came into the inn from the Swiss side. Setting foot in the common room, I was looking about for the cuckoo-clock when my attention was drawn to a group of *Jäger* — the militia which Austria posted to guard the frontiers. Among them, welcome sight! the uniform and well-known face of one of those guardian angels who, early that very morning, had come to impress upon my wrists their holy stigmata. I could not restrain a burst of loud laughter.

"Holla, friend! Bad hunting this morning. And to think the blackbird was in his cage. Had you but climbed the stairs, the six thousand florins of the reward would have clinked in your pocket sooner or later!"

He admitted that I had proved the cleverer, and carried his graciousness so far as to offer congratulations. "Indeed, to show you my sincerity," said he, "come, and I'll stand you a bottle!"

— And I a whole cask, if it please you. But you must be so good as to come over to this side! — And I rubbed my hands together.

But that gleeful exuberance, so natural in one who has left the open sea behind him, was for many long months my last; for many long months, thinking back upon it, I felt as it were a pang of remorse.

The failure which had already been manifest to me grew more pronounced with every piece of news gathered as I pressed forward into free territory, until it assumed the proportions of a true disaster, and my spirit was left as though engulfed by it.


### Chapter Twenty-Two

<!-- pages:108-112 -->



HISTORIANS affirm that the spy Felicita Buanvecchiato, having caught some fragment of a conversation held by Pietro Fortunato Calvi, hastened to reveal our attempt to the Authorities, who thus had full wind of it even before we had taken the first steps to carry it into effect.

To what extent that denunciation went, I know not; but I do know that in bringing about our ruin it was well-nigh superfluous.

How I came from London to Belluno without molestation or mishap, I have already narrated. Others, having set out from different points but with the identical objective, did not have equal fortune.

Foremost among the merits of a conspirator is that of knowing how to judge, at a glance, which papers he must keep and which destroy — a simple art, and yet so difficult. Ignorance of it has caused the collapse of infinite enterprises that would have altered the course of History; it has furnished the executioner with innumerable victims.

Francesco Chinaglia and a certain Cerruti, both from Lonato, both of our number, cross the Alps in all safety and arrive at a small village on the border of the Tyrol and Cadore. Here they commit the fatal indiscretion of raising their elbows too much and picking a quarrel with the innkeeper. They are arrested, taken to the gendarmerie, and searched. What is this? An itinerary marked with initials and annotations that arouse some suspicion. The Police take notice and detain them, and arrest, at brief intervals, Luigi Moratti of Castiglione delle Stiviere, one Roberto Morin, and a Fontana. Either they carry in their pockets the same compromising itinerary, or they cannot give a satisfactory account of themselves. The official report states that these men were arrested at the same time as Calvi, who passed through those parts on the 17th of September. When the latter was interrogated, he gave himself out as a certain Meyer, a subject of the Duchy of Baden, and produced a corresponding passport; but it availed him nothing. He was taken to the Police and searched, like the others — and from the lining of his overcoat was drawn forth, signed by Mazzini, Kossuth, and Ledru Rollin, his commission as general-in-chief of the Italian revolution. It was Calvi himself who, out of an excessively modest conception of his own influence, had insisted upon obtaining that document. A formality as vain as it was fatal. Who would have contested the first place, or denied obedience to the modern Leonidas, in that Cadore which was his Thermopylae?

He was conveyed to Mantova. The grim walls of that fortress had already swallowed up don Bastiano Barozzi, my father, and my sister Luisa. Poor Luisa! — her indomitable instinct for self-expression confined within a veritable sepulchre; her delicate constitution tortured by the iron, inhuman discipline; her sensitive heart rent by the thought of her father’s sufferings and by the unheard-of miseries that surrounded her; the modesty of her girlhood violated at every hour by the coarse searches of the turnkeys, insolent and brutal — not a word of comfort, not love, not light!

Did she receive compensation for it? Margherita di Savoia, having become Queen of Italy, was pleased to pay a visit to the women who had, for political reasons, savoured the delights of Mantova. She haughtily forgot Luisa of the Counts of Rudio. From her point of view, she was not wrong. Our family had fought and suffered, in the dark times, so that Italy might exist; but it had not fought and suffered to round out the dominions of the House of Savoia.

The Castello di San Giorgio at Mantova had become, by then, what the Spielberg had been in the days of Ferdinando. Other fortresses compressed, in their dungeons, the pulsings of hearts that yearned for the redemption of the Fatherland; but in none, as at Mantova, did the ferocity of the oppressor manifest itself in tragedies so vast, so sinister, so unrelenting. And yet, fatally, from that torment of bodies the Italian soul blazed forth. In their fearful caution, the Culoz, the Kraus, the Benedeck did their utmost to conceal from the civilised world the identity of the imprisoned and the terrible barbarity of their methods of procedure. But there are virtues that cannot be hidden, that spread their light beyond the walls of any Bastille; revealing, by their very excellence, the full abjection of the ephemeral supremacy that has sought to crush them.

Italy saw, trembling, passing through the narrow compass of those secret cells without pomp, in rags almost, yet no less bright than of old, the iron temper of her Attilio Regolo, of her Trasea Peto; no less serene than in the ancient world, the soul of more than one Socrate — and it was no small spur towards the finding of herself again. A period of history sweating martyrdom, it found keen researchers who co-ordinated its pages, who documented, with meticulous care, its every phase — but not yet its own Tacitus to immortalise it in prose of granite, to draw from it an austere monument worthy of such greatness. Let him arise! — on that day the thousand and thousand statues that Italy lavishes upon her crossroads will seem the toys of pygmies.

When Calvi was thrust into the Castello di San Giorgio, don Giovanni Grioli, Pietro Frattini, Angelo Scarsellini, Count Carlo Montanari, Carlo Poma, Giovanni Zambelli, don Enrico Tazzoli, Tito Speri, the hero of the ten days of Brescia — giants of duty, a chosen contingent from every class of society — had already submitted, beneath the shadow of the noose, to the extreme insult of despotism.
