A designated victim, there was instead immured, some months later — precisely on the 28th of March 1855 — Felice Orsini, who was afterwards, by sawing through the double bars of a window and lowering himself on his bedsheets and crippling a foot, to regain his liberty and exercise so great an influence upon my destiny.

Felice Orsini was, for a certain period, confined in Cell No. 9, where my father was held together with several other patriots. He told me, in 1857 in London, that the good old man, breaking prison discipline through the kindly condescension of the warders, used to help his fellow captives to bear the bitterness of their long imprisonment by gathering them about him and humming the romances then most in vogue.

Felice Orsini also had the opportunity to correspond, by tapping on the wall, with Pietro Fortunato Calvi. They encouraged one another, expressing the hope that they might soon go forth, free among the free. But the heroic defender of Cadore harboured no illusion whatsoever. He knew too well the systems of government dear to Austria and, from the same wretched fate of the ten martyrs who had preceded him within those walls, he inferred his own inexorable condemnation.

Throughout the long, tedious trial, he displayed the imperturbable calm of a man accustomed, upon the field of battle, to look death in the face, and only kindled with noble indignation when his judges, having brought him face to face with an abject informer, did not blush to prefer the purchased word of that creature to his own — the word of a loyal enemy, a man of honour.

His participation in the movements aimed at overthrowing, by force, a tyranny that had imposed itself by force, he admitted without reticence; the companions who had been arrested with him he defended to the last, affirming that they had joined him to take part in an alpine excursion, knowing nothing of his subversive designs; the secret of the other conspirators’ complicity remained jealously guarded within his adamantine breast, and my father and Luisa and don Bastiano Barozzi, held long on simple suspicion, eventually came out into the open by virtue of a decree of amnesty.

And the sad foreboding of Pietro Fortunato Calvi came true. By sentence of the 17th of January 1855, confirmed at Vienna the following June, he was condemned to die by the noose. At thirty-eight years of age! For an enterprise that was scarcely conceived, much less begun. So anxious did Austria show herself to suppress the men who, to the power of thought, would have known how to couple action, and become agitators and leaders of other men!

In his last days, he desired a meeting with his companions in misfortune, and, rather than seeking comfort from them, took pains to comfort them. He wrote most affectionate letters to his brother Luigi and to his father who, a faithful servant of Austria, condemned as madness and worse that headlong patriotism of his, that irrepressible need for action. And this thought cost him the only tear that History records of him.

And he wrote a letter to Teresa Duodo of Padova, the young woman whose kisses he had promised himself as the ineffable reward, once the Fatherland was redeemed. That same young woman, broken by the tragic parting, lived on in voluntary, perpetual widowhood.

Austria had devised, in those times, a new species of mercy — to grant some clemency, provided the condemned man requested it in humiliating terms and as a repentant sinner. If the clemency were not sought, she could say: “The charge of cruelty flung at me from every corner of the world is unjust. I was prepared to pardon, but it was not wished.” Whereas if the petition for clemency reached her, the very terms in which it was couched stripped all prestige from the imprudent patriot — to live physiologically, but at the price of one’s own moral suicide.

It was affirmed, in my time, that Calvi was urged to solicit that singular clemency. He is said to have answered with scorn: “My hatred for Austria is more intense than my love of life. I shall ask nothing and I want nothing from the oppressors of my Country!” If this be legend, how well it harmonises with that strong and upright temper!

But what is certainly not legend is the dignified bearing with which he faced death. He moved towards the gallows as towards a goal foreseen from the very outset of his heroic struggle with the oppressor, and it seemed that, having meditated long years upon its horrors, his spirit had grown impervious to them.

Having crossed the Ponte di San Giorgio, he thrust his head from the funeral carriage and gazed, with sorrowful eye, upon the city and the countryside. I am firmly persuaded that he, a lover of “the truer and the greater,” mourned at that moment his inability to have realised the yearning of his life. Over those lands he saw no sacred colours of redemption raised aloft; he saw no free men scattered across the fields, cheerfully at their labours.

He drew back and returned to the profound meditations upon what lies beyond the grave — which he, despite the residue left in his soul by sinisterly reactionary schools, had always pictured as Hellenically serene.

Having arrived at the foot of the gallows, he took the cigar from his mouth and gave it, still lit, to one of his guards. He thrust aside with scornful roughness the arm of one who sought to help him mount the plank set beneath the column from which he was to hang. From that height he looked down, smiling, upon the Austrian officers, several of whom had removed their hats in a sign of respect. And having kissed monsignor Martini repeatedly — a priest held in disfavour, for his honesty, in those places where prebends are dispensed — he said in a firm voice: “I am ready!” Thereupon the executioner set about his work.

To crown the misfortune, the gallows did not correspond to the great stature of Calvi, whose feet tickled the earth. His agony was long and exceedingly painful. Edoardo Winkler, commanding the military escort, was seized with fierce indignation and could not contain himself. He cursed the executioner and, once the execution was complete, departed almost fleeing, his hands thrust into his hair.

Pietro Fortunato Calvi was the last of the great men murdered for the Italian cause. He remained hanging for several hours; lowered at evening, he was cast into a pit dug at the foot of the gallows.

But his greatness moved even a hyena — the notorious warder Francesco Casati — who composed his remains in a coffin and preserved his relics for the veneration of resurgent Italians.

And the virtue that the Mantuan Bastille had been unable to conceal seemed to blaze forth from that humble grave, dug at the foot of a scaffold. His moral influence was so great, so intense the treasury of latent energies that it stirred to life, as to disquiet the oppressors. The Marshal Radetzky, already decrepit, already on the point of abandoning for ever the hateful contests of the world, felt no shame in pressing the Special Tribunal that had condemned Calvi to draw up a defamation, in order to paralyse the “deleterious” influence of that shade.

He was to find in the President Visentin the base soul disposed to second him. How many official lucubrations, which nonetheless serve as a guide for exalting or vilifying the individuals belonging to history, repeat an analogous origin and have no greater worth than that of Visentin’s!

The sycophancy that blossomed after the hour of triumph, busily engaged in the jealous care of attributing all merit for the redemption of Italy to the great and the small who knew how to profit by it, glides with the lightness of an aeroplane over the beneficent influence exercised, beyond the grave, by the martyrs who sacrificed their existence to rouse the indolent. It is an influence that may be intuited, but that eludes all calculation — all the easier, therefore, to adulterate the truth!

Yet Calvi was fortunate in this: the spite of the partisan cliques, which spared no adversary, however great and unblemished, seemed to blunt itself before this figure so disinterested, so calm in its audacity. And it was said that Italy “would have had in the hero of Cadore a second Garibaldi.” But the opportunity of becoming one was criminally wrested from him.


### Chapter Twenty-Three

<!-- pages:112-120 -->

<!-- pages:113-120 -->



I PUSHED on as far as Zurich, where I found a nucleus of compatriots who had escaped, by way of exile, the reactionary storm that had followed the disturbances of February. Among them was Radonitz, who received me with great courtesy. He made not the slightest allusion to the incident at Locarno. I also paid a visit to Caronti. His words could not have been more flattering; but upon first seeing me, his face had assumed an expression of wonder not unmixed with a tinge of unease. Perhaps, I thought, it is my imagination. Giuseppe Mazzini had so thoroughly prejudiced me against him!

That dealer in trinkets, who had gone bankrupt at Como, was living in Zurich in great luxury, at the expense of a wealthy lady who manufactured chocolate. He had, however, contrived to set up a modest felt hat factory, and several Italians found there the means to earn their sustenance. I became one of their number, and it seemed to me a ray of fortune. But sniffing the air, I was not long in discovering something heterogeneous. There were handshakes that resolved themselves into a mere contact of skin; evasive replies; monosyllables, innocent enough in themselves, but which acquired a subtly treacherous flavour from the grimace of the face that accompanied them. In all, a coldness, a coldness! — even at the afternoon meal, when lips exchanged more spontaneous phrases, the mysterious threads that connect souls were wanting.

Of what were they finding fault with me? Perhaps of having come there about their bread and filching from it a small portion?

Things suddenly changed, and so radically as to make me suspect some understanding among them. But what understanding, and why? — The sharp edges were gone; they used the familiar *tu* with me; they clapped me confidentially on the shoulder, offered me tobacco — it seemed they had at last made up their minds to regard me, in every respect, as one of themselves. So much so that one Saturday, they proposed a nocturnal pleasure excursion on the lake. Four strokes of the oar, four tankards, eight perhaps, a patriotic chorus under the starlit sky. It would be like a reliving, beyond the Alps, of that fair land where our sweethearts languished.

So as not to seem discourteous, I accepted. It so chanced that, to remedy some forgotten errand or other, that very evening I returned to the factory. To my great surprise I found it lit up. As I drew nearer I heard distinctly the excited voice of the exile Orioli, saying: "By God, it does not suit me! I should never have the courage to think of my poor old mother again!" I entered, and it seemed as though an iron hand had gagged every mouth. *(1)*

From that day forward, no one spoke to me again either of the lake, or of the tankards, or of the starlit sky. The reason I never learned, nor did I trouble to investigate it. Nevertheless I had the certainty that the sudden cordiality of my companions was feigned; that I found myself, like an intruder, alone and hated in their midst. I ended by feeling thoroughly disgusted, and resolved to depart for Paris.

Without bidding farewell to mine hosts? Not entirely. Caronti, honest or dishonest as he might be, had procured work for me; I considered it my duty to pay him a farewell visit. I thus obtained a new surprise. Upon my arrival in Zurich, he had received me with cheerful and courteous words, but with a face that betrayed his disillusionment; now, having barely learned the reason for my visit, he poured forth a flood of phrases expressing regret; but his entire feeling could not keep itself compressed, and stamped his face with signs which I judged unmistakable. My departure was causing him intense pleasure.

I reached Paris in December of 1853 with two francs in my pocket and a letter of recommendation for a certain Zappa, a Turinese hatter, which had been given me by my foreman at the factory — the one person in Zurich who had shown me any sympathy. With the two francs I did not make much headway; but the letter served me well. Zappa, a man of liberal opinions and generous heart, immediately offered me his hospitality. I had to make do with sleeping on a mattress in the kitchen; but penniless and in that season, the offer was anything but contemptible. The more so as I found a way to repay the debt, by giving Italian lessons to the mistress of the house.

Signora Zappa was French. She had ideas that were more than liberal — they were revolutionary. These were sufficient to establish between us the most cordial relations. She was not young, she was not beautiful; but she preserved a grace whose charm it was not easy to escape. When she spoke of the 2nd of December, in which she had taken an active part, and of the fierce repressions from which, by good fortune, she knew how to extricate herself, she became eloquent, and in the fervour of her evocations forgot even the corsets she manufactured and to which, truth to tell, she owed her popularity rather more than to heroism.

In order not to live at the expense of those good people, I set myself to looking about, and ended by finding employment near the Pont de la Bastille. It earned me two francs a day. What employment? For fourteen unbroken hours I turned, by sheer force of arms, the wheel of a merry-go-round.

Having my hands to the wheel did not prevent me from keeping my eyes on politics. And in that epoch, even for one who was not in France, to occupy oneself with politics was to occupy oneself with Louis Napoleon.

The coup d'état, regarded with execration by uncorrupted consciences, blessed by the Pope, was greeted by the other Sovereigns as a great feat of prowess combined with enviable sagacity. The blood that was spilt was not of the kind deemed blue, and the stupefaction into which liberal France was thrown was, in their eyes, peace restored. And since Louis Napoleon was freeing the reactionary Governments from the spectre of the Republic — too often confused, and artfully so, with that of the Terror — it was the most natural thing in the world that the reactionary Governments should hasten to bend their gaze benevolently towards Louis Napoleon, to recognise his advent, and to expect great things of him.

After the coup d'état, the Dictatorship! Napoleon was too cunning to abuse it without counterposing to odious persecutions a series of measures calculated to seduce the multitude. France was overflowing with hands disposed to raise barricades. Unable to chain them, he provided, with shrewd haste, to win them over. By impulse sent out from Paris, every centre of France, great and small, was seized by the fever of loans and public improvements. And the wave of well-being that spread through the working classes received a sensible increase from the sanitary laws designed to improve their dwellings; from the reordering of taxes, which, lifted from articles of prime necessity, were laid upon luxury goods. To this one must add the generosity of the agricultural loans.

Throughout France, the cannon-shots had thus been succeeded by the blows of the hammer, and the moral effect which naturally sprang from the improved economic conditions was reinforced by the splendid promises that kept hope green and made that unexpected period of extraordinary prosperity seem likely to endure. Louis Napoleon, to hear him tell it, abhorred war. He proposed many conquests — but they were the conquests of Peace: lands to be reclaimed, vast tracts to be opened to cultivation, harbours to be deepened, canals to be dug, rivers to be made navigable. In these useful and glorious undertakings, the sword would yield primacy to the implements of labour, and every peasant, every workman animated by good intentions would become his faithful warrior.

Sentiments worthy of commendation! But in order to place faith in them, it was necessary to wipe from the mind every bitter memory; to lose sight of the fact that that fateful man spent half his day dissembling, with flattering phrases, the evil deeds he reserved for himself to commit in the other half.

Unfortunately, from the multitudes — constrained almost perpetually to consecrate every faculty to the scant satisfaction of the body's needs — it is not reasonable to expect a worship of the ideal that is far-seeing and tenacious. The popular soul does not press forward to its holy vindications save under the impulse of exceptional circumstances, and therefore by fits and starts, necessarily as tumultuous as they are fleeting. And it is a great deal if it does not then attempt to make up for lost time by defiling its magnanimous wrath with ferocious manifestations. Then the ineluctable need drives the herd back to graze — the soul defers its strivings toward the better to happier times, and falls asleep again.

The French people did not understand, or would not understand, that every new prince, risen to power having tasted crime and inclined to perpetuate it, does not venture upon it so long as, craftily practising virtue, he has not allowed the evil seed time to consolidate its roots. The laborious digestion stupefied thought; the rosy hues of the present prevented men from divining the threatening phantoms that were massing on the horizon. And when the servile Senate restored, in the person of Louis Napoleon and his male heirs, the imperial dignity, the multitude hastened to ratify it. Almost eight million Yeas — a meagre minority of two hundred and fifty-three thousand Nays.

On the 1st of December 1852, only a year after having stained his hands and soul in the blood of his fellow-citizens, Louis Napoleon donned, at Saint Cloud, the crown of France; and in assuming it, inscribed in History the name of Napoleon III.

The Empire reconstituted, the Constitution retouched, the various branches of Government assumed, in appearance, their regular function; in reality, France continued to be governed by Dictatorship — veiled as much as one might wish, but no less absolute.

The Prince Consort, who came from London in the spring of 1854 to cement the friendship that was taking shape between England and the new Empire, had occasion to express his own great wonder in this regard. Napoleon himself candidly confided to him that he never permitted his Ministers to assemble together to discuss public affairs. Each individual Minister dealt directly with him on any matter of importance, and what he had deliberated with one was rarely confided to another.

This wholly personal government, deemed pernicious even by the old sovereigns, led us liberals to frame the most sombre prognostications regarding France's future. The fixed idea upon which the politics of Napoleon III were to pivot became manifest at once — to emulate the great intellect that had drawn its house from nothing; that had impressed deep traces upon so great and so elect a portion of the world. He was embarking upon the arduous enterprise with unbridled ambition, a measureless mania for intrigue, an absolute want of faith to the given word and to treaties, a corruption fit to undermine quite other constitutions; the absence of every regenerating principle; a mediocre intelligence, barely so; his own presumptuousness; his great name.

How could the immense disproportion between the means and the object have failed to alarm us? The thought, the driving force of a great State, concentrated in that dark and ambiguous nature, were, in our eyes, the power consecrated to the accomplishment of evil, to propelling, with precipitate steps, a noble Nation toward disgrace and ruin. I must surely be permitted, after half a century, to avail myself of History in order to verify, with the indisputable wisdom of hindsight, how well founded our forebodings were.

Napoleon III had one flash of practical intelligence in allying himself with England. But in crowning his head with the imperial diadem, he had not blown from his soul his natural duplicity. He knew not how to show himself a sincere ally, and his gross machinations so thoroughly irritated the fine diplomacy of Britain as to find it hostile to him in his moments of supreme need. England would indeed have moved declared and open war against him, had the Germanic victories not forestalled her.

Napoleon III knew not how to transform the war with Russia into a glorious uprising of peoples yearning to crush the tyranny of the barbarians. It was reduced to a long and bloody siege, and when Sebastopol fell, it was because General Pelissier manfully refused — and not without pungent phrases — to submit to the orders that came from Paris.

The war for Italy! It is insisted that it was championed by him alone, forgetting the impulse with which the French people subscribed the loan raised to cover its expenses. There are two pitched battles in that war: Magenta and Solferino. The first was won almost in spite of the Emperor, through the disobedience of General Mac Mahon. In the second, the Napoleonic fibre had something of a posthumous gleam, and it was the only victory of the Second Empire owing to the personal merits of Napoleon III. But immediately afterward, his vulgarity regains the upper hand. He tore up, without warning, the articles of the alliance; insulted the King who had heroically fought at his side. And the essential object of the war — that of substituting his own influence in the Peninsula for the Austrian — was destined, thanks to the virile patriotism of the Italian party of action, to fail most miserably. Napoleon III, even as a conqueror, emerged from the bloody struggle irremediably defeated.

The expedition to Mexico, originating in financial speculations that could not be avowed, is an adventure worthy of a filibuster. It began with a betrayal analogous to that which smoothed the way to Rome for his Oudinot, and ended, as had the war for Italy, with the shameful tearing up of solemnly undertaken obligations. The fact that it was Maximilian, and not the Emperor, who fell a victim to Mexican lead, serves to demonstrate how little human or divine Justice governs the affairs of the world.

In the secret negotiations that preceded and followed the defeat of Denmark and the resounding victories of Prussia over Austria, Napoleon, playing a double game and simultaneously offering his alliance to both contending parties, aimed at obtaining a gratuity from the victor and at reclaiming for France the line of the Rhine. But he was disappointed and mocked and covered with ridicule by the sagacity of Bismarck.

What is to be said of the Franco-Prussian war, which he willed, which he undertook without the most elementary knowledge of the enemy's forces or his own?

I do not judge the greatness of men by their success, for I know defeats infinitely more glorious than any triumph. But for that to be so, the ruins must appear to us as irradiated and rendered sacred by the grandeur of the thought, the nobility of the sacrifice, the sublimity of the purpose. The life of the third Napoleon seems to me, from this point of view, arid as a desert.

It is understandable, perfectly understandable! No one could have outrun the times and discerned in the uncertain future the positive catastrophe. But already in the spring of 1854 there abounded tangible reasons that legitimised the mistrust — nay, the profound hatred — of liberals. Louis Napoleon, trampling underfoot the principles he had sworn on pain of death to defend, had, by his crimes, broken France and deprived her of every possibility of those democratic initiatives which are the greatest of her glories. He had shown himself most cowardly and ferocious precisely against the factions that had contributed most to drawing him out of the near-obscurity in which, for so long a time, he had lived. Gratitude weighs heavy upon vulgar souls. He was yet to render a stricter account of all this than those that are balanced upon a page of History — History, too often venal and mendacious, like the consciences of the men who write it.

For Italian liberals it was, in particular, a torment to contemplate the power of that man who had repaid their benefits and their hopes by championing the interests of the ancient enemy of the Fatherland; who had sacrificed the flower of Italian youth to dishonour Rome reborn; to thrust her back into the arms of the papacy; to prostitute her by main force to the most monstrous things that had survived from the barbarism of ages past.

In the spring of 1854, it was already considered more than probable — imminent — that Napoleon III would ally himself with the House of Savoy. And since it was known that the Empire did not desire the unity of Italy, and that Vittorio Emanuele II and his Ministers did not deem it possible, it was inferred that from such a union there would derive, at most, a territorial enlargement of Piedmont. Some French prince substituted for the petty tyrants who infested Italy; but the Pope at Rome, sustained by the imperial arms and subservient, by force of instinct and circumstance, more to the interests of the Empire than to the cause of Italian independence. History would renew its ancient phases; we should have upon our necks, along with the old masters, the new ones.

This train of ideas derived further strength from the marriage of Napoleon III to Eugenia Montijo, Countess of Teba — a Spanish lady of matchless beauty, restless, intelligent, astute, tenacious, and Catholic not by calculation but by deeply rooted convictions. Thanks to her influence, upon the throne of France there sat virtually, alongside Napoleon III, the priest.

The cause of Liberty could not have resumed its fatal course, either in France or in Italy, so long as Napoleon III stood interposed between it and the future. Therefore it was necessary to think of suppressing him — to hasten the return of things to their natural order; to spare, at slight sacrifice, a sequence of tumults and hatreds and rivers of blood.

I meditated long, took my notes, and wrote. And to whom could I write, if not to the most serene conscience I knew — to the man who had been able to surprise the heartbeats of the Fatherland and proclaim her living, and to foretell her greatness, when all believed her inexorably laid out in the tomb?

He was at that time in London. I communicated to him in minute detail a plan of mine — of which I shall have occasion to speak further on — requesting his assistance in carrying it out; disposed to go to the uttermost limit, or to draw back, as his counsel might dictate. Felice Orsini, who at that period was still on the closest terms of friendship with Mazzini, saw my letter, and to it I owe my future relations with the patriot of Meldola.

But neither to that first communication of mine, nor to two further ones that followed, did Giuseppe Mazzini reply. I recalled Pianciani, and in a fourth letter, addressed to him, I poured out all my bitterness at that silence — undoubtedly deliberate — which tasted to me of undeserved contempt.

The reply was not long in coming, and it was brief and terrible. "In London I was accused of having been the voluntary cause of the arrest of Fortunato Calvi and of the failure of the attempts on the Calore. The accusation came from Zurich."

So Austria, powerless to strangle me, had avenged herself for my escape — to isolate me, to annihilate me with calumny. And it was as though a veil had suddenly fallen from my eyes. Now I understood the conduct of my companions in Zurich; nor did it require any great penetration to interpret the secret motive behind the eccentricities of Caronti. The Austrian poison had been diffused by his lips.

I replied to Pianciani without losing a moment, and in my indignation I did not even measure the phrases that alluded to Mazzini — so quick, I observed, to lend his ear to the defamations of a man concerning whose honesty he himself, a few months earlier, had expressed to me the gravest suspicions. I concluded by requesting the means to go and confront my accuser.

Pianciani sent me one hundred and fifty francs, but accompanied by what was almost an injunction to keep quiet and to await my vindication from time. Having pocketed the money, I embraced the Zappa couple with great warmth, and turned my back on Paris. It was a splendid morning in May of 1854 when, suddenly, from the heights, I saw spreading at my feet the blue lake, and glittering in the first rays of the sun, the damp rooftops of old Zurich.

I had myself announced to Caronti, without revealing my name, as an exile who wished to communicate an important message to him. The anteroom was long, but our interview brief. He was still in his dressing-gown and received me standing upright, his two thumbs thrust into his waistband. As soon as he recognised me, he went pale and muttered between his teeth: "What has he come here to do?" — I shall tell you in a moment! — I shut the door, and stepping forward so that our very breath was almost mingled, I satisfied his curiosity to the full, reinforcing my words with a demand for satisfaction. "And mark well: I require it, and I shall have it before I leave this room!"

He peered furtively out of the wide-open window and attempted to gain time, protesting his innocence. On the contrary, he had always entertained for me an unconditional esteem, an almost brotherly affection. Speak ill of anyone? He would have preferred his tongue to fall from his mouth in shreds!

— Very well! But words are carried off by the wind, and writing remains. Set down in black and white your unconditional esteem; furnish me with a formal retraction. That and nothing else will satisfy me!

As I spoke, his lips trembled. I believe he understood, from my expression, that this was no time for evasion. He went to the writing desk, wiped the honest sweat from his brow, and wrote. Then, without turning round, he extended his arm and held out the sheet to me.

I read it, and contented myself with saying: Very well!

Thereupon, as though we were old friends vying with one another in civilities, he fell over himself to offer me his services — in whatever part of the world, at whatever time. I had only to express the wish. If moreover I was making for Italy, and thought a brief halt at Lugano advisable, he would furnish me with a letter of introduction for Count Grillanzoni, who had settled there.

He was evidently seeking to reconnoitre the ground in order to prepare some fresh villainy for me. I cut off his phrases at the mouth. What I would do I did not know; in any case it concerned me alone. Turning the key, I went out.

In the corridor I felt a hand laid upon my shoulder. And a voice whispered in my ear: "How goes it, Moretto?"

It was Angelo Fumagalli, pale and smiling. He put a finger across his lips and added: "Come to the stables. We can talk at our ease!" He took my right hand and, with gentle violence, dragged me along as though I were a child. "I know everything, I heard everything! I saw you cross the garden and recognised you at once. I said to myself: There is something fresh afoot here. What, though? I had some ugly suspicions, and — why hide it from you? — I did a rather shabby thing: I put my ear to the door. But better so! There will be no more misunderstandings. Do you know that that scoundrel even accused you of having stolen a watch from his coachman? That one I never believed. As for the rest — you must forgive us. What would you have? We fell into the trap like a pack of imbeciles. And you had a narrow escape, let me tell you! Do you remember that plan for a nocturnal excursion on the lake? A plot had been hatched to stun you with a blow of a cudgel on the head and to throw you, hands and feet bound, as food for the fishes! Caronti would then have denounced you all, and at one stroke freed Austria of an entire hornets' nest. The very devil! Fortunately Grioli had sense enough for everyone and saved you. God bless him! We thought we were avenging the Fatherland!"

He embraced me and was sobbing. Then, precisely as the other had done, but with different intent: "And now what do you mean to do?"

— My dear fellow, I have tried everything within my power to do my duty, and I have found myself suspected by the very men who should have defended me. One road alone remains to me: to persist, to answer the accusations with silence and with action.

I left Zurich with the intention of taking refuge in Piedmont, and made my halt at Lugano and my visit to Count Grillanzoni. He received me coolly — I understood, and held out to him the retraction signed by Caronti. He thereupon showed me in turn a letter received a few days before. In it that same worthy was urging him to do everything in his power to prevent my return. My irascible character, he said, was the cause of continual vexations and scandals which, for the good name of our emigration and in the interest of the cause "so dear to us all," it was imperative to avoid.

I renounced Piedmont and returned immediately to Zurich. In the meantime Angelo Fumagalli had, with indignant manner, taken his leave of Caronti. From him I learned that Felice Orsini had been making enquiries to discover my address.

A few days later I was engaged in the attempt at Maloggia.

*(1)* This Orioli was the brother of the canon who was martyred at Mantova. When, after the *Risorgimento*, Francesco Giuseppe went to Venice, a Regiment was detailed to form his guard of honour. Orioli, who belonged to it, found his soul revolted by the ties of blood and preferred to be brought before a Council of Discipline rather than pay homage to the gaoler of our Fatherland. And there were those who, for that act of noble pride, called him a fool!


### Chapter Twenty-Four

<!-- pages:120-127 -->



THE plan, on this occasion, was to seize the steamboats that ply Lake Como and to attempt the capture of the city itself. At the first signal of insurrection there, several armed columns were to descend from the Grisons to raise the Valtellina and lend force and breadth to the movement.

The proposal to take part in it came to me directly from Pompei of Pavia, of the *Giovine Italia*, Stradella section — though he spoke to me in the name of Felice Orsini. I note this because Orsini, in his *Memorie*, names Giuseppe Mazzini as the effective and acknowledged leader of this attempt, seizing upon the paltry number of those who answered the call to demonstrate "the negligible influence" of that honest man among the ranks of the party that was, in the strictest sense, his own.

In all fairness, both Fumagalli and I came to know of Mazzini's direct participation and his presence in the Grisons only some months after the enterprise had failed.

Great is my veneration for the memory of Felice Orsini; yet the motive behind his virulent denunciation of this and other Mazzinian ventures has never been altogether clear to me. Had he not implicitly approved them by taking part in them, and at times accepting a leading rôle? And in approving them, had he not assumed full responsibility for them? Every word of censure that Orsini levels at the Master seems to me censure that falls back upon his own head. And never, as when I read his autobiography, did I have occasion to suspect that the flashes of that ardent spirit were not always worthy of the hero.

I shall add, in passing, that the very phrases of recognition which the fierce Francesco recalls and holds up to ridicule are a variant of those we actually employed, according to the instructions we had received — whether he allowed himself to be carried away by the fervour of polemic, or whether his memory failed him.

The man posted on the Via Giulia did not say to us — as we walked wearing a flower in our hats — "Hola, good fellows, where are you bound?" He asked whether we were Italians. And our reply was not, as Orsini claims, "We are going to Signor Francesco, or to Signor Giuseppe," but simply: "Conti." And when the other man shook our hands, I added, still according to the instructions received: "Where is Tito Gelsi?" *(1)*

In what condition Fumagalli and I arrived at San Maurizio, Orsini has noted: "weary, famished, tattered, dressed like beggars." He might have added that, notwithstanding all that, we were no less impatient to come to blows for our Country.

Be that as it may, the man who had questioned us led us to an inn and left us alone. Several days of fruitless waiting went by. Although I had somewhat improved my appearance, I could not assume the air of a prosperous burgher who had come to those parts to enjoy his honest income in peace. This circumstance, which might attract attention, caused me some unease. Nor did the neglect in which we were left tend to reassure me.

Enterprises such as ours are sometimes secretly set in motion by governments with an interest in stirring up some complication. It is enough, for us who live in California, to recall General Frémont.

But Frémont must have had a comparatively easy mind, for he knew that at his back stood a great force, watchful and ready to spring into action. And, moreover, the happy outcome of the attempt matters little when little depends upon it. The important thing was to furnish the United States with some pretext for intervention: a trifle suffices the wolf who slakes his thirst in the same stream as the lamb.

Things stood very differently for the Italian patriots, who were compelled to hinge all their plans upon the fortunate outcome of a first, uncertain surprise. The bitter experience of recent years led me to suspect, in our abandonment, the cruel sport of the unforeseeable: a squall that had escaped all calculation, a ford momentarily impassable, a letter gone astray, an order misunderstood, the brief hesitation of a conspirator, an unguarded word, the meddlesome curiosity of an idler, the bark of a dog — a thousand petty causes sprung as if from nothing — and nothing at one's back to fall back upon!

And the consequences of failure? The reproach of the timid, the haughty contempt of grave men, the venom of cowards. Any rogue may stone you, any oracle of the Law spit in your face, any miser sell your head, any hireling shoot you in the back for the sheer lust of slaughter. And the hatred of the modern inquisitors that multiplies the hardships of your flight, that finds means to slam every door in your face, to take the bread from your mouth, the pillow from beneath your head; that would grip you with pincers, wring your neck on the pretext of restoring the age of easy digestions; that, when it cannot rid itself of your body, assassinates your soul with slander.

With what intensity must the forerunners of the third Italy have kept their eyes fixed upon their ideal, to prefer, over a life of ease, the bleak odyssey of the rebel!

One evening Fumagalli appeared before me brandishing a letter in the air. It was from Tito Gelsi, announcing that his arrival was imminent — and he fell upon us at that very moment as we were reading his brief lines. In our conversation, Felice Orsini mentioned no names; confining himself, in what concerned his plans, to assuring us that he could dispose of two thousand rifles. He gave us a letter of introduction to an innkeeper at Maloggia and, assuring us that we should shortly receive his instructions, took his leave.

The innkeeper at Maloggia was not privy to the secret affairs, yet had some inkling of them and looked upon them with favour. He informed us that he was expecting a crate of "hardware" from Saint-Étienne — it arrived, and with it, the instructions from Tito Gelsi. These were the arms that were to serve for the redemption of the Fatherland; Orsini directed us to a spot not far from the Muretto glacier where we were to put them in order. The innkeeper quietly furnished the means of transport.

The chosen place was favourable. A crag sheer on three sides; an abandoned shepherd's hut hidden among thickets and chestnut trees. From up there we could follow every winding of the path that led to it. No danger of surprise.

It was the middle of August, 1854, and we made the most of the long days to press on with our work briskly. We rose from our pallet of grass at first light and, after contemplating the dawn, set ourselves to the task. Having assembled a rifle, one would level it, test the action with a silent motion, then stand it along the wall interwoven with fronds. We leant them there with a kind of tenderness, as though they were delicate trinkets.

During those days I committed an imprudence which, though it had no bearing whatever on our enterprise, had a very marked bearing on my own small bark and caused it to change its course. I had just finished setting the fiftieth rifle in order and, as I examined it and ran my hands over its various parts, it seemed to me so exceptionally fine that I should have counted myself a fool not to make it my own. I used the screwdriver as a graving tool and carved my initials on the stock. Fumagalli, who was watching me, asked: "What are you doing?" And I, paraphrasing an expression of old Domenico's: "A tool like this, and I'll ruin the livers of half Bohemia."

The mountain calm took hold of us again. But there came, all too soon, the agitation, the collapse.

I had gone out on lookout and had barely shaded my eyes with my hand when, in the distance among the thickets, I caught sight of a cloud of dust. The breeze, which was blowing toward the mountain, drove it in the wake of those who were raising it; but it could not conceal from me the glinting of arms. They were militia men, and in good number; and they were certainly not climbing the slope in pursuit of hares.

I had a great deal of trouble persuading Fumagalli that we must seek safety by making for the heights. He wanted to stand his ground and "sell his skin dearly." We crawled through the undergrowth and gained the mountain.

They arrived, surrounded the hut, and called upon us to surrender, with rifles levelled. Half an hour later they were departing in small detachments, carrying off our rifles and our hopes.

We came down to Maloggia after nightfall, and the innkeeper, on seeing us, shrugged his shoulders. He advised us to remain quietly out of sight at his inn until he had sniffed the wind well enough.

Two days passed. On the third he appeared before us with a crestfallen look and announced that Tito Gelsi had been arrested. He added: "As far as I am concerned, no fear! You are free to make use of my inn for as long as you like — so long as there is polenta and milk for me, there will be for you as well. But if I were in your shoes, I should prefer to pitch my tent a mile and a half further down the valley, precisely there where the coach changes horses. The news reaches you sooner, and if matters grow cloudy you will have, close at hand, the means of slipping away. Besides, a brother of mine lives thereabouts, and I shall commend you to him. It will be as though you were still among family."

We followed his advice, and on the very evening of our arrival there, having barely forced down a forkful of food, we heard the jingling of the harness bells and the crack of the postilion's whip. Going out with the other curious bystanders, I noticed a figure all muffled in a cloak who had just stepped down from the coach and was eyeing me from head to foot. I returned the scrutiny in kind, and he, stepping forward — the signal! Drawing me aside, he handed me a roll and a note: the roll contained two hundred in gold, and the note a single word: "Save yourselves!"

With the help of the Maloggia innkeeper's brother, we hired two saddle-horses. We left word for someone to knock at our door at three in the morning; but I was roused abruptly long before that. At the foot of the bed, arranged in a semicircle, stood several uniformed men — the barrels of their rifles converging upon my chest.

We were conveyed to Chur and brought before a Federal Commissioner who had come expressly from Berne. The interrogation was brief. We declared ourselves deserters. We had no knowledge whatsoever of political conspiracies or of clandestine arms. The Commissioner let us speak; then he made a sign. They brought him a rifle, and he, with a smile, held the stock toward me. I had no need to strain my eyes overmuch. In characters which, by some strange illusion, seemed suddenly to have grown much larger, my three initials stood out clearly: "C. C. R." Up there, near the Muretto, without knowing it, I had carved my own condemnation and another's.

And we were shut up *in domo petri*. My first thought was of my father and of Luisa, who were in the prisons of Mantua; and never did I see them more vividly in my mind, nor feel for them a more profound compassion.

I should say at once that at Chur the delightful methods of Austria were unknown. Imprisonment, but with respect for misfortune; observance of regulations, but humane regulations; some privation, but not the Lent of Galeazzo.

The greater torment for me was the forced inaction — though I was, more or less, acquainted with where patience had its dwelling. Not so Fumagalli, who, as the gaoler told me, paced ceaselessly back and forth in his cell like a beast in a cage.

He was confined on the first floor, I on the third. It was impossible to see one another; but taking advantage of the brief moments when the sentinel was at a distance, we exchanged a greeting. If the sentinel admonished us, or the warder struck the door, I would draw back and let myself sink onto the bench with a calm that astonished even myself; whereas Fumagalli would furiously resume his pacing to and fro. He had upon his feet heavy mountain boots, reinforced with certain nails no more massive, nor more lacerating, than those that hold the Christ to the cross in our country churches. The floor, which was of brick, ended by bearing grooves that might have been cart-ruts, and the warder, pointing them out to him, remarked half in jest, half in earnest: "You cost the Republic too much."

Our fate depended upon the decision that the gentlemen of Berne would take. But before any news at all should arrive from that capital, the heralds of winter were already making themselves felt.

Along the prison wall there grew a small kitchen garden. A great pear tree spread its wide canopy almost level with my little window. When it rustled, the cell filled with a murmuring that my mind transformed into vague, melancholy melodies. I often asked myself: "Who can tell me whether I shall still be here when the peasant who tends the garden comes to gather the pears?"

One morning in November, pressing my face to the grating, I saw among the leaves a man with a thick beard picking the fruit and filling his basket, which hung by its hook from a branch. We looked at one another — the peasant swings his arm and tosses me one, two, three, half a dozen. There was fog; the leaves had turned yellow. And I said to myself: "Who knows whether I shall still be here when the tree puts forth its blossoms again?"

Just at that moment, the warder flung open the door, and, rattling his bunch of keys and smiling, invited me to follow him.

In the corridor I found Angelo Fumagalli, stamping his iron-shod feet like an impatient colt.

We were conducted to Berne, and for the second time I heard from the Federal Authorities the sermon on the sacred duties incumbent upon those who enjoy generous hospitality. As a recidivist, I was banished in perpetuity from the borders of the Republic. And they warned me not to set foot there again, or I should be made to undergo severe penalties. They did me the courtesy of reading the articles of the Code in which these were specified.

To the question of choosing the part of Europe or of America to which I wished to be conveyed, I replied, as on a previous occasion: "To England."

And so ended, for me, for Fumagalli, and I believe for a pair of other participants, that attempt which Felice Orsini calls "more comedy than tragedy." The same Orsini was also arrested, as I have already noted; but he slipped from the gendarmes' hands and, after having briefly sought refuge in Chur and Zurich and then made some attempt to stir up trouble in Milan, departed for Vienna, where he entered into negotiations — first to enlist, for some purpose unknown to me, in the Russian army, and then to conduct revolutionary propaganda within the Austrian one. When both attempts came to nothing, he passed into Hungary and, arrested at Hermannstadt in Transylvania, was conveyed in weary stages to Vienna and from Vienna to the castle of Mantua, which swallowed him up, as I have already had occasion to remark.

Giuseppe Mazzini, Maurizio Quadrio, Nicola Ferrari, and a few others, who had gathered in the Grisons to prepare the armed bands, had managed, after various vicissitudes, to reach safety.

Of the arms gathered here and there at the various passes, I believe that only those entrusted to Fumagalli and me fell into the hands of the Federal Authorities. These, already on the alert since the time of the Clementi trial, had redoubled their vigilance upon warnings received from the police of the Lombardo-Venetian kingdom.

And since there were no victims to lament, we might perhaps accept without rancour the definition of "comedy" that issued from the wrathful mind of Felice Orsini.

Yet to anyone who reflects that in those days there were afoot, through the strangest of alliances, schemes to redraw the map of Europe, that affirmation of the persistent restlessness of the Italians — that fresh protest against the tyranny which forbade us to constitute ourselves a Nation — will seem neither ridiculous, nor untimely, nor vain!

*(1) This was the name assumed by Felice Orsini to mislead the Swiss Police.*

END OF PART ONE


### Chapter One

<!-- pages:127-134 -->

<!-- pages:127-134 -->

AS an introduction to this second part, a page essentially of love claims its rightful place. What I here relate I had, in part, from the Count di Rudio himself, and in part from his wife. Nor was I without the aid of certain intimate letters, yellowed by time, yet still exhaling, fresh as ever, the fragrance of the past.

The little politics that peeps in through the doorway carries the soul of Giuseppe Mazzini. For what, after all, is di Rudio, if not a reflection of that great conspirator? Setting aside the disproportion of their genius, the only substantial difference between the two seems to me this: the one held that without faith in God neither a spirit of sacrifice nor an enduring honesty is possible; the other managed to sacrifice himself and to live honestly, having set aside that mighty hypothesis altogether.

In London, di Rudio made his way to the innkeeper already known to us, at whose establishment Fumagalli had preceded him. Tressoldi introduced him to the chorus-master of Drury Lane, a Milanese who engaged him as a chorister in the Italian Opera company. Thus, as in other days from flowers, di Rudio drew his sustenance from music — that is, music sung by others; for, on the whole, he was obliged to content himself with opening his mouth wide and waving his arms. The important thing was to pocket five shillings each evening, and in this he succeeded marvellously well, as any seasoned chorister could have done.

Let it be added that he was paid to listen to Madame Glassier and to the tenor Armandi; whilst, to be transported into ecstasy by the high notes of this Bolognese cobbler, the wealthy paid relatively fabulous sums.

When the season ended, the Opera Company drifted back, one by one, beneath the fair skies of Italy; but di Rudio remained beneath the leaden skies of London. He persisted in reading the newspaper advertisements and at last found work at the Wapping Docks, a locality situated at the eastern extremity of the metropolis. The work consisted of handling rushes and fashioning coverings for bottles, making use, for this purpose, of a newly invented machine, the contrivance of a Frenchman.

The excessive distance of the workshop obliged him to forgo the cordial hospitality of the good Tressoldi. The necessity of bread had, in any case, already thinned out that pleasant company. To follow the manifold threads of their lives, to trace worthily the stubborn, exhausting struggle sustained by our exiles in order to keep afloat, would require a volume unto itself.

We should encounter feet peering out between sole and upper, bearing witness to the long and fruitless hunt for employment; bellies pinched by the belt, betraying the enormous disproportion between appetite and the means of satisfying it. I should have to tell of nocturnal hunts across the rooftops and the mewing of innocent cats destined for the pot; to describe the cramped lairs shared between half a dozen human creatures, each instinctively reclaiming, in sleep, their portion of the blanket — a blanket sometimes pieced together with the art of a mosaicist, compounded of newspapers gathered from the benches in the public gardens.

The drama would not fail to chill us with its cold breath... virile constitutions corroded by hunger; sensitive natures ruined beyond all hope of recovery; poems vanished silently amidst the clamour of a thieving, pleasure-seeking world; the death-rattles of the consumptive; corpses cut to pieces upon the cold marble of the mortuary slab... acts of heroism without witnesses, unrecorded, yet no less worthy of history than those which unfolded in the light of great occasions.

Against this backdrop, rendered now in strokes of lamentation, now of comedy, now of the sublime, stands out the pensive figure of Giuseppe Mazzini who, to feed the generative flame of divine conceptions, of genius and of virtue, goes knocking at the moneylender's door and pledges... his own boots.

Nor should we want for hearty laughter hurled, like defiance, in the face of misery... frequent dreams of glory; sentimental love affairs piercing to the heart through threadbare elbows; long fasts forgotten in the reading of Byron... and likewise those occupations dripping with grease that know nothing of poverty. Angelo Fumagalli, to cite one example, made an excellent living manufacturing sausages.

The Count di Rudio had found lodging at Baldwin's Garden, in the house of one Mancherini, a figurine-maker from Lucca. Fumagalli and a certain Leoni were the only exiles with whom he maintained, in those days, relations of close friendship.

He had come to know Leoni at Tressoldi's inn, and they had grown intimate through a characteristic incident which I am pleased to recall.

Austria and the petty tyrants of Italy, who had been given so many headaches by the exiles, devised a new means of defence. They granted to the birds of prey chained in the prisons of their homelands full freedom of flight, on the condition that they should pass themselves off as political refugees and insinuate themselves amongst the honest company.

The calculation was that a change of sky would not change their ways, and that the public, implicating the good in the blame provoked by the wicked, would cease to pity the exiles and to lend moral support to their noble aspirations.

A veritable flock of such gentlemen descended upon Tressoldi's inn. They were led by one Bonacina, a Milanese, a swaggering instigator who, backed by his confederates and emboldened by the innkeeper's soft character, soon turned that congenial establishment into a sinister den of ruffians.

Tressoldi's comical despair, and the fact that he had caught the scent of the troublemakers' special mission, induced di Rudio to resort to an extreme expedient. He found willing souls in Leoni, a kind of athlete, and in other well-built young men; then, having wrested from Tressoldi the promise of the strictest neutrality, he awaited a favourable occasion.

One evening Bonacina and four or five fellows of the same stamp were demanding to drink at no cost and threatening to turn everything upside down if they were not served instanter. Di Rudio stepped forward and ordered them to clear the room... and never to set foot in it again.

"This beardless boy must be tired of living!" replied Bonacina, making as if to deal him a backhanded blow. It was the signal for a furious brawl... Fumagalli had his head broken; but the ruffians, overwhelmed and badly mauled, ended by beating a retreat, leaving their leader to make the best of things as he could. Alone, with one eye closed and the other barely open, his mouth bloody, Bonacina backed away to the window and, swearing he would have his revenge, leaped out and vanished into the darkness of the street.

Not a single grim face, that evening, at the sign of Tressoldi's! But the affair had, for di Rudio, an unexpected sequel. One foggy night, returning from the Opera, with his head full of trills and high notes, turning a corner into a deserted lane, he found himself face to face with Bonacina, heavily muffled, fist raised and in that fist a large knife.

"Ah, ah!" said the scoundrel mockingly. "Now we shall see whether your liver is sound!" But instead of delivering the blow, he drew back... he had caught the gleam of a pistol barrel.

Then di Rudio was seized by a singular impulse: to add, to the silent eloquence of the weapon, that of a brief homily. He recalled to Bonacina the heroism and greatness of his fellow Milanese, in such striking contrast to his own conduct. And probing that heart, to rekindle beneath the ashes the gentle sentiments of childhood, he asked him, among other things, whether he did not believe that his mother was ashamed to have brought into the world, nursed and raised a son who was surely betraying her finest hopes; who mocked the sufferings of his fellow citizens; who spat upon her grey hairs; who renounced the Fatherland.

Bonacina, hard and contemptuous at first, ended by absorbing, head bowed, his dose of medicine. Then, flinging away the knife with a sudden movement, he fled headlong, sobbing like a child. The love of the Fatherland had known how to kindle some glimmer even in that base and debased soul!

The Count di Rudio, from his lodging at Baldwin's Garden, went every evening to exchange a few words with Fumagalli and Leoni. After their customary long walk, they pooled what few coins they had and allowed themselves a bottle... of concentrated sunshine. Such was the name they gave to the product of the Lombard vineyards; the only available means by which they could warm themselves at the sun of Italy.

The first to appear rarely at the accustomed gathering was di Rudio.

Apropos of these absences, Leoni asked him one evening, with an expression that revealed the unspoken meaning, whether he was lodging in a house of young ladies. "Without a doubt!" replied the Count. "At the Mancherinis' there are two. One is called Sara and is the daughter of the household; the other goes by the name of Elisa and is their niece. Her parents live in some corner of the County of Surrey and have sent her to London, if I was correctly informed, to train in the art of artificial flowers."

Leoni pressed to know more, and the Count di Rudio, who had picked up the musical jargon at Drury Lane, replied:

"Miss Sara has a soul tuned in the minor key, soft and caressing; Miss Elisa, on the other hand, has vibrations in the major, impetuous, conquering."

And Leoni, looking fixedly at Fumagalli and winking at him: "Let us recite the *de profundis*. The *Moretto* is ill and... we shall soon lose him!"

The following evening and the next and many more besides, di Rudio was nowhere to be seen. His two friends grew anxious and resolved to go to Baldwin's Garden to investigate. They found the door ajar. To ring the bell, or to step boldly forward without asking leave? Whilst they stood in two minds, they heard a sweet, gentle voice saying: "In my garden there grows a pear..."

A masculine voice, that of di Rudio, interrupted: "I have already told you that the names of trees are masculine, and the names of fruits feminine. Excepting *dattero*, *limone*, *ananassa*..."

— Except *dattero*, *limone*, *ananassa* and *fico*! — observed another woman's voice, more vibrant than the first.

Leoni drew Fumagalli away. Then he said: "I foresaw it. The fellow is done for!"

Signora Mancherini, a Booth by birth, a distant relation of the founder of the Salvation Army, was English through and through; but her husband's occupation had dusted her, not to say plastered her, with art more or less classical. She spoke with evident ostentation of Venuses and Dianas. And with the Gods of Olympus she had overburdened every piece of furniture, every shelf, every projecting surface of her little apartment. She was also accustomed to dressing her hair in a fashion that recalled times long since vanished.

Her daughter Sara was a fair flower of eighteen years. In the judgement of the Count di Rudio, she took a little from her father and a little from her mother... a blend of English and Italian. But the northern disposition, active and assertive, had, by an apparent contradiction of nature, ended by remaining as if atrophied, whilst the gentle and submissive disposition of the Italian woman, developing and all but bursting forth, stamped the whole of her character in a very marked degree. She did not seek to shine; she did not imperiously demand love; she preferred to love without being loved in return to being loved without loving in return. Not yet having reached the age at which feeling, fully developed, knows what the tempests of passion are, she was content with the tepid breath of friendship, and lavished upon her cousin Elisa the tender devotion which she would later reserve for a husband; in her cousin she rejoiced, suffered at her vexations, and exulted in her joys. Happy in her own half-light, she asked for nothing more, provided that Elisa should triumph.

This latter, meanwhile, as if to make it so deliberately, was endowed with a temperament that tended to accentuate Sara's disposition. Of the English people it is generally said that they have strong teeth and that once they succeed in seizing their prey they never let it go. Beneath Miss Elisa's fair hair, beneath the rosy skin, behind the blue eyes that even today call forth a sense of sweetness, there nestled a tenacity of purpose and a courage by no means common. These qualities, finding occasion to manifest themselves, appeared as a paradox and astonished the superficial observer; but not one who, accustomed to analysis and deduction, had noted the significance of that broad, firmly drawn chin and the energy of her gesture, however rare and sparing in every English young lady.

The Count di Rudio told me that, as a girl, she possessed a certain suppleness of character and intellect; but she could never have been brought, even had she wished it, to the point of self-negation. To love strong souls; to open to them, as a corolla to the kisses of the sun, the most hidden gradations of her heart; but in order to be stronger, greater together as two. To conclude a treaty of alliance — that yes, with all her ardour, and to remain faithful to it unto the last; to sign even an honourable surrender, never!

Di Rudio had resolved to teach Italian to the two young women at the urging of Signora Mancherini. Why refuse? To study our language becomes fatally synonymous with loving our Fatherland, and our friends are always something of enemies to our enemies. The sympathy of the English was greatly coveted by the Italians, and even the arts of peace may serve to win a battle. Besides, every small stone has its value in great undertakings!

And so it came about that the Count di Rudio, every evening, after having laboured prosaically among rushes, applied himself to instilling the sweetest and the strongest of modern tongues into the charming heads of two young women born and raised upon the foggy banks of the Thames; and perhaps the Gods of Olympus, from the heights of their shelves, smiled down upon the scene.

Accustomed to the blunt directness of military life, the Count di Rudio proved a rather brusque master, and the hour of the lesson assumed, so to speak, the rigidity of a geometrical problem. But no sooner had that hour passed than Signora Mancherini set her steaming tea service upon the table and the atmosphere changed as if by enchantment... books and notebooks vanished, and animated conversation flowed without interruption, punctuated and, as it were, annotated by sips of the warm beverage. Signora Mancherini, who was stout, filled the large armchair entirely with her own person. Sara sat, by a habit consonant with her inclinations, upon a low footstool, resting her forearm upon Miss Elisa's lap. The Count di Rudio, lean, nervous, dark, amidst so much triumph of fair complexions and rosy hues — like a grain of pepper — sat at first calm and composed; then, little by little, he grew all gestures and starts, and his countenance, most mobile, flashed and sparkled.

Before long, through his doing, the titanic figures who had striven for our *Risorgimento* were embroidered upon the fibres of those souls, all the more clearly for the fact that the fabric receiving them was sensitive and unversed in powerful emotions. And they were transformed by it: they felt, suffered, hoped and willed in the Italian manner.

What was bound to happen, happened. The heroes were dead or far away; the Count di Rudio was close at hand, young, attractive. He too had suffered for the sacred cause. In the eyes of the two young women he ended by growing to heroic stature, and they blended about that head his own prestige and that of others.

Angelo Fumagalli had a singular expression to characterise that situation: "Venice," he would say, "had the Moor; Belluno, being smaller, has only the *Moretto*. But the Moor was loved by a single Desdemona; that rascal di Rudio has two. Perhaps... and why not?... with not a shadow of malice, mind you!... perhaps he has three!"

The only one who seemed unaware of this was the Count di Rudio himself. Love knows no tropics; hearts burn at the Pole as furiously as at the Equator. It is the manner of expressing affection that varies with latitude. The Italian impetuosity is unknown to the young English woman, who perhaps senses instinctively how discordant it would sound in that world of subdued tones. Free and mistress of herself, she adorns herself, when she loves, with a composed dignity... the distinguishing mark of the free. Hence her unjust reputation for coldness. That charm glided over the spirit of the Count, absorbed and all but saturated with a wholly different passion.

True it is that in the long hours spent at the workshop, whilst his practised hands carried out the habitual labour mechanically, the mind of di Rudio strayed toward the two young women. Often the daydream absorbed him so completely that the noise of the tools and the faint odour of the rushes were, as it were, suppressed. In those moments — so he affirmed to me — a mysterious fragrance of youth and health penetrated his lungs and gave him a subtle intoxication. Sometimes he had caught himself yearning for the evening... But was it not in the evening that, having scraped away the scales of vulgarity, he conversed with his great ones, upon great things? The explanation seemed more than sufficient to him, and he did not trouble to enquire any further.

Unexpectedly, his employer, a Frenchman, withdrew from the business. A new proprietor, new methods: the foreign labour was dismissed, and the Count di Rudio found himself once more without immediate resources, very nearly destitute. For a time he went forward on the small savings he had put aside; then he found himself reduced to utter penury. The day he turned out his pockets and searched his every pocket within, he reflected, not without dismay, that he already owed a month's rent to Signora Mancherini. For several evenings past, at the lesson, he had appeared pale and listless. Miss Elisa asked him whether he was unwell. "Not precisely," he replied, "but I must say something that weighs upon me. I have most pressing obligations. Tomorrow evening I shall be engaged elsewhere; and I do not know when we shall be able to resume our lessons."

The festival of rosy and azure hues was over... the *via crucis* of the hero reduced to the level of the pariah began, the one to whom society denies salt and fire. He went out early in the morning, and returning at a very late hour, he managed to slip along the corridor, avoiding unpleasant encounters — he had come to call them such. He was of such a temper that he would have preferred to face the mouth of a cannon to the face of a creditor.

Unfortunately, one cannot leave hunger on the threshold of one's own house, nor hurl it into the street like an awkward burden. He hurled instead to the moneylender his few superfluous possessions, one by one. He had left behind his old friends, and to approach them now, when he was in extreme need, seemed to him a deliberate courting of rebuff. He would have been glad of any work; but these were times of crisis and the unemployed formed an army. In the newspapers the "Help Wanted" columns were conspicuous by their absence; those of "Situation Wanted" were measured by the yard. He went about soliciting here and there; but to compound his misfortune, he had the appearance of a gentleman. The more humane contented themselves with answering with some pleasantry. Others, having barely glanced at him, grunted the laconic "Go on!" which to Italian ears seems saturated with so much contempt. He walked as long as his legs would support him; then he resorted to the recourse of those without recourse... sitting upon a bench in some park and waiting for the late night... a painful repose.

Relief came to him unexpectedly, one night by mysterious ways, when he had not tasted a morsel in forty-eight hours. He had returned home with his customary precautions. He did not light the lamp, and before undressing, from the force of habit, turned down the bedclothes. Something heavy rolled upon the bed. Shortly afterwards he was unwrapping a parcel tied with great care, and a delicious fragrance tickled his nostrils. Before him, entirely at his disposal, was a marvel of roasted viands. And he bit into them with canine voracity, without reflection, without thought. Only when he had wiped his mouth did he, wondering that he had waited so long, ask himself: "Whence has all this abundance rained down upon me? Who can have taken care of me?"

His first suspicion fell upon Signora Mancherini and her two gentle creatures whom she, in the manner of Providence, sheltered beneath her ample wings. But he reflected, not without logic, that Signora Mancherini would have laid out a proper table, and that a parcel tied with care must have come from a distance. None of his old friends knew of his critical circumstances.

What mystery, then, was this?

To put it briefly, he had already stretched himself beneath the bedclothes and the outer world was already receding, and even the consciousness of his own being had grown dim; but a small part of his brain still worked on mechanically... Silence; the limitless and tenebrous void. Now and then, within it, the two questions flashed unresolved: "Whence this abundance? Who is taking care of me?"

And since life and the universe, in practical terms, exist only insofar as they are reflected in our minds, we may gauge to what minimal terms they may be reduced even within the skull of a hero!


### Chapter Two

<!-- pages:134-136 -->



THE AGREEABLE nocturnal surprise continued with mathematical regularity... a well-tied and well-stocked parcel, every twenty-four hours. The Count di Rudio resolved to get to the bottom of the matter, and one fine morning, having set his room in order as usual, he crouched in the window recess behind the curtain, determined to remain posted there in silence were it to take the whole of the day. From that vantage above he commanded a good stretch of the pavement to right and left, and he reasoned, not without justice, that the morsels could hardly reach him through the air on the wing. He would discover his mysterious benefactor.

A little before midday, while leaning against the sill and photographing with his eye the dark to-and-fro that stirred confusedly at his feet, a sudden awakening of vital vibrations, a gentle warmth, a sense of penetrating light, startled him. He turned with a start... he was no longer alone!

Wrapped in a white combing-cloth, her hair in part knotted, in part flowing, Miss Elisa stood upright on the near side of the threshold. The fingers of her right hand held by its cord the restorative packet, and her blue eyes wandered in an unspeakable perturbation.

"Signor Carlo!..." she murmured. "I did not know you were here!" She gathered herself at once and added with composure: "Someone brought this parcel for you!"

— When? Not a living soul has entered by the door.

— Early this morning. Had I known you were at home, I should have brought it to you at once.

— This morning, you say?

— Early. A well-dressed gentleman, Italian in appearance and in accent. I asked his name; but he answered that, for reasons entirely his own, he did not wish to make himself known. It did not seem proper to insist. Besides, he spoke English so very ill, and I know so little Italian!...

She added nothing more and remained with eyes downcast, waiting for some phrase, for a word — were it only an allusion — to the fine weather. And as the word absolutely did not come, she nervously arched her brows and raised her eyes again. The mild enquiry that glimmered in them was soon lost in an expression of pained wonder. The Count di Rudio appeared absorbed in thought of distant things... his mind was running upon the well-dressed Italian who, without being a sorcerer, knew the secrets of his stomach so passing well. And he chafed at the mystery which, despite his tedious vigil, he had not succeeded in resolving. When he became aware that he was being observed... silently, with dignity, he brushed from his jacket with his right hand the whitish dust acquired by its long contact with the sill.

The situation was growing awkward when, in the corridor, there sounded the tread of hurrying steps, and there appeared Miss Sara. She was carrying, clasped to her bosom, a great vase of roses.

She set it down by the window and after a few seconds of mute contemplation: "It looks wonderfully well there!" She turned to Elisa and added: "The idea is yours and it falls to you to give the explanation!" But she allowed her no time for it, and fixing her eyes upon the Count: "One evening you said that the rose is the emblem of your family and that, for that reason too, it is your favourite flower above all others. Elisa had an inspiration. ‘Let us procure a bush; perhaps he will hold it dear.’ See what luxuriance! So as not to trouble you, we shall continue to water it ourselves. Elisa observed to me that it will put you in mind of your mother and your Fatherland... for Italy is the land of roses. Is it not so?"

Here she thought — or feigned — that someone was calling her, and ran off. Miss Elisa took her leave with a nod of the head, and swift and light as a shadow, vanished.

The Count di Rudio remained a long while motionless, fixing his gaze upon the void, as though something of the two young women had lingered there. From that day they occupied the greater part of his dreaming hours. They insinuated themselves, unbidden, into his thoughts, and no sooner did they appear there than they were received with a tenderness more than brotherly. His sad eyes, which had never beheld aught save the torment of the innocent and the triumph of the base, found renewal in that inward contemplation. And for the first time, the Count felt profoundly that, above the world of spite, of men who gnaw one another, of insatiable cupidity, of pitiless arrogance, of the manifold and nauseating mire in which our civil life sinks and wallows, there floats suspended in the blue sky — of which it is the very substance — a serene force that transforms, that redeems, that creates, that can make us good and happy... Love!


### Chapter Three

<!-- pages:136-141 -->

<!-- pages:136-141 -->



LOVE!... for Miss Sara or for Miss Elisa? The problem, however simple it might appear, stirred within him a singular dismay, followed by a no less singular irritation. “Why torment oneself to resolve it,” he concluded, “when the settling down into marriage is denied me by other ideals?” The Count di Rudio was acting unconsciously, and in a different arena, much as a man acts before new ideas when prejudice has taken root in his brain. Such a man intuits the reasonableness of those ideas, yet — so it is! — he obstinately closes his eyes to the truth, and the very certainty that his idol is crumbling makes him a yet more strenuous champion of old fables and falsehood.

And now — here is how the Count di Rudio’s long drought of purse came at last to an end.

From the day on which the coup d’état had triumphed definitively, Victor Hugo, having taken the road of exile, had settled on the island of Jersey. There he published *L’Homme*, a periodical whose pages blazed forth, with all the intensity lent by genius, the condemnation of the baleful imperial politics. The hero of the Second of December, keenly sensitive to the pricks of criticism, as every criminal who aspires to glory must be, contrived to lodge his complaint adroitly with the Cabinet in London, which, with equal adroitness, fended it off. But when the complications of European politics pointed out to England, in Napoleon III, the sovereign best suited to lend her a strong hand in the meditated enterprise of breaking the Czar’s pride, to humour the whims of that man presented itself as an act of wise diplomacy. And the freedom of thought and the traditional broad hospitality of the English people were, at least in part, curtailed and sacrificed to the true, or supposed, interests of the Nation.

Victor Hugo was surrounded by various brilliant minds, to whose company Pianciani now attached himself. That nest of intellectuals was suddenly thrown into confusion. And the thing seemed such a sacrilege that even the French reactionaries dared not applaud.

Pianciani resolved to repair once again to London. Before leaving the island, he dispatched, together with his other papers, the manuscript of his *Storia dei Papi*, insured for ten thousand pounds sterling. The papers arrived at their destination; the manuscript vanished. And rumour ran that the Jesuits had taken possession of it, most probably to cast it into the flames. A certain colour of truth was lent to this rumour by the fact that the Insurance Company disbursed the considerable indemnity immediately, without initiating the least inquiry, without uttering a single word.

Pianciani and the Count di Rudio met and conferred, and that Roman gentleman, who by his expansive warmth and his generous impulses made men forgive the disfigurement of his face — smallpox had blunted his nose — received the gentleman from Belluno as an equal among equals. At parting, and almost by the way, the conversation fell upon the latter’s financial straits.

“You could not have come to me at a better moment!” said Pianciani. “I have set myself to attempt the *Storia dei Papi* anew. Will you be my amanuensis?”

The man accustomed to the boldness, the labours, the perils of action was confined to an armchair, and made a virtue of what he had always accounted a fatal defect — patience.
