In recompense, he was brought into contact with those men luminous in genius, in character, in sufferings borne with virile fortitude who frequented the house of Pianciani: rebels, persecuted, slandered, without roof, without bread, unvanquished — great in their loves, their hatreds, their utopias. Noble and proud company!

Most of them had one fixed idea that seemed almost to stamp them. They endeavoured to convince, to draw the young amanuensis into their orbit.

Pianciani discerned in the troubles massing in the East the probable salvation of Italy. Liberolle, the historian, dreamed of the union of the Latin peoples, but under the predominance of France. Victor Hugo could not make his peace with the foolishness of his Fatherland, dazzled by the equivocal splendour of the Court of Napoleon the Little. He had lost all faith in the virtue of a People insensible to great ideals, ready to shoot and to be shot for the lying enchantment of fatuous phrases. And his every outburst closed invariably with the bitter sentence: “The people is a great child that wishes to be deceived!”

And amid those modern figures there passed, now mild and smiling, now flashing with sinister light, the phantoms of History. Nineteen centuries of glories and of squalors went filing across those brief pages, renewing in the Count’s heart the shuddering, the grief, the wrath of the generations that sleep as dust.
Yet in his mind, grown as though swollen with that vast entanglement, there arose, from hour to hour, two simple and smiling figures that stirred him more profoundly than genius; that stripped emperors and pontiffs of every ornament and thrust them back into their vulgarity. They appeared to him constantly adorned with roses, the fair flower that is the emblem of his family, and a sense of serenity pervaded and abided with him: a sense that tempered the stench of Vatican orgies; that stripped the horror from the maledictions which, from every corner of Italy, from every corner of Europe, had ever and always been raised against the brigands who trafficked in their Fatherland, in humanity, in Christ.

The Count di Rudio no longer debated them; they had become as part of himself. Not that his ideas were, in this regard, clear and well defined. Love, not forgetful of its accustomed arts, still dissembled its own vigour behind the shelter of other affections more anciently rooted and in full flower. But he was persuaded, by now, of having found upon his path the beautiful, the good, the chaste, wondrously made flesh. To behold them, to drink in their radiance, to be elevated — to feel himself less unhappy! He surrendered to the wholly novel sensation without the least misgiving. When the revolutionary tempest seized him again, the festival of feeling would be brusquely cut short; but he would carry into the greater trials a richer treasury of dear remembrances — noble affections lend wings to reach the arduous heights!

And he yearned, with an ever more intense anxiety, for the day when, having received the reward of his labours, he might present himself with head held high before Signora Mancherini, pay his debts like a man of honour, resume the course of the interrupted lessons; drink again from that font of the beautiful, the good, the chaste, drawing his two young friends ever more deeply into the cult of martyrs and heroes!

The day came. With what light a step he covered the long road, with what eagerness he mounted the stair! Mamma Mancherini stepped forward and intercepted the threshold with her ample and flourishing person. Whereupon he, extending his right hand without delay — and the sterling — poured forth his apologies for the involuntary delay. But the lady looked at him with a suspicious air. The two young girls came running in, and the Count di Rudio learned, to his great astonishment, that the rent had been paid at the end of every month. Miss Elisa brought up once more the mysterious stranger, and Sara affirmed that she had never delivered the proper receipts to the Signor Conte, because the Signor Conte had not let himself be seen for a great while. But the portly Mancherini appeared not altogether satisfied, and went off with a frown, muttering: “Oh, what a muddle, what a muddle!”
In those days the Crimean War was dragging on, limping forward. From the very first manifestation of the troubles that led to that war, Giuseppe Mazzini had proposed making an appeal to the unitary sentiment of Germany; of seconding the national aspirations of Italy and Hungary; of sending a breath of new life through the Balkans; of forming, to put it briefly, around the Muscovite Empire, a great rampart of free States that would have compressed and paralysed Russia.

This conception was not displeasing to the Cabinet of Turin. But Napoleon III and England were aiming to provoke no hostility from Austria, and indeed to induce her to participate actively in their alliance. And it was precisely because Austria would never have resolved to enter the campaign so long as the terrible hornets’ nest of Italy hung over her neck, that the co-operation of the House of Savoy was accepted with good grace, was in fact solicited. The Piedmontese army was the only strongly organised nucleus; the only one deemed capable of precipitating events in our Peninsula.

Austria would not be moved and thus spared the Italians the humiliation of seeing the heroes of our independence fight side by side with the Croatian hordes, to maintain upon the European Continent “a sorry projection of Asia.”

And the meditated great uprising of peoples was reduced, in practice, to a simple, long, and tedious siege. England, shaken in her military prestige, short of soldiers, resolved to open enlistments, to organise some foreign legions.

No sooner was the appropriate decree promulgated than di Rudio found Fumagalli and Leoni presented before him. The latter had decided to depart for the Crimea and had come to pay a farewell visit. “And if it be for the last time,” he said, pressing the Count’s hand, “then for the last time — farewell!”

They went to pass the few remaining hours before departure at the *Public House* of a certain Shaw, wife of a fellow-countryman of theirs. It was precisely the gathering-place of the recruits. The setting, with a parade-ground elegance, was already exceptionally animated — a confused animation, full of starts and bursts of laughter. Military men and civilians, in clusters, were slapping one another on the shoulder, exchanging nicknames and hearty, good-natured epithets. In secluded corners, a few gentle figures in bonnets, with pale and tear-stained faces. Flowers and green sprigs on every breast; bluish spirals of cigarette smoke from almost every mouth.
But the noise and confusion did not suit di Rudio’s temper; the more so because the sight of so many Italians on the point of shedding their blood for a cause he held foreign to the Fatherland put him in mind of the woeful days of the *soldati di ventura* — soldiers of fortune. And when Leoni, half in earnest, half in jest, said to him: “Come now, Moretto! Make up your mind and embark with us!” he answered, with excessive bluntness: “What harm have the Russians done you, that you should offer your youth as a holocaust, with the intention of swelling their number of widows and orphans?”

And Leoni: “Italian colours are flying in the Crimea!”

— Any flag is a mockery when it does not stand as a symbol of truth and justice!

The conversation was taking a bad turn. Fortunately a Milanese conscript, picking up the thread of a previous discussion, expressed his regret at not having thought to bring with him *I Sepolcri* by Ugo Foscolo, which would have contributed so greatly to shortening the hours of the crossing.

— I have a copy at home, di Rudio told him. — Accept it. It is an inexpensive edition, with the margins full of scribbles, but they do no harm to the substance.

In four bounds he was at Baldwin’s Garden. He mounted the stairs and entered his room, leaving the door ajar. He was already turning the chest of drawers upside down when, from the other side of the corridor, there reached him distinctly the sounds of moaning and sobbing. What could this mean?

Going to the threshold, he found himself face to face with Miss Sara, who, flushing briefly and without waiting to be questioned: “Elisa is weeping because she is persuaded that you are leaving tonight for the Crimea. Love is consuming her. And she has also compromised herself in Mamma’s eyes. It was that business of the receipts — do you remember? Mamma has drawn everything from her heart, and the least that awaits her now is a thorough scolding. The mysterious stranger is there, in despair. It was Elisa who provided out of her own savings — for everything, everything!”

The Count di Rudio gripped the great poet, turned him over and over in his hands, then thrust him furiously into the bottom of his pocket: “Tell Miss Elisa that the di Rudios are not soldiers of fortune. For Liberty — always! Mercenaries of foreign despots — never!”
And he descended the stairs at a run. But the tempest that Miss Sara’s words had roused followed him into the street; entered, through the crack of the door, into Mrs. Shaw’s *Public House*, and swirled about his head the whole evening — so much so that the talk and the literature and the politics and the noise and the chaotic scene of the embarkation and the farewells all reached his brain not as vivid and clearly defined reality, but almost as a pale evocation of images emerging confusedly from among the mists of times gone by.

A second surprise awaited him. Making his way home toward one o’clock, he glimpsed, seated on the landing, the figure of a woman in a white dress — in the darkness she seemed to glow with a faint phosphorescence. A moment of stupefaction seized him... and she rose and, retreating, seemed to vanish. He would have taken her for a phantom, had not a door creaked. Miss Elisa had passed a great part of the night keeping watch, in tears perhaps, to assure herself that the sea and the war had not taken him from her.

And di Rudio, in the tumult that threatened to overwhelm him, clung desperately to this resolve: “Before afternoon, undeceive her and flee!” With that resolution he went to bed; thereupon the thoughts of the night, inspired by the painful remembrance of so many martyrs both living and dead, made him yet more stubborn. When he presented himself, rigid and self-possessed, before the two young girls, Elisa greeted him with: “Are you unwell? You are pale this morning!”

— It seems to me that you are both pale! the cousin observed. And she offered to prepare a cup of warm milk — Mamma too would be glad of some when she came in. As she reached the threshold she turned and, smiling: “Do not be foolish. Every moment lost is stolen from your happiness, and by forever hiding from each other you might end by never finding each other again. You love each other, you were born one for the other — what more is needed?” And she was gone.
The decisive moment had come. Elisa was nervously twisting one of her ribbons, and di Rudio watched her, keeping at a respectful distance. He began by thanking her for all that she had done for him. Any denial was useless — Sara had confided everything to him. The misfortune was that he could demonstrate his gratitude only in words.

And he went on to recount the sad vicissitudes to which one must submit who champions a holy and persecuted cause. With what conscience could he have dared to offer her his hand, to drag her into his tumultuous life, into his poverty? Where find for her a nest that would not, sooner or later, be tainted and destroyed by the fury of men? Would it be wise, humane, honest to reserve an environment haunted by the terror of conspiracies, the snares of arms, the clank of chains, the dread of blood, for children born to perpetuate the gentle virtues of their mother? Infancy yearns for the kisses of smiling lips; it needs to mirror in its candid soul eyes that are serene. And what else could he say to her; what happiness could he promise, without resorting to mendacious phrases? He had chosen, between two evils, the lesser; he had resolved to withdraw — for ever. He implored her to interpret his resolution with that rectitude of feeling that had suggested it to him; to preserve a kindly memory of him.

Here, and here alone, Miss Elisa raised her face and looked him in the eyes. With the acuteness of simple souls, she knew how to sound a soul. She expressed herself as a creature conscious of the power to dispose of her own destiny, with that calm and chaste eloquence which to some of my readers, accustomed to exalting “the opulence of the hip and the quiverings of the flesh,” might seem exhumed from some museum of archaeology... Other times, other men, other loves!

“Toward a soul such as that of di Rudio she could advance without suspicion and without artifice: she would have preferred to see herself definitively rejected over becoming his through simulated virtue. There were Italian women — she was not ignorant of this — who had suppressed their serene aspirations, imposed solitude and silence upon their hearts, so as not to become wives and mothers in calamitous times. She admired their austerity, but did not feel that she could approve of it. The wicked rear the wicked freely, and should the good withdraw themselves from the duty of rearing those who shall perpetuate honest traditions? The surroundings that seemed unfavourable to cultivating, in tender hearts, the gentle virtues he supposed her to possess, would favour the development of the virile paternal virtues. To temper virile characters is a mission that refines a mother’s pride. Or did he perhaps take her, like his ancient Roman women, as fit only to preside over the good order of the kitchen and to spin wool? The anguish of hearts that sweat blood, the terrible effort of muscles that swell to burst their chains, the tormented labour of intellects that seek everywhere for equity and light — were these things to have left her indifferent? She had a deep sympathy for the country he had painted for her so beautiful, so great, so unfortunate. The love he bore his Fatherland did not make her jealous — to distract him from his aims, to blunt his generous impulses? Precisely the contrary! They would work together, and with redoubled energy, for the triumph of Liberty. He had kindled a flame that made her greater than herself — let him try to extinguish it, if his heart were equal to it, if he had the strength!”

The Count thought: “Blessed the rosy mouth that vanquishes the eloquence of so many martyrs, living and dead!” Nevertheless he persisted:

“No, Elisa! The conspirator is alone even in the bosom of his family. He must at times forget that he has a mother, a wife, children. If his predestined hour arrives, he stops his ears to their prayer, he sees not the tears and he departs — departs without confiding his secrets even to the air he breathes. If that hour comes —”

— One last embrace, a wrench of the heart, perhaps — but no indiscreet question. Am I not certain that you can do nothing of which I need be ashamed!

When Miss Sara returned with the cups and the milk, the lover who but a short while before had wished to flee far, far away, had one knee on the ground. Holding Elisa’s hands clasped within his, he was kissing them, kissing them again. On the ninth of December, 1855, a flame was made lawful, upon which misfortune hastened to breathe — in vain! The hard vicissitudes of more than half a century, far from extinguishing it, were only to make it burn ever more brightly!


### Chapter Four

<!-- pages:142-143 -->

<!-- pages:142-148 -->



THE Count di Rudio speaks: The very year I took a wife I was drawn into an ill-starred affair that made a great deal of noise. The exiles who had no abundance of coin used to frequent a certain eating-house in Rupert Street, where for little outlay they could savour some dish prepared after our own fashion. I myself had gone there more than once and had struck up an acquaintance with one Rossi, a Mantuan, a young fellow who soon won my sympathies by his open and cheerful character. Rossi was excessively hasty in the matter of amorous relations, but no other vices could I discover in him; and when the Romagnol Foschini warned me, in strictest confidence, to keep clear of that "ugly customer" because he was a spy in the pay of the French Government, I observed to him that to lend easy credence to calumnies insinuated, in all likelihood for base ends, by the enemies of our party, was to lend oneself most childishly to their game.

Foschini, nettled, turned his back on me, and I... drew closer to Rossi... in part, also, from that instinct, so imperious in me, of wanting to see clearly, to the very bottom of things. I came to be convinced that Foschini was genuinely mistaken. That conviction, to tell the truth, is not so firmly rooted today. A certain Rossi, as I learned several years later, had been sinisterly implicated in the conspiracies at Mantua. Nevertheless the surname Rossi is exceedingly common in Northern Italy, and I never had the means of ascertaining whether my London acquaintance and the spy of the Austrian Government were one and the same person. For anyone interested in probing these minutiae, I will say that the Rossi of

London had a brother who was a priest — a circumstance which might serve as a guide to establishing the truth!

One evening, at a late hour, I found myself in the tavern in Rupert Street in the company of the "ugly customer." Foschini was there too, sitting entirely alone, apart from the rest; but from the black looks he had cast at us and from certain monosyllables muttered between his teeth, I had been able to gather that there was a risk of sailing in troubled waters.

The other customers thinned out and we were left almost alone. At a certain point, Foschini rose and, passing stiffly beside Rossi, jostled him at the elbow and said: "Spy!"

Rossi retorted with a worse compliment, and Foschini, lashed across the face, turned furious. I thought it my duty to intervene, and was urging them to make no scandal, so as not to compromise our whole emigrant community, when the Romagnol, having drawn a dagger, made a spring. One second more... and Rossi would have been finished! To forestall the deed, I seize a chair and do my best to bring it down upon the assailant. Unfortunately I strike the lamp hanging from the ceiling; it shatters and we are left in darkness. I remember the sensation of the cold blade entering my chest, then... nothing more!

When a glimmer of consciousness returned to me, I understood that I was in a hospital. I could not keep my eyes open; but I heard a voice, the physician's, pronouncing that I should not see the morrow's sun. They made me swallow some preparation or other, and that brief effort was enough for me to lose my senses once again.

But my youth reasserted itself and, in spite of the gloomy prophecy, my first impression was precisely that of the sun's rays beating upon my face. They stirred in me a firm will to cling to life.

I learned that the doctor, considering the case desperate, had resorted, in order to arrest the internal haemorrhage, to a heroic remedy of his own. He regarded the outcome as a wonder, and for a long succession of days brought to my bedside now one colleague, now another, entering upon discussions of presumably great scientific value. To me, a layman and a man of irritable nerves, they were supremely inopportune.

Before I received the regulation visit of a Police Inspector, the doctor informed me that Foschini had made two other victims. But Rossi, the one undeniable target he had wished to remove, had come out of it without a scratch.

Upon Foschini, who had gone into hiding, the Authorities placed a reward of two hundred pounds. But he was a good patriot, and though his hiding-place was known, no one breathed a word. Indeed, in the laudable attempt to shield him, the political motive which had driven him to act was greatly exaggerated, with scant regard for whether, in order to save the culprit's reputation, the reputations of innocent men were being diminished.

Now let one mark the strangeness of destiny: that in Europe first, then in America, I faced the gravest dangers, and though I was more than once within a hair's breadth of leaving my bones there, I always came away with my skin intact. It was reserved for my companions in faith to repay my disinterestedness with six-and-thirty dagger-thrusts, two of which penetrated into cavities and were adjudged mortal!

Foschini closed his days splendidly, fighting at the side of Carlo Pisacane in the ill-fated, glorious expedition of Sapri.

Sapri was the first serious manifestation of the new change of tactics adopted by Giuseppe Mazzini. The Crimean War being ended, the great Cavour, in spite of the splendid showing of our soldiers and their victory at the Cernaia, had obtained nothing for his King — not even the hoped-for annexation of Parma and of Modena, whose petty princes they wished to foist upon Moldavia and Wallachia. But Napoleon III had promised that one day he would do something also for Piedmont, and Mazzini held for certain, even from those days, the alliance of the Empire with Vittorio Emanuele II. He was, however, firmly convinced that from it nothing would derive save an enlargement of the Sardinian States, or at best, a federation of Italian States governed, for the greater part, by French princes. To forestall such a calamity, he abandoned the old field of action and laboured, with his most trusted followers, to raise the Kingdom of the Two Sicilies in revolt: to set against the monarchical agitation of the North the insurrection of the southernmost extremity of Italy; to impose from Palermo and Naples the unity of the Nation. The brilliant conception earned for Giuseppe Mazzini and for Garibaldi a sentence of "ignominious death," but it was destined to prove one of the greatest factors in our future greatness.

Left entirely to one side — presumably because I was thought to have been softened by new affections — I was already resigning myself to a long inaction, when suddenly the call came to me from whence I least expected it.


### Chapter Five

<!-- pages:143-148 -->



IN September of 1857, while I was making shift to live by giving language lessons in Nottingham, an Italian exile passing through that city came to call on me. He found himself in circumstances far more critical than my own. I came to his aid, and that same day he continued on his way. Arriving in Birmingham, he applied to Felice Orsini for assistance — Orsini being, if I am not mistaken, his fellow-countryman. In the course of recounting his misfortunes, he made mention of the visit he had paid me. “By God!” exclaimed Felice Orsini. “The very man I am looking for!” And he wrote to me, giving me an appointment at the Great Northern station in London.

I had not seen Felice Orsini for a long while. But no sooner had the train puffed in under the station roof (Charing Cross) than I caught sight of him standing erect amid the crowd, with his little cases and his very black moustaches. A few seconds and I was face to face with him. “Where the devil have you been all this time?” he said to me. And I told him. When I touched upon my marriage, his face clouded over. “Then, farewell to patriotic resolutions!” I disabused him. Indeed, I now felt all the greater obligation to conspire and to fight — my son was not to have a servile and unhappy fatherland.

He asked me whether I knew the reason he had given me the appointment, and upon my replying that I did not, he invited me to step outside the station. Once in the open air, he reminded me without preamble of the letter I had written to Giuseppe Mazzini and of my proposal to rid France of her troubles by suppressing Napoleon. He had it in mind to translate that old idea of mine into action. The times seemed to him propitious. Was I still of the same opinion?

— What does Mazzini think of it?

At this he made a gesture as if to say: Why do you speak to me of archaeology? And he strengthened his point by declaring that he no longer spoke with Mazzini. Moreover, Massarenti, the *alter ego* of the “prophet,” was a discredit to the Bolognese breed, and would, the moment he caught wind of the matter, undoubtedly denounce him. For this reason too, it was necessary to leave Mazzini out of it.

In the end I told him that I was not averse to taking part in the enterprise. But I wished to see things clearly. Napoleon once killed, what would happen? What were his plans for the aftermath?

I shall repeat, in summary, his explanations. They reveal, if not the plan, the aims of a conspiracy about which so much has been written, yet without ever fully laying bare the truth of it. They serve, at the same time, as a posthumous refutation of the charges which certain historians, lacking the necessary grounds for judgement, chose to bring against Orsini.

Felice Orsini had come to a full understanding with the French Republicans. The coup d’état had surprised and paralysed them — but had not tamed them. They were conspiring to prepare a revolution. A successful bold stroke in Paris would mean the liberation of France, and France restored to liberty would mean all Europe in ferment. Ledru Rollin — Orsini assured me — despairs of success so long as he finds the sinister figure of Napoleon before him. There is more force than one might suppose in a name. If France stirs, Kossuth vouches for Hungary. As regards our Italy — Napoleon suppressed, Paris throws up barricades. The enemy, caught unawares, without an authoritative leader, with the cohesion of the army already undermined by subtle intrigue, falls ignominiously. The Republic is proclaimed, and immediately I am appointed, with full powers, Commissioner with the French army stationed at Rome. Those are thirty thousand seasoned and well-equipped men that I win, at a single stroke, to the Italian cause. What do I do? In a single day I cancel the work and the shame of Napoleon’s deed of 1849. The Pope and the Cardinals become my prisoners and I lock them up in Castel Sant’Angelo — their fate will depend upon the attitude of Europe. Meanwhile I sever the temporal power from the spiritual, and proclaim from the Campidoglio the resurrection of the Roman Republic. The French garrison quits Rome, and I drive it swiftly forward in support of the Italian revolution. Do we win? So much the better! Do we suffer a reverse? Republican France, stung to the quick, rises like a fury and lays down her arms no more until the prestige of her own name has been fully vindicated. Austria, assailed on every side, without friends in Europe, with rebellion blazing within her very borders, is swept away and never again sets foot in Italy...

Certainly, it would remain to establish the definitive political order of the Peninsula. But there are things which, in despite of their vastness, arrange themselves almost of their own accord. The blind, obstinate dualism between republicans and monarchists has already proved fatal to the Italian People; the lesson has instructed it. The People desires independence and unity, and now places everything else in the second rank. If the Savoyard Monarchy showed itself capable of giving the People the strength it lacks and of leading it to victory, the People would throw itself, as one man, at the feet of Vittorio Emanuele. If victory should come from republican Rome and from republican allies, what sovereign would feel himself great enough to oppose the proclamation of the Republic?

In the present moment the noble contest is open. Should we republicans spontaneously renounce the glorious initiative?

Everything depends upon audacity! One must know how to cast the first spark. Without it there is no conflagration, independence is impossible, the Republic is impossible. You know that I am opposed to the shedding of blood. Nature has endowed me with a humane temperament: I aim high, and the means imposed by these dark times repel me. But I say that the blood of Louis Bonaparte — of the man who has knowingly broken faith with solemnly sworn obligations, who has betrayed us, who has put our Fatherland back in chains, who tramples upon every noble aspiration of his own Fatherland — must not weigh in the balance against the liberty of seventy millions of human creatures. To shed it averts the sacrifice of thousands upon thousands of honest lives, and the cruel act becomes a work of mercy. Decide!

And I decided. The man’s rectitude was known to me; the revolutionary undercurrent in France was known to me, more or less; the conditions of Italy were known. To probe further I deemed superfluous, and I contented myself with asking the names of those who were to execute the plan materially. But Orsini fended off the question. The great danger in great conspiracies — he pointed out to me — is precisely their magnitude. The more numerous those who carry the secret in their hearts, the greater become the chances that some lip will let it escape. To guard, in part, against this unavoidable peril, he had decided that the participants in the attempt should not know one another.

To shelter ourselves from the cold, which that day was rather sharp, we entered a public house. The conversation continued by fits and starts. Orsini had to be in Liverpool on the morrow, and he begged me to return to Nottingham. “Stay quietly at home and work, until you hear from me.” If he could not come in person, he would send a trusted agent with a letter from him and with his instructions. And as a further precaution, we established a password. I asked him whether, with regard to the attempt, he had any well-defined plan already. He knitted his brows and appeared vexed. Orsini was unable to dissemble his emotions. After a brief silence, he answered me that he could communicate nothing precise. “My intention,” he added, “would be to have ourselves invited to Court. There is someone in a position to procure us as many invitations as we wish. To approach the usurper during a ball is a matter of little moment, and it might be possible, with cold steel, to achieve the object without spilling a single drop of blood more than necessary. But it is feared that interested parties would close the doors of the palace and prevent news of what had occurred from spreading, until all the orders necessary to avert the consequences had been given; until they had proclaimed the Regency. Perhaps it will be necessary to resort to a noisier means. However that may be — your word is given. Can I count upon it with certainty?”

I replied that Carlo di Rudio had never failed in his undertakings, and since, by simple intuition, I believed I had understood, more or less, what the “noisier means” might consist in, I observed to him that the retinue of the usurper must necessarily be composed of supporters of the empire and of soldiers complicit in the coup of the 2nd of December. The more of them one killed, the fewer we should find before us on the morrow’s barricades.

We parted that same evening. The following day I returned to Nottingham, and turning over in my mind, to the rhythm of the train, what had been confided to me and the prospects of drawing from it a broad movement that might prove salutary to the popular cause throughout the whole of Europe, I understood at once what an immense advantage Mazzini’s awareness of it would prove. I knew no influence more profound or more efficacious than his. To leave such a man aside was to run the risk of losing the fruit of our daring. The figure of the “Bolognese” presented itself to my mind, but did not strike me as a matter of consequence. I knew well enough that it sufficed to narrate the affair with discretion and to exact a promise of absolute silence for the Maestro to face and endure any torture whatsoever without letting fall a single compromising syllable. The “coward” of the Savoy expedition, as certain moderate newspapers called him, was incontestably among the most humanly courageous individuals of the century.

The ill-feeling between the Maestro and Orsini? I felt certain that it would vanish in the face of the supreme interests of the fatherland. And from that moment I resolved to open my heart to Mazzini, to confide to him as much as would suffice that events should not catch him unawares.

My wife was pleasantly surprised at my return, but did not venture a single indiscreet question, and for several days our life passed in its usual calm, as though nothing were in the air. Towards the middle of November, a letter was delivered to me bearing the Birmingham postmark. It contained these simple words: “Go to London and send me your address.”

I understood that the knot was drawing tight, and reflecting that, in all likelihood, once I had quitted English soil, it would be given me no more to see again either my wife or my child, I said curtly to Elisa: “Pack your trunk; I intend to settle in London!” She set about complying at once. I watched her. From her every movement there shone the loving conviction that she ought not to seek beyond; that everything I asked of her was for a good end and for a just cause. She brushed, folded, and put away — gentle and silent.

We departed and took up lodgings in Bedman Buildings, on Shoah Square. Two days of waiting. On the morning of the third, I was tickling the child under the chin — Elisa had just lowered him into a basin of warm water — when a ring at the bell made me start. On the threshold I found myself face to face with a well-dressed gentleman, evidently a foreigner; who, having asked whether I was such and such a one, without adding anything further, handed me an open letter. It was from Felice Orsini, begging me to place “full confidence in Simon Bernard, a French physician from Carcassonne.” By way of greater surety, I asked for and received the password. I then invited my visitor to enter.

The child was kicking in the basin and beating the water with his little hands, spattering the table. At that sight, Simon Bernard fixed his eyes upon my face as if putting to me a mute question; then he looked at the child again, and ended by casting at my wife a troubled and perplexed glance.

I believed I could divine the cause of his embarrassment, and observed to him that he might speak freely in French; my wife would not understand a syllable.

“Oh, that is not precisely the point!” And he became thoughtful again. Suddenly he appeared to make up his mind, and having recovered all the gallantry natural to his race, he lavished compliments upon the mother for the thriving appearance of her little one, and upon the husband for his good taste in the choice of a wife. He spoke of London and its accursed climate; of France and the matchless gaiety of Paris; he touched upon a hundred subjects without going deep into any of them, with an elegance of phrase and a volatility of thought more suited to a drawing-room of idle gentlefolk than to the humble lodging of a conspirator and among conspirators.

When he took his leave, I accompanied him to the door, and he, touching my arm, said to me in an undertone: “Let us take a few turns about the square; we shall have more freedom to talk.”

Scarcely had we descended than he placed himself before me and, at last giving rein to the thought which had preoccupied him: “*Parbleu!* I did not know you were a family man!”

— Felice Orsini knows it.

— But the Secret Committee here in London does not. In any case, this visit was necessary for us to make each other’s acquaintance. This evening the Committee assembles, and I shall report. Tomorrow we shall meet again.

And the following day he came. From the moment I opened the door to him, I noted that his expression was that of a cheerful man. This time he ventured to speak in Elisa’s presence — in French, naturally. He had nothing new to communicate regarding the conspiracy. But he was pleased to announce to me that the Secret Committee had decided to pay my wife, during my absence, fourteen shillings a week. In the event of misfortune, the allowance would be continued until my child had reached the age of fourteen years.

Rightly or wrongly, the overriding concern of being able to serve my Country had almost entirely displaced from my mind the other concern of the future. Elisa’s mother belonged to that order of women whose name cannot be separated from the idea of veneration. The certainty that she would never leave either her daughter or the little creature destitute had sufficed to keep me, in that respect, perfectly tranquil. And, to tell the truth, the news communicated to me by Simon Bernard, at first, nettled me. But had I the right to yield to the impulse of pride; to impose upon weak and unknowing creatures the whole weight of my actions? Would it have been honest to refuse?

I gave thanks, and Simon Bernard, citing urgent business as his pretext — business which, moreover, would prevent him from showing himself for some days — pressed my hand and departed. No small was my wonder, therefore, when on the following morning I found him once more upon my threshold. He set before me, without even crossing it, the reason for that unexpected visit. The Secret Committee had reconsidered its decision and had concluded that it could not place at risk the life of a man who had a wife and was a father. I was therefore relieved, with a thousand thanks, from taking any part in the affair. I contented myself with replying: “Very well. Let us say no more about it.”


### Chapter Six

<!-- pages:148-152 -->

<!-- pages:149-157 -->



FOR several days, I forced myself to suppress the thoughts that surged tumultuously through my head, renewing old acquaintances. At the same time, I sought some foothold that might put an end to my extremely thorny financial situation, and secure for me work and bread for the future.

One evening, a little before sunset, I was returning, unusually tired, almost dispirited, from my customary excursion, when, turning the corner into Shoah Square, I literally ran into Simon Bernard.

"Bravo! Bravo! I have just come from your lodgings. Signor Orsini has written. Do you know what he has written? That he knows you by proof. That he wants a man of his fullest confidence. Either you, or he sends the whole business to ruin. What do you intend to do?"

This time I answered: "I have given my word to Felice Orsini; I shall keep it."

Instead of going straight home, we walked three or four times round the square. Orsini's letter had dissipated Simon Bernard's coldness and loosened his tongue. I learned that Felice Orsini was already in Paris, gathering again the threads of his conspiracy; that the means definitively chosen for the attempt was that of bombs. That the Baron Egassy di Torocfalda had devised a wholly new type, fashioned in the form of a hedgehog, each spike armed with a percussion cap, so that they would explode in whatever manner they were thrown. The Taylor foundry in Birmingham had cast them piece by piece, in the guise of gas-fittings. The substance destined to make them explode was mercury fulminate. The same Baron di Torrocfalda had taught Orsini how to prepare it, and Orsini had so thoroughly profited from the lesson that he had managed without mishap, scorching but a few fingers.

On taking his leave, Simon Bernard recommended that I hold myself in readiness, since from one day to the next the order of departure might reach me. And I concluded at once that the time had come to seek the cooperation of Giuseppe Mazzini.

To obtain an interview with him was not so easy as it might appear; whether the Maestro, spied upon by every Police in the world, had grown distrustful; or whether, as many maintained, certain persons with whom he had surrounded himself were at pains to keep at a distance all those who might have aided him effectively in some daring enterprise of his. In conversation among friends it was said, sometimes with a smile, but more often not without a touch of resentment, that it was more difficult to obtain an audience with Giuseppe Mazzini than with the Queen of England.

In those days, truth to tell, several individuals had been drawing near me, together with Massarenti, men whom I knew to be in close relations with the Maestro. But my well-founded distrust had led me to play, in their company, the part of the man who listens and does not speak.

Unexpectedly, chance seemed to favour me. I was walking in Cheapside with a certain Gori, formerly a band-leader of the Zambeccari Legion, when I caught sight, at a short distance before me, of Mazzini arm in arm with a stranger. I quickened my pace and caught up with him. Naturally, I could not speak of so important a matter in the presence of strangers, and after the necessary greetings I was obliged to confine myself to telling Mazzini that I desired an interview, and to ask him to name the hour and place. Giuseppe Mazzini gazed into the air, as though seeking inspiration there; then, having asked for my address, said that Massarenti would be able to tell me something in that regard. Massarenti... precisely the fish that stuck in our throat!

We parted. Events came headlong and swept me into their vortex before Mazzini made any sign of life. What would have happened had I been able to speak with him? Would he have dissuaded me from the enterprise, or would he have brought to it the contribution of his influence and his ardent soul? These are questions that were put to me repeatedly. Certainly, to answer them is of no consequence. Yet I observe: the man who wrote that certain exceptional actions, undertaken in vindication of human rights, ought not to be judged by the standard of common morality; the man who pointed out to Gallenga the dagger that should have killed Carlo Alberto; who had a part — not clearly defined, yet certain — in the Tebaldi attempt against Luigi Bonaparte, could not logically have opposed that of Felice Orsini. And the Maestro knew full well where logic has its dwelling!

The Tebaldi attempt had been organised precisely in that year, 1857. Napoleon III frequented the house of the Countess Sommariva for reasons, it was said, better known to him than to the Countess's family. Tebaldi and his two accomplices were to gain entry, by means of a false key, into that house, and take possession of the Emperor's person. The intention appears to have been to force him to abdicate. But the capture of a man so vigilantly guarded could not be accomplished without bloodshed, and in the Maestro's eyes the life of a simple policeman was worth no less than that of an Emperor. However that may be, Tebaldi's two accomplices could not keep the secret, and all three were arrested. Tebaldi was exiled to the Île du Diable; his two companions to Algeria.

The reaction aroused by the misdeeds of Luigi Napoleon was too intense not to manifest itself in violent and personal fashion. Regicide was in the air, and Napoleon knew it so well that, as early as 1852, after the coup d'état and before the proclamation of the Empire, he had the Romagnoles Galli and Rossini deported to Cayenne — guilty of nothing save that of having fallen foul of the infamous Law of Suspects.

The attempt of that other daring Romagnole, Giovanni Pianori, failed because Napoleon, unable to shelter himself

"Under the hauberk of feeling himself pure,"

wore, in secret, a coat of chain-mail. Pianori was struck down before he could fire his second shot. He swelled the company of those martyred by the guillotine. His brother, already embittered by political hatred, felt his fury redoubled in his breast at that tragic death. He left Italy, meditating a resounding vengeance. But no sooner had he set foot on French soil than he was arrested and deported to the hell of the tropical islands, like Galli and Redini, without a shadow of a trial.

Fierce tempers, these Pianori brothers! They recognised no duty save the vindication of their own rights and those of others, and toward it they ran resolved, heedless of the Laws, which they held to be a scarecrow contrived by the cunning, for the purpose of domination and plunder; regardless of their own lives, which they said were well spent, if spent in the erasure of an injustice.

Giovanni Pianori, the guillotined, stands forth in all his grim wholeness from yet another tragic incident. He loved his wife to distraction. Compelled by the turbulent vicissitudes of his existence to part from her, he had entrusted her to the care of a close friend who, having insinuated himself into the heart of the comely ward, proved too tender a lover to remain a faithful friend. Some zealous informer breathed the painful truth into the ear of the betrayed man who, braving a thousand dangers, made a sudden return. It was no great difficulty to wring from the terrified woman her imprudent confession. He grew calm again and did not lay a hand upon her; but he imposed absolute silence, with that resoluteness which commands and admits no reply.

The intimate friend arrived. Pianori, dissembling, invited him, after a brief conversation, out of doors. They might discuss certain secret political affairs with greater freedom. All at once, in the darkness, the friend heard himself addressed with this question: "What would you do to a friend to whom you had entrusted your honour, and all that is most precious in the world, if he were to dishonour you, if he were to betray you?"

— You have pronounced your own sentence!

And without another word, he laid him gasping in his own blood.

A crime? I do not say otherwise. I say only that iron times make iron men, and that it is folly to expect mild manners and sheep-like sentiments from people accustomed to the daily spectacle of virtue swinging from a gibbet. And I say further that abstract ideas are fixed in practical life, embodied in History, not by timid bleatings, but by the impetuous action of tempers such as these.

The next letter sent me by Felice Orsini had been written from Paris, but its envelope bore no other postmark than that of London. Evidently some trusted third party had received it and posted it in the latter city. Orsini asked me to call upon Miss Elisa Cheney, residing at Camden Town, who would hand me a travelling-bag and a pair of gold spectacles. What was actually meant by "gold spectacles" I learned shortly afterwards from Simon Bernard. Probably so as not to attract the attention of the customs officials with an excessively bulky parcel, Felice Orsini had carried with him, on his journey to Paris, only a portion of the mercury fulminate he required. He was now entrusting me with the transport of the remainder.

I went to Camden Town that very evening. Miss Elisa Cheney — a woman who appeared to be somewhere in her thirties, slender, slight, dark, not beautiful in the classical sense, but most attractive — received me with exquisite graciousness and a smile upon her lips. The bag she handed me was, more precisely, one of those receptacles the English call a "carpet-bag" — its sides of fabric, its base of leather. Upon the sides a tiger was embroidered. The container of the mercury fulminate was of mahogany and had every appearance of a dressing-case. It stood on the mantelpiece among a few trinkets and a clock. Before stretching out her hand to it, the young lady cast Orsini's letter down upon the marble. She was perfectly calm; so much so that not even the faintest tremor disturbed her persistent smile when she held the box between her fingers. Either she possessed nerves of the most proven temper, or she was ignorant of the terrible power of the elegant article she was handing me. She stood and watched me in silence while I closed it again inside the bag; then all at once, smiling still: "You are going to Paris? A safe journey, and my compliments to Signor Orsini!"

When I returned home and Elisa came, with the child in her arms, to offer me her cheek for the customary kiss, I was obliged to make an effort to carry the bag with an air of unconcern. "Have you been shopping?" she asked. And she reached out her arm. But I said playfully: "Have a care! The tiger is alive and might scratch you!" Then, turning serious again: "These are papers of no interest to you." And I locked away in a cupboard the tiger capable of rending asunder, disguised beneath the other, embroidered and harmless one.

The day after, the 8th of January 1858, Simon Bernard appeared once more upon the threshold. He wore a grave, almost solemn expression, and I, who was now expecting definitive communications, understood at once that a few turns about the square would be most advisable. Nor had I been mistaken.

"You must depart," Bernard told me. "Orsini calculates — indeed he insists — that you set out tomorrow morning. In that case, tomorrow evening, between six and eight o'clock, you would arrive in Paris. He will be at the station... the rest will take care of itself. But in the event that you cannot depart in time, or that Orsini, by some unforeseen mishap, should fail to keep the appointment, take note of his address!" And he dictated it to me.

He left with a vigorous handshake and the assurance: "Au revoir, in a few days, in Paris."

That evening, on returning, I did not give Elisa the usual kiss, as though to prepare her for the parting. While she laid the table for supper, I sat to one side, nervously tormenting my goatee. She caught me with my eyes fixed upon her and, unable to restrain herself, threw her arms about my neck, murmuring softly in my ear: "Carlo, what ails you?"

Then I rose, stroked her hair, kissed her brow, and spoke to her the words that, on another occasion, in a very different moment, I had pressed upon her tender heart as a seal upon a solemn bond: "The conspirator must be solitary even within his family. He must sometimes forget that he has a mother and a wife and children. If his predestined hour arrives, he closes his ears to entreaties, he does not see the tears, and he departs... he departs without confiding his secrets even to the air he breathes. Elisa... that hour has come!"

She grew pale and, at first, found not a word, breathed not a sigh. But it was as though a certain key had been struck; it necessarily calls forth a certain note. She smiled sadly and, with a thread of voice that nonetheless revealed the imperious sovereignty of her spirit: "What can I say, Carlo? I know that you fight for a just cause; that there is nothing you can do of which I need be ashamed. Go, and come back soon — as honest as you are, and more glorious!"

I knelt down and covered her hands with kisses, as on the day we had pledged ourselves to each other.

So as not to prolong the painful hours, I had resolved to depart that very evening from the house, if not yet from London; I should be more alert and more composed in the morning, at the embarkation. We supped in silence; the fullness of our affections we exchanged through glances, through long caresses. At a certain moment I drew out my watch... She understood, and rising, hurried into our other small room. She returned carrying in her arms our firstborn — plump and rosy among his white linens.

He slept, and smiled... dreaming, perhaps, at his mother's full breast. I clasped Elisa's head to me with deep tenderness, whispered to her some counsel... One more kiss with trembling lips... then I fled!


### Chapter Seven

<!-- pages:154-156 -->

<!-- pages:154-156 -->



THE next morning, I was pressing forward at full steam towards my destiny. To face it, I had my carpet-bag... between the leather and the wooden panel of the bottom, it concealed mercury fulminate and a blade which had already, in the insurrectionary attempts of 1853, known the beating of my heart. I had moreover a passport in the name of Giovanni da Sylva, a Portuguese, travelling salesman for a brewery, and some ten shillings. Money and passport had been placed in my hands by Dr. Bernard during our last colloquy. I had, above all, my youth and my courage. What would come of all this? The spark capable of altering history, or nothing other than desolation and death?

Leaving the mouths of the Thames behind us, we encountered a very rough sea, a veritable tempest. The stomach's distress and the terror cast a great confusion aboard. But the infinite, which roared on every side, filled me with an exaltation that redoubled my consciousness of life. For some time a certain quietism had seemed to impose itself upon my nature, and that kind of immersion in force and motion seemed to regenerate me.

Yet the storm itself, which I almost blessed, was, unknown to me, the answer of things to the question I had put to myself, and which pressed upon my mind with great insistence. The steamship, slowed by wind and billows, reached Calais at five in the morning of the ninth of January... twelve hours late! And when, in Paris, I descended from the train, I sought in vain, among the crowded throng, the noble figure of Felice Orsini. This mishap made a visit to his lodgings in the rue Monthabor imperative — a circumstance which was to have consequences far graver than its apparent trifling nature led me to suppose.

Here, in brief, is what came to pass during that first visit of mine. Orsini's apartment was on the ground floor. The porter accompanied me to the door, knocked, and when the door opened, discreetly withdrew. I entered with a bound and was on the point of embracing Orsini as a brother — he stood erect and waiting — when I noticed the presence of a third person... a young man sprawled with easy familiarity in an armchair. He was fair-haired, with cheeks and lips cleanly shaven, and had the look of a good-natured priest. I thereupon assumed the deferential air of the travelling salesman and, giving the name from the passport, offered to present “Tomaso Allsop” with a few samples of beer.

I was rummaging in the carpet-bag, already turned inside out by the customs officers, when the false Allsop broke into a loud laugh. “We are among friends,” he said. And he introduced me, by my true name, to Antonio Gomez. He then asked me to explain my delay, and, helping things along, said: “How did Miss Cheney receive you! Did she hand over the affair to you!”

I turned out my few belongings upon the table and set about removing the panel that concealed the mercury fulminate. A moment later it lay spread upon a newspaper — *Le Droit*, if memory does not deceive me. Felice Orsini was running his fingertips over it, as though to judge the degree of its moisture. His face, ordinarily so mobile and open, had assumed a rigid expression of deep melancholy. At last he said: “The supply is now complete, and not for nothing shall we have cast twelve apostles.” And he sighed.

Struck by that melancholy air and by that sigh, I recalled his original plan, inspired by his horror of bloodshed. Evidently, he still clung to the idea of entrusting to the blade the liberation of France.

“This devilish stuff is very damp,” he resumed, “we shall have to make use of the fireplace and the thermometer. But this does not concern you. I owe it to your wife and your son not to expose you beyond what is strictly necessary.”

At that moment there came a knock at the door... undoubtedly a prearranged signal, for Orsini did not stir and left the fulminate on the table, in full view.

There entered a man of my own stature, with a grizzled beard and an intelligent eye; the pallor of his face lent extraordinary intensity to his dark pupils. He stopped short on seeing me and contented himself with uttering a simple, commonplace greeting. But his words resounded in the room in an extraordinary manner. A voice that recalled the monotonous sound of a bag-pipe. It was the first time I had heard it, yet it would have sufficed for me to recognise it among a thousand, even many years hence. And, there and then, it struck me as the most distinctive mark of the man. Undoubtedly the beard concealed a full throat.

Felice Orsini made the introduction. In this manner I came to know personally Giuseppe Pieri, with whom I had already exchanged, by post, a brief correspondence. He at once struck up conversation with great familiarity, as though for long years we had shared good and ill fortune alike. Such, in practice, was the outcome of Orsini's prudent resolve to reserve to himself alone the knowledge of those taking part in the attempt.

I had intended to speak to him of this at the first opportunity, when he, as though to accentuate the unfortunate turn of affairs, broke in upon the boisterous loquacity of Pieri: “By the way!” he said to me. “Where have you taken lodgings?”

I observed that I had arrived but a short while ago... barely time enough to eat a bite at the nearest eating-house and make my way here. I had been left with few coins in my pocket. Since the understanding had been that I should lodge, as a greater precaution, in a first-rate hotel, I was awaiting his instructions.

“You know!” he resumed, after scratching his head. “Our friend Pieri is lodging at the Hôtel France et Champagne, rue Montmartre. He has a room with two beds. Go with him, and matters will be kept simple.”

He gave me no time to raise objections. He leant his person backwards and, letting his gaze pass in turn over each of us, said: “A Venetian, a Tuscan, a Neapolitan, a Romagnole. And other, other shreds of Italian soil shall be represented in this proud enterprise! It is not this or that individual, not this or that region, but all Italy that awakens, that labours to open for herself the road to redemption. The significance is great, and Europe will comprehend it. And this union of right hands that seek one another, today compelled to do so in secret, pledges us that other union which shall be accomplished in the sight of Peoples and in the light of the Sun... which shall come to pass, shall come to pass!” And I bowed my head, and a powerful tremor ran through my bones, as though in that place there had suddenly passed the great soul of a prophet!


### Chapter Eight

<!-- pages:156-160 -->



That same evening, I had quietly taken shelter at the Hôtel France et Champagne, in Pieri’s room.

Giuseppe Pieri has had no biographers until now. The writers who touched upon him in passing, drawing upon the documents of the trial, exaggerated his faults so as to eclipse his fine and uncommon virtues. I myself, for a time, held him to be the principal, half-voluntary cause of our ruin, and did not always treat him with that serene impartiality to which he too had a right. I shall not here compose his apotheosis; but I say that, contrary to the majority of civilised men, who have polished the surface while rotting within, he had nothing of the vulgar about him save the surface alone.

In the brief days we spent together, following his open, trusting, impulsive nature, he revealed to me his every thought, every secret of his intimate life, and seemed happy to have found a favourable ground upon which to pour forth the torrent of his speech — more than Tuscan, purely Lucchese. For all that strange, monotonous voice of his, his talk was anything but tedious; often, indeed, it proved most interesting. He knew how to make himself forgiven for his somewhat uncouth phrasing by interspersing it with flowers of practical philosophy that betrayed an eccentric intelligence and a life tumultuously lived.

His philosophical ideas, freely expressed and all too frequently with excessive emphasis, did not help to multiply his friends about him. Even today, despite the fact that certain doctrines have become so democratised, his manner of speaking would generally be met with a hostile countenance. Yet that phenomenal sincerity of his — open as a book — and his habit of acting in complete harmony with his own convictions ought to be enough to raise him above a legion of petty creatures, the ballast of History, whose names have nonetheless filled so many pages, whose nullity was honoured with a pedestal.

I wish to offer a specimen of that manner of his. Pieri’s wife lived in Paris; he had separated from her. But the complete cessation of all conjugal relations did not prevent him from holding her in deep esteem and from calling upon her as upon a tried and faithful friend. Once, when he spoke to me of this, I — newly married and imbued with altogether different ideals on the matter — looked at him with wondering eyes; whereupon he clapped his hand upon my shoulder and said, roughly to this effect: “Young man, the love that seeks happiness in the gratification of the senses at the cost of another’s happiness is not love — it is a savage’s egoism. You labour that a People may become master of its own destiny, and you are worthy of praise. But you would have it, you little rogue of a god, that the fairest, the noblest half of that People should not even be mistress of its own heart and its own person! The tyranny of the male degrades the woman, who takes her revenge by repaying him with feigned kisses and betrayal; it leads to sorrows all the more terrible for being the better concealed; to sorrows that dissolve the integrity of the soul and reverberate upon the souls of children; to those domestic tragedies that end with the pistol shot. My theory? It reconciles liberty with human dignity, and in the place of love that is waning, substitutes gratitude founded upon the sweet memories of the past, friendship founded upon mutual esteem.”

In religious matters, Pieri revealed to me a mind free from every prejudice — not through convictions imposed by reading, but by natural bent. He had settled into serenity, I believe, out of necessity, as a light body detached from the murky depths of a swamp must of necessity rise to the luminous surface. This atheism of his brought him at times into conflict with Orsini, who, from the upbringing received in childhood, had — like Mazzini — retained something nebulous that persisted in both men throughout the whole of their lives.

During the brief days that preceded the attempt, both Pieri and I endeavoured to live as though nothing of gravity hung in the air. We generally dined at a *trattoria* in the Galerie Montmartre, and the frugal meal was preceded and followed by long, aimless walks. We paid some visits to Orsini; but he showed himself pensive and taciturn, and these were short visits. The afternoon of Saturday, the 12th of January, was to prove an exception. We found Orsini and Gomez fully dressed, overcoats on and hats upon their heads. No sooner did he see us than Orsini let slip an exclamation that seemed to carry away with it the impatience accumulated by a long wait. He asked us to accompany him. We went out, and as we walked he said that he wished to take us to the little piazza of the Opéra to study, on the spot, his plan of action. We made our way slowly along the Rue Le Peletier, and upon arriving opposite the theatre we halted, as though to admire its architecture. There was a long silence. Felice Orsini broke it with these words: “My plan fits perfectly to the locality.” And, without making any gesture with his hands, which he kept thrust constantly into the pockets of his overcoat: “You, Gomez, will post yourself on the far side of the street, to the right, as close to the peristyle as you can manage. Pieri will take up position precisely on the opposite side. Moretto and I will plant ourselves here in front, more or less where we now stand. I know that the Emperor will shortly be attending a performance with La Ristori. The imperial carriage must be allowed to proceed, undisturbed, up to the theatre. The moment Napoleon alights, Gomez will hurl his bomb. If it takes effect, each one slips away by his own route, as best he can, and we meet afterwards on the Place de la Concorde, where I shall give instructions as to what is to be done. If it does not take effect, then one of two things: either the Emperor attempts to save himself by taking refuge in the theatre, in which case Pieri hurls the second bomb at his feet and finishes him; or else he retreats into the carriage and it drives off at the gallop, in which case Pieri strikes it from behind, whilst di Rudio and I strike it from the side. That ought to suffice to shatter a small fortress. But even supposing the Emperor should be able to proceed, whether to the right or to the left along the Rue Le Peletier, before he reaches the Boulevard Montmartre or the Rue Rossini, he will find patriots who will know how to send him the salutation he deserves. In any event, once the attempt is carried out — all of us, by different routes, to the Place de la Concorde!”
The following day, Sunday, Pieri proposed an excursion to Passy. The broad horizon of the countryside seemed to augment in the Tuscan patriot his need to expand himself, and against the exceptional chill of the day he opposed an exceptional warmth of phrases, of images, of evocations. In that interminable succession of words, the man of fierce resolve who yearned for freedom, the practical philosopher, now rose above the sensual man serene in his easy loves, now appeared subject to him and as though eclipsed by him. Never before had I found myself confronted with so complex an amalgam of solidity and vanity.

Of all his revelations, that which impressed me most was perhaps, in itself, the least significant. Pieri had come to know Orsini at Birmingham, and it was precisely from there that he had set out to come to Paris. On his journey he had stopped in Brussels to call upon a mistress of his. Under the impulse of emotions that dispose any man to easy expansiveness, he had become, even more than was his wont, heedlessly candid. To the mistress who begged him to stay longer, he had replied that his immediate departure for Paris was irrevocable.

— What! You are going to France? And you have no fear of falling into some ugly trap, you who were expelled from it?

And Pieri, in reply, declared that this time he was going thither with the hope of repaying, *a misura di carbone* — in full and burning measure — those who had persecuted him.

That evening I knocked at Felice Orsini’s door. A single glance sufficed for me to understand that he was more preoccupied than I was myself. For all that, I insisted upon an immediate conversation. He looked at me with searching eyes but said nothing. And I, leaping straight into the matter, recalled to him his first, wise resolve to keep the conspirators entirely unknown to one another. That resolve, I added, had been flouted and well-nigh blown away by circumstances — our security had become precarious and ephemeral. I did not speak, in particular, of Pieri’s imprudence, but I dwelt upon the likelihood that some imprudence had been committed. Account must be taken of unforeseen contingencies — unquestionably numerous — that might imperil not only our own lives but the destiny of two Peoples; and I concluded by affirming that, in my opinion, we were wrong to attempt the execution of the enterprise with a superfluous number of participants. Two resolute men — or even one alone — might suffice.

I also reminded him that the resolve to strike at the heart of the French reaction by suppressing Napoleon was not new in me — he well knew it, having full knowledge of the letter I had written as far back as 1854 to Giuseppe Mazzini. Why not allow me to accomplish alone an enterprise upon which I could claim a certain right of priority? If I succeeded, our cause would have nothing to lose; if I failed, other forces unknown, intact, reanimated by the desire for redress, would renew the attempt. The certainty of success lay in persistence.

— The imminence of the action, Orsini replied, not only persuades me that your fears are groundless, but also renders repentance impossible. How do you expect, moreover, that an enemy destined to ride into our ambush at the trot should escape us — and almost as though hastening to his own triumph? What is there to wait for? That the Emperor, deciding upon the evening, should give us, with his own hands, the signal to strike him. That is all!

I persisted, and pointed out to him that my plan grafted itself admirably upon his, diminishing, by its very simplicity, the risks. If he could procure for me — and it ought not to be difficult for him — the uniform of a *sargent de ville*, I should mingle, thus disguised, among the guards assigned by the city to protect the Emperor. Each section furnished a contingent, and those from one part of the city did not personally know those from the others. When the Emperor wishes to alight from his carriage, one of these guards, by a custom become a right, hastens to open the door for him. Let us consider: Louis Bonaparte proceeds, amid his police, up to the peristyle of the theatre. I fling myself to the carriage door. I am a non-commissioned officer. But he steps down! It is a matter of an instant. For the Roman Republic — for those cut down by grapeshot on the 2nd of December. From that moment, the history of popular retribution resumes its course!

Felice Orsini paced the room its full length and breadth. He was agitated, and upon his most mobile face thoughts pressed upon one another like lightning flashes. At last he planted himself before me, picked up and threw back down upon the table several papers, then burst out: “It is too late, it is too late! And besides — equal the glory, equal the risk. Let us say no more of it!”

With that refusal, he sealed his own destiny irrevocably.


### Chapter Nine

<!-- pages:160-162 -->

<!-- pages:161-165 -->



THE evening of the following day, the 14th of January, Pieri and I dined, as was our custom, at the trattoria in the Galerie Montmartre. We repaired immediately afterwards to the hotel, and the doorkeeper, handing Pieri the key to his room, announced to him that half an hour earlier a gentleman had come asking for him. He had not disclosed his own name, but had assured them he would return shortly.

And so we found ourselves in the room, feverishly searching for a match to light the gas. We had left the door open, to make temporary use of the light coming from the corridor. Turning instinctively, I caught sight, outlined in black against that light, of the figure of Felice Orsini.

His words were brief: "Compatriots, the Emperor has given the signal. Come to my lodgings to collect the implements. I shall be there within the hour. Everything is arranged for the best... success cannot fail us. But given that the unforeseen may arise... here is the means to facilitate your flight and your safety." And with that he drew out a purse and counted out two hundred francs for each of us. Then he continued: "I should have wished to provide better for your needs, but it is beyond my power. To the triumph of great principles denied, one contributes more through integrity of soul than through the force of gold. Gold is wanting to the persecuted, and is more properly the weapon of despotism. Bear in mind that upon your punctuality, upon your steadfastness, depends the redemption of the Fatherland. I say no more to you."

He took leave of me with a powerful handshake and vanished. Yet something remained.

A tormenting spirit that seemed to saturate the air, and my heart was very nearly overcome by it. The moments that precede action always arrive more painful than those in which the action itself is accomplished. The doing produces a fever that mounts with the danger and leaves no room for reflection or repentance. Be that as it may, from the tumult of thoughts that assailed me, the sense of duty emerged triumphant, and my soul was left, as it were, clad in armour.

I cast a glance towards Pieri and, at that very moment, to my astonishment, I saw him fling upon the bed the money left him by Orsini, as though he were vexed by it. A moment later he drew from a case a revolver — that marvellous American invention which had but recently been introduced into Europe — and holding it out to me said: "Take it! It holds six rounds... six friends, true friends! They might serve you well!" He threw the case into the drawer of the chest, locked it, picked up his money once more, and we went out.

We went out to walk, in silence and for the last time, along the road that led to the well-known lodgings in the Rue Monthabor. Having arrived before the apartment of Felice Orsini, and as we were about to knock, the door opened and two men crossed the threshold. They had the collars of their overcoats turned up, their faces turned towards the darkness of the courtyard. I did not recognise them.

Once again we found ourselves gathered together — Pieri, Gomez, Orsini, and I — as on the day when I had wished to submit my samples of beer to Tomaso Allsop. But no sooner had Gomez caught sight of us than he begged leave to absent himself for a moment to make, he said, a small purchase. We awaited him in silence, Orsini leaning, almost seated, against the table. Those long moments of waiting made him nervous. He rose and said: "It crosses my mind that that man has gone to sell the mice to the cat."

But Gomez returned, and Orsini smiled. He then proceeded to distribute the bombs. He said simply: "For you... for you..." So that they should not slip from our hands, he had wrapped them in a black handkerchief which, held by the knotted corners, would have acquired the force of a sling. Gomez and I received one each of large dimensions; Pieri one small one; Orsini stowed carefully about his person two small ones, one in the right pocket, the other in the left pocket of his overcoat. We set out, I at Orsini's side; Gomez and Pieri went ahead of us. At this point I could not help but remark that Pieri had kept his lips continuously sealed, as though the imminence of the action, or some secret thought, had rendered him suddenly mute. I drew from this none too charitable conclusions. We soon lost sight of him, and Orsini observed to me, without my having opened my mouth, that "the loquacious Tuscan had every appearance of a man who intended to desert."

Now I must relate an incident which was recounted to me later by Orsini himself, and which Pieri confirmed. At a certain point, Pieri turned back along his path and passed close beside us; but he was not alone. Accompanying him was a stranger, correctly dressed, with a contented, almost cheerful expression. This man walked straight on, paying us no heed; Pieri, instead, winked at Orsini. Absorbed in my own thoughts, or distracted — I could not say which — I noticed nothing, and Orsini did not see fit to mention it to me. He imagined that some entirely harmless intruder had attached himself to the heels of "the loquacious Tuscan," who would undoubtedly have known how to rid himself of the fellow, perhaps in some liquor-shop, and would then appear at his appointed place at the designated hour. That silence exercised a decisive influence upon my conduct immediately after the attempt.

We were now on the point of setting foot in the Rue Le Peletier. Felice Orsini stopped and looked about him as though impatient to see someone he was expecting. A few seconds passed, and a man with a hurried step, long moustaches protruding above his upturned collar, emerged from the crowd and moved towards him. Out of courtesy, I drew back a few paces; but the ear of the conspirator acquires habits that are not precisely those of the well-bred man. For this reason, perhaps, and not by deliberate design, I caught the following brief exchange:

— How does the affair stand?

— All is well!... This evening!

And they clasped hands. The man with the long moustaches immediately withdrew, as hastily as he had come. We resumed our way, and Orsini said: "Do you know that man?" — "Certainly! It is Francesco Crispi!" Whereupon he, with a slight shade of vexation: "Confound it! I had supposed you did not know him!"

Ought I, or ought I not, to add that with that final handshake Orsini had concluded the distribution of the bombs, and completed his part as organiser? I noticed that only one pocket of his overcoat showed any bulge...


### Chapter Ten

<!-- pages:162-165 -->



A FEW minutes later we were in position opposite the Opera. The light cast by the premises of a trattoria — the Broggi, if I am not mistaken — struck me from behind; the extraordinary illumination of the theatre fell full upon our faces. The entrance to the trattoria was flanked by columns. Against one of these Orsini leaned his back; I placed myself upright to his right. We were among a great many people, most of them standing still, awaiting the imperial cortège. Opposite us, just beyond the pavement, a cordon of municipal guards had drawn up in line, arms at the foot... beyond the rue Le Pelletier, along the sides of the little square, a dark crowd surged and pressed... among it, at the place assigned him, I saw Antonio Gomez, tall, erect, motionless as a statue; but though I searched as keenly as I could on the other side, I did not see Giuseppe Pieri.

And already one could hear, in the distance, the confused clamour of the multitude acclaiming the Emperor... a few seconds more, and the voices grew sonorous and distinct. To cry up to heaven the man who had mown them down with grapeshot... a deplorable degradation of Peoples who prefer a quiet life to the arduous struggles for Liberty!

The imperial cortège arrived. Its forerunner was a carriage containing several Court officers. The one in which, behind well-concealed iron cuirasses, Napoleon III sat as though in hiding, came second and was preceded and followed by half a squadron of mounted guides... the very same who had so ably seconded the heroic enterprise of the 2nd of December. After the drunkenness of blood and the proclamation of the Empire, they had been rechristened with the designation of Lancers of the Imperial Guard.

A simple and strange thing occurred. My gaze had flown to the Emperor's carriage and was attempting to penetrate beyond the opaque veil which the cold had condensed upon the glass of the door. At that moment, a white handkerchief, which betrayed the restless nervousness of the hand that clasped it, wiped away that veil, as though some sensitive soul, having fully caught my gaze and the hatred with which it was charged, were scrutinising the street with a suspicious and watchful eye.

I did not see, but I felt the presence of Eugenia Moutijo and the magical power of the eternal feminine, which knows how to work its way through cuirasses; which, by caressing and softening, bends force and triumphs over it.

But to sweep away the sentiment of pity, I had at once the intuition of the presence of a grim figure, with a nose like a vulture's beak, and with vibrations of the most repulsive kind. And I felt — oh, let it be believed as truth, even though it wear the face of falsehood! — I felt the warm odour of republican blood made to run in the sewers of Paris; blood of generous souls, slaughtered under cover of night, their hands bound behind their backs; slaughtered like herds of cattle. And the Roman epic, glorious with deeds and sacrifices, flashed through my mind... not as a pale memory drawn from the remoteness of time and space, but almost as a present tumult, full of tangible forms and of maledictions. I was seized by the fever that gunpowder gives on the field of battle. "It is just, it is just that the liberty of nations should spring from the death of him who has betrayed; it is just that this man should fall headlong even as he savours the triumph procured for him by his crimes! The Italian does not pay on Saturday — but he does pay!"
