The carriage slowed. I sought Antonio Gomez with my eye and found him again. But overcome, perhaps, by a generous impatience, he could not bring himself to wait. His hand, wrapped in the fateful black handkerchief, rose threateningly before the carriage had reached the peristyle, and those few seconds of anticipation brought the trajectory of the bomb into a line perpendicular to the trattoria of the Broggi. I had barely time to fling myself to the ground and to give a tug at Orsini's overcoat so that, following my example, he might save himself. Orsini remained rigid as the column against which he had planted himself as though rooted.

A sinister flash of light, accompanied by a deafening roar... then a darkness that seemed to me infernal. I sprang to my feet as the air, but moments before ringing with cheers, trembled with shattered glass and screams. "Your turn, your turn!" Felice Orsini whispered to me, in a strangled voice. Blood was spurting from a wide wound on his brow and his face was all besmirched with it. He bent down and laid the terrible black handkerchief upon the flagstones. I hurled myself forward, crouching low,

I hurled myself forward, crouching low, jaw clenched, passing over municipal guards stretched out in the convulsions of agony, or dead. The Lancers of the Imperial Guard were at that moment taking to headlong flight, abandoning to his fate the man they had sworn to defend. A few paces... I threw!... I threw!... the second flash, the second thunderclap! The diffused light reveals to me the Emperor's carriage lurching toward the theatre, one wheel in pieces, the coachman rolling on the cobblestones, the horses down and ripped open. Every lantern is extinguished and a black and terrible night enfolds men and things alike.

I had flung myself face down among the dead and the half-living, waiting in anguished suspense for the throwing of the remaining bombs. How long did I lie there? Ten seconds, it is maintained. I was prepared to die, but I should be lying were I not to say that those seconds seemed to me a century. And a third bomb came. Who threw it? Felice Orsini said it was the Italian to whom he had handed it over, in the rue Le Pelletier, a few minutes before the attempt. He was not believed, and the question still awaits an explicit answer... for others, not for me, who saw and knew that Italian. Be that as it may, whoever threw the third bomb had a stout soul and a steady wrist. It went straight to its mark and burst directly beneath the shattered carriage. Its detonation seemed to strike my very brain... but in an instant I had recovered full possession of my senses. I heard the ground resound with the trampling of horses galloping up. The Lancers of the Guard came thundering in at the gallop and reacted against the impulse that had swept them into flight, firing point-blank upon those who fled. The flashes of their weapons leapt in the darkness and revealed to me, in fitful glimpses, the horror and confusion. And the victims — unquestionably numerous — of that heroism which bore so strong a resemblance to cowardice, were afterwards attributed one and all to the "blind and ferocious fury" of the conspirators.

From that horror, from that confusion, I extricated myself, now crawling, now in lurching bounds, escaping — I could not tell you how — the horses that seemed to pour forth in swarms from the night, and the bullets that whistled about my head.

Repeatedly, in the course of my brief but tumultuous existence, my spirit had been saddened by the spectacle of innocent victims sacrificed to the security and the placid digestions of despotism. But this was not enough to render me impervious to a deep sense of compassion for those caused by our attempt. Nor was it sufficient to suppress that sentiment, the idea that, by inexorable law, nothing is granted to the

People without grave sacrifice of tears and blood.

Nevertheless, I must also say that the depressing effect of pity was, in great part, paralysed by the conviction that France, that Italy, had now a clear road to reach their glorious destiny; that Napoleon III was no more; that my own deed had helped to lift that nightmare from the world.


### Chapter Eleven

<!-- pages:165-168 -->

<!-- pages:166-172 -->



SCARCELY had I found myself out of danger than my first thought was for Elisa. I had made my way towards the Boulevard des Italiens… and entered a café. I must recount things that will seem trifles; yet they had their weight. The café was empty. The proprietress, behind the counter, was speaking in a frenzied manner with a customer who stood before her. She did not see me enter and took no notice of my presence until I asked her, if she would be so kind, for paper and inkwell. I wrote briefly: “Elisa, all is well. I shall leave tomorrow for Oporto.” Having sealed the letter, I returned the inkwell to the counter and asked for an eight-sou stamp. The lady rummaged in a drawer and, holding them out to me, asked whether four two-sou ones would not serve my purpose.

I made my way to the Place de la Concorde. It was deserted. I stood long at my post on the bridge. The thousand sounds of the city reached me confusedly, the humming of an immense hive. The Seine, made darker by the restless reflections of the gas-lamps, flowed towards the sea mysteriously, like the hour that hung above us. None of the conspirators appeared. When to wait longer would have been madness, I rid myself of the revolver by casting it into the river, and set off towards the Boulevard, resolved to reconnoitre the ground.

During that passage, every faint breath of voice caught upon the air descended into my breast like a gust of storm. The noble daring, the boundless hopes, the pertinacity to descry a thread of blue sky in the most cheerless of situations; the instinctive faith in the slow but certain justice of things — all these were as if uprooted, ground to dust, and scattered into nothingness. Napoleon III was alive!…

Sheltered by the iron plates of his carriage, he had remained therein as though paralysed until the bursting of the third bomb… Then all at once he appeared in the theatre, erect in the imperial box, ashen as a corpse. At his side was Eugenia Montijo, deathly pale yet beautiful still, and a few drops of blood scored her brow with crimson. At the frantic applause that broke out on all sides, she had let herself sink into her chair, her breast heaving, as though she had all at once recovered the only atmosphere capable of breathing life into her with full intensity. But Napoleon had remained standing. The sensation of being Emperor once more had restored colour to that pulsing cadaver, which now acknowledged the ovation, bowing complacently to right and left.

Outside, meanwhile, the Police and the most trusted battalions were making his road to the Palace secure.

Thus fell the intended revolution; thus the Empire remained, in all its integrity, stronger perhaps than before. Napoleon III clinging to France more than ever, like a vampire; France interposed, more than ever in my reckoning, between Italy and her independence and her greatness. And I asked myself, trembling, whether it were the inexorable law of Nature that the vulgarity and the treachery and the corruption and the cupidity of the few must hold eternal dominion in Europe; whether noble aspirations mocked, courage, persecution, misery, the halter must be, in eternity, the only heritage of the righteous.

And I despaired. But not as the man who, paralysed by destiny, does nothing but gnaw his own hands. Not a single fibre was there in me, in those moments, that was not swollen with turbulent spirit, that was not all impulse and rebellion. Had I found the occasion, I feel I could have killed even without a lofty purpose; simply as a human protest; to die, perhaps, but avenging the humble and the oppressed…

I resolved to return to the Hôtel France et Champagne. On the way I had occasion to persuade myself that the situation was growing murky… public opinion, as it revealed itself from the groups scattered here and there, was already attributing the attempt to foreigners. It was no longer merely a matter of renewing the unequal struggle; one had also to think of saving one’s neck. And perhaps from that very moment I should have seen to my own safety, had it not seemed to me an act of cowardice to depart without ascertaining at least the fate of my room-companion.

Nothing out of the ordinary at the hotel. Pieri had not returned… the room in precisely the order in which we had left it. I prepared my valise — the one with the quilted tiger — went down to the office and asked for my bill. From that moment events came thick and fast.

While the proprietress was confirming, in the register, the date of my arrival, I settled myself at a small table and began to leaf through an illustrated newspaper. Just then, several individuals entered noisily… plain-clothes policemen; their uniformly cropped hair was enough to betray them. They asked leave to inspect the register. One of them, the leader in all likelihood, leaning towards the lady, murmured: “Who is the Monsieur who is reading?” The lady indicated a point on the register with the tip of her pen. A dozen eyes fixed themselves upon me with such insistence that I felt their gaze driven into my skin, and the maître d’hôtel came and, with every show of deference, asked for my passport. I drew it out, unfolded it… the policemen took possession of it, scanned it, looked at one another. “Pardon, monsieur!” And they departed.

They departed; but from where I sat, I caught with the corner of my eye two hounds remaining on guard at the entrance. Was I to consider myself a prisoner in that hotel? The proprietress came with the bill, and I was compelled to sustain the conversation in a gallant vein which she was pleased to strike up. All at once I sighed; yet not for the reason perhaps imagined by my fair temptress. Glancing beyond the window, I had seen the policemen move away. The way was clear!

And I took advantage of it. Slowly at first, so as not to arouse suspicion; then, when I was quite certain I was not being watched, off at very nearly a running pace.

I was by now persuaded that Pieri had thought to get himself out of Paris. I came upon a public cab and secured it for myself: “Gare d’Orléans… a good tip if you get there in time!” The coachman laid on his whip as though he meant to flay the hide off the nag. We arrived… it was eleven o’clock… four minutes too late!… no train for Bordeaux until four in the morning!

What was to be done? Doze at the station? Seek hospitality at the house of Zappa, or with some other trustworthy friend? I decided to return to the hotel. The Police had already poked their nose in there… a circumstance which seemed to me sufficient, in itself, to render it an inviolable refuge.

And I set off on foot, my valise hanging on my arm. I had gone some five hundred paces, or thereabouts, when there came galloping up at full tilt a squadron of municipal guards. It swept past me like a whirlwind, slackened pace at the station, surrounded it, and enclosed the passengers within a ring of iron. I thought: “If the woodland bird was slow to take flight, the hunters are no less slow in setting their snare.”

At midnight I was in my room. I had left orders to be wakened in good time; I had turned off the gas; I had stretched myself out, as is my custom in such circumstances, without disrobing in the least. And I was already promising myself that I would take good counsel from the night, when I heard the bell of the street door ring. My room window gave onto the courtyard. I sprang to it, and down in the depths I made out a dark group of figures upon whom I could harbour no illusion; though, had I been disposed to, it would not have lasted long. In the silence of the night, there reached my ear the words: “We wish to see the foreigner in number 53!” The number of my room.

In haste I undressed and thrust myself under the covers. To make a greater show of untroubled composure, I had left the door ajar. The guards reached it accompanied by the maître d’hôtel; they entered by the scant light of a candle, turned on the gas… I felt a gentle touch upon one shoulder. I thereupon ceased to snore. “Forgive us! We must do our duty. Your name? Would you be so good as to furnish us with your passport?” They examine it minutely, then look at the maître d’hôtel: “But is this not the very gentleman with whom we spoke an hour ago, in your office? A night of misunderstandings indeed! Pardon, monsieur!” I heard their step recede along the corridor and fade away down the staircase.

But it was my fate not to rest in my bed that night. Half an hour later they were back, and this time they dispensed with ceremony. “A certain Monsieur Pierey,” they said, “who lodged in this very room, declares himself to be a friend of yours. He is at the Prefecture, in safe hands. You must resign yourself to keeping him company. We have orders to arrest you!”

While I dress, two policemen stand at my sides, at attention; others busy themselves picking the lock of our chest of drawers. From it they drew the revolver case, which Pieri had tossed there, and some cartridges. “What is this?”

— I do not know! The wardrobe is for the exclusive use of the gentleman who lodged in this room. Of my own I have nothing but this bag.

They felt it, rummaged through it, drew out, piece by piece, the little it contained; but the dagger concealed between the small board and the bottom escaped their investigation.

On arriving at the Prefecture, another blow awaited me. I had been conveyed there in a cab, with two guardian angels at my side, a third seated on the box with the coachman, a fourth behind the carriage. I was on the point of alighting and had not yet set my foot upon the step, when another laden carriage entered the courtyard at a trot. I make for the grand staircase and, seized by a dark presentiment, turn my head. A few paces behind me, preceded and followed by a guard, came a man who at that moment surely bore within his breast all the sorrows of his Italy. His face was pale, his side-whiskers black, his head bound in bandages… Felice Orsini!


### Chapter Twelve

<!-- pages:168-172 -->



They made me pass through three rooms occupied, despite the hour, by clerks intent at their writing-desks. Having arrived in a fourth, I sat for a long while before a fireplace, upon which two brands burned crosswise. And since, the better to collect my thoughts, I had closed my eyes, I heard a guard say in an undertone: "A false alarm! Were he an accomplice, he would not sleep so soundly!"

At length they drew me into a great hall, whose brilliant light for a moment dazzled me. The first impression I distinctly perceived was that of the door closing behind me; then I saw take shape before me — I must needs lose myself in these particulars — a writing-desk strewn with papers, standing perfectly isolated in the middle of the hall. An old man, apparently in his sixties, of aspect still vigorous, with smooth skin full of bright reflections, was seated at one side of it, frowning over a document. At my appearance he spread it across his knees and raised his face. It was the Prefect of Police.

Another writing-desk stood some four paces off along the right-hand wall. Its supports were set so high as to allow me to discern, of the man seated behind it, only the crown of his skull and nothing more — the skull of the Examining Magistrate. Beside the desk, partly seated, partly standing, were various high dignitaries of the military, their chests laden with medals, and among those gaudy uniforms there darkened sadly several black beetles in civilian dress. Among the latter I recognised a personage of the imperial family — Plon-Plon! There were guards of various ranks and in various attitudes, and as I swept my eyes over those gaudy uniforms, now here, now there, with the intensity of one who peers into the abyss by which he might be swallowed, I saw stand out from the walls of red ground, and as though emerging from them at that very moment, several marble busts that fixed me with their pupilless eyes. And the men, too, fixed me without the flutter of an eyelid, as though they partook of the marmoreal nature of those busts. The Examining Magistrate rose and advanced towards me, and his countenance was not unknown to me. He had a history. In the days when Louis Napoleon was brought low, this man had trampled upon those very Laws of which he was, now, the salaried champion. He owed his position to the old services rendered to the Emperor. At a sign from him I sat down, and two guards who stood beside the door came and placed themselves upright at my sides.

The magistrate then asked me for my particulars, and I replied in accordance with the passport they had confiscated. But when he asked whether I knew my room-mate, and I had answered in the negative, his face grew merry.

"How can that be," he asked, "you sleep with an individual and do not even know who he is? Where did you first encounter him?"

"During my journey from Calais to Paris. It was the first time I had come to this city, and I asked him the address of an hotel. 'Come down to mine,' he replied. 'I have a room with two beds. He understands a little of my language. You will spend less.' I accepted. He told me his name, I told him mine. That is all! Why should I have called him to account for his past?"

"But what were you coming to do?"

"To earn my bread, selling Burton Ale."

"How did you pass yesterday?"

I had already committed my answer to memory, which, moreover, differed but little from the truth. Monsieur Pierey, as one well acquainted with Paris, had taken me in the morning to the Louvre Gallery. Later, Pierey himself had gone to pay a call on some acquaintance of his, and I had awaited him in a tavern. I then recounted the dinner taken at the restaurant in the rue Montmartre.

"At what hour did you dine?"

"Towards seven o'clock. We returned to our hotel. Scarcely arrived, the porter told Monsieur Pierey that a gentleman had come seeking him — he should wait, he would return shortly. And so indeed it fell out. That is all!"

"Who was that gentleman?"

"I do not know. He had come for the other, not for me. Moreover, perceiving that he wished to speak of business, I withdrew out of prudence."

"Where did you go?"

"To the water-closet at the end of the corridor. When I returned, the gentleman had departed."

"But do you not recall his appearance?"

"The room was in darkness. I remember, however, that he wore a full beard."

"Would you recognise him on seeing him?"

"Perhaps yes, perhaps no."

At this point, the magistrate turned to a guard and ordered him to show in "the other prisoner." When the door reopened, on the threshold appeared Felice Orsini. The bandage that swathed his head was soaked in blood. He had the air of a man weak and spent. His first glance was for me. He seemed to address me a mute question, and across his features I saw pass a fleeting unease.

"Is this the man?" the Magistrate asked me.

"The man who came to the hotel wore a full beard. I believe I said as much."

The magistrate then left his desk and, drawing close to Orsini, ran the tips of his fingers under his chin. He wished to assure himself that a razor had not recently passed there. Fortunately, the prisoner's skin must have felt, to his touch, bristling with points.

It was to Orsini that he put his next question. He spoke in English.

"From which part of England do you come?"

"From Kent."

"How far is Kent from London?"

"Thirty leagues."

Those two words sufficed. In England one speaks of miles, not of leagues. Moreover, Orsini had pronounced his "thirty" by converting the sound of the *th* into a *t*, and causing the *r* to ring out vigorously — which is not in the habit of English mouths.

Said the Magistrate: "You are as English as I am a Turk. Indeed, to tell you the full truth, you are not even French."

The pale face of Felice Orsini flashed. He drew himself up, with Roman disdain. He said — nay, he cried: "Ah no, by God! I am Italian and proud of it. My name is Felice Orsini. Ask me nothing further; I shall not answer you."

He had poured his very soul into his words — the soul of the noble and strong man who commands the pygmies of character. They were subdued by it and dared not oppose him. He was forthwith conducted elsewhere. But the Prefect of Police, who throughout the interrogation had remained standing and motionless beside his desk, now fell to rummaging feverishly in the drawers. "Ah, ah!" he said at length, advancing towards the uniformed and civilian personages alike. "Here is a secret circular from Austria. Felice Orsini — the dangerous revolutionary who escaped from the castle of Mantova. We have made a good catch!"

He then returned to his desk, and turning to me: "Monsieur da Sylva, do you know these implements?"

I looked, and it was as though a ray of light illuminated the cells of my brain. Now I understood why Napoleon lived and triumphed! Two of the "implements" which should, by their explosion, have given the signal for awakening to two Peoples, lay idle, like paperweights, upon the Prefect's desk. By what means had they arrived there? There they were — that was sufficient for me. Their dimensions were equal; on both, at the tips of the lower half, the deadly detonating caps still glittered. An inexplicable imprudence!

That sight sufficed to rouse, in my breast, the furies which the predominant thought of one's own preservation had, for a time, lulled to sleep. And my every fibre swelled again with turbulent spirit; my every fibre recovered an impulse of rebellion.

I thought: "Perhaps that which has not been obtained by direct means may be obtained indirectly, by bringing fresh confusion into the enemy's camp, undoubtedly already thrown into disorder — and why not? But in any case — to kill as a human protest, avenging the betrayed and the oppressed!"

I do not say, now, that my thought was that of a measured man; I say that such was my thought. And I say that even today I hold supremely human the cry: "Let Samson perish, and with him the Philistines!"

I rose and bent my body forward, half-closing my eyes, as though I were short-sighted. Two steps — only two steps without arousing suspicion — and I should have made a spring, a rapid movement, a thunderbolt — guards, Prefect, Magistrate, generals, myself — ambitions and hopes, servility and lust for power, crimes and virtues — a few shreds of flesh — nothingness —

A hand, heavy as fate, seized me by the shoulder, and I fell back into the chair. My heart was beating convulsively, but I made an effort and said in a composed and almost perplexed tone: "I do not understand — what is the matter?"

From that moment my interrogation proceeded rapidly and according to my wishes. In giving the final touches to the account of my actions, I took care to transpose, so to speak, a few minutes, and made my entry into the café coincide precisely with the hour of the attempt. I alluded also to the letter I had written there. The game was dangerous; but the proprietress had not seen me enter, and upon that I was counting. The Magistrate whispered a few words into a guard's ear, and the latter departed forthwith. I gathered from this that a verification was to be carried out. No great time passed before the same guard returned and fully confirmed my deposition, recounting even the incident of the four two-sou stamps which I had forgotten. The Magistrate then turned to the Prefect and said to him: "For my part I have no further question to put."

"Very well!" the latter observed, "but Monsieur da Sylva might prove a valuable witness. I would request him to remain at his hotel, at the disposal and expense of the State. His passport will be returned to him later. No objection?"

"None whatsoever — particularly if the State undertakes the expenses. My means are extremely limited!"

And for the third time that night, I heard the French Police lavish apologies upon me. The high personages, uniformed and otherwise, fell to conversing among themselves, glancing at me from time to time as though exchanging their impressions on my account. And the Magistrate, having wished me good night, turned to the guards at my sides. His last words were in Portuguese: "Monsieur da Sylva outside the Prefecture!"

As I was crossing the various rooms, my thoughts retraced my steps and I considered the extraordinary perspicacity of a Magistrate who let the wolf escape from the trap in such a fashion. It would have sufficed for him to have questioned me regarding my place of origin; to have asked me with which clients I had dealt in my capacity as seller of Burton Ale; to have addressed, or caused to be addressed to me, a single word in Portuguese — and my true nature would have been laid bare. From this I concluded that Magistrate and Police found themselves in a state of over-excitement, perhaps also of vague apprehension, which clouded their vision and halved their dynamic power.

Yet he who tonight — I thought — might be awake and on the alert tomorrow… Tomorrow? Let me but set foot out of doors again and shrewd is he who finds me! The Gare d'Orléans is watched; the same must hold of the other stations — but many roads lead out of Paris, and Paris is vast, and never has it lacked for friends and hiding-places.

I had reached the last room and there I found the unexpected. Through the door, which had opened to grant liberty to the wolf, there entered at that very moment, under close escort, Antonio Gomez. I looked away at once, the only means within my power of letting him understand that he must be ignorant of my presence. But his own wits were likewise clouded, and, passing close by me, he murmured in a low voice, in French: "We are lost!"

It was my fate that among his escort there should be an intelligent man. "Ah, ah! You know each other?" And Gomez, driving home the nail that was being hammered into me: "Of course we know each other!"

I had hastened to set foot upon the threshold. "Halt!" a voice thundered behind me. I was invited to sit down and the door closed. Gomez continued on his way to the great hall; I remained in the trap in great anxiety, biting my lips. Ten minutes passed. A clock whirred and struck four strokes. Other guards came. They took me by the arm, without opening their mouths, and led me away. We traversed an underground passage.

I passed the rest of the night in a cell of the Conciergerie!


### Chapter Thirteen

<!-- pages:172-177 -->



Here, traced step by step, is the staircase leading to the abyss that was to swallow up both us and every hope we harboured: Pieri’s childlike trust in the Hartmann; the storm at sea that prevented my interview with Orsini at the Paris station; the association formed between the executors of the attempt, contrary to the understanding; Pieri’s failure to act and his all-too-ready loquacity immediately after his arrest; Gomez’s nerves. To these must be added — I shall speak of it later — the character of Felice Orsini, little or not at all suited to the life of a conspirator, and the good faith of an English young lady, blind with love for him. If other causes have been put forward, they were invented by the malice of men or the fancy of novelists.

I had forgotten! There was yet another cause, secondary perhaps, but one that must nonetheless be taken into account: the defection of Simon Bernard. He never came to Paris. While others, for having attempted to serve his France, were being driven by great strides toward the scaffold, he gave a fine proof of his courage by strolling tranquilly, prating of his own heroism, through the streets of London!

On the very morning of my arrest, after a few hours of tormented sleep and as many of wakefulness spent in brooding over the revelations that Gomez might have made, I was conducted to the Palais de Justice.

I had barely set foot in the antechamber before I was obliged to persuade myself of the most ill wind that was blowing. Seated in a row along the wall, almost displayed in a shop window, stood four persons of my acquaintance: the landlady of the Hôtel France et Champagne; the *maître d’hôtel*; and monsieur and madame Morand, the concierges of Felice Orsini’s house. All people summoned, beyond any doubt, for the purpose of identifying me. And so far, no great harm. I had passed myself off as da Sylva, and as da Sylva they would denounce me. But I had denied all acquaintance with Felice Orsini: the Pipelet couple would give the lie to that — my denial was becoming untenable!

Yet the heaviest blow came when I was brought before the magistrate. It was the same personage who had questioned me a few hours earlier. He received me with a smile, as though seeing me again afforded him immense pleasure. “Ah, ah! And how are we this morning, Signor Conte? No longer da Sylva this morning; but Carlo of the Counts di Rudio!”

No doubt remained: Antonio Gomez had let my name slip. I must say at once, in his defence, that both Pieri and Orsini had always called me, in his presence, either Moretto or di Rudio. He, in all probability, had no knowledge that I carried a Portuguese passport.

I put a good face on a bad business and answered, smiling no less than he: “Carlo di Rudio, precisely!”

He grew serious: “The devil! A young fellow like yourself, entangled in this wretched affair. Have you given no thought to your family? Come now! One must redeem oneself. Tell me with manly frankness how you passed yesterday.”

And I went through once more, point by point, the little story I had told at the first interrogation. But, bearing in mind the Pipelet couple waiting in the antechamber, I admitted to having paid a brief visit to Orsini. With no mysterious purpose, for that matter. We had conspired together in Switzerland, and I had wished to see him again to revive old and cherished memories.

— But why did you deny it last night?

— Simply so as not to compromise a man who, from what I could gather, was already in difficulties. I believe that none of your Laws required me to fail in this duty of chivalry.

— And how does it come about that you carry a false passport?

— As a most natural precaution on the part of a political exile who might have Austria’s spies at his heels. And how can it be held against me by one who did the very same thing when he landed at Boulogne with Louis Napoleon?

The thrust went full to the chest; but the magistrate contented himself with pulling a grimace. He resumed: “Now tell me everything you know concerning this attempt.”

— Whatever the guards who arrested me have told of it. I know that the intention was to kill the Emperor, and nothing more.

— That may well be. In any case I must inform you that your friends have denounced you as their accomplice, and that you will be tried together with them!

And he gave the order that I be conducted to Mazas.


### Chapter Fourteen

<!-- pages:177-183 -->

<!-- pages:178-185 -->



FROM that moment I harboured no further doubt as to the fate that lay in store for me; and if I resolved to struggle to the end, it was solely, as one says, for honour of signature — so that I might cry out, upon reaching the bottom of the abyss: "I have done everything humanly possible to avoid plunging into it!"

That very same day I was compelled to convince myself that an overbearing will was working in the contrary direction, reinforcing the means honourably at its disposal with fraud and perjury. I was confined in a ground-floor cell. A companion was assigned to me — an agent of the secret Police, dressed in civilian clothes, who informed me that he would pass the day and night in my company. Certainly not to keep my spirits up, I thought — but to catch some word that might escape me, in the anguish of the soul, during sleep.

He appeared to divine my thought and begged me not to judge by appearances. If he could do me no good, he would certainly do me no harm.

Half an hour later, the bolts grated and the heavy door swung upon its hinges. Two persons entered: the examining magistrate and a man dressed as a municipal street-sweeper, with one empty socket, horribly scarred. Two guards remained standing on the threshold.

— Well then! said the magistrate, after a brief silence.

— It is he!

— Are you quite certain?

— As certain as I am baptised.

— Repeat your account.

— I was on duty at the Opera and was spreading sand at the entrance reserved for His Majesty. It would have been seven o'clock, or a quarter past at the most. I saw two gentlemen who were conversing in undertones and gesticulating. They had the air of studying the locality. I warned them that this was no place for the public and asked them to move along. They told me to mind my own business. The gentleman who is here said: "Shut your mouth, you scoundrel!" I made as if to strike him and both of them cleared off.

— And this took place?

— Yesterday evening, the fourteenth of January.

— An hour, or a little before, the attempt upon the life of His Majesty the Emperor?

— An hour, or a little more.

I had understood — perfectly understood! The object was to establish, in a manner that should appear irrefutable, our presence at the scene of the attempt. For Orsini there was his wound; for Gomez the arrest effected in front of the Opéra theatre. To bring about my own destruction and that of Pieri, they had placed in the mouth of that miserable wretch a tale invented out of whole cloth.

Nevertheless I did not feel that anger which drives a man to outbursts, and I contented myself with asking my strange accuser how much he had been promised in payment for his falsehoods. He turned aside the one greenish eye remaining to him and made no reply.

Late in the evening, a second agent was shut into my cell. Scarcely had he entered when he greeted me and, offering me his right hand, said that he too would not leave me either by day or by night. They were locking him in as well, as far as he could judge, in order to prevent any communication between myself and the accomplices whom I was suspected of having outside. He hoped that all three of us would, like good comrades, endeavour not to embitter our captivity with needless frictions.

Several days of relative quiet passed. No contact with the outside world, of which little air penetrated into my cell, still less sunlight, and the turnkey but rarely. He had orders to speak neither with me nor with my guards, who thus became the only human beings with whom I was permitted to exchange my thoughts. And I availed myself of this largely, telling them the history of Austria's horrible persecutions and of the suffering they visited upon my Country. One makes propaganda where one can and as occasion serves.

But this was not enough to deliver me from periods of deep prostration, in which the image of Elisa and of the child would rise before me, vivid and clear — the child to whose innocent years and maternal smile I had brought ruin. And my thoughts would wander at such times to my companions in misfortune as well, of whom I knew nothing save that they were, like me, confined at Mazas; subject to the same snares, plunging inexorably toward an identical fate. In those moments I would sit apart on the plank bed, motionless, my eyes cast down. And here I owe my guards a word of thanks — somewhat belated, it is true — who, so as not to disturb my meditations, paced on tiptoe.

Another of my tormenting preoccupations arose from the certainty that my enforced inaction must be matched by feverish activity on the part of the Police. I endeavoured to put myself, so to speak, in the place of the Authorities, and to school myself in my own defence. But every effort mocked every faculty I possessed, and the subtle conjectures and elaborate reconstructions ended in this: nervous irritation and exhaustion. There remained, then, nothing but to resort to the great remedy of youth: either I slept, in whatever fashion I might, or I abandoned the effort altogether.

The first certain sign of the inextricable net that was closing about me came when, conducted under heavy guard to the Chancellor's office, I found placed before my eyes, first a certified copy, and then the original itself, of a letter I had written from London to Felice Orsini. In it I informed him how a certain Carlotti, a man from Brescia, had, greatly to my surprise, made mention to me of our conspiracy. I had good reason to suspect this person of being a spy in the service of the French Government. He had to be set aside. If he already knew too much — one must either be rid of him by some means, or abandon the enterprise altogether.

Felice Orsini, more ardent patriot than prudent conspirator, had preserved among his papers so compromising a document, and the English Police, urged on from Paris, had carried out a search of his lodgings and taken possession of it.

My close connexion with the conspirators, my knowledge of their plots, appeared only too firmly established. Another good step further down the fatal slope! And when I attempted, pro forma, to deny the undeniable, the examining magistrate, by way of reply, smiled in a mocking fashion and sent me back to the darkness, launching after me, by way of a parting valediction, the shattering phrase: "Oh, we have far stronger evidence than this! and your accomplices have fully corroborated it!"

The following day I was transferred from Mazas, in a closed carriage, to the Conciergerie. I was assigned cell No. 1, and it was not long before I learned that Pieri was confined in No. 2, Gomez in No. 3, and Orsini in No. 4.

My new lodgings were approximately ten feet by fifteen in extent. The light entered timidly through a small window set with thick iron bars, situated near the ceiling. In the door, at a man's height, was a circular aperture — a five-franc piece would barely have passed through it. It permitted the warders to keep watch over my every movement; it permitted me to observe, within certain limits, what passed in the corridor. On the walls, the hands of prisoners who had preceded me had doubtless engraved verses and maxims, scraped thereafter inexorably away by the warders' knives.

What fragments of human hearts, and what reflections of souls refined by suffering, withdrawn from my meditation by the petty observance of duty! The story of one such soul I had known in all its particulars since my childhood. It was from my very cell that Marshal Ney had walked forth to have his patriot breast shattered by Bourbon lead!

At the Conciergerie I too had two guards. Of them I must say something. The more agreeable was a former petty naval officer. The State, having wrung his youth from him, had rescued him from penury by entrusting him with duties of dubious character that clearly revolted his disposition. A large family, poor and without connexions, kept him in thrall to his own fate; but his resignation was only apparent, and I was not long in perceiving that his secret rancour, finding no outlet, was slowly accumulating, drop by drop, within his heart. Nature had denied him the resolution and the impulse that drive men to virile acts; unless, indeed, a foolish discipline too long endured had severed every thread of his will. But he could still admire in others the virtues that were not in him, and his instincts betrayed themselves, beneath the surface and perhaps without his own knowledge, as rebellious. The years have erased his surname from my memory. His Christian name was, if I am not mistaken, one very common among the people of Paris — a mythological name: Saturno!

The other guard was a former soldier of the marine infantry. A rather stout man of few words, he would sit for hours with his hands upon his belly, fingers interlaced, placidly revolving his two thumbs one about the other, now in one direction, now in the reverse. He was fond of dozing.

At the Conciergerie I received my first intimation of what was passing in the world outside. My two guards kept their lips sealed on this subject. But other persons employed in the service of the prison attended to my thirst by the simplest of expedients: planting themselves in front of my cell and conversing with one another in loud voices.

In this manner I came to learn how public opinion, which had at first shown itself hostile to us, had rapidly shifted in our favour. The substance of entire newspaper articles drifted through the small circular aperture to my ear. Among them, those translated from the English press struck me as particularly explicit and almost laudatory.

But why did the staff of the Conciergerie take such pains? I believed I divined the reason on the day I heard a warder declare that "in this affair" — our affair — "mysterious and powerful influences were at work." He had added: "It is not difficult to foresee that not one of the four prisoners will be executed." I inferred from this that he who had spoken thus was not altogether unconnected with the direct inspiration of the mysterious influences to which he had permitted himself to allude.

And now we approach the precipice! From the far end of the corridor a voice cried: "Prisoner of cell No. 4!" It was Felice Orsini's cell. I ran to press my eye against the circular aperture. Let it now be seen how capable a thing it is of gnawing away a man's very existence — even in the most intrepid — to be tormented by the ruin of a great enterprise and by the consciousness of one's own responsibility! Felice Orsini passed rapidly across the narrow field of my vision; but that was enough for me to discern, upon that sculptural head, the traces of the storm that must have been raging within. His hair had turned almost entirely white.

Half an hour of sad reflection elapsed, and I heard Orsini's step returning. At once, from the far end of the corridor, the same voice: "Prisoner of cell No. 1!"

I was conducted into a long and narrow room. The single window that lit it was directly opposite me. At a writing-desk beside the window sat the usual examining magistrate and another individual dressed in black — the Imperial Prosecutor. But my attention was violently drawn to a third figure, seated a little way from the desk, to the right of the magistrate. Her profile stood out sharply against the light of the window. I knew that profile — it was of a woman small, slight, dark, not beautiful in the classical sense, but very attractive; and at the sight of her there awoke in my memory the impression of her persistent smile. It was Miss Elisa Cheney, the friend of Felice Orsini.

I dissembled the agitation that seized me so intensely as to prevent my distinguishing the other persons around me, and barely noticed a second lady who, seated at a table near the entrance, rested her arm upon it and upon that arm buried her face.

The examination proceeded with a swiftness that struck me as lightning-like. The Magistrate addressed himself first to the English young woman, speaking to her in her own language.

— Is this the man who brought the letter found upon your mantelpiece, and to whom you handed a travelling bag belonging to Signor Felice Orsini?

— It is he.

— Signor Count, what have you to say to that?

Genteel people, seated at table in a warm room with their feet upon soft carpets, may perhaps be unable to forgive me my ungallantry. But the question was one of saving my head from the guillotine and preserving it for better employment. I replied: "How can that woman claim to know me? She may have caught a glimpse of me some night, in passing, as she was walking the pavements of Haymarket. The testimony of a woman of doubtful character amounts to nothing."

The young woman turned pale and, in a voice broken by anger: "Let the travelling bag be examined. On the leather, at the bottom — in indelible characters—"

She could not continue. And the magistrate: "Compose yourself, compose yourself, Miss!"

"— in indelible characters, the name of Felice Orsini is written!"

At a sign from the magistrate, the Chancellor flashed before my eyes, produced I know not whence, the travelling bag with the embroidered tiger. Time and dust had blackened the bottom. But it was immediately washed, and the indelible characters appeared. Mere participation in a conspiracy that had led to the shedding of blood was sufficient — those characters were my death sentence.

But I did not concede defeat, and attempted a desperate move. I had clearly understood on the spot that Miss Cheney was acting neither from venality nor from hatred. The spirit that animated her must be love. "The Police, past masters in the art of turning passions to account," I thought, "must have promised her the salvation of the man she adored, in exchange for a complete revelation." I learned later that I had guessed the truth. To the detective Sanders, who was urging her to disclose all she knew, she had replied: "Everything, everything, to save that man!" And she had allowed herself to be brought to Paris.

Be that as it may, impelled by my own conviction, I raised my voice as I had never raised it before my judge, and turning to my accuser: "You are attempting to save Felice Orsini, and to that end you are betraying the innocent!"

What I had not expected then occurred. My voice, vibrating through the room, had entered a heart, had shaken a creature, had lifted a head bowed upon its arm in painful languor.

I found myself clasped by two trembling arms, and my muscles were forced to make an effort to support the weight of a being who surrendered herself to me. And I, who still did not comprehend, felt something like a wave of rose and blue penetrate to my depths and temper my storm; whilst a cloud of hair, fair and light, caressed my face and circumscribed it, as though to hide it from profane eyes. A sonorous, long kiss —

For a moment, the magistrate, the Imperial Prosecutor, the room, the conspiracy, France, the whole world ceased to exist. I was in the arms of my wife.

They parted us. The magistrate was the first to break the silence. How shrill his voice seemed to me! He said: "Is the Count di Rudio your husband?" And she, with honest pride: "I should not be here if he were not!"

We were then permitted ten minutes for a private interview. But when we drew aside, Sanders, the head of the Scotland Yard detectives, who had accompanied my poor Elisa to Paris, followed us and planted himself at our side. I foiled him by opening our conversation in Italian.

— The child?

— A darling! Mamma is taking care of him. Carlo, Carlo, what can I do for you?

And Sanders, brusquely: "Speak in English!"

— Go to the devil!

He lodged a protest with the magistrate, who, thinking perhaps that speaking the language of Shakespeare presented difficulties for me, gave orders that a Corsican interpreter should be present at our conversation. Sanders withdrew. And I then resumed speaking — but in English.

"Elisa, this is no time for illusions. All is lost, and I shall be brought to the scaffold. What can you do? Nothing, but bear your fate with fortitude. Unless—"

Here, strong protests from the Corsican, who demanded that we speak Italian. And thereupon the magistrate gave orders that both interpreters should stand at our side. But before Sanders had returned, I had already expressed my meaning — "unless you can manage to engage the interest of some English personage of great influence in our case. In that event, perhaps—"

At this point, the arrival of the second intruder exasperated me, and my overwrought emotions could no longer be contained. Which was the stronger impulse? Whether it was horror at the prolonged dissimulation and the need to vindicate, in that supreme moment, my own share in events; or whether it was the presence of Elisa that rekindled my pride — the fact is that, raising my voice once more, I flung into the astonished face of the magistrate these words: "Yes, I too attempted to destroy the assassin of my Fatherland and of the French People. I have but one regret: that I did not know how to strike. The second bomb was thrown by me!"

The magistrate, pale and grave, made as if to speak. But I forestalled him: "Do not trouble me with needless questions. What I have said is sufficient for your Justice!"

I saw the gesture slicing the air that dismissed me back to prison, and I turned to my wife to embrace her once more. Alas! the rose had vanished; the blue eyes had lost their brightness. Elisa, who had fainted, lay abandoned in a chair, her arms hanging loose, her head thrown back against the back of it. My kiss — the last, perhaps — I pressed upon those lips grown pale and insensible, yet still trembling.

Then I hurried away, so swiftly that the guards who came after me seemed almost to be giving chase!


### Chapter Fifteen

<!-- pages:183-186 -->

<!-- pages:184-191 -->



WHEN the door of the cell closed behind me, it was as though the turbid passions that had agitated me remained on the other side of the threshold. Saturno and the other questioned me with the expression of their faces. "It is all over! Every aspiration I had towards liberty is cut short from this day. One must prepare oneself to die." So I said; but within, I felt that the pride of having sacrificed myself for my Country would sustain me.

No sooner had I finished relating what had passed than they strove, as if by tacit agreement, to drag me back from my convictions. They put forward, among other things, the sympathies of the population and the favourable dispositions which, as it was asserted, animated the imperial Court. To this I replied with a shake of my head and a smile. A strange dispute arose from this, and we ended by making a wager. If the Tribunal pronounced a sentence of death, they would undertake to pay me a bottle of champagne, and of the finest. If not, I should pay it. We sealed the compact by exchanging a vigorous handshake...

Mathieu de la Drôme paid me his first visit that same day. He was a man of dignified appearance, tall, already more than greying, placid in his manner and speech. For several days past he had spontaneously offered to defend me; but we had not been permitted to meet until the Police were well assured of having their claws fast about my throat. He drew near, so to speak, to my soul with the delicacy of a physician who knows he must touch limbs that are sensitive and in pain. And I related to him at once, without reserve (the guards had withdrawn) the whole of my past.

When I had finished, he lowered his eyes and withdrew into his reflections for some seconds. Then, with a paternal affability: "The damaging admissions might be retracted. It is within your right. But to what extent it would serve you, I cannot say. You are accustomed to facing danger, and I prefer to speak plainly. The direct evidence gathered by the Police makes an acquittal beyond your reach. Were it a matter of considering only yourself and your bearing before your countrymen, I should say to you: Go forth to meet death majestically, whole and unbroken, blessing your Country, cursing her oppressors. The People will accord you, in their great heart, the place that is your due. But the wise man—"

Here he drew out at length, setting before me what, in his view, the wise man would have done in my situation. To hurl oneself headlong towards the scaffold was not to serve one's Country, nor would my sacrifice improve the position of my fellow conspirators. And how would my aged parents, who had already suffered so greatly, endure a blow so desperately past all remedy? A young wife trembled for me, and I had made her a mother. Why not attempt every honourable means of living, when, by cheating the executioner, I should spare needless grief to those unhappy souls?

I ended by asking him for an explicit explanation of what he required of me. "A free hand," he answered, "in attempting the means which might save you, and a formal promise to moderate your impulses, whatever the part I might judge it expedient for you to assume."

I consented. And this explains why, in the accounts of the trial, I emerge colourless, almost without a will of my own, easily swayed, driven to the terrible act by the iron mind of the leaders and by a morbid anxiety not to appear pusillanimous, not to be confounded with Rossi of Mantua, not to be taken for a spy.

I have never harboured towards Mathieu de la Drôme anything other than sentiments of filial gratitude. Unfortunately, his noble efforts to "cheat the executioner" served afterwards as a convenient instrument of defamation for the ill-disposed, blinded alike by personal rancour and by partisan rancour — not the least of the motives that, for fifty years, shut me in a disdainful silence!

My fellow conspirators were caught in the selfsame vice, sunk to the throat in the selfsame quicksand.

The evidence of which the Authority had possessed itself was, of itself, sufficient to secure a conviction in perfect accordance with the Law. To reinforce it with the admissions of the accused would give bolder relief, greater theatrical effect, to the cunning of the Police.

The better to attain its end, it had begun its subtle work with me, who was the youngest; then came the turn of Antonio Gomez, who was my elder by some years. To this man the Examining Magistrate placed in triumph before his eyes a letter of recommendation issued to him by Pieri, addressed to Orsini. Worse still, a letter written in his own hand to Orsini himself. In it he laboured to dispel certain suspicions that had insinuated themselves into the mistrustful mind of the chief, and, pressing for confirmation of the charge promised to him, he declared his desire to take part in the conspiracy, inspired solely by the love he had always borne and still bore to their common Country.

Italians, reborn as a Nation, have the moral obligation to set such solicitude and the sentiments that inspired it to Gomez's credit — but, in the face of the French Police, could there be a more compromising document?

As though this were not enough, Gomez found himself required to account for his every movement, step by step, from the moment when, on the 6th of January, he set out with Pieri from Birmingham, halting first in London, at the house of Miss Cheney, then at Lille, awaiting Pieri, who was making his dash to Brussels; and so on until the moment when he set foot for the first time in Orsini's furnished lodgings in the rue Monthabor, No. 10.

And here, as a finishing stroke, came the artful insinuation: "his fellow conspirators had confessed their own part and his. What purpose was there in persisting in denial?"

After various equivocations and various manoeuvres too desperate to savour of sound tactics, hearing it said openly that I, di Rudio, in admitting to having thrown the second bomb, had revealed everything else, he ended by confessing to having thrown the first. But even in this extremity, he was not forsaken by an instinct very widespread among the people of southern Italy: that which leads a man to draw from his pocket the packet of feigned simplicity and to smear it over his face. He, who had been a soldier nonetheless, attempted to give the impression of having thrown his projectile as a faithful servant, without forming any clear notion of its deadly effects. It was upon this supposed simplicity that his defence was constructed...

More arduous proved the struggle, amid similar straits, for Giuseppe Pieri. The police had placed before his eyes several revealing letters; had recounted to him, with indisputable precision, step by step, his movements in the period preceding the attack; his relations with Orsini, with Gomez, with Simon Bernard, with me; his complicity in importing the empty projectiles into France, in the purchase of certain revolvers found near the scene of the attack! Elisa Cheney identifying him and Hartmann denouncing him had, by their personal testimonies, corroborated the written evidence. The arrest effected within minutes and paces of the attack, the loaded bomb in the pocket of his overcoat, the boasts that had escaped him after his arrest — could there be a more enveloping net, a more complete case?

He added nothing to it, because nothing remained for him to add. He attempted instead to adorn the facts with fantastic fabrications and to draw from them deductions tending to distort their significance.

"He had come to Paris solely to settle some family business, and intended to proceed to Italy, where a revolution was imminent. He had discussed bombs with Orsini, but solely with a view to putting them to use at home, on the barricades. And since he had expressed some doubt as to their efficacy, Orsini had handed one to him; but with the understanding that they would cause it to explode, by way of experiment, at the Barrière des Martyrs. Only Pieri had gone to the rendezvous; for which reason the bomb had remained in his pocket and had been found upon him there. If on the evening of the attack he had gone to the rue Le Pelletier, it was simply because he had been drawn thither by the extraordinary illumination."

A shield assembled with sufficient cleverness; but wasted labour! — a few of his written phrases and a little analysis sufficed to shatter it. He had the merit of perceiving this in time. Thereupon, pleading as a pretext "the inquisitorial manner of the Examining Magistrate," he declared that he would answer no further questions; that he would refute the charges solely during the trial proper, before those whom he considered his natural judges.

He conjured away, by this means, the danger of compromising himself yet further. But was the worst truly past?

Nugent de Saint Laurent, who afterwards fully approved of Pieri's tactics, attempted to lend them greater weight by laying great emphasis on the animosity existing between Pieri himself and Orsini; as though to infer that a conspiracy, and the fraternal relations it entails, were impossible between two men who cordially hated one another. Unfortunately, the reason for the disagreements was never made clear; while it became sufficiently plain that they were of too recent a date to have had any power of dissolution upon the conspiracy.


### Chapter Sixteen

<!-- pages:186-190 -->

<!-- pages:186-190 -->

AND so we come to our chief. I shall call up old impressions; I shall express judgements which fifty years of meditation have not availed to alter.

We find ourselves before a character capable, in the highest degree, of generous impulses. Yet, through the mysterious reactions of his soul, the frequent exaltations of feeling were followed, always or almost always, by profound depressions. A nature excessively nervous and sensitive, he oscillated fatally from one extreme to the other, laying bare strange deficiencies which I regarded, even in those days, as maladies — of what precise nature I could not then define, nor can I now.

The conspiracy against Napoleon III reveals in him a genius adroit in conceiving a design in broad outline. But the success of such undertakings, when analysed, proves to consist in the sum of trifles patiently worked with a chisel. Felice Orsini, in the practical act, showed so little regard for the most elementary precautions that I do not hesitate to affirm that the essential gifts of the conspirator were conspicuous in him by their complete absence.

Giuseppe Mazzini, censured by Orsini to the point of denigration — to the point of allowing him no genius save that of the spoken word — would certainly never have committed the folly of leaving England, of plunging into an enterprise of such magnitude, while forgetting behind him, intact, recoverable, all but in plain sight, the correspondence that revealed every preliminary of the attempt.

This imprudence will appear all the more egregious when it is known that he himself, in writing to me, frequently urged me, urged us, to destroy his letters and to leave not the slightest trace of them.

It weighs upon me to say it, but I must say it in the name of truth: the trouble with Felice Orsini's mind lay in an overflowing imagination that prevented him from discerning the exact contours of things, despite his pretension to being a practical man.

Having shouldered a difficult enterprise, he would construct in imagination the events which ought, through a long chain of consequence, to lead to triumph; and dwelling upon that triumph, he would exalt himself so thoroughly as to regard it as inevitable, savouring it before it had come to pass.

With an irony that seemed cruel, it was observed how Felice Orsini, on the eve of one of his desperate attempts in the Lunigiana, had carried his faith in success to the point of procuring for himself a general's uniform. One recoils, before the majesty of martyrdom, from being compelled to recall how, setting out for Paris to attempt the life of an Emperor, to initiate a revolution, he — who stood in such need of obscurity and silence — purchased a saddle-horse and rode frequently through the streets of the metropolis, as though anticipating therein the emotions of the epic moments to come.

This explains so many other things. The excessive development of one mental tendency is accomplished at the expense of other faculties, which remain all the more deficient as that tendency manifests itself more turgid and overbearing. An immoderate faith in success, which rests in substance upon an exaggerated estimate of one's own powers, is answered, too often, by a corresponding failure to perceive the true strength and character of others. When catastrophe abruptly cuts short the fantastic dreams, the proud and haughty spirit, overflowing with bitterness, will strive, in perfect good faith, to demonstrate that said catastrophe is principally owing to the worthlessness of one's companions in action.

What reason led Felice Orsini to break so wrathfully with the Maestro? Let historians seek it in this or that incident, and from their point of view they are right to do so. But the primary cause is entirely psychological. Historical incidents are, in reality, nothing other than the expression assumed, in a given environment, by the deplorable flaw of a soul otherwise noble and great.

In a different field and in different measure, yet from the selfsame source, Felice Orsini was destined to treat us also — his companions in danger and in misfortune — in like fashion. The bitter words uttered during the brief absence of the Gomez, in the house in the rue Monthabor, on the evening of the 14th of January; the no less stinging phrase launched, shortly afterwards, at Pieri's address as we were making our way to the Opéra, demonstrate that a dark reaction was already fermenting in his soul even before the catastrophe.

An ill-starred incident provoked by Gomez served to lend some substance to the phantoms that troubled him. Before resolving upon a full confession, Gomez had attempted to persuade the examining magistrate that Orsini, exasperated by his hesitation, had at the moment of the imperial carriage's approach brusquely snatched the bomb from his hands, and that Orsini himself had hurled it against the Emperor. When confronted, he immediately retracted, and the matter had no legal consequence whatsoever. But it certainly did not help to restore Orsini to a just perception of reality.

He was undoubtedly in such a state when he was ensnared and beguiled by the arts of the police. The insinuation contrived against the integrity of his companions made a wide breach in a soul already so open to suspicion. And whereas before that moment, following his generous impulses, he had shown a wish to assume every responsibility for what had occurred, he now repented of this and dictated a detailed account in which, while assigning to each his share in acts already known to the Police, he let fall towards us words full of bitterness.

He then repented of his repentance and attempted, with a vigorous counter-declaration, to take every responsibility back upon himself; but at bottom the doubt remained, and the bitterness found, from time to time, the means to burst forth impetuously — always overcome thereafter, and with equal impetuosity, by feelings more consonant with his greatness.

A pendulum that bewildered us most grievously!

Even today, I could not interpret with any certainty certain of his denials, certain of his disavowals. These might have been inspired as readily by a transient determination to sever himself from us and stand alone, as by an ardent desire to open for us a path to safety, to offer us the means of softening the rigour of the fate about to strike us.

Here, to avoid misunderstanding, I must insist upon one point: the insinuations of the Police were undoubtedly intended to irritate Orsini, in order to wring from him a full confession. Orsini had his faults, but he was not a coward. He did indeed acknowledge everything that the Police showed they knew with certainty; but he took good care not to disclose particulars that were unknown to them, unless those particulars concerned himself alone.

I will say this further: no human soul could be polished and perfect on every facet of its prism. To constitute greatness, one great side sometimes suffices. Felice Orsini had more than one.

It is easy, for example, to display courage in the light of day, upon a field of battle. So easy, that I have known men transformed into heroes by a single spent bullet, who were incapable of standing without trembling before a snarling cur. The disciplined multitude carries along even the most timid and feeble individual — to keep his legs from giving way beneath him, he has, or imagines he has, the eyes of his fellow citizens fixed upon him in their thousands. But Felice Orsini, alone in his room, spreading mercury fulminate before the fireplace and drying it over the flame; with a watch in one hand and a thermometer in the other, watching for the spark that might reduce him to fragments, without leaving of his work one atom to earn him the applause of men — nothing, not even the memory of generous intentions — Felice Orsini, I say, gave proof of a courage whose temper eclipses any noisy and belligerent heroism.

There is furthermore a trait which has escaped, so far as I am aware, all who have written of him hitherto, and which reveals a courage yet more refined and sublime. The seizure of his correspondence had, among other things, revealed him as the centre from which radiated the entire clandestine operation; the point towards which, in turn, converged the concealed hatreds, the subterranean forces working to undermine the Empire. To suppress Napoleon for the mere pleasure of suppressing him would have been the work of madmen. The French political police had well understood that the blow, by its diligent preparation, must be intended to impart an impulse to broad forces, ready to reap its fruit. To lay hold of secrets which it sensed to be of vital importance was a matter to which it attached careers, wealth, power, glory — everything.

Felice Orsini found himself, therefore, in a position not of submission but of power. A single thread of the vast web, secretly surrendered, would have led him to safety, to opulence perhaps. He would have looked again upon the white crests of the mountains, the green fragrant plains, the incomparable sky of Italy; he would have conspired and fought for her still; he might have cherished the hope of witnessing her third rebirth. Nor was his heart deaf to more modest affections, no less intense — he was a father; on the banks of the Thames, a woman hoped and trembled for him. But between that tragic present hour and that rejoicing lay the blood of the generous-hearted, lay the tears of orphans. He chose instead to set forth, pale and silent, towards the scaffold. An austere greatness, worthy of an ancient Roman!

The defence of Felice Orsini arose spontaneously from the circumstances, and Giulio Favre was not the manner of intellect to ruin, through pusillanimous scruple, the splendid material those circumstances offered him. He laid upon it the powerful hand of the artist who knows the mastery of effects; of the man of heart who makes his own the trembling of a glorious and unhappy People.

His masterstroke was the letter addressed by Felice Orsini to Napoleon III. Without revealing anything, without compromising anyone, it permitted him to flash before the Judges, the jurors, and the civilised world the loftiness of our purpose. It flatters the lion who is exercising his claws in our flesh; but amid the blandishments there is prudently inserted the muffled threat. A single phrase honestly expressed suffices to pierce a wicked heart. In the silent hours, in the hours of remorse, it will reproach the broken oaths; it will exude the undying wrath of a race righteously vengeful, which was his own and which he has betrayed. The impetus of noble passions raised Giulio Favre above petty legal manoeuvres and mean things. Moving among great matters, he was able to accomplish a great defence. He did not snatch a man from the executioner, but he did something better — he restored his character. He transmitted to posterity a spirit, already so virile in itself, transfigured, as it were, by strokes of captivating beauty.


### Chapter Decimosettimo

<!-- pages:190-195 -->

THE twenty-fifth of February arrived — the day appointed for the proceedings of the trial. I was brought to the Palais de Justice and for the first time, since the evening of the attempt, I saw my companions in misfortune fully and plainly.

The assembly-hall of the Tribunal was packed with men and ladies. There was a great display of fine apparel... people flocked to the trial as to the theatre. Yet, through the perception of I know not what mysterious sense, I understood at once that the manifold soul of that audience was not opposed to us. Hostile, by contrast — deliberately hostile — were the individuals seated upon the bench, and the handful of hired servants who surrounded them. These men perhaps imagined themselves to be the Empire and its might, and regarded our attempt as one regards a personal affront.

The trial was, at its core, the repetition of the preliminary examination... but revised and corrected. The right of defence exists for the virtuous and exists for the wicked, and I should be a fool were I to reproach the French judicial authorities for not having composed a panegyric upon the accused; but equally foolish would he show himself who, blinded by the prestige inherent in social position and with the instincts of a fetish-worshipper, took the opinions expressed by those authorities as a measure by which to judge us.

From the earliest unfolding of History down to our own days, privilege has been obliged, in order to persist, to fortify oppression with artifice. Oppression is, by its very nature, intermittent and does not reach everywhere. Its deficiency is repaired by a discipline refined across the centuries, which manifests itself in various ways according to the times and the customs, but which aims constantly at portraying the heads of a State or of a religion — at least while they live — as very arks of ingenuity, as very arks of sanctity; which aims, at the same time, at portraying the recalcitrant who refuse to enter the orbit of those supreme ones, those saints, as fanatics, as degenerate and wicked creatures.

This discipline creates the larvæ devoid of substance, yet rosy and phosphorescent, which draw the multitudes along tortuous ways; it creates the fearsome phantoms that drive them back, with wild terrors, from the direct ways. It was this discipline that rendered the Grand Lama inaccessible, lest the eye that sees, analyses and judges, should discern, in his flesh and in his spirit, the defects inseparable from human nature. It induced the Sultans to have themselves preceded and followed by a troop of assassins whirling the scimitar. The audacious who dared raise inquisitive eyes to the demigod provoked their own swift death.

This discipline has taught, on the other hand, that the rancour against rebellious souls must continue even after the punishment. To sever the heads that would not bow is not enough; they must be exposed, bloodied, to the mockery of the unthinking. To burn the thinker at the stake is not enough; a fierce malediction must be pronounced over his ashes — they must be scattered to the winds!

Austria would have been ingenuous had she contented herself with hanging Italian patriots. She buried them without funeral rites and without a bier, in out-of-the-way pits, as was the practice with the carcasses of quadrupeds.

It is this baleful discipline, modified by the times, that brands and persecutes as crime the honest expression of an honest thought; and where certain extremes are no longer possible, the livid war against reputation and memory is ever in fashion. Which war, if it proves useful to privilege more or less legally constituted, becomes an indispensable necessity for the privilege that has attained such standing by violence and by crime.

At my age one records facts; one does not vent rancours. In the trial constructed against the authors of the attempt of the 14th of January, the denigration mounted against us was all the more necessary inasmuch as the very origin of the Empire was, more or less openly, in question. We sat upon the bench of the accused before Napoleon; but Napoleon sat upon the bench of the accused before France and the civilised world. Every nobility of spirit in him, every loftiness of purpose, became an aggravating circumstance for us; every loftiness of purpose in us tended to diminish him — it was a condemnation upon him.

Nor should it pass unmentioned that violent action sometimes provokes incidents which demonstrate it more efficacious than any other manner of propaganda. To denigrate us, to have us passed off as malefactors of the basest sort, to lessen the likelihood of finding imitators — this, therefore, had to be the unconfessed yet essential aim of the trial. I acknowledge that, from their point of view, the French judicial authorities acted wisely in attempting to decapitate, together with our bodies, our reputations. I acknowledge, at the same time, that they plunged into the enterprise with all the sinister dexterity of the servants of the Second Empire.

The bill of indictment, formulated and read by the Imperial Procurator Chaix d'Est-Ange, glided deftly over the initial uncertainties and the police machinations; it portrays the examining authorities in the attitudes of genius that perceives, probes and vanquishes. It suppresses, by contrast, every impulse of ours that might reveal a character above the vulgar; and, dwelling upon the most easily censurable aspects and giving emphasis to our weaknesses, it loads us down with commentaries — often absolutely fantastic — that savour of cowardice.

The President of the Tribunal, Monsieur Delangle, for his part, laboured with viperous persistence to lend the Procurator's semi-literary creation its proper embellishments, dissembling its improbabilities by introducing incidents which, even if true, would have had no connection whatsoever with our conspiracy. Worse still, he cast "compassionately" over the threadbare garment of disinterestedness the ample cloak of the hired assassin; he branded with the mark of the charlatan a virtue in its death-throes.

An easy task, withal! We have lived in times of intense passions — times in which the attractions and repulsions of souls alternated with the violence of the tempest. What man, having taken part in them, to whatever degree, has not found himself treated, to his own astonishment, now worse than the devil, now better than a saint; the object, often simultaneously, of profound veneration and immeasurable hatred! One can well understand that the Police should have been in no want of mud to pour forth.

Thus upon Giuseppe Pieri the President Delangle poured, in Paris in 1858, the hatred of the Tuscan reactionaries which had pursued him since 1830 — the hatred that, portraying him as a beast of liberalism, had succeeded in having him stripped of his colonelcy; the hatred that had seized upon a prank among bohemians, in which a group of young scapegraces had relieved another scapegrace of a watch worth a few *baiocchi*, as a pretext to defame him.

The generosity of spirit, the haughtiness, the disdainful impulses of Felice Orsini were represented as a "ridiculous ostentation." And when Orsini persisted in maintaining that the third bomb had not been thrown by him but by the Italian to whom he had handed it but a few minutes before the attempt, Delangle demonstrated his serene impartiality in the search for truth by bursting out and portraying him as a liar seeking to make one believe that his hands were clean of the blood of the children, the women, the old men wounded and killed; "for the odour of that blood rose to him and mounted to his brain."

To complete his meritorious work in the service of lofty and moral principles, President Delangle then lent a complaisant ear to the sacristy whisper; and Felice Orsini — Commissioner of the Roman Republic in a remote province, who, surrounded by foreign enemies, weakened by enemies within, maintained his phalanx by means of requisitions — was depicted to the jurors as a common thief who had then escaped, by flight, the delights of the papal penitentiary.

As for what concerned me personally, Delangle had not the shadow of a pretext for reproaching me with any irregular family connection, nor with any real or apparent misappropriation; but this was not to render me immune to his venom. I had revealed how and why, on the evening of the 14th of January, Felice Orsini had handed us the sum of 200 francs. My revelation aimed at making our Judges understand that venality was not in our hearts. But I was compelled to realise that I had not reckoned with the refined malice of my Judges. That meagre drop, in such a moment of crisis, was sufficient for the upright Magistrate, striking the pose of indignant surprise, to accuse me — I who belonged to an honourable family — of having abandoned school and taken a loathing to work until I had so degraded myself as to become, step by step, a mercenary assassin for the sum of 200 francs!

A word or two upon the official trial reports. The tactic of denigration could not arrest itself at the margin of its papers, as the storied progenitors of our race at the threshold of paradise lost. They were retouched and mutilated and distorted, like all the rest, at the arbitrary pleasure of His Excellency. One example: when President Delangle asked whether we had anything to submit on the imminent application of the sentence, Gomez and Orsini replied: "Nothing!" Pieri observed that he had shed no blood. I pointed out Kim, the municipal street-sweeper who, filing past with the other witnesses, had given his false deposition. I denounced him aloud, protesting that in pronouncing the sentence, justice demanded that no account be taken of a perjured deposition.

The official report states that, contrary to my three companions, unanimous in pronouncing their "nothing," I invoked "the clemency of my judges."

The meeting between Francesco Crispi and Orsini? I should be lying were I to say I did not deny it. But one must remember that Orsini himself had, a few seconds before, declared that the Italian with the third bomb was not known to any of his accomplices, not even to Pieri; that he had revealed his existence to no one; that none of us had suspected him.
