Was this not an almost open suggestion to me of my line of conduct? And was there not, moreover, in my denial, the most expeditious means of avoiding insidious questioning and machinations that might have compromised a man who remained unsuspected and at liberty?

Had the charlatans of the Empire not felt within their souls the terror of the truth, they would not have imposed upon the newspapers the obligation to adhere strictly, in their reports, to what was published in *Le Droit* and the *Gazzetta dei Tribunali*. A half-truth is no longer truth, and the reason of State that does not scruple to adulterate it by paring it down cannot restrain itself out of scruples when it is convenient to deck it out like a harlot, or to line its skirts with some tendentious little falsehood.

Let him believe blindly in the official reports who swears upon the infallible sanctity of the powerful, with that simplicity of soul which induces others to believe in the infallibility of the hypocrite into whose ear a dove might coo the truth. Infinite and manifold is human folly!

But the discerning man who does not let any broth drain through his intellect without first filtering it will certainly not fail to reflect that we find ourselves, if nothing else, under the unfavourable light of those whose history has been written by their own enemies, in times and circumstances which made those enemies believe any refutation on our part impossible.

The sentence was pronounced on the night of the 26th of January; and, as if to heighten the theatricality of that denouement, when the Court, having concluded its deliberations, re-entered the great hall of the audience-chamber, the clock of the Palais de Justice was striking its chimes.

For Gomez, imprisonment for life; for Pieri, for me, for Felice Orsini, the penalty reserved for parricides. Luigi Napoleone had become, after having cannonaded it, the father of his people — History is full of such bloody ironies! And lest any doubt remain to us as to the nature of that penalty, its full description was read aloud: "led to the scaffold in a shirt, barefoot, the head covered by a black veil; then the reading of the sentence; then the decapitation."

I looked full in the face of my companions in faith and in misfortune. Gomez, exceedingly pale, maintained a marble immobility — the grief of the condemnation and the entirely natural satisfaction of having escaped the worst seemed to cancel each other out within his heart. Pieri was shaking his head and kept his eye fixed upon the President, with an indefinable mingling of scorn and commiseration. I noticed that his lips were moving, and received the impression that he was pressing home, at that moment, a certain phrase he had addressed to Delangle himself during the interrogation: "I knew well enough that we should never come to any good end with that pigheaded man!"

Felice Orsini was slowly turning his eyes from one judge to another. He seemed to say, without the shadow of bravado: "Your pettiness does not touch me!"

As for my own state of mind... what am I to say? I had long foreseen the condemnation; now that the foresight had been borne out, I struggled to be fully conscious of it, to grasp its full import. It was to assure myself that I was indeed awake that I had looked my companions in the face. All at once there rose vividly before my mind the image of Elisa — not radiant and smiling, but as I had kissed her that last time, fallen back upon her chair, her eyes extinguished, insensible, as though the tribunal of the Seine had pronounced her sentence and not mine.

That image remained so fixed in my mind as to allow me to see or think of nothing else. As I traversed the underground passage that led us back to the Conciergerie I roused myself, and, turning to Orsini, who followed a few paces behind me, I said: "You who have means — remember them!" He understood, and murmured: "I promise you!" The door of the prison had barely closed behind me when I felt a strange sensation, as of being plunged into a quiet that was almost familiar. Saturno and the other guard already knew everything... they had lost their wager; they had made ready the champagne. But they wanted the chance of a return match. Should the sentence not be quashed by the Supreme Court or by a decree of pardon, they would pay another bottle; otherwise I should pay. Never did a wagerer so ardently desire to lose!

Thus, by a caprice of fate, chatting merrily and raising our glasses, I passed the night on which I had been condemned to the guillotine. I have wished many times over that I might know how my Judges fared in the end — Napoleon III, the street-sweeper Kim, and the others who had contributed to saving the social order and the usurped Empire... but my wish has remained always unsatisfied.


### Chapter Decimottavo

<!-- pages:195-200 -->

THE following day, at La Roquette. They made us put on the grey uniform of prisoners; they fitted us with the straitjacket... not from any lust for torture, but to forestall attempts at suicide.

We had been conveyed to the new prison separately and confined separately; yet our cells were so situated that we could see and speak to one another. A rectangular chamber, rather spacious, bare... four white walls, four windows, four doors. Whoever enters by the door communicating with the courtyard perceives, directly opposite, the door and window of Felice Orsini's cell; Pieri's cell lay beyond the left-hand wall; beyond the right-hand wall, my own.

This constituted, for us, a great advantage over the Conciergerie; death was in prospect, yet it was preceded by some measure of relief.

Another circumstance contributed to render the present sad hour more bearable to me. My guards had followed me to La Roquette. They would sleep in turns in order to keep me under continuous surveillance; but this did not trouble me in the least, for I had no intention whatsoever of anticipating my end by self-destruction.

On the very day of our removal, our lawyers came to counsel us to lodge an appeal. Nothing concrete was hoped for; nevertheless, both I and Pieri consented without raising any objection. But Orsini would hear nothing of it. He maintained that death would by now relieve him of a great burden, and that it would come the more welcome the sooner it arrived. Giulio Favre, to obtain his consent, was obliged almost to force his hand.

The days of agony began and passed swiftly. In a state of mind only too explicable, the memories of those days flash through my mind in sudden leaps, standing out like disconnected images against the sombre background of those hours in which, meditating, I prepared myself to die.

The chaplain of La Roquette, Monsieur Hughon, paid us a visit at least once every twenty-four hours. He was a former captain of dragoons and had retained the colouring of that life; thickset and robust, in spite of his years; liberal in spite of his position. From his very first appearance I told him plainly that he would always be welcome on one condition: never to speak to me of religious subjects. He promised, and kept his word.

Giuseppe Pieri, on the other hand, willingly acknowledged his right to exercise his ministry, but not to undergo it passively. From the very first evening he provoked him to disputation with an air of open defiance, and allowed himself in subsequent days to be carried away by a demolishing spirit, by a manner of arguing *ad hominem* that was in the extreme degree sharp and almost offensive. Constrained by the pitiable circumstances, the ex-captain was obliged to put a good face upon more than one lance-thrust full in the ribs.

The voice of Pieri was, moreover, the predominant note in that confined and austere place. And how indeed could he, already so loquacious even in the most colourless circumstances of life, have brought himself to silence, now that powerful emotions exalted him?

He talked and argued about everything with obstinate persistence... at times even with an abundant vein of comedy, especially when it fell to him to confute the extravagances of our judges. But turn it as he might, he returned by preference to his political and philosophical ideas; as though a vortex drew him inexorably into the narrow spout of that funnel.

A strange contrast with the bearing of our leader, from whose cell there issued, for hours on end, not the smallest sign of life; so that more than once I had asked myself whether it were not the tomb of Felice Orsini; whether Felice Orsini had not become the tomb of his own soul.

One hour each day we were permitted to walk in the great courtyard; but not simultaneously. Pieri was taken out for his breath of air alone; upon his return, Orsini and I went out together. We wore our straitjackets even while walking, and were watched not merely by sight but at musket-point.

During that brief hour, Orsini would expand his lungs like a man reborn; but the gaze he turned upon the blue sky was full of melancholy. He walked at a rapid pace and I, reared in military discipline, followed him, instinctively keeping slightly apart. This would occur: if I was in ill humour and did not speak, he would turn his head and question me with a look, as though to say: “A storm brewing?” But if I spoke, he would stride on as though absorbed in the vision of other worlds, or, if he answered, the words came from the depths, drawn out as with a corkscrew.

Those brief words sufficed to reveal to me the man who was undergoing rapidly a radical transformation.

Felice Orsini had brought into prison two resentments; one against Pieri and one against Giuseppe Mazzini. It is well known that in his *Memorie* the impetuous Romagnol had portrayed Mazzini as a modern Mahomet who presumed to model the destinies of Europe upon the caprices of his odalisques — a circumstance of which the monarchist Cavour made admirable use. He distributed those *Memorie* in thousands of copies, thus cunningly throwing a bridge which afforded the opportunity to cross the Rubicon, to furl the banner bearing the motto *“Dio e Popolo”* to all too many Mazzinians, either wavering from weakness of fibre, or eager to justify with any pretext their base and mercenary calculations.

But the old resentment against the Master dissolved on the day when the guards and Giulio Favre informed Orsini of the noble defence of him made by the “Prophet” on the morrow of the attempt, at a time when other patriots, fastidious regarding the means of redeeming the Fatherland, had well-nigh branded him as a common assassin.

To this transformation, which I might call regenerative, another and vaster one of quite a different order corresponded. Better to face the subject at once. Felice Orsini was the only one of the three *morituri* who listened to the exhortations of the chaplain-dragoon more than passively, with a complaisant ear. And our daily walks insinuated in me a presentiment of what afterwards came to pass. The convictions slowly acquired through a life of struggle were, in the man, gradually disintegrating, and allowed to reappear, nay to predominate, the underlying strata saturated with the fables absorbed in unknowing childhood. In the imminence of his end, he returned to those religious practices to which only the blind multitude should still attribute essential virtue.

He therefore deceives himself, and unwittingly belies the truth, who, admiring the stoic fortitude with which Orsini mounted the scaffold, would make of him a model of consistency to be set against the figure of Silvio Pellico and others, softened, as it were, by their sufferings. But he would be equally mistaken who should presume to safeguard his greatness by passing over his conversion in silence. Silence attenuates nothing and cancels nothing.

One may well say in his defence that he succumbed to a cause that was anything but ignoble. Capable of leonine daring under a fleeting impulse, he was powerless, by reason of his supremely sensitive nature, against a thought that was tormenting, insistent, obstinate, fixed... not the thought of the death that hung over him, but that other, springing from the consciousness of his own responsibility in the ruin of high ideals; from the vision of the tragic death of others. His hair had rapidly turned white with it, and more than one delicate fibre of his brain must have been shaken and, as it were, scarred by it.

Felice Orsini, moreover, never posed before his contemporaries as a discoverer of philosophical or moral truths. A patriot he called himself instead, and was so from earliest youth; a patriot he was and proclaimed himself until his head lay beneath the blade of the guillotine. He had pledged to Italy his every energy and the last drop of his own blood. Italy has had greater sons; she has had none more unconditionally faithful. I say that there is no need to glean in other fields in order to establish the glory of Felice Orsini!
Beyond the chaplain-dragoon, there appeared almost daily at La Roquette a very powerful personage, the Prefect of Police, Pietri. He treated us in a most affable manner; nor did he ever fail to exert himself to instil into our minds some ray of hope... now affirming that the Court of Appeal would in all probability annul our trial; now assuring us that the Empress and a powerful court party showed themselves so openly disposed to clemency that Napoleon's mind would end by being influenced and overcome thereby.

But neither his manner nor his intelligent countenance availed to dispel the instinctive distrust, to bridge the abyss that separates the conspirator from a man who must, by force of circumstance, hunt him down and derive his own satisfaction from his ruin. This distrust was in me strengthened by other circumstances. On several occasions I had sent to Elisa letters of the most passionate character. When I wished to write, the guard on duty would free my right hand more or less; then, having bound it again, would carry the letter to the prison Director to have his endorsement placed upon it. But my ardent appeals received no reply... absolute silence, as though Elisa had utterly abandoned me, or were dead. It is easy to imagine my torment. Then, all at once, there entered my heart the suspicion that it was a matter of refined cruelty on the part of the Police, in pursuit of ends I could not clearly comprehend but whose existence I divined. From that moment my torment ceased... to give place to a sullen rancour against those who had inflicted it upon me.

Moreover, Pietri had the fault of bringing to the prison, almost without exception, one personage or another. A morbid curiosity appeared to drive the upper classes to verify, who knows, perhaps whether those who had dared to provoke such disorder were indeed composed of flesh and bone. To me, that being put on show, like a wild beast in a cage, weighed terribly.

One day I found in my cell, with Pietri, a high-ranking military man who had his head bandaged. I do not clearly remember whether it was General Macan or General Roguet. Pietri introduced him to me simply as “one of your victims”; which caused my ill humour to break forth. I retorted sharply that I had sought to strike no one save the man who had been the ruin of my Country. And as for being a victim — I would most willingly have exchanged places with that wounded man.

At this the officer stepped forward and, extending his right hand, declared that he bore not the least grudge, either against me or against any other. And I clasped, as best I was able, that hand; but I made no reply and, turning to the Prefect, asked him what interest the Police could have in intercepting my wife's letters.

He appeared surprised; but contented himself with a shrug of the shoulders.

The following day he returned alone, and before entering the cell stood for some seconds upright upon the threshold, filling the doorway with his fine and tall person. I thought I perceived in that countenance something unwonted, something inquisitive. He entered and, after repeating in a manner more affable and, I was about to say, more paternal than usual, the customary phrases, rubbed his brow and, lowering his voice somewhat — the guard had, moreover, withdrawn at his appearance — spoke to me with intensity of his deep sympathy for my youth. And, lamenting the sad fate that lay in store for me, partly through force of circumstances and partly also — he said — through the supreme recklessness of my companions, he assured me that he had pondered long to find a means of saving me, and not me alone! I already knew how at Court there was no reluctance to adopt measures tempered with clemency. But I was unaware — he added — that His Majesty the Emperor was himself animated by the most lively sympathies for Italy, whose greatness he admired and whose misfortunes profoundly moved him. Once set upon such a course, a man of that stamp — who could predict where he would halt? Certainly no sovereign in Europe was in a position to render greater service to the redemption of Italy than Napoleon. So that if I viewed the matter with the eye of an enlightened patriot, I would not be slow to understand that I could serve myself and my Country better by aiding, and so to speak allying myself with, the man I had attempted to kill.

I believe my face expressed my profound astonishment in full, for Pietri laid a paternal hand upon my shoulder and, lowering his voice still further, said:

“I know that I am dealing with an intelligent young man and I therefore expect not to be considered a boor. Between His Majesty and his high designs stand the internal enemies of the Empire. Too weak to act openly, they have secretly allied themselves with the external enemies and have conspired. I have not come to propose anything dishonourable to you. The safety and welfare of States have their ineluctable demands. Aid the eminent personage upon whom the triumph of the Italian cause may depend; shield him against his internal enemies, and I give you my word of honour that you will not be long in rejoining your young wife. By what strange aberration would you leave her poor, abandoned, and alone to struggle in the world and against the world?”

Now the true reason for Elisa's pretended silence appeared to me not merely hinted at but manifest; now my indignation had twofold cause to break forth. “I know nothing,” I replied, “and have nothing to reveal. But even if I had... you yourself, if I were to accept your proposals, would be the first to feel horror for me. Nor should I have expected such proposals from you, a Corsican... Italians abhor traitors!”

My natural vivacity urged me to gesticulate, and since I could not, I trembled. But without intending it, I had touched a sensitive chord in a heart not entirely stripped of noble feeling.

I saw that man, hardened as he was to so many emotions, cover his face with his hands. A few seconds passed, and he looked me in the face... his eyes were red with tears. He departed, promising that he would do everything in his power to save our heads. Should he fail in his purpose, he would tender his own resignation.
I have no reason whatsoever to doubt the sincerity of that man; but what he actually did, or attempted to do, I cannot say. This much is certain: on the eleventh of March he came to La Roquette and communicated to Felice Orsini, if nothing else, his hopes. It was shortly before the hour appointed for our walk. The conversation between the two was still at its height when that hour struck and the guards led me into the courtyard, alone. On my return, Felice Orsini, always so taciturn and, one might almost say, sombre, appeared to me suddenly transformed and, I was about to say, cheerful. The moment he saw me he said in a loud voice: “Di Rudio, take heart. We shall not be executed!”

This occurred, I repeat, on the morning of the eleventh of March. That same evening, the Court of Cassation rejected our appeal. If Orsini's affirmation was destined to find its correspondence in reality, the events of the twelfth of March would have demonstrated it. The day passed and our expectations were disappointed. That very night of the twelfth, by torchlight, work was proceeding in the square of La Roquette to erect the scaffold.

The explanation of the enigma?

I received it later from the lips of Mathieu de la Drôme. Public opinion was exerting a powerful pressure in our favour. In defiance of the Police, in the popular meeting-places a stirring song was being sung in praise of “the heroes of the fourteenth of January.” The Empress and a powerful court party had declared themselves for clemency. Napoleon III called their demonstrations indecent; yet he lacked the heart to oppose them openly. To eliminate the enemies who had fallen into his power, to strike terror among those who had escaped him... such was the secret yearning of the liberticide; but it had to be done while affecting to have had his hand forced, as though from scrupulous respect for the Law, shifting the responsibility onto others.

The Council of State was convened to decide upon the greater or lesser expediency of the imperial pardon, and the Emperor made a brief appearance before it. “The Councillors of the Crown,” he said, “were free to vote according to their own conscience; but he could not refrain from expressing one conviction of his own. Within that Council, traitors lay hidden.”

It was the sombre threat. The Councillors were assembled not to give way to the tide of pity, but to render a service to their master. Could the dignitaries of an Empire whose very essence was sharp dealing and corruption — and which in such a vortex would have swept all France along with it — hesitate between their lucrative sinecures and the lives of a few outcasts, rendered incapable of reaction and of doing harm? And had not the reason of State dissimulated far other acts of cowardice?

As if this were not enough, the Archbishop of Paris, scenting the situation, deadened the mild influence of Eugenia Montijo by interposing between her and the Emperor the measured, subtle exhortations of the Church. Never had the hatreds and interests of clericalism and of the State shown themselves so closely akin... the conspirators smelled of Freemasonry; they had fought body and soul to put an end to that temporal power which is the ill-concealed and supreme goal of every religious imposture; they, were it in their power, would have renewed the struggle. It was well worth suppressing them, even if in doing so one disowned Christ yet again.

The Council of State and the clergy... Napoleon III obtained from them more than he had hoped, and his mind, which knew how to sound characters in spite of their masks, must have exulted darkly, and perhaps, like the despot of tragedy, was saying within himself: “Oh, how many are the consciences that are sold!”


### Chapter Nineteen

<!-- pages:200-206 -->

<!-- pages:200-206 -->



ON the evening of the twelfth, the chaplain Hughon came to pay me his customary visit. He was grave, grave, grave. I spoke to him of our rejected appeal, and he became embarrassed. Then the suspicion of the grim truth flashed upon me, and I resorted to several subterfuges to confirm it. But Hughon promptly assumed the air of the ingenuous, and sheltering behind that simple artifice, became unassailable. Perhaps every suspicion of mine might have dissolved, had my visitor not extended his hand as he took his leave. Those fingers seemed to me to trace mysterious signs in the air, so visibly did they tremble.

I awaited the return of Saturno who, of the two guards, seemed to me the more candid, and without even giving him time to remove his cap: "Saturno," I said, "I know that tomorrow morning I shall be led to the scaffold. Heed me and see to it, you who are good, that my last wishes are carried out to the letter. At five o'clock you will wake me..."

Here he began to protest. Why had I put such nonsense into my head? The appeal being rejected, there remained still, thanks to our connections, a great deal of fighting to be done, and before anything... eh, eh!... it would take much more than that!

"My friend, Monsieur Hughon intended to hear my confession and instead... I have confessed him. He has revealed everything to me!"

At this, Saturno, who was still gesticulating to give emphasis to that *eh, eh!* of his, let his arm fall as though I had snapped it off. "He revealed everything to you? That is against the Regulations and he ought not to have done it." He stood for a moment with eyes downcast, then murmured: "Since you know everything... well, then — yes! there is nothing left for you now but to die manfully, as you have lived."

Then, all at once, a great silence fell within my soul, and with it a kind of great darkness; but in that emptiness, life still manifested itself powerfully through an instinct that drove me to crave something yet darker, yet more silent. *"Tanto meglio quanto più presto!"* — "The sooner the better!" — is Orsini's expression; only then did I understand its full meaning.

"At five precisely!... I have a great deal, a very great deal to write. My last thoughts, from the prison, to my kinsmen and to Elisa; my last thoughts, from the scaffold, to the Patria... And you?... Take this! (I kissed him.) Tomorrow, at this hour, think of me. Say to yourself: Human miseries touch him no more!"

Saturno was punctual. My first sensation on that memorable day was caused by the extreme pallor of his face. I wrote to my father; I wrote to my wife's kinsmen, commending that unhappy woman to them from the depths of my heart. Six o'clock struck at the Roquette.

Shortly after, ten or twelve persons entered the large room adjoining. Among them I recognised the chaplain Hughon and the Governor of the prison. They made their way to Orsini's cell, and I heard them announce to him his final hour. The Governor of the prison then pronounced, in a low voice, a few phrases, the meaning of which was plain to me from Orsini's reply. A magistrate stood ready to receive the dying man's disclosures, and he had been urged to make a full confession. The reply of the martyr rang through the sepulchral silence, subdued yet distinct: "Felice Orsini knows how to die; he does not know how to be an informer!"

Then came Pieri's turn, and his nerves gave way. "He was old, he was bound; but he would have had spirit enough..."

The Governor of the prison admonished him courteously, and he recovered himself at once. "Be it as it may," he said, "to die now, to die in some few years — what does it matter? My executioners too shall die..." He added that he intended to plunge into eternity singing.

My turn came. I replied calmly that the Count Carlo di Rudio was ready — an expression which, in the official reports, was afterwards attributed to Orsini. As my lips closed, I made out more clearly, in the dim light, the man who had accompanied the Governor of the prisons into my cell. He was an elderly abbé... A fine soul, perhaps. But to me, so ill-disposed toward everything that recalls the tonsure, he had the effect of an enemy come to ascertain, *de visu*, my final features. And since death was near and certain, I could well, without fear of giving harm to any living soul, give free rein to my feelings. He drew close to me and made to speak. I imagined he wished to exhort me to die like a Christian, and I forestalled him: "I prefer," I said, "to consecrate my last thoughts to my wife and my little child. As for your God, I shall see Him, if He exists, but only to tell Him from what manner of ministers He has the simplicity to let Himself be represented upon this earth."

We were conducted into the large room. Our hands were unbound. Grave, and belonging to people who were tragically moved, the faces... the rest, almost like a scene of friends who bid farewell to friends, on the eve of a long journey.

Pieri, firmly resolved to treat the matter as an ordinary event, would not renounce his breakfast. The appropriate orders were given. While waiting, a round of introductions even took place. In this way I learned that Hughon was to be the spiritual comforter of Orsini; the chaplain of the Conciergerie, that of Pieri; the abbé fastened to my side was the chaplain of the House of Correction.

An elegant *cabaret* — a tray with coffee and rum — was brought. Felice Orsini sipped from the latter, toasting the health of the Governor of the prisons. At this point, my "comforter," humanely overlooking my rough rebuff, offered me a small glass; but he made the mistake of observing that it would give me courage. I refused out of pride, retorting that Italian patriots had no need of artificial courage. Then there was a rivalry in asking one another for small keepsakes. The three executioners, upright and silent along the wall, watched us with a strange persistence.

Suddenly the Governor drew out his watch and made a sign to the guards. The Regulations, heedless of conscience, required us to attend Mass. Felice Orsini moved sadly toward the prison chapel. I protested, and Pieri added to my curt refusal his own impetuous torrent of words. Those present glanced at one another; the Governor shrugged his shoulders and in the end consented to compromise. Orsini's absence was very brief. No sooner had he returned than they led us toward the Chancellery, whence we passed into an adjoining room. On entering, Pieri, who preceded me by a few paces, turned round and said to Orsini: "Well then, my old friend?" Orsini raised his head, looked at him steadily and replied: "Calm, calm!" Those were the first words those two men, united by their ideals and their martyrdom, had exchanged since their arrest... and they were the last!

We were in the hands of the executioner. They shaved my nape; they made me put on a low-cut shirt; they removed my shoes with their own hands. The same preparations, carried out rapidly, left Orsini absorbed in his thoughts; but the voluble man from Lucca grew almost cheerful from it all... the strangeness of nerves! He wished to see himself in a mirror, and his executioner — something of a dandy, most likely, despite his foul trade — drew a small looking-glass from his waistcoat pocket and held it out to him. He examined himself at his leisure and, smiling, exclaimed: "You have got me up like an old *cocotte*!" While they were removing his shoes he broke into a frank laugh and said: "Lucky thing I washed my feet!"

As for me... why should I not say it? Those moments were extremely painful to me. For several years my life had been a succession of events in vivid colours; but every trace of them seemed suddenly effaced by the memories of childhood that assailed me in a throng. And I saw again my poor mother, all tenderness and all smiles, as on a feast-day when she dressed me and, gazing at me, took her pride in it. What a contrast!

But the Count di Rudio was not to be seen, at such a moment, with tearful eyes, and if a tear or two wished to spring forth, his pride took care to drive them back into the depths and dry them there.

While they were wrapping my head in the black veil, Giuseppe Pieri was already walking out without a word. But I reached him in time to hear him utter that phrase which, slightly distorted, afterwards served almost as a pretext for demonstrating his grandiose and eccentric mind. To me it seemed, and still seems, the virile expression of a patriot who, having fulfilled his mission to the utmost limit of his own powers, feels himself lightened of every perishable and vulgar part of himself, and almost sublimated by martyrdom. The chaplain of the Conciergerie had taken his arm, as though there were need to support him. He, without even turning his head: "One does not feel fear when one goes to Calvary!" And he moved away between the two foul props of every tyranny, the priest and the executioner... and I saw him no more. But I heard his voice distinctly strike up, in the courtyard, the hymn of the Girondins: *"Mourir pour la Patrie"* — "To Die for the Fatherland." He went to the scaffold, as he had promised, singing!... it rains.

I am in the courtyard... it rains and snows. Weather that reconciles me to the thought of the imminent warm shelter underground. I pass alongside a company of prison guards drawn up in line, presenting arms... the rifles have their muzzles pointed to the ground, their stocks in the air. The great gate stood wide open; the scaffold, erected a few paces from the pavement, cut off my view; but not so much that I could not make out on the square a considerable body of foot-soldiers, of light cavalry, of artillery, drawn up as if in the imminence of action.

My hands were bound behind my back and fastened, moreover, to the bonds that constrained my feet. I advanced in steps necessarily small and slow. The executioner, on my right, stood a full head taller than I; the old priest, on my left, must with his white hair have contrasted strangely with my dark, restless youth. He was murmuring prayers, his eyes upon a crucifix. I had expressed the desire to draw a few puffs of smoke, and this had been granted me. But the water and snow, which I trod with bare feet, which fell upon my bare shoulders, provoked shiverings that I strove in vain to suppress, and that apparent weakness, vexing me, drove me to bite savagely at the stem of my pipe.

Near the gate, the field of my vision widened, and I saw the windows and saw the rooftops thronged with people. The spectacle of ill-starred virtue, the Greeks declared, is worthy of the Gods, and to witness it, men had paid as much as a thousand francs for the hire of a balcony. The mood of that multitude? "It" — says the official report — "remained constantly calm and silent throughout the great and legitimate expiation." In truth, a robust voice from above cried out: "People, bare your heads! This is the execution of heroes!" A commotion broke out on the square. They were attempting, I believe, to surround the house from which that human protest had come. But just at that moment my attention was drawn elsewhere... the unexpected occurred!

A man on horseback had entered through the gate... he was dressed in civilian clothes, his chest covered with decorations. He dismounted from the saddle and guards and soldiers saluted him in military fashion. It was Monsieur de Collet, the Empress's major-domo. He came up to me, looked me full in the face, and noticing my trembling, said: "It is cold, Monsieur Rudio!" I replied: "Yes, but they will not leave me time to catch a chill." A puff of smoke struck him full in the face. "I beg your pardon; my hands are bound and besides... it is the last!" — "Oh, not at all; you will smoke again!" He murmured a few words in the ear of the executioner first, then of the old priest, and both, slowly and silently, withdrew. Then, turning once more to me, as I questioned him with the expression of my face: "I shall come to your cell towards nine o'clock and explain everything." I spun on my heels and found myself face to face with Felice Orsini.

He slowed his step: "What is this?"

— They are sending me back to prison. I know nothing more.

— And Pieri?

— He is up there!

— Better two than three!

We continued on our opposite ways... he to fecundate with his blood the ideal of the Patria; I toward the prison... and that return to life came upon me as something grievous as a violence, so fully was I prepared for death! After a few steps, I turned my head, and, by a singular correspondence of souls, Orsini turned his as well. "Farewell!" he said, with a nod of his head. The black veil that wrapped it, grown thick and opaque here and there in its folds, concealed his face, so that he already seemed to me half lost in the mysterious shadow of eternity...

I found the large room and my cell crowded with soldiers and officers. A group was busily gathered about my bed, upon which lay, in a dead faint and in convulsions, my faithful guard, Saturno. I made my way through to him, and while a compassionate hand unbound my arms, I kept calling him by name. My voice proved more efficacious than the sprinklings of water and the smelling-salts... He opened his eyes, looked at me as one lost in a dream, then his face brightened with a light that revealed a friendship profoundly sincere. All at once, he assailed me with these words: "The wager! The wager!... you have lost!... you shall pay the champagne!"

"Later, my friend. This is an hour for grief. Down there, men are dying!" Almost as though to confirm my words, the clock of the Roquette struck: seven strokes. In the prison a sepulchral silence fell. The tragic hour bestows strange clairvoyance... the officers uncovered their heads. At that moment, the blade of the guillotine... the heads... the vile basket...

The Count di Rudio, overcome by emotion, could not continue. The insufficiency of the heart, which was already undermining his robust old age, took away his breath. I knew that to observe him at such moments irritated him and worsened the ailment, and it was with a true sense of relief that I heard the quick step of Signora Elisa. She entered and did not perceive the gravity of the moment. She stretched out her arms, and her left hand was laid over her open right palm. She was smiling with her lips, smiling with her blue eyes, as though she carried a great happiness within her soul and wished to parcel it out and share it with us. She uncovered her palm and displayed... two chicks nestled there, of a luminous whiteness. Who remembers the white hen that was brooding in the courtyard? "They have hatched!" said the Signora, still smiling. Those two little atoms of life looked at us in wonder, as though asking: "Who are you?"

I turned my face away... the Californian sun, near its setting, peered through the green foliage. My sad eyes, intolerant of light, closed; but the reddish disc remained imprinted on my retina. O ardent imagination! O vividness of evocation! In that blood-red glare, the severed head of Felice Orsini appeared... Gripped by the hair in the executioner's fist, it gasped, breathless, that last, fateful wish of his: *"Viva l'Italia! Viva la Francia!"*

My lips did not move, but my soul gave a cry: "Oh, how dearly the Patria has cost us!"


### Chapter Twenty

<!-- pages:206-210 -->



AT precisely nine o’clock, Monsieur de Collet was in my cell. He did not dwell upon great particulars, but what he narrated to me was more than sufficient to put an end to my conjectures. Influential English personages had interceded on my behalf with his august Empress. It so happened that those very days fell upon the second birthday of the crown prince, and she, who was above all else a woman and a mother, had been pleased to solemnise the auspicious occasion by according a grace that would lift from her affliction a woman, a mother. My wife had already been advised by telegraph of my good fortune.

Monsieur de Collet lavished congratulations upon me at this point; then he turned to take his leave, but retracing his steps: “It was the Empress who sent me this morning. She wished to ensure that her will had not been violated. Nevertheless, it is well to bear in mind that much good, and much of stern justice, may proceed from her august consort. A phrase of gratitude directed toward him could certainly do you no harm. I say this of my own initiative; but I am persuaded that I interpret the thought of Her Majesty the Empress, to whom you are moreover indebted for your life.”

What was I to answer? For a couple of hours a profound reaction had been at work within me and, in place of that first, strange regret at the unexpected return to the sensations of the living, a desperate yearning toward an intense life, toward liberty, had already taken hold! Hope, violently expelled from the spirit, was returning in torrents, and no human force could have resisted it. I say that if the expression of feigned sentiments could contribute even the smallest straw toward plans devoid of any definite form, yet unquestionable in their tendency, which were already germinating in my brain, I ought not to hesitate to write one, ten, a hundred letters — to the Emperor, to the Pope, to the — I was entirely without defence in the hands of all-powerful enemies; any subterfuge, any falsehood became a legitimate art of war and of self-preservation. To slip from those claws, at whatever cost, in order to stand more proudly erect with hands unbound; to fight on — that was the problem!

I gave my promise!

I was brought once more to the Palais de Justice, before that President Delangle who had instilled so much of his venom into our trial. By one of those hypocrisies dear to a society that attaches more importance to appearances than to substance, it was deemed proper that I should appear free in the Tribunal, and my chains were removed. The portion of the trial proceedings that pertained to me was read aloud; thereafter the advocate-general Oscar de Valle requested the ratification of the imperial letters patent commuting my death sentence to perpetual imprisonment. These letters bore the date of the 12th of March. Why, then, had I been led to the very foot of the scaffold? Were the delays of bureaucracy to blame, or was it the deliberate intention to subject me to a moral pressure that might induce me to make revelations?

I must note that the official account of the execution dwells at length upon Pieri and Orsini — concerning myself, not a word! I believe they did not wish to offend the susceptibilities of a people by implicitly acknowledging that chivalrous France had abolished physical torture only to replace it with that of the intellect. The pardon having been ratified, President Delangle attempted to deliver a final blow in favour of the social order as he understood it, or pretended to understand it, by asking whether I was repentant “of my crime.” Upon the protest of my advocate, who feared some fierce reply, he did not insist, and contented himself with saying to the gendarmes: “Take him away!” My manacles were replaced. I was conveyed to the Conciergerie and from there, in a prison van, to La Roquette, where I was thrust into the courtyard among the other prisoners. One of them came toward me at once, offering me his right hand. It was Antonio Gomez.

What a look passed between us, and how many thoughts we exchanged in a few seconds, without uttering a word! It was Gomez who broke the silence. The carelessness that had provoked my arrest still gnawed at him, and he begged my pardon for it.

“Do not trouble yourself over it,” I told him. “We have all committed errors enough. But one may well affirm that our spirit knew no base impulses.”

We ended by leaning against a wall, as though to expose our limbs to the sun — a cheerless sun that gave no foretaste of spring. We spoke of the two martyrs — they who had shed no blood, they were the ones who had been beheaded! Into my breast there crept and grew a sensation colder than winter, and the words died upon our lips. I was drawn from my thoughts by a warder who, passing by, rattled a bunch of keys with a violent clatter.

Several days elapsed, made the more painful by the humiliating regulations of the prison; made long by the feverish wait for news from my wife. I imagined that the Police would now interpose no obstacle to our correspondence, and Elisa, in delaying to send me word of herself, had revived all my anxieties. My start was therefore violent when I was informed that my presence was required at the Chancellery.

I was brought directly before the superintendent, the Marchese Lassalle, who handed me a letter, furnishing me by that simple act with a threefold surprise. The first, because, contrary to the iron discipline of the place, it was still sealed; the second, because the fine characters appearing on the envelope were not Elisa’s — they were entirely unknown to me; the third, because, looking the superintendent in the face as though to draw some explanation from it, I perceived that, despite his outward gravity, he was, beneath the surface, smiling.

I tore open the envelope and experienced a fourth surprise, before which the three preceding ones grew pale and insignificant. The letter began by urging me to read to the end before passing judgement. It then announced that the writer was the Viscountess d’Hasting, a Scottish Protestant who had converted to the Catholic faith and become Sister Teresa of the Discalced Carmelites. She was enclosed in the convent of Montauban, near Toulouse. “I must also premise,” said Sister Teresa, “that the rules of our Order require us to go every two hours through the night to pray in the church of the Convent.” One night, while she was praying, Christ had appeared to her unexpectedly. He had communicated to her, with an ineffable voice, that within the prisons of La Roquette there was a young man of patrician family, courageous, with a spirit nourished upon good and elevated sentiments. The contact of wicked persons had led him astray from the right path and driven him toward perdition. Christ had concluded by entrusting her with the sacred mission of saving the soul of that erring one, who was dear, notwithstanding his transgressions, to the Heavenly Father.

There followed a detailed account of Sister Teresa’s life and conversion, and a fervent exhortation that I should emulate her; that I should return to the bosom of the true Church of God.

The letter had also a postscript, in which Sister Teresa assured me she had obtained from the Archbishop of Paris permission to establish with me a fraternal correspondence. The Archbishop himself had then prevailed upon the prison authorities to permit me to seal my replies, which would be delivered to her without being read — neither by said authorities nor by any person whatsoever.

No very great perspicacity was needed to understand that the fine, elegant handwriting of the Discalced Carmelite concealed the foul union of the Church and the Empire. To please the adulterer, the bride of Christ had assumed the vile trade of the spy.

I handed the letter to the Marchese Lassalle, observing to him, with irony, that if certain absurdities were still possible in the full light of the nineteenth century, under the Empire, in the France of the Encyclopaedists, they had no purchase whatsoever upon the spirit of Italian patriots. The Marchese Lassalle shrugged his shoulders without replying; but his ill-concealed smile would not be contained and seeped from every pore.

Nevertheless I considered that from that farce, insinuated into the fullness of the drama, I might draw some advantage for my family; leaving aside, moreover, that the act of writing would serve me as a diversion. Sister Teresa had my reply.

What did I say to her? I thanked her, first of all, for the interest she had taken in me. Christ, who has knowledge of all things, must surely have informed her that I was a free-thinker and that I believed not in the least in him, nor in his doctrines. If, however, there truly existed a Divine Mercy disposed to do something for me, let it lay the soul momentarily to one side and think upon the extreme necessities of my earthly existence. I had a wife and a child, to whose welfare I was indispensable. If Christ were to work the miracle of restoring me to liberty, perhaps my scepticism might be shaken thereby and — one thing leads to another. At liberty, we should speak of my soul with greater ease!

Now observe the subtlety of the servants of God! They took good care not to induce their principal to work the miracle; but understanding that something must nonetheless be conceded to me, if only to bait me with the hope of better things, they drew the strings secretly and — One day (only five or six had passed since I had written to Sister Teresa), as I was walking in meditation, a friendly, rough-hewn hand descended upon my shoulder. It was the head warder, inviting me to follow him to the Chancellery. He had the mysterious air of a man leading a child to where the apple is hidden.

A few minutes later, I too had my apparition. Not imaginary, not feigned; but true and living and trembling more on my behalf than on her own. She stood erect in the shadow of the great hall, like the Madonna in a Flemish painting, and no sooner had I entered than she ran toward me with the impetuosity of passion and embraced me. Elisa! — Elisa! — She was not alone. She had with her her child: no longer wrapped in white linen, as when I had left him in London; but grown somewhat larger, he was attempting within the prison his first uncertain steps. Freeing myself from Elisa’s arms, I lifted him and kissed him resoundingly; he clouded his round face and fixed his large eyes upon his mother’s countenance, as though to ascertain from her expression that no danger threatened. He was saying in his mute language: “Ought I to weep, or ought I to laugh?”

The warder, seated near the door, a great key in his right hand, informed me that for our final farewell we were permitted no more than a quarter of an hour. We might, he said, speak freely in English.

Our brief meeting was exceedingly agitated. She informed me that, following my advice, she had succeeded in interesting in our fate a certain Sir Walter, of her own Nottingham, who was one of the pillars of the London *Times*. The powerful journal had circulated a petition, subsequently signed, through the humane influence of Sir Richard Main, by several personages of note at Court. Queen Victoria, moved by an adroitly dissembled pressure, had authorised the English Ambassador in Paris to endeavour to have the petition delivered personally to the Empress Eugenia.

Elisa had learned of the grace accorded me from the very night of the 12th. A telegram descended, through a long chain of hierarchy, to the head of the detectives, Sanders, and from him relayed to her, announcing that “not out of any regard for me, since I merited none whatsoever, but out of compassion for a young mother and an innocent child, the Empress had disposed that my life should be spared.”

Elisa had referred to the first part of the telegram with a smile — smiling through her tears, an ineffable smile! For the rest, just as no letter of hers had been delivered to me, so not one of mine had reached her hands.

I looked at her and thought: What will the poor creature do, bound by the Laws and still more by love to a man perhaps destined never to see her again? I felt that I ought, there and then, to release her from every obligation, and to urge her to seek a support worthy of her seemed to me a virtue. But one does not relinquish, without some supreme effort, dominion over a noble heart. I began by informing her of Sister Teresa’s letter, and continuing: “They will surely send,” I said, “some nun or priest to you as well. Whatever they may say to you on my account, take no notice of it. If they put indiscreet questions to you, answer steadily that you know nothing. If they offer you money, accept it. If they invite you to go somewhere, never go alone — never! Within a year I shall be free; I shall find the means of being so. If by the time the year has passed I have not rejoined you — act as though I were dead, and I shall be! A di Rudio cannot live long in chains!”

The quarter of an hour — how swiftly it passed! — was over. We embraced, and her tears began afresh — but I perceived clearly that they were tears of compassion, not of despair. Evidently she still placed all her trust in me and expected that I would keep my promise to the letter. For her greater comfort I added: “Yes, you shall see me free again — within a year!” Then I sought out my child with my eyes. He was upon the warder’s knee, and was clasping the great key between his slender fingers with a look of rapture, as though it were a toy. The warder himself, handing him to my embrace, remarked upon the bizarre caprice of fate that amused the little one with the very key that robbed him of his father, that made an orphan of him!…

The door opened. Upon the threshold I turned to engrave the image of Elisa upon my heart.

I saw her, standing in the darkness, like the Madonna in a Flemish painting. The child was reaching for her kisses and tugging at her dress. One instant more and my apparition had vanished!


### Chapter Twenty-One

<!-- pages:212-215 -->



"FREE... within a year!" I had not said it out of egotism alone, nor out of pity alone, and still less out of boastfulness. My first attempt at escape had been conceived during precisely those days at La Roquette. There I had made the acquaintance of an Englishman who was serving six months' imprisonment for fraudulent bankruptcy. He was a man possessed of a certain culture, of few words; he had, so far as I could infer, means more than sufficient. Like the great majority of his countrymen, he took an interest in politics and hated Napoleon. He came, so to speak, of his own accord to meet the inclinations of my heart and offered to assist me with all the resources at his command. "In church with the saints, in the tavern with the gluttons!" And, moreover, if hypocritical society finds it convenient to take account only of a man's faults in order to depress him, despite his virtues, I find it more logical, more fraternal, to fix one's mind upon his virtues and to exalt him, despite his faults. Had my Englishman truly acquired his riches by reprehensible means? To spend them in order to restore the husband to his widowed consort, the father to his son, was to put ill-gotten gains to good use. I accepted.

We had calculated that he would return among the free before I was conveyed to Toulon. We knew that the condemned, before being transported to the tropical territories, make a stay of greater or lesser duration at that penal establishment. We had no plan of the locality, and to concert a well-defined scheme proved a vague and uncertain business; but it was not unknown that if it is possible to induce a human creature, by means of money, to commit base acts, it is equally possible to induce one, by the same means, to attempt acts of generous daring.

Once out of La Roquette, my Englishman was to make his way to Toulon. It would be easy, after my arrival, to ascertain the place where I was confined and the persons into whose custody I was entrusted. At the same time, he would have organised a means of rapid transport, by sea or by land. Money, lavished with a free hand, would have broken my chains, thus offering me the opportunity to avail myself of that means... Italy and Spain are not, from the French coast, the ultima Thule. The more I studied the character of my Englishman, the more I became convinced that he possessed qualities which rendered him capable of bringing the enterprise to a successful conclusion.

I shall say that the day of his liberation came very soon, and I, embracing him, slipped into his hands, clandestinely, a letter for Elisa... the only one, as I later learned, that she ever received from me.

A week later, I was transported by railway, in a cellular compartment, as far as Marseilles, and from Marseilles, by horse-drawn coach, as far as Toulon. It was the month of April (1858), and nature was reflowering in a manner most wondrous, when I was shut away in a casemate below the level of the sea and chained, together with other wretches, to an iron bar fixed into the damp wall.

One wishes to know what occupation fell to Count Carlo di Rudio during a period of approximately seven months? I wore a red jacket and a pair of yellow trousers; I wore a Phrygian cap of green colour, this being the distinguishing mark of those condemned in perpetuity (those with a term sentence wore it red); I was without moustaches and had my hair cut in strips. I slept, when I could sleep, upon a broad plank. The hours in which I lay wakeful I passed in waging a furious war upon the insects that tormented me, and in chafing the ring that bound me. This last labour of mine was so persistent and so tenacious that, on no fewer than four occasions, I succeeded in having myself conducted to the blacksmith's forge, and thus traversing, holding my chain between my hands, a quarter of a mile in the open air... on no fewer than four occasions thus beholding the Sun again!

As at La Roquette, morbid curiosity exposed me to the humiliating inspection of one personage and another. At that period, the Czarina was endeavouring to restore her ailing health by breathing the balmy air of the Riviera. The officers of a Russian squadron, en route for Nice, where they were to take the pallid sovereign on board, put in at Toulon and wished to see the man who had attempted to kill Napoleon... the man who had inflicted upon their Fatherland the humiliation of Sebastopol. They offered to bestow upon us a sum of money to provide for my most pressing needs. But the Regulations permitted me nothing beyond bread and water, and the jailers opposed it. So that I derived from it nothing but the barren satisfaction of being able to say that even the steppes of eastern Europe had contributed their contingent to the swarm of importunate visitors.

But among my visitors there was one, of an entirely different sort, who merits a particular mention all his own.

Sister Teresa had not rested content with her first letter and my first reply. She had sent me at least a dozen more. They all exhorted me to trust in God, to repent of my past transgressions; to turn, with fervour, towards the outstretched arms of divine mercy, which would assuredly not repel me. Wearied to death, I had written to her, a few days before leaving La Roquette, that I had been educated to place faith in facts, and in facts alone. Words slid inexorably off my soul, and hers were nothing but words! I believed I had put an end to the comedy... but it is not for nothing that one is deemed worthy of an apparition of Christ. Unexpectedly, I saw again, even at Toulon, the fine and elegant handwriting of my sister in God.

It announced to me, no less! the visit of a bishop travelling, as a missionary, to China. I was to open to him my every thought. He was a holy man, replete with celestial grace, and he would not fail to "touch my heart."

One day, at a most unusual hour, the gate of the casemate opened and there entered an old man of about sixty, enveloped in a cloth of violet colour. The jailer pointed me out and departed. I found myself face to face with the announced missionary bishop... who evidently proposed to inaugurate his apostolic triumphs by rendering, through my conversion, a service to the Empire.

He opened the conversation by affirming that he was in that place at the behest of Sister Teresa, and added at once that, heeding the exhortations of that pious lady, he had provided, with sufficient liberality, for the earthly needs of my wife and my child.

At this point I scrutinised his face intently and discerned therein nothing but an expression of candid good nature. Yet those saintly episcopal lips had lied like those of a vagabond.

Be that as it may, supposing that he had thus well disposed my spirit with the pretended beneficence, that most pure vessel of celestial grace proceeded to apply the balm he had laid upon my breast, and, in a calm voice, announced to me how Sister Teresa had thought of me as well. He was the bearer of a gift from her. Without further preamble, he drew from beneath his violet robe a coral rosary mounted in silver, from which I saw suspended a crucifix and a medal.

I looked without stretching out my hand, and with a voice no less calm: "A most beautiful gift... if I could pawn it and exchange it for tobacco. But many things are denied in here that are permitted to simple mortals. Thank Sister Teresa on my behalf and write to tell her that I have refused."

The bishop's eyes flashed with the gleam of an Inquisitor. "My son," he said, "you will not refuse at least this other token of brotherly love. It is blessed by the Pope and will bring you good fortune!" And with that he set swinging before my eyes a pair of small scapulars of satin, with the image of Christ embroidered in gold.

— Oh, Father! In this wretched place, with so much filth... what could they serve for, save as a comfortable nest for my lice?

He did not admit defeat, and, without uttering a word, drew out and spread before me an elegant missal. But I observed to him that I had long since ceased to pray, and that in that jumble of words my mind would find no sustenance whatsoever.

The conversation came to an abrupt end. The bishop gathered up his gifts and, turning his back to me, departed without so much as bidding me farewell, with a step that was intended to appear dignified and solemn. I believe he was thinking: "If the Chinese are all like this wretch, I am in for a poor time of it!"

Then, shrugging my shoulders, I looked at my fellow prisoners in the chain. Some regarded me with an air of stupefaction, as though I were the devil; others with sparkling eyes, as if my hair cropped in strips did not suffice to conceal from them the features of a hero. But I was neither a hero nor a devil. I was simply a rational man who, guided by his own deep convictions, was evading the underhand arts of imposture.

I consider it fitting to recount here, by way of analogy, an incident that befell my wife while I was already serving my sentence in Cayenne.

Nottingham, where Elisa had retired to spend, together with her mother, her days of widowhood, was, and certainly still is, an episcopal seat. Catholics regard that bishopric as an advanced stronghold of truth against heresy and, in their fancy, surround it with the serene air that inspires chaste thoughts... a sort of oasis where, by day, doves flutter about; and in the dark of night, luminous angels.

Unexpectedly, Elisa received an invitation to present herself at the episcopal residence on a matter of the greatest importance. Mindful of my counsel, she did not venture to go alone. But here I think the words come better from the lips of my Elisa herself. He pressed a bell, and the lady came at once. He asked her to relate, in simple terms, the conversation she had had with the Bishop of Nottingham.

"There is not a great deal to tell. I went to the episcopal residence with my mother. She was asked to wait in the anteroom; I was immediately conducted into Monsignor's study... a tall, thin man, all skin and bone. He received me with a solemn face and, unfolding a letter which he was careful not to hand to me, told me he was exceedingly grieved to have sorrowful news to impart. A colleague of his, serving at the penitentiary of Cayenne, had written to inform him of my husband's death; which had moreover been confirmed by the French press and the French Authorities. Although the calamity was irreparable and he sympathised with my inevitable grief, it was not without a profound and serene satisfaction of his priestly soul that he could assure me you had sincerely repented of your past transgressions, that you had departed this life acknowledging the sanctity of the religion of your fathers. On this subject you had indeed expressed the ardent desire that I, for the sake of my eternal salvation, should embrace the Catholic faith and enter a convent if at all possible. You further enjoined me, as the expression of your last and absolute will, to entrust our son to the care of some Catholic, apostolic, Roman institution, so that he might be reared in the principles which had sustained you in your misfortune and comforted you upon your deathbed."

"And what did you reply to this excellent ecclesiastic?" "For a moment I found neither tears nor words. Then a flash crossed my mind and, fixing him with my gaze, I said: 'Count Carlo di Rudio cannot have died in such a manner. And since this is a lie, it is equally a lie that he is no more. I feel it; my husband lives!'"

— The priest crumpled the letter, flung it onto the writing-desk, and with a terrible expression — which betrayed the desire to burn me alive — opening and closing his fingers as though they were talons, he said through clenched teeth: "Ah, if we were in France!" I was seized with great terror and, letting out a cry, I fled. I was so shaken that I could say nothing to my mother until we were in the street, far away, very far away!"

Thus — continued the Count di Rudio — ended the attempt of the Catholic clergy to act as informers on behalf of the Second Empire.

While I, amid the horrors of the casemate, toiled at gnawing through the ring of my chain in order to catch a fleeting glimpse of the Sun, my Englishman was working, for his part, to bring our agreement to fruition, with all the astuteness and tenacity so thoroughly Anglo-Saxon. He had gathered every possible piece of intelligence concerning the locality where I was confined and the habits of the prison; he had procured disguises; he had prepared places of temporary refuge; means of transport as far as the Pyrenees. With the aid of some woman or other, he had managed to enter into direct communication with my immediate jailer. It had even been settled that the day of my escape should coincide with my next visit to the blacksmith's forge. Unexpectedly, I was brought before the Commander of the Arsenal.

I was informed that on the following day I would be embarked on the frigate *La Durance*. "You are going to Cayenne," added the Commander in a dry tone, yet not devoid of an underlying affability. "A wretched climate! Europeans, compelled to live in your conditions, cannot survive there more than two or three years. I am authorised to tell you that, if you reveal your accomplices, you will be immediately released and furnished with the means necessary to leave the borders of France. It is within your power to die in the penal establishment, or to live in the bosom of your family. Choose!"

I replied that I knew nothing. But even had I disclosures to make, the opinion in which I held the servants of the Empire was far too low for me to place the slightest faith in their promises. I chose the penal establishment!

— As you please! retorted the Commander roughly, and gave orders that I be conducted back to the casemate.

On the following day I was on the high seas. My first attempt at escape had failed!



*La Durance* sailed on, bearing in her broad belly, divided into three decks, some three hundred condemned men. Gomez and I were confined in the deepest. But the Commander, who was an Orleanist and could not have harboured excessive antipathy toward us, no sooner learned who we were than he had us transferred to the upper deck. He even carried his benevolent partiality so far as to permit me to go up on deck from time to time and smoke freely... the Regulations prescribed the trifling penalty of fifty lashes of the rope for anyone who had done so in the compartments.

We put in at the island of Santa Cruz (Canaries) to take on water and remained there two days, with portholes shut, to deprive us of every possibility of escape; then we proceeded directly to the Île Royale du Salut, where some two hundred convicts were disembarked.

Gomez, myself, and about a hundred more wretches passed on board a small steamer, and on the 11th of December, 1858, after a day and a half of additional navigation, we were anchored off the Montagne d'Argent, which rises at the confluence of the Saint-Georges and the Oyapock, at a relatively short distance from the border of Brazil. There had once flourished in that place a free colony, and free hands had there cultivated coffee and every tropical fruit capable of tempting the human palate. The French Government had purchased it. Its officers occupied the planter's house; numerous shelters of rushes, supported by posts, were erected there as cover for the condemned; the ground was bored here and there to bury, in a slow agony, those refractory to discipline; some substantial buildings were also raised for the storehouses, for defence and for offence... the vanguard of civilisation had been transformed into a penal establishment. Yet the beauties of nature marvellously dissembled the hell that men had accumulated there.

The commandant, a Major of the marine infantry, whom the Republic had placed on half-pay and the Emperor reinstated and decorated with honourable insignia, hastened to receive us "in charge" and to call our roll. When he pronounced my name, the officer who had accompanied us from the Île du Salut handed him a letter. It evidently concerned me. He read it and, looking me up and down: "Ah, so you are that—" (down came a term fit for a sty!) "I am glad to see you here, and I swear to you that here you will leave your skin!"...

That act of vileness was so entirely uncalled for that even the guards made a grimace of disgust. But I did not lose my composure, and, as though flattered by a compliment: "I thank you for the good wish and return it with all my heart!" He clenched his jaw; but he had Gallic ferocity, not the French spirit, and he remained silent.

That small incident convinced me that at the Montagne d'Argent the air blew ill for me and, from that very moment, I resolved to repress every impulse. The relative liberty granted to those who kept good discipline — I reflected — may offer me the opportunity to earn a greater one... the liberty of civilised men. But it was my fate that my wise resolutions should vanish no later than the following day.

In the hut assigned to me were about fifty galley-slaves... they formed my squad. I passed the first night sleeping, in my hammock, a sleep tormented by a swarm of insects. At sunrise we set off, our bones aching, towards the forest. I wore a great straw hat, a pair of wooden clogs, a pair of canvas trousers, a brown blouse, upon the back of which was marked, in large characters, my number... the baptism of the penal establishment!

In crossing a swamp, I had the misfortune to lose one of my clogs, and the stumps of the rushes, cut level with the water to facilitate the ford, cut into the sole of my foot. I was obliged to manage three miles of limping, marking every footstep with blood; until I arrived, the last of my squad, in the heart of the forest... a splendid forest that, for a moment, almost made me forget that I was a convict and the pain.

A pile of trunks. The task of the day was to carry them on our shoulders to the penitentiary, making five consecutive journeys. I picked out a trunk that appeared light to the eye; but it was *bois fer* — ironwood — and when I had raised it to the height of my knees, it fell back with a crash upon the pile. Then, instead of making another attempt, I sat down, resolved to rest long enough to recover my strength. The convict guard stood watching me, and when he was certain of my intention: "Get up, you filthy wretch! If you don't stir yourself, I'll send you along with a kick in the seat of your trousers. A curse on you and all the Italian breed!"

Had he contented himself with goading me with a simple threat, I should, in all probability, have taken the matter with full philosophy; but that contempt for my nationality aroused in my breast the fury accumulated against the tyrants who made sport of it. I swung my *sabre d'abattis* and made a leap of which, given my condition, I should not have thought myself capable. "Take care! Italians do not suffer insults! The first one... let it pass! At the second, your skull will see the sun no more from the top of your shoulders!"

My companions, having set off one by one, were already far away. Alone, perfectly alone in a deserted spot! He swallowed, turning pale, the bitter pill. Nevertheless, by a show of bravado, he demanded my name. "Be content with the number!" and I turned my back on him.

A poor beginning! But it so happened that my guard, though he could spell out words after a fashion, could not write. To make his report, he had to have recourse to the clerk of the supervising Engineer; who, with the aid of my number, verified my name and identity in the registers. What feelings animated him toward me I cannot say; but I learned that he persuaded my guard not to be harsh with me. And in his exhortations he deftly insinuated the element of fear: "That man," he said, "fears neither Laws, nor punishments, nor God, nor the devil. If he swears vengeance, sooner or later you are done for!"

On his return to the forest, the guard called me aside. "We have at the penitentiary certain rough sorts!... But I know how to make my distinctions." And to prove it, he begged my pardon for his foul words and granted me leave not to work, neither then nor henceforth. I was to conceal myself, each day, in the depths of the forest... I was to reappear only when the last departure of my squad was imminent. And he extended his right hand, as if to seal a compact.

Thus, for a good month, I passed the hours appointed for labour stretched amid a wondrous vegetation, smoking a cigarette, listening to the complex murmuring of the forest. In my eyes during those hours there was a fantastic interweaving of embroideries never before seen... enormous leaves cut from velvet, trunks tall and straight as minarets; flowers that seemed proud of their colours, pressing into the service of their ill-concealed loves strange insects; birds with metallic glints, bursting from shadows all a-rustle, flew past me with wings outspread and swift as lightning above my head. There were recesses of deep green, full of mystery; there were majestic vaults dripping with dew and fragrance, through which the Sun gazed down placidly, majestically, as if watching over every blade of grass to ensure it had its portion... the Sun which, worshipped, consciously or unconsciously, in all ages, by all Peoples, had with its age-old energy drawn upward into itself that temple, worthy of a God. Oh how beautiful is the Earth when it is not defaced by the cupidity of man!... A temple worthy also of Elisa!... Her image, intensely evoked, held dominion over me as though she were present!

It was in that forest and in those hours that I conceived my second attempt at escape. The plan to which I fixed myself definitively came to me from the primitive art of the Indians ranging through the southern regions of Guiana: to fell a great tree, and hollow it out so as to fashion a pirogue of sufficient capacity. With it I would have ascended, by night, the Oyapock for a stretch of fifty miles, and then crossed the Saint-Georges, to land on the territory of Brazil. Having evaded the pursuit of the French Authorities, I would have had to face, in all likelihood, the ferocity of savages, and, beyond doubt, the obstacles, well-nigh insuperable, of unexplored regions and the climate. But the prize was worth the risk!

All of this could not so much as be begun without the assistance of several accomplices. I sounded the spirit of Antonio Gomez. But the ardent political conspirator, satisfied with his position as assistant canteen keeper and in all probability dreading the worst, pretended not to understand me. It was necessary to have recourse to less noble material. By what criteria was it to be chosen? How to acquire over it the ascendancy necessary to bind it to my purposes?

In this case too, my good star, more than my own wisdom, came to my aid.

The penitentiary received its supplies of fresh meat from Brazil. At more or less regular intervals, a sailing vessel arrived with a cargo of cattle. The beach, which shelved gently into the Ocean, afforded insufficient depth for mooring close to the shore, so that the anchor was cast at some distance out, at a distance of about three miles. The guard would then choose one convict from each squad of ten and form them into a special detachment which made its laborious way, with the water above its waist, close up to the vessel. The wild oxen, maddened, were thrown into the sea, and the men drove them, with great shouts and great blows of the whip, as far as a stockaded enclosure within the penitentiary.

Once that work had begun, it proceeded without interruption until the entire cargo was landed, skipping, as need required, even a meal or two. At the Montagne d'Argent, meals were taken by squads of ten. The squad leader received the provisions in his charge, thinned down as they were by the squalid speculation of the officers, and distributed them, on a sort of table, into the ten mess-bowls. Each convict ran to his own and devoured it, seated on some step, or on a tree-trunk, or on the bare ground. On days of disembarkation, the squad leader was obliged to keep the absent convict's ration and hand it over on his return.

Having been chosen to lend a hand at that furious labour, one day I made my return at eight in the evening, when my squad was already snoring in their hammocks. Hunger, sharpened by the incessant exertion, the long immersion, and the sharp sea-breeze, had turned fierce. I shake the squad leader, raise something of a commotion, and he, showing me his fist instead of the mess-bowl: "*Tais-toi, Italien de m...*" (The word of Cambronne).

I chew on nothing and keep silent. The evening of the following day, I take my place in the squad's rank and wait around the table. When the squad leader arrives, bearing a pot brimming over with soup, I lunge forward and deal it a powerful kick. The squad leader stands stupefied, arms outstretched, hands empty. "It takes," I say, "nine Frenchmen to make one Italian go hungry, but one Italian alone suffices to make nine Frenchmen go hungry!"

Now it is the others who raise the commotion. The guard, a certain Cosson, comes running, and having been informed of the matter, demands of me brusquely: "Why, instead of avenging yourself, did you not lodge your report?"

— Because Italians do not inform!

Cosson, borrowing Solomon's trade, sentenced both the squad leader and Count Carlo di Rudio to fifty lashes of the rope. But my resolution and, above all, my proud reply had instantly raised me in the estimation of those wretched men. They surrounded the guard, and said so much, that he consented to swallow his sentence. From that day on, the convicts condescended, in their goodness! to treat me as a brother worthy of their fellowship; from that day on, it proved a comparatively easy undertaking to study the various temperaments, to select eight individuals sufficiently enamoured of liberty to face with a light heart, in the attempt to regain it, whatever danger might arise.

We left our hut by night, creeping, like beasts issuing from their lair, so as to deceive any eyes that might still be awake. We proceeded by different routes as far as a previously appointed spot on the edge of the forest; then, reunited, we advanced into its labyrinths. From the very first night, we had blazed our trail with marks well-nigh invisible to other mortals, thus warding off the danger of losing our way.

A tree of gigantic proportions was felled at its base, with relatively little effort; but in falling, it came down upon the dense undergrowth and remained propped up there. We had to climb upon it and hollow it out while clinging on... a complication that required precious time. We ended by deciding to make use of fire. The light could not be seen from the penitentiary, and the smoke was rendered invisible by the night; but amid so much resinous vegetation, amid so many dead shrubs, amid so many chips and so much debris accumulated over centuries, the operation demanded great circumspection, or it would have fed a conflagration so rapid and so vast that even the tropical deluges would have had difficulty in mastering it.

That nocturnal enterprise of ours constitutes for me one of those incidents whose impression only death can efface.
