One was not in that land as a pioneer of civilisation, and someone might even observe, ironically, that we were not the stuff of missionaries. But the surroundings — oh, the grandeur of the surroundings, rendered ineffable by the silent night — how deeply they penetrated, with their own essence, into ours, and how they transformed us! About us, there was nothing that had not been born and grown in the most boundless freedom, and freedom became almost tangible in every atom of air we breathed. It seemed to me that the muscles, as they toiled, exhaled from their every fibre something luminous, as though they were giving back to things the untainted life that things lent to us. There were moments in which I could have sworn myself as vigorous and as eternal as the virgin forest. And we became so thoroughly a homogeneous part of the surroundings that, after the first nights, countless troops of monkeys, attracted by the glow of the resin, would come to contemplate, and almost to encourage by their enlivening presence, our work.

After a couple of months, or a little more, we succeeded in dragging into a bend of the river, and concealing among the lianas, a canoe seven feet broad and twenty-five feet long. It floated admirably. "And now," I exclaimed, when we parted full of enthusiasm, "to the oars and the sail!"

Unexpectedly, an event to which, in its first manifestation, no one paid serious attention, convulsed the entire penal colony and with it every hope of mine.

In broad daylight, beneath a clear sky that is itself a lyric, in a sun capable of hardening ostrich eggs by its direct rays... a sentry is seized with violent shivering. Replaced as quickly as possible, he was seen making his way at slow steps, trembling, into the antediluvian shelter that bore the pompous name of "Cabinet of decency." He came out of it ashen, distraught. He affirmed that, try as he might, he had been unable to produce a single drop of water. The convicts laughed heartily... the thing seemed to them so comical! By evening, the poor fellow was passing jets of coagulated blood, black as coal... two days later he was a corpse!

But before he had even drawn his last breath, five, seven, ten convicts, while showing a high temperature on the thermometer, were trembling like leaves. The penitentiary had been invaded by yellow fever.

The catastrophe was rapid beyond all telling. In those days, it was held that the seat of the disease lay in the intestines; the remedies, scanty in the extreme and altogether inadequate to the vastness of the mortality, were, to make matters worse, applied according to erroneous principles. The Commandant gave orders that the storehouses and the wine-cellar be opened and left entirely at the disposal of the healthy. From that wine-cellar, flung wide open and dark within, I seemed to feel emanating the very sensation of fatality. If yellow fever was not crouching in there, it had some mysterious ally within. The convicts were inexorably drawn thither by vice and fear. There they brutalised themselves, and passed from drunkenness to delirium; from delirium to oblivion!... with a death all the more sudden, the more robust they were.

The arrangements for the victims, most simple; to cast them, without ceremony, into quicklime.

Each evening the head guard went to make his report at the Commandant's house.

One night the Commandant's wife and two children died almost simultaneously, and he ceased to take any interest in my welfare. Not long after, it was the head guard who ceased to make his customary report. He had too readily yielded to the temptations of the wine-cellar, and when he was found stretched out on the beaten earth, it was suspected at first that he was simply drunk. But his gums were covered with bloody froth... he died without having recovered either speech or any glimmer of consciousness, and he was among the many whose feet I was obliged to take hold of. "One, two, three..." Before swinging him in, I would have liked to bid him a "Requiescat!"... he had spared me fifty lashes of the rope. But down there in that pit... it would have seemed like mockery!

I lent a hand to bury, one by one, in that fashion, my eight accomplices in the attempt at escape, and others, and others still... I counted to well over a hundred; then I lost count and counted no more. Some poor wretch, who already seemed to have crossed the threshold beyond which suffering is no more, scarcely thrown into that horrible common grave, was seen to revive and writhe in agony!

When the fever, finding no more flesh to devour, at last disappeared, the French Deputies raised, in Paris, the usual humanitarian clamour. But the Minister replied to them, with an air of triumph, that he had already given the appropriate orders for the transfer of the entire Colony to the Île du Salut. And indeed, one evening we saw taking shape against the twilight glow the black trail of smoke from a vessel proceeding at full steam towards our shore. It was the *Abeille*, bringing us the orders of His Excellency. The following day the entire Colony was on board... Of more than six hundred, comprising condemned men, military, civil employees and servants, we were reduced to sixty-three! The greater part of the survivors had already suffered from yellow fever at other times and in other places; which had rendered them, it appears, resistant if not absolutely invulnerable; many, shadows of their former selves, showed signs of having been touched, or rather smitten, just then, then and there... among these, Antonio Gomez... Oh, how transformed was his face from that of the easy-going priest! Three alone had not contracted the infection of the blood, either then or before. Among the three, the convict di Rudio! From the deck of the *Abeille*, round which the Ocean already foamed and broke, I cast a last glance at the Montagne d'Argent and thought, with profound regret, of my canoe floating solitary and motionless beneath the dense lianas. The landscape shrank rapidly away. The hell that men had accumulated there was no more; there remained one of its most fatal consequences, the vast charnel-pit from which we had miraculously escaped. Nature, indifferent, spread over so much horror enchanting hues, and in the scintillating air there vibrated her mysterious amorous stirrings!


### Chapter Twenty-Two

<!-- pages:215-222 -->

<!-- pages:215-222 -->

*LA DURR* ploughed the waters, bearing in her ample belly, divided into three decks, some three hundred condemned men. Gomez and I were confined in the deepest. But the Commander, who was an Orleanist and could not have harboured excessive antipathies towards us, no sooner learned who we were than he had us transferred to the upper deck. Indeed he pressed his benevolent partiality so far as to permit me, from time to time, to come up on deck and smoke freely... the Regulations prescribed the trifling penalty of fifty lashes of the rope for anyone who had done so in the compartments below.

We put in at the island of Santa Cruz (Carmarie) to take on water and remained there two days, with hatches closed, to deprive us of any possibility of escape; then we proceeded directly to the Île Royale du Salut, where some two hundred convicts were put ashore.

Gomez, myself, and another hundred wretches passed aboard a small steamer, and on the 11th of December 1858, after a day and a half of additional navigation, we lay at anchor off the Montagne d’Argent, which rises at the confluence of the Saint-Georges and the Oyapock, at a relatively short distance from the frontier of Brazil. A free colony had once flourished in that place, and free hands had cultivated its coffee and every tropical fruit that might tempt the human palate. The French Government had purchased it. Its officers occupied the planter’s house; numerous reed-thatched shelters, supported on stakes, were erected there for the condemned; the earth was pierced here and there, that refractory souls might be buried alive in slow agony; a few substantial buildings arose too for the storehouses, for defence and for offence — the vanguard of civilisation had been transformed into a penal settlement. But the natural beauties dissembled marvellously the hell that men had accumulated there.

The commandant, a Major of the marine infantry, whom the Republic had placed on the retired list and the Emperor reinstated and adorned with badges of honour, hastened to receive us “in consignment” and to call our roll. When he pronounced my name, the officer who had accompanied us from the Île du Salut handed him a letter. It evidently concerned me. He read it and, surveying me from head to foot: “Ah, so you are that —” (here came an obscenity fit for a pigsty!) “I am glad to see you here, and I swear to you that here you will leave your skin!” ...

That piece of coarseness was so entirely uncalled for that even the guards pulled a grimace of disgust. But I did not lose my composure, and, as though flattered by a compliment: “My thanks for the good wish — and I return it with all my heart!” He clenched his jaws; but he possessed the Gallic ferocity, not the French wit, and remained tight-lipped.

That small incident persuaded me that at the Montagne d’Argent the air blew ill for me, and from that very hour I resolved to repress every impulse. The relative freedom granted to the well-disciplined — I thought — may afford me the occasion to earn myself a greater one... the freedom of civilised men. But it was fated that my wise resolutions should vanish no later than the following day.

In the hut assigned to me were some fifty convicts who formed my squad. I passed the first night sleeping, in my hammock, a sleep tormented by a swarm of insects. At sunrise we set out, our bones aching, towards the forest. I wore a great straw hat, a pair of heavy wooden clogs, a pair of canvas trousers, and a brown blouse, upon the back of which was marked, in large characters, my number — the baptism of the penitentiary!

In crossing a swamp, I had the ill fortune to lose one of my clogs, and the stumps of the reeds, cut flush with the water to ease the passage, lacerated the sole of my foot. I was obliged to contrive a way of covering three miles hobbling, and marking, in blood, every footprint; until at last I arrived, the last of my squad, in the depths of the forest — a splendid forest that, for a little, almost made me forget that I was a convict, and the anguish with it.

A pile of logs. The task of the day was to carry them upon our shoulders to the penitentiary, making five consecutive trips. I singled out a log that seemed, to the eye, light; but it was *bois de fer* — ironwood — and when I had raised it to the height of my knees, it fell back noisily upon the pile. Then, instead of making another attempt, I sat down, resolved to rest just long enough to recover my breath. The convict-overseer stood watching me, and when he was certain of my intention: “Get up, you filthy wretch! If you don’t stir yourself, I’ll send you running with kicks to your backside. A curse on you and all Italian stock!”

Had he contented himself with goading me with a mere threat, I should in all probability have taken the matter with complete philosophical calm; but that contempt for my nationality stirred in my breast the anger accumulated against all the bullies who made sport of it. I swung my *sabre d’abattis* and sprang forward with a leap of which, given my condition, I should not have thought myself capable. “Take care! Italians do not suffer insults. The first — let it pass! At the second, your pumpkin will see the sun no more from atop your shoulders!”

My companions, who had set out one by one, were already far away. Alone, perfectly alone in a deserted place! He swallowed, turning pale, the pill. Nevertheless, by way of a show of bravado, he asked my name. “Content yourself with the number!” and I turned my back on him.

We were making a bad beginning! But chance would have it that my overseer, though he could spell more or less correctly, could not write. To make out his report, he was obliged to apply to the clerk of the supervising Engineer; who, with the help of the number, verified in the registers my name and who I was. What sentiments animated him towards me I cannot say; but I learned that he persuaded my overseer not to press his cruelty. And into his exhortations he deftly insinuated the element of fear: “That man,” he said, “fears neither Laws, nor punishments, nor God, nor the devil. If he swears vengeance, sooner or later, you are a dead man!”

On his return to the forest, the overseer drew me aside. “We have a certain sort of stuff here at the penitentiary!... But I know how to make my distinctions.” And to prove it, he begged my pardon for his ill words and granted me licence not to work, either then or thereafter. I was to conceal myself daily in the depths of the forest... I was to reappear at the last departure of my squad. And he extended his right hand, as though to seal a pact.

Thus, for a good month, I passed the hours allotted to labour stretched out amid a wondrous vegetation, smoking my cigarette, listening to the intricate murmuring of the forest. In my eyes there was, in those hours, a fantastical interweaving of embroideries never before seen: enormous leaves cut as from velvet, trunks tall and straight as minarets; flowers that seemed proud of their own colours pressed strange insects into the service of their ill-concealed loves; birds with metallic lustre, bursting from shadows all a-rustle, swept past overhead on outstretched wings, swift as lightning. There were caverns of a deep green, full of mystery; there were majestic vaults, dripping with dew and fragrance, through which the Sun gazed placidly, lordly, as though keeping watch that every blade of grass had its due.

The Sun which, consciously or unconsciously, in every age and by every People has been adored, had with secular energy raised up to itself that temple, worthy of a God. Oh, how beautiful is the Earth when it is not defaced by the cupidity of man!... A temple worthy, too, of Elisa!... Her image, intensely evoked, held dominion over me as though she were present!

It was in that forest and in those hours that I pondered my second attempt at escape. The plan to which I definitively committed myself came to me from the primitive art of the Indians ranging the southern regions of Guiana: to fell a great tree, to hollow it out so as to fashion a serviceable pirogue. With it I would have ascended, by night, the Oyapock for a stretch of fifty miles and then crossed the Saint-Georges, to make landfall on Brazilian soil. Having eluded the pursuit of the French Authorities, I should then have had to face, in all probability, the ferocity of the savages, and beyond any doubt the well-nigh insuperable obstacles of unexplored regions and of the climate. But the prize was worth the risk!

None of this could even be begun without the help of several accomplices. I sounded the spirit of Antonio Gomez. But the ardent political conspirator, satisfied with his position as assistant canteen-keeper and probably dreading the worst, feigned not to understand me. I was obliged to have recourse to less noble material. By what criteria was I to choose it? How was I to acquire over it the ascendancy necessary to bind it to my purposes?

In this case too, my good star, more than my own wisdom, came to my aid. The penitentiary received its supplies of fresh meat from Brazil. At intervals more or less regular, a sailing vessel arrived with a cargo of cattle. The shore, which shelved gradually into the ocean, afforded it no sufficient depth to moor near the mainland, so that the anchor was cast offshore at a distance of some three miles. The overseer would then choose one convict from each squad of ten and form them into a special party which made its laborious way, with water above the waist, up to the vessel. The wild bullocks, maddened, were flung into the sea, and the men drove them, with great shouts and great lashings, into a stockade within the penitentiary.

Once that work had begun, it proceeded without interruption until the unloading was complete, skipping, as needs required, even a meal or two. At the Montagne d’Argent, meals were taken by squads of ten. The squad-leader received in charge the provisions — already whittled thin by the sordid speculation of the officers — and set them out, upon a sort of table, in the ten mess-bowls. Each convict ran to his own and devoured it, seated on some step, or on a tree-trunk, or on the bare earth. On unloading days, it fell to the squad-leader to keep the absent convict’s ration and hand it to him on his return.

Chosen to lend a hand at that infernal labour, one day I made my way back at eight in the evening, when my squad was already snoring in its hammocks. Hunger, sharpened by the incessant exertion, the prolonged bath, the keen sea-breeze, had become ferocious. I shake the squad-leader, raise a half-commotion, and he, showing me his fist instead of the mess-bowl: “*Tais-toi, Italien de m...*” (the word of Cambronne).

I chew on nothing and hold my tongue. The evening of the following day I fall into line with the squad and wait about the table. When the squad-leader arrives, holding up the pot brimming with soup, I fling myself upon it and deliver it a mighty kick. The leader stands there dumbfounded, arms outstretched, hands empty. “It takes,” I say, “nine Frenchmen to make one Italian go hungry, but a single Italian is enough to make nine Frenchmen go hungry!”

Now it is the others who raise the commotion. The overseer, a certain Cosson, comes running, and having been informed of the affair, demands of me curtly: “Why, instead of taking your revenge, did you not file your report?”

— Because Italians do not turn informer!

Cosson, stealing Solomon’s trade, condemned to fifty lashes of the rope both the squad-leader and Count Carlo di Rudio. But my resoluteness, and above all my fierce reply, had in an instant raised me in the estimation of those poor wretches. They surrounded the overseer and pleaded with such effect that he was prevailed upon to swallow his sentence. From that day, the convicts condescended, in their goodness, to treat me fraternally as one worthy of their fellowship; from that day it proved a relatively easy undertaking for me to study the various temperaments, and to select eight individuals sufficiently enamoured of freedom to face cheerfully, in the attempt to regain it, whatever danger might arise.

We left our hut by night, crawling like beasts emerging from their lair, to deceive any eyes that might still be awake. We proceeded by separate paths to a pre-arranged spot on the edge of the forest; then, united, we pressed on into its labyrinths. From the very first night we had marked our route with signs almost invisible to other mortals, thus averting the danger of losing ourselves.

A tree of gigantic proportions was felled at its base with comparatively little effort; but in falling it struck the dense canopy and remained propped upon it. We had to climb up and hollow it out while clinging to its sides — a complication that consumed precious time. In the end we resolved to have recourse to fire. The light could not be seen from the penitentiary and the smoke was rendered invisible by the night; but amid such resinous vegetation, amid so many dead shrubs, so much dry kindling, so much fallen matter accumulated over centuries, the operation demanded the greatest circumspection, lest we feed a conflagration so swift and so vast that even the tropical deluges themselves would scarce have mastered it.

That nocturnal enterprise of ours constitutes, for me, one of those incidents whose impression only death can efface. We were not in that land as pioneers of civilisation, and someone might even remark, with irony, that we were not of the stuff of missionaries. But the setting — oh the grandeur of the setting, rendered ineffable by the silent night — how it penetrated, together with its own essence, into ours, and how it transformed us! All about us there was nothing that had not been born and grown in the most boundless freedom, and freedom became almost palpable in every atom of air we breathed. It seemed to me that my muscles, in their exertions, exhaled from every fibre something luminous, as though giving back to things the unstained life that things were lending to us. There were moments when I could have sworn I was as vigorous and whole as the virgin forest. And we became so thoroughly a homogeneous part of our surroundings that, after the first few nights, numberless troops of monkeys, drawn by the glow of the resin, came to contemplate and as if to encourage, brightening it with their presence, our work.

After some two months, or a little more, we succeeded in dragging into a bend of the river, and in concealing among the lianas, a canoe seven feet broad and twenty-five feet long. It floated admirably. “And now,” I exclaimed, when we parted full of enthusiasm, “to the oars and the sail!” Unexpectedly, an event to which, at its first manifestation, no one paid serious attention, threw the entire penal colony into turmoil — and with it every hope of mine.

In broad daylight, beneath a cloudless sky that is a whole lyric poem in itself, with a sun capable of hard-boiling ostrich eggs in its direct rays — a sentry was seized with violent trembling. Relieved as quickly as might be, he was seen making his way at slow steps, still trembling, to the antediluvian hut that bore the pompous name of “Cabinet of Decency.” He emerged from it ashen, undone. He declared that, try as he might, he had not been able to pass a single drop of water. The convicts laughed loudly... the thing seemed to them so droll! By evening the poor wretch was vomiting clotted blood, black as coal... two days later he was a corpse!

But before he had even drawn his last breath, five, seven, ten convicts, while showing at the thermometer a high temperature, were trembling like leaves. The penitentiary had been invaded by yellow fever.

The catastrophe was swift beyond all telling. In those days it was held that the seat of the disease lay in the intestines; the remedies, scarce in the extreme and wholly inadequate to the vast extent of the mortality, were applied, for greater misfortune, on erroneous principles. The Commandant gave orders to throw open the storehouses and the cantina and to leave them at the full disposal of the able-bodied. From that cantina, flung wide and black with shadow, there seemed to issue the very sensation of doom. If yellow fever was not crouching within it, it had there some mysterious ally. The convicts were inexorably driven thither by vice and by fear. They brutalised themselves within it and passed from drunkenness to delirium; from delirium to nothingness! — their death the more sudden the more robust they had been.

The arrangements for the victims were of the simplest: to cast them, without ceremony, into quicklime.

Every evening the chief overseer went to make his report at the Commandant’s house.

— How many, today?

— Twenty, thirty, forty! ...

— And the convict di Rudio?

— Sound as a fish!

One night the Commandant lost his wife and two children almost simultaneously, and he ceased to concern himself with my health. Not long afterwards, it was the chief overseer himself who ceased to make his customary report. He had too readily yielded to the temptations of the cantina, and when he was found stretched upon the beaten earth, it was at first suspected that he was simply drunk. But his gums were coated with bloody froth... he died without having recovered either speech or the faintest glimmer of consciousness, and he was one of the many whom I was obliged to seize by the feet. “One, two, three...” Before casting him in, I should have wished to say a *Requiescat* over him — he had spared me fifty lashes of the rope. But down there in that pit it would have seemed...

I lent my hands to the burying, one by one, in that fashion, of my eight accomplices in the attempt at escape, and of others, and still others... I counted past the hundred; then I lost count and counted no more. Some poor wretch who already seemed to have crossed the frontier beyond which one suffers no more, scarce flung into that horrible common sepulchre, was seen to revive and writhe in agony!

When the fever, finding no more flesh to gnaw upon, at last disappeared, the French Deputies raised in Paris the customary humanitarian clamour. But the Minister replied to them, in a tone of triumph, that he had already given the necessary orders to transfer the entire Colony to the Île du Salut. And indeed one evening we saw, traced against the crepuscular light, the black streak of smoke from a vessel proceeding at full steam towards our shore. It was the *Abeille*, bringing us the orders of His Excellency. The following day the entire Colony was on board... Of more than six hundred souls — convicts, military, civil servants, and attendants alike — we were reduced to sixty-three! The greater part of the survivors had already suffered from yellow fever at other times and on other shores, which had rendered them, it appears, resistant if not absolutely invulnerable; several, shadows of their former selves, showed signs of having been but lately touched, if not cut down — among these, Antonio Gomez... Oh, how transformed was that face — that good-natured priestly face of his! Three alone had not carried away from it, then or before, any taint of infected blood. Among the three, the convict di Rudio! From the deck of the *Abeille*, about which the ocean already foamed and broke, I cast a last glance towards the Montagne d’Argent and think, with profound regret, of my canoe floating solitary and motionless beneath the dense lianas. The landscape dwindles rapidly. The hell that men had accumulated there was no more; what remained was one of its most baleful consequences, the vast charnel pit from which we had miraculously escaped. Nature, indifferent, spread over so much horror her enchanting hues, and in the scintillating air vibrated her mysterious amourings!


### Chapter Twenty-Three

<!-- pages:222-225 -->

<!-- pages:222-225 -->



I HAD scarcely thrown my canvas sack upon the mole of the Île du Salut when hope already flooded my breast, intense, overpowering.

The island, with a little exercise of the imagination, presented the shape of a figure 8. Upon the larger loop, which was also the more elevated, stood the dwellings, the hospital, a church; upon the smaller loop were the workshops, and along its outermost curve, the barracks. From that extremity, directed more or less exactly to the north-west, one could toss a stone by hand onto the long islet of the Diable, the same that afterwards became sadly notorious for the murky drama of which Captain Dreyfus was the victim. At low tide, it was no great difficulty, leaping upon the jagged rocks left exposed, to pass from one island to the other dry-shod.

To the east, there rose from the waters another small islet, called Saint-Joseph, an uneven, nearly circular expanse of ground, scattered with a few trees, stunted and dwarfish.

The island upon which I found myself had, measured by the eye and at its widest diameter, an extent of some two miles. Its shores were rocky and all indentations.

These were circumstances I had noted in passing. What knew how to draw my attention and to fix it so as to absorb me in deep reflection was a small rowing-boat, gliding over the waves at a short distance from the shore. She had on board four or five convicts, who were rowing and singing. I thought to myself: "What prevents these men from making for the open sea? True, one does not cross the Ocean by the strength of oars. But why do they not procure themselves a sail? I left a canoe in the waters of the Oyapock, and here I find another at the Île du Salut. Courage, di Rudio — you have lost nothing!"

I was still standing motionless, my eye fixed upon that nutshell of a boat, when I felt a touch upon my shoulder. I turned with a start and found myself face to face with a man wearing the grotesque uniform of the convict.

I scrutinised him, and he broke the silence:

— I have been told you are Italian...

— And who are you? By your accent you seem to me a Roman of Rome.

— I am from Trastevere, and proud of it!

— Why are you here?

— That is quite another matter. But may I never see my Country again, and may I die with the noose about my throat, if I would not do it all over again, should the need arise: a French dog seduced my sister and left her in trouble... I put a hole through his heart. Long live the Trastevere blood, and to the devil with all who speak ill of it! For this I lost my Rome... ten little years on my back!... but they will pass. My name is Morbioli.

We exchanged, so to speak, our visiting cards; then, all at once: — How long have you been here?

— Seven years!

— Seven years!... you are Italian, Roman, Trasteveran, and you have never attempted to escape?

— Impossible! Alone, one cannot even think of it. In company, worse than going out at night! In this accursed place, to the cowards who turn informer, not bullets but a pardon! Do you follow me? To denounce a plot leads to freedom more surely than attempting flight.

— I believed there was more solidarity among those who have fallen than among the fortunate of this world.

— Even so! It is plain you are a newcomer to this place and have no exact notion of the difficulties. Ah, do you think there have been no bold fellows with the stomach to risk it? But to what end? Listen!...

And he told me how, thirteen years before, thirteen convicts from the Île Mère, another French penal establishment situated in those parts, had seized a pilot-boat and made for the open sea. The *Abeille* gave chase, but in vain. Some three months passed, and already it was thought they had reached free land, when, on one of her crossings from Cayenne to the Île du Salut, the *Abeille* sighted a boat drifting with the current. She came alongside — and whom did she find aboard?

— Three men only. The three had devoured the other ten.

An attempt at escape had also been made overland, from the Montagne d'Argent. There were twenty of them, and once they had penetrated into the forest no one troubled himself further to pursue them. After several months, one man, gone wild, made an unexpected appearance at the penal settlement. He was the sole survivor... he confessed he had kept himself alive until then because he had, more or less thoroughly, stripped the flesh from the other nineteen. Having now nothing left upon which to set his teeth, he resolved to give himself up. They ruled that he had eaten quite enough, and... cut off his head!

An escape had also been attempted from the islet of the Diable. The son of Giacomo Pianori, forerunner of Felice Orsini in the attempt and in martyrdom, had been interned there as a suspect. He had noticed that the tide cast up upon the shore numerous tree trunks. He gathered them together and succeeded in putting together a raft. The sinuosities of the shore had lent themselves to the secret labour. He set out by night, with twenty companions. On the high seas he was overtaken by a squall. The waves broke the raft in two. One half drifted for a long time with the currents, until it ran aground down there, somewhere in Dutch Guiana. The fugitives were succoured, and... handed back to the French authorities. Chained in pits, exposed to the torrid sun and to torrential rains, they soon breathed their last amid their sufferings. Twelve corpses flung to the sharks!

The other eight, Pianori among them, also reached the Continent upon the other half of the raft; but unhappily they ran into a *vase*. Do you know what a *vase* is? A bank of bluish mud that grips like cement. The air is full of *mosquitos* capable of stripping the carcass of a whale to the bone within a few days; the ground swarms with crabs... the terrible crabs of Guiana that dig long galleries underground, invade the cemeteries, and thanks to their good fortune the corpses sleep heavily. The *Abeille*, in passage through those parts, found the raft and what little remained of Pianori and the other wretches. The crabs and the insects had not even had the courtesy to wait until they were dead, and had straightway made a feast of them!

I had listened without interrupting. Meanwhile the nutshell had drawn nearer to the island and, skirting its shore, disappeared from time to time behind some rock, only to reappear always closer. The convicts were gathering on the beach and exchanging shouts with the oarsmen. I asked: What is that boat, and what are those men about?

— They fish on behalf of the penitentiary, and when fish is plentiful they sell some, more or less covertly, to whoever wishes to buy.

He scratched his head and added: — Morbioli is no empty gourd. You are thinking that one might take advantage of that boat to try something new. Count your reckoning without our four gunboats. They would merrily take aim at you as at game. They would laugh to see you go under. And then there is the *Abeille*: a mere canoe is no match for her!

I was assigned to the anchor workshop. I worked half naked... a heat that shrivelled the skin; sparks flying from the anvil speckled my body all over. But never, as in those moments, did I understand the power of the intellect in opposing itself to painful sensations and in mastering them. The obstinate meditation upon a plan of escape transported me elsewhere, among the island's rocks, out upon the open sea, and the impressions of the workshop reached me as if enfeebled by distance. My plan was taking shape from the very history of my predecessors' failures, and it seemed to me that its chain of reasoning issued from my mind more rigid and more resistant than the iron I was hammering.

Pianori had run headlong into his dreadful fate by trusting to a raft... therefore, no raft! And why, moreover, should I undertake the slow and thankless labour of building one, when I had at hand a small vessel lacking only a scrap of sail? I reckoned that seizing her by a bold stroke need not prove an excessively difficult enterprise for men of resolute temper, all the more so since the fishermen would doubtless have resigned themselves with good grace to a piece of high-handedness that would have restored them to liberty.

True, being upon a boat had profited the other fugitives not at all; but evidently they had not known how to handle one, and they lacked provisions... it was therefore necessary to secure the complicity of some man of the sea; it was necessary to accumulate a great store of victuals.

Where? If the low-lying Île du Diable had offered a safe hiding-place to a raft, the high, broken shore of the Île du Salut might well offer one equally safe for a few sacks of bread, for a few demijohns of water and of rum.

The *Abeille*! She made regular crossings from the island to Cayenne: one must put to sea during her absence.

And the gunboats? There lay the problem! Every time I set myself to solve it my brain became barren... barren as a wrung-out sponge!


### Chapter Twenty-Four

<!-- pages:225-228 -->

<!-- pages:225-228 -->



DURING my hours of rest, I neglected nothing in order to obtain full intelligence of the men who lived and suffered about me. Antonio Gomez, having been assigned for some time to the service of the Officers, had recovered, though bronzed in colour, his good-natured priest’s face. But an unexpected order removed him from any influence I might have over him. He was dispatched, with others, to the penitentiary at Corù, on the Continent, and I had barely time to press his hand. From that day forth, though I believe the political upheavals of France may have procured him better days, I never saw him again nor heard any tidings of him.

Yet on the island there were other Italians, a dozen or so; Romans for the most part, all condemned for having redoubled their political hatred by superimposing upon it a private one; for having avenged, with our native tyranny, the outrage inflicted upon them by a foreign tyranny. Christ forgave much, it is said, to those who loved much; He ought to forgive far more to those who hated much and who, in their hatred, knew how to find the virtue of not being sheep. Among these Romans there was one Morelli, a tailor, condemned to life imprisonment. Who lends a readier ear to hope than he who has lost all hope?

I found moreover Italians condemned, or simply relegated, for political motives. They dwelt in part on the Île du Diable; but they crossed over frequently at low tide, on the pretext, more often than not, of availing themselves of the dispensary attached to the Hospital. There was Galli, there was Rossini, there was Tebaldi, and we quickly struck up a fraternal intimacy. Their history was known to me, and I could be certain that even were they not to take part in the escape, they would have aided it with every means at their disposal.

Among the men I had met during my first crossing from Toulon, and who had been landed on the island from the very beginning, was a French archpriest, condemned to life for the murder of a child of whom he had taken criminal advantage. Throughout the entire voyage, he had jealously kept under his arm a great Bible of his own. In a moment of expansiveness, he confided to me in a trembling voice that he had concealed, within the binding of that volume, something in the nature of twenty thousand francs, and that the tormenting thought of being strangled by hands covetous of what was his gnawed at him more than remorse. He intended to employ that money to procure the means of escaping. I kept it in mind and reckoned upon it. To save up the indispensable provisions from our limited daily ration required a sacrifice too grievous and too prolonged for our hunger and for my impatience. They would need to be purchased from the penitentiary stores gradually, so as not to arouse suspicion, paying into the bargain usurious prices. Why should not the archpriest furnish us with the *conquibus* — the wherewithal?

But my good fortune willed it that on the island there was another individual who might prove far more useful to me still. He was a former officer of the French navy, a rabid Orleanist, a man all daring and all nerve; he was serving a life sentence, one further circumstance which I reckoned favourable and would almost call propitious. That man had already battled storms; the Ocean held no terrors for him; it was not for him a boundless plain without pathways.

All these thoughts, spokes of a broken wheel, remained for a long while motionless and as if crystallised in my mind, until all at once they received an extraordinary impulse through the almost spontaneous formation of the very piece I had lacked; which in vain, during my long meditations, I had endeavoured to devise.

One evening, having left the workshop, I was making my way towards my barracks, which lay a good mile distant, when, having come close to the jetty, I caught sight of Morbioli bent over a gunboat, polishing its brasswork with perfect composure. The gunboats were in reality modest longboats carrying, fore and aft, a toy cannon apiece. They were lowered into the water early in the morning and hauled back up dry at five in the evening.

Morbioli, a boatman in Rome, was assigned to their cleaning. How many times I had watched him busy about those trinkets that were the bane of my existence! But only now, by some singular disposition of my spirit, do those soft rubbings of his, which had the feeling of a caress, stir up a great vexation in me, and I cry inwardly: “Stave it in, you great blockhead; sink it, if you can!”

That simple word — “sink it!” — flashed through my mind like a bolt of lightning illuminating a pitch-dark night. It was a revelation entire, and I shuddered at it.

I approached Morbioli, and I believe my face betrayed the secret start within me, for he looked at me with evident unease and said: “What on earth is the matter!” — Morbioli, when I was down there at the Montagne d’Argent, work had begun on erecting a hospital and we had already reached the whitewashing. A convict who held me in great affection was one day perched atop his ladder, brushing the façade, when from a nearby window there crept the murmur of voices. It was the Commandant, the Head Warder, and a condemned man in conference together. The whitewasher distinctly heard my name pronounced and stood stock-still to listen. I shall tell you briefly: the Commandant was guaranteeing the convict his immediate freedom provided he could succeed in implicating me in some escape attempt, in such a manner as to give the sentinels occasion to shoot me dead. — Why do you tell me this? — To persuade you that in the registers, beside my name, a cross is marked, and that even if I were a man contemptible enough to draw you into a conspiracy with the intention of denouncing you... what good would it do me? For me there would be no reprieve in any case. Now listen...

I confided to him the secret workings of my mind, the circumstances and the persons upon which I founded my hopes; the tactics which would undoubtedly make us masters of the boat and of the fishermen; the stratagem which he himself, unwittingly, putting an end to my long torments, had inspired in me. This was precisely the part I meant to entrust to his loyalty and his intelligence.

He scratched his head, as was his habit in moments of anxiety; then he raised his face, and I read his wholehearted assent in eyes that were ablaze. There and then, I received a compliment of a flavour as purely *trasteverino* as could be; the strangest that has ever been addressed to me in all my long existence: “You’re a devil! Blessed be the woman who bore you — and may you die... die murdered!”

With the assent of Morbioli, the first link of the chain was forged. The others were added one by one, slowly, but admirably.

On a cloudy night, troubled by the din of a fierce storm at sea, I slipped silently from my barracks and, armed with a chisel, crept along the rocks as far as the warehouses, a large timber structure founded in part upon piles. I tried and tried again, and managed at last to prise up one of the planking boards. I crawled inside, groping, nosing out the way like a rat, and when I emerged I had upon my back six great hammocks of stout canvas, which Morelli, the Roman tailor, found the means to sew up with perfect workmanship. We had our sail!


### Chapter Twenty-Five

<!-- pages:228-232 -->

<!-- pages:228-232 -->



It was precisely in those days that the frigate *Amazzone* made land at the island of the damned and blessed, bringing with her a wave of news from the old Europe.

Men of retrograde ideas and men of the new order had continued their fierce struggle for the conquest of the future, and great events had pressed upon one another and fixed themselves in History — though Orsini and Pieri and I and so many, so many others had been wholly eliminated from the political stage. What a fine lesson in humility! Magenta had cut its name there in letters glittering and blood-red. And I was saddened by it. Why, why — what did I discern in all this? The King of Piedmont had followed the tactics of his predecessors: to avail himself of the French to beat the Austrians; reserving the option, at a later hour, of allying himself with the Austrians so as to put a brake upon the French — and in either case, what results? A paltry aggrandisement of his Piedmont; a trifling increase in revenue for the old dynasty — that is what I discerned there! Napoleon would never have consented to have, upon the borders of the Empire, a strong Italy capable of withdrawing herself from his influence, from his predominance; Napoleon, who exalted the Church and was made in turn, in the hands of the Church, an instrument of reaction and oppression, would never have said to Italy: "Go, advance triumphantly upon the high road and reclaim thy Rome!"

Vittorio Emanuele II had insinuated, between the Italian People and its Unity, that dissolvent element which would put an end — so I thought — to the heroic times that regenerate character, and usher in those of vulgar opportunism, of craven tergiversations. He had insinuated — I felt it in my deepest presentiment — that profound scepticism which would come to invade the Latin soul, so prone to exaggerated despondency; the Latin soul that cannot shine without enthusiasm, as blood neither blazes nor turns crimson without oxygen. The glorious sign, foretold by so many elect intelligences, the longing and the torment of so many virile consciences, seemed to me fatally receded. In the best of hypotheses, between the present and that sign lay a treacherous waste, wherein liberty could take root only stunted and debased by the ineluctable demands of a Monarchy. Never, as much as in that hour, and with the garb of the outcast clinging to my flesh, did I lament that I had not known how to strike home.

An officer of the *Amazzone*, struck by my melancholy, enquired of me its reason with a kindly air.

"If Italy," I answered him, "in order to rise, however little, to liberty, had absolute need to have recourse to foreign arms, I should have preferred to see descend from the Alps men whose wrists were not, as ours, heavy with chains. Magenta will doubtless mark a memorable date in the annals of Italy; whether equally auspicious I do not know. I know that it would be auspicious if Napoleon had been beaten and destroyed there! Oh, what a provident disaster that would have been! The two Peoples would have extended their hands to one another without hidden meanings, without ulterior aims; the one, after having declared the usurper stripped of his throne; the other, after having freed itself — by the third overthrow of the Monarchy — from the equivocation, from the nightmare that possesses it and well nigh unsettles its very nature. Who could have resisted their impetus? They would have done far more than beat the Austrians and sweep away the petty tyrants who encumber the Peninsula; they would have practically abolished the Alps!"

"A generous dream!" said the officer, bowing his head. Then he leaned close and murmured in my ear words that made me start: "On board, we carry a Decree of amnesty — and it is for the political prisoners!"

An hour of suspense, and we were drawn up in line before the barracks. The Captain of the frigate delivered four sentences, alluding to the new glory with which the French arms had "covered themselves," and following these with the obligatory encomiums upon the lofty wisdom and valour of the august Sovereign. Having extolled his clemency to the skies, he proceeded to the reading of the Decree of amnesty. The Director of the penitentiary pronounced one by one, with a heartrending deliberateness, the names of those upon whom this unexpected dew descended. That of Galli, that of Rossini, that of Tebaldi, my own — were not pronounced. The Decree concerned French subjects only.

Several officers gathered about us and, expressing their regret, suggested that a petition from us, supported by the Director of the penitentiary, would in all probability be favourably received by the Emperor, who would thus remedy what was beyond all doubt an involuntary omission. Why not draw it up at once? The *Amazzone* herself would carry it to France.

Galli, Rossini, and Tebaldi consented and, then and there, informed me that they placed greater faith in their petition than in the escape attempt, which they were naturally renouncing. And this was not, from my own entirely personal point of view, the least grievous of the sorry consequences of the Franco-Piedmontese alliance and the victory of Magenta.

To remedy that defection was a delicate matter, and not one for me, who did not know sufficiently well the other inmates of the house of sorrow. I entrusted the task to the Cousin, the former officer of Louis-Philippe. He set about it with such circumspection that it required a full two months. On the first of December he informed me that all was ready. On the morning of the ninth, I and Morbioli stood at the quay watching the departure of the *Abeille*, bound for Cayenne. Her white wake was still gleaming when I turned to the proud *Trasteverino*:

"Morbioli, today we play the last card resolutely. Warn our companions, with due caution. Who exactly the latest additions are I cannot say with precision; but I know they are not of the same mettle as those we have lost. On this point I must make you a recommendation I consider important."

And I urged him to leave the archpriest aside. True, he had contributed to the expenses; but the cargo he carried in his Bible — no longer so very secretly by now — might have been capable of arousing, in others if not in ourselves, perverse instincts. To abandon him to his fate was, in all probability, to save his skin. Besides, we must not run the risk of dishonouring, by dishonest actions, an enterprise which our very enemies would have been careful, in their hearts, never to censure.

Morbioli rubbed his hands and observed that the archpriest remained a caged bird, precisely on account of that little hoard upon which he had so greatly counted to take wing. "It seems the holy unction has not infused a scrap of prophetic spirit into him." And he added: "The idea suits me well; for, to tell you the truth, that priest, in so hazardous an attempt, struck me as an ill omen. It was as though we had secured him expressly to sing us the *De profundis!*"

I gave him a few further instructions and we parted. At five o'clock, leaving the Workshop, I saw him again, busy about the gunboats. An hour later, while the multitude of the wretched sat in the refectory before their pewter plates, I stood alone in a secluded spot, upright upon the sand, with my feet in the shallows, waiting anxiously; in that low place, hemmed in between the rocks, no one could see me save from the sea.

And from the sea the oarsmen saw me. The desire for their supper lent vigour to their muscles and the little boat darted lightly over the waves, as though barely grazing them. I gave a shout and raised my fist high in the air, displaying, pinched between thumb and forefinger, a five-franc piece.

They cursed; but, induced by the Cousin, who was in command of the little crew, they changed course, and in an instant the keel scraped and stopped. "Quick — quick — what pay is there in it!"

For all answer, a whistle — and from the rocks five surly faces burst forth, such as might have struck awe into a pack of savages. Before the fishermen recovered from their surprise, they found them at their sides, blades raised. But the threat was an almost superfluous precaution. Questioned one by one, the oarsmen had for some time already made clear their ardent desire to flee. They feared their comrades, and not one had dared to utter an explicit yes. The very kernel of my enterprise consisted, therefore, in binding into a single sheaf, by bold initiative, all those pale individual assents; in transforming them into one strong, collective, and dynamic will.

When I had drawn from their hiding-place the provisions and the mast and the sail, the waverers looked one another in the face and smiled. They contented themselves with saying: "Let it be understood that we yield to force!"

It falls out precisely thus in great political and social affairs as well. How many truths are already firmly rooted in the heart of man, how many aspirations already germinating there in luxuriant growth! Yet nothing is realised until the bold arise who dare to be the forerunners. And it is this deplorable inertia, this fatal cowardice, that lends such power to the few to tyrannise over the many.

Four strokes of the oar and we were from the shore as far as a musket-shot. We were eleven. The archpriest was absent; but he was not the only one. Morelli, the Roman tailor, who had likewise rendered us such effective service, seized by a sudden fever, lay delirious in the hospital. I and Morbioli — the only Italians.

Now the rocks no longer intercepted the view between us and the shore, and the unwonted spectacle of a sailing boat revealed in an instant the attempt so jealously concealed. The entire refectory was in uproar, and in the confused symphony of cries, of laughter, of curses, borne to us upon the wind, there rang out the terrible command of an officer: "*Aux canonnières!*" A file of men issued from the refectory and stretched out, with sinister windings, towards the quay.

Our little boat flew on; yet by a manoeuvre I could not comprehend, the Cousin, instead of keeping the prow directed towards the Continent, described a wide circle, as though he intended to round the island. The convicts, who on the rocks were traversing a short chord of that circle, arrived swiftly at the quay and launched a gunboat in time to intercept our way. In other words, we were running at full force, and almost as if by design, straight into the danger.

I cast anxious eyes at the gunboat, which rode the water with an apparently perfect ease; with even greater anxiety I looked at Morbioli. He understood and said: "Trasteverino blood never fails! You see? No one goes on board because the plugs are at the bottom of the sea — they are scrambling now to find new ones at the workshop! And the keel, on the bottom — four deadly bung-holes!" And having said this, he clenched his jaws and pressed with arms outstretched into the void, as though striving to make the gunboat heavier still and to do violence to the sea.

All of a sudden he drove a fist into the air and burst into a great laugh! The four holes had done their duty; the gunboat, swamped, had found those plugs where the light of the Sun does not reach. And the dark swarming mass of men on the shore acclaimed us with a tempest of *evviva*, with a feverish waving of rags and caps.

The tormented were exulting at the defeat of the tormentors!

Now the Cousin completed his mysterious manoeuvre and turned the prow resolutely towards the North. I cried out to him nervously: "What are you doing?"

"Hold your tongue! The *Abeille* will give us chase before long, and it is off the coasts of Guiana that they will expect to intercept us. To the north, to the north! Liberty lies amidst the tempests; our safety is in danger itself!"

We sailed strenuously the whole night, always keeping towards the Pole. Great winds, great waves! The Ocean drenched us with salt spray, roaring in great voice through the darkness.

Was it warning us? Was it claiming us? Our senses, strung to a terrible tension, could not decipher it. Now it flung us upward as though wishing to hurl us beyond its dominion; now we were drawn headlong towards the abyss, as by a powerful, irresistible suction. Our sole assurance lay in the marble impassivity of the captain. No human voice, amidst that roaring fury, save his — measured, calm, cold, metallic.

The dawn found us drenched and shrivelled. But we had left behind the waters muddied by the great rivers that pour into them from the Continent, and we were already cleaving the deep blue. The Sun and reviving hope transformed us within a short hour. The Ocean might well agitate itself, roar its unceasing menace — every wave of it might engulf us! What did it matter? Every pulse of the sail might draw us ever nearer to a final, tragic struggle — for upon every face appeared the steadfast resolution to cut throats, or to have one's own cut, should the fruit of our daring be wrested from us. What did it matter? We felt ourselves free as the wind that ran through the infinite!


### Chapter Twenty-Six

<!-- pages:232-235 -->



I COULD not recount, in any perfect chronological order and as though reading from a ship's log, the manifold vicissitudes of our arduous trial. I shall speak briefly of those which determined its course most properly.

The storms, invoked by the Cousin, came, and came with such fury as to imperil vessels far stouter than our own. It was not the sea alone we had to fight; but sea and sky in league together; which battered us with a pertinacity that almost betrayed some deliberate intent.

To comprehend our peril in its full intensity, one would need to be broken to the art of navigation. For those who are, any description is superfluous; for all others, no description suffices. For two days and three nights, we found ourselves in worse conditions than the wretched convicts condemned to labour in those subterranean dungeons where water seeps in jets of calculated measure. Either work the pump without ceasing, or drown.

It was then that I understood, in its fullest import, the phrase: "And the floodgates of heaven were opened." Whoever was not keeping every fibre, every atom of himself grappled to the oars that kept us balanced, was working feverishly to bail out the boat. Our palmetto hats served us for that purpose; but the tempest had left several among us bareheaded — these had recourse to the only implement still within their reach: their wretched wooden clogs. In such a crisis, no resource, however trifling, was to be despised.

The driving rain, the waves that made any long view impossible, and above all the profound darkness, held in store for us, on the third night, yet another grave and unexpected peril. Blacker than the night itself, and like a phantom risen menacingly from the abyss, a great sailing vessel loomed before us. The Cousin had barely time to wrench the tiller; but the surge was so violent that, for an instant, we believed ourselves swallowed up and done for. The fearful apparition swept past us, gigantic and swift; then vanished, as though dissolved into the darkness.

Not even in this extremity did the Cousin lose his composure, and having remarked that, in all probability, it was a Dutch vessel, he added: "Courage, lads! In this blackness, I'll undertake to slip between the legs of Death without letting her take hold of us!"

I reflected that we owed our salvation to the fever that had confined Morelli to the hospital — had the little vessel been even slightly heavier, no human force would have been sufficient to keep her afloat. After that encounter, my companions saw a phantom on the crest of every wave.

The following day, the storm subsided. Hoisting the sail anew, we were able at last to think of restoring our exhausted strength. Through the long hours of peril we had swallowed, with difficulty, a few mouthfuls of rum, and what vigour we had was furnished, in some measure, by the danger itself. Now that the tempest had settled around us, a tempestuous hunger arose. But a terrible disillusionment awaited us: the salt water had in part swept away, and in part spoiled, our provisions, rendering them well-nigh useless.

The horrors of cannibalism then flashed grimly through our imagination, and into the air burst imprecations fit to shame those attributed to the damned of hell. The Cousin judged it more than opportune — urgent — to change course, and turned the bow to the south-west, our natural heading.

That very day we observed a long streak of dense smoke in the distance, a few spans above the surface of the water — the *Abeille*, perhaps, hastening westward in pursuit of man. We lowered the sail and, for a long hour, remained in great anxiety, pressed flat in the bottom of the canoe. At dusk, the ominous streak disappeared below the horizon. Two days later we were coasting the Continent.

At what point? Did our anxious eyes scan a hospitable shore, or did it hold in reserve for us dangers greater than those we had surmounted with such toil?

For a great distance we could discern no trace of human life; then all of a sudden, rounding a headland, there appeared spread before us a small, tranquil cove, and on the beach a little hut, and around the hut several men — some labouring to shift baskets and barrels, some spreading nets. Others, seated on the sand, were mending meshes.

We bent to the oars and came ashore. As we hauled the boat up the beach, a dozen Negroes gathered about us, their bearing not openly hostile, yet that of men who harbour some suspicion. Among them was a white man, dressed in the European fashion, armed with a musket.

I understood at once that I was among slaves employed to catch and dry fish, toiling under the lash of a white man — of a civilised man, a believer in Christ.

I asked him whether, for payment, it was possible to obtain some provisions. He had some French and understood me. But his only reply was: "Where's your money?"

I made my few coins jingle and, pressing my first question, added: "What land is this?"

— Dutch Guiana! The very land that had driven back to certain death the comrades of Pianori. My men felt their hunger no more, felt their weariness no more. They ran to the canoe and leapt into it, ready to face whatever adversity lay ahead — such was the power, even in them, of the love of liberty!

We sailed on towards the west: the wind that blew was laden with the fragrances of the Continent. We reckoned that the *Abeille* had already passed that way and was leaving us ever further behind with every stroke of her piston. The Cousin calculated moreover that British Guiana was not far distant, and our canoe was moving with such speed that it seemed more than reasonable to regard the end of our torments as close at hand.

All at once, the boat slowed, dipped her bow, and moved no more — as though a mysterious hand, having seized her by the keel, had forcibly held her back. I glanced instinctively at the Cousin. He was pale, cadaverous. To my mute enquiry he answered: "We are lost!" A thing simple and terrible had occurred — we had become fast in a *vase*. But we were not men to submit to fate without first fighting with every force at our disposal. And the Cousin himself, after that first moment of dismay, having recovered all his composure, gave us the order to leap into the water and dispose ourselves around the canoe to support it upon our shoulders. "If the tide ebbs," said he, "commend your souls to God — if you believe you have souls, and if you believe in God. If the tide rises — perhaps — Come now, all hope would not be lost!"

For a couple of hours we remained in that tenacious mud; sunk to the knee at first, then little by little to the waist, with the water at the same level. The mosquitoes, fortunately, did not swarm; but the crabs were not long in tormenting us with their pincers. There were moments of impotent rage bordering on delirium; moments of grim phantasms barely dispelled by my robust philosophy. To be eaten alive, in small morsels, or to devour one by one a thousand devouring monsters — what became, in that unequal contest, of the pride of man? The more the minutes passed, the more closely the invisible enemy pressed about us. In many and grave perils I found myself exposed, both before and after; but it was reserved for the dagger of one of my own countrymen and for the loathsome crustaceans of Guiana to pierce my flesh so deeply as to leave indelible scars.

It was our good fortune that the tide rose. The boat we had been sustaining ended by becoming our sustenance. We climbed back into her — begrimed with mud, bleeding, spent!


### Chapter Twenty-Seven

<!-- pages:235-240 -->

<!-- pages:235-240 -->

And the little vessel glides on, glides... until before our weary and tormented eyes there appeared, drawn against the blue sky, the black hull of a cutter. It had emerged as if by surprise from the shore, at so short a distance that the mere thought of evading it would have been madness. For a moment we flattered ourselves that those aboard would pay us no heed; but a cannon-shot, fired as a warning, blew away every illusion. The little herring moved to meet the shark, the shark moved to meet the little herring... the distance vanished so rapidly, and before long we were able to make out the flag flying from the stern-mast.

Hearts aloft! It was the symbol of pride and of liberty; of humanity and of progress... the English flag!

Leaning over the rail of his cutter, a Negro captain, surrounded by half a dozen Negro sailors, demanded of us, in a tone of command, who we were and whether we had on board any goods subject to customs. When I had answered him, he drew himself upright in astonishment and, pointing his forefinger: "You cannot mean to tell me that you have covered more than a thousand miles of sea on that hand-span of hollow wood?"

I indicated in turn our trousers which, despite the coat of mud that covered them, revealed here and there the place of our origin. Once persuaded, he expressed a desire to question us at greater length aboard his cutter. It was like touching an electric button... we all sprang to our feet simultaneously. But whether he had no wish to besmirch his vessel with that species of ambulant *vase*; or whether our appearance put him on his guard, he cried out: "Not all, not all! You come aboard — you who have been speaking." I was the only one who spoke English.

On board, I had the opportunity to recount our adventures to him in full detail, and to request of him, by way of conclusion, assistance for our most pressing needs. He listened without interrupting me, giving expression, at the dramatic moments, to his emotion in the manner natural to his race: by bursting into unrestrained laughter. When I fell silent, he began: "Well, well, well, well!..." But he broke off and, turning to the sailors, ordered them to lower a sack of ship's biscuit and some fresh water into the dinghy.

I asked him for precise directions to reach the nearest English port and, to my intense relief, was informed that we were not far from the mouth of the Berbice and the town that takes its name therefrom. "Once you have rounded the headland that lies dark over there, the town will rise up suddenly before you. The tide will be going out then. Keep this side of the buoys and you will run no danger whatsoever."

The cutter's crew, made up of sailors who had once been slaves, felt sympathy for us. After the biscuit was lowered they vied with one another in throwing us tobacco and other small gifts, and when the dinghy got under way we were saluted by a chorus of "good bye" and "good luck" that for some moments drowned out even the resonance of the sea itself. We showed our gratitude by breaking the hard crusts on the spot with crocodile teeth.

And the little vessel glides on, glides... until it enters the turbid, yellowish waters that announce the mouth of the Berbice as imminent. My companions, rather than rejoicing at this, began to give voice to the strangest apprehensions. Who could assure us, among other things, that the Negro captain had not taught us how to blunder headlong into some trap?

We fell in with a fishing smack. Two small, sun-bronzed men, Portuguese in appearance, were hauling in their nets. "Which course must we steer to avoid the current and enter the river without danger?"

We heard, in different words, instructions identical to those of the Negro captain. Half an hour later, before our astonished eyes, Berbice, or New Amsterdam, spread itself out upon either bank. On one side, low houses set on piles, among which, evidently, the water crept in at high tide; on the other, the broad and solid government buildings. In the middle, several anchored vessels drew against the sky the spars of their yards.

We did not remain long in doubt as to which direction to steer. We had barely entered when a launch came swiftly towards us, in which stood upright an old man with long white side-whiskers. He was surrounded by a dozen policemen. In an instant he was upon us and the old man, shaking his honest whiskers: "Who are you? Whence do you come?"

As though it wished, once and for all, to have done with the matter, fate had steered us directly into the path of the Governor. Who, perceiving us so dilapidated, aboard a vessel flying no flag of any kind, and supposing us to be shipwrecked men, had approached to obtain reliable news of us and to render us whatever assistance might be required.

I answered him in four brief words, and he, with that English tact which never permits a man to give a direct contradiction:
"You surely cannot mean to say that you come from the Île Royale?" Then he turned to his helmsman, the only one of his following with a white complexion, the only one dressed in civilian clothes, and said to him: "What do you make of it? In a shell like that! Old England has set the example!"

He sent one of his guards aboard our dinghy and, having given orders to prepare us a substantial meal at the police station, made a sign, and his launch darted away, nimble as a fish.

The police station was situated on the left bank, near the wharf, and four strokes of the oar sufficed to bring us there. But from the French oaths chewed between my companions' teeth, I could not help noticing that the dinghy was proceeding contrary to their wishes. True enough, the "substantial supper" was a splendid prospect; but the escort of Negro policemen smacked too much to them of a dismal prelude for them not to suspect that they were about to slide from the table into some large room locked with a double turn of the key. And matters grew worse when, upon arrival, a zealous officer in gold braid launched into formal questions, writing down in a register the answers which I translated for him. Had we then faced death so many times only to tumble so wretchedly out of the frying-pan into the fire?

To convince them they were not prisoners, I made the pretext of wishing to purchase tobacco and went out. Then, lightened of every excessive preoccupation, I had leisure to observe my surroundings more carefully. It was the 15th of December, 1859, and the luxuriant vegetation pressed close about the settlement, as though intending to dominate it and flood it with deep green. Superb, impenetrable forests extended, as far as the eye could reach, along both banks, and the seawater issued from them in little cascades, returning with the ebb-tide to the sea. The sky, a deep blue, had to the west the glare of a thousand conflagrations.

The news of our arrival had spread rapidly and I was soon surrounded by a crowd eager for particulars. My convict's garb, filthy as though I had wallowed in a pigsty, aroused no disgust whatsoever. I was addressed with no questions that were not honourably discreet; I saw none but faces marked by the most genuine sympathy. And there was one man who offered me a thousand *duros* for the cession of our dinghy.

I cut short the conversations and the handshakes to hasten at the call of a policeman, who announced to me that supper was ready. Before the profusion of fresh bread, of ham, of cheese, and of beer spread out upon the table, every trace of disquiet seemed to melt away from my companions' breasts. Every last crumb was devoured in deep silence, amid the jests of the policemen, who were astonished and rendered merry by that rapid, phenomenal disappearance.

The dishes were already being cleared away when an unexpected visit plunged us back into painful uncertainty. The old Governor with the long white side-whiskers had entered silently and stood before us, conducting a mute inspection. At his back was a group of citizens, whose sentiments in our regard were perfectly concealed behind impassive faces.

We remained motionless and expectant. The Governor began by expressing, in French, the following thoughts: "You have come to a land that does not turn away those who seek asylum within it. Respect the Laws of the Country and the Country will not fail in its duties of hospitality. But one guarantee is required of you... that of labour. Labour and thrift smooth the path of honest men, whatever depths a man may have fallen to."

He was silent for a moment, then proceeded to interrogate us one by one, asking us not our names, not the nature of our sentences, but the occupation we would be capable of honourably discharging. As each of my companions declared his trade, the Governor would turn to one or another of the citizens who had accompanied him and say to him: "This one I entrust to you... and this other to you."

My turn came. I answered: "My profession is that of the sword."

— What do you mean by that?

— That I was educated at the military college of San Luca, in Milan.

— Where did you learn English?

— In England, where I have a wife and a son.

— You are Italian, you have a family in England, and you have escaped from a French penitentiary. What sort of muddle is this?

Thereupon I revealed my name and the cause of my condemnation. His face grew dark: "I believe you are mistaken. The Count di Rudio is dead." I was about to reply,
I was about to reply, when a young man of handsome appearance, notwithstanding the hue of his complexion which called to mind chocolate — he was a mulatto — interposed and said in perfectly correct French: "If Your Excellency will permit me, I will take this man into my house. I stand surety for him."

That courteous man was the physician of New Amsterdam. He had completed his studies at Brussels, where he had managed to acquire, alongside his degree, the love and hand of a charming Belgian young lady. He was a free-thinker, the proprietor of a local newspaper, and correspondent of the London *Times*... the last of these, perhaps, not the least of the reasons that had prompted him to come to my aid with such eagerness.

He assigned me a comfortable bedroom, where I had at last the opportunity to scrape off — literally! — the crust of the savage, and to restore my natural complexion to the surface.

Thus transformed, I was presented to his wife. She struck me for her very fine figure and for her refinement of culture, which was polished, even though she had been educated in a convent. She spoke Italian with a sweetness unusual on foreign lips, and was more than merely an amateur in drawing melodies from the pianoforte.

The hour preceding the brief tropical dusk I spent in friendly conversation, recounting, at the request of my hosts and of the visitors who had gathered on account of the unusual circumstance, now this, now that adventure of mine. At a certain point the lady, who was probably intent upon extricating me from the excessive importunity of the curious, said aloud: "The Count di Rudio, as an Italian, doubtless loves music." And without more ado she seated herself at the piano.

What enchantment!... and how expertly the human heart shows itself in lulling importunate phantoms to sleep and in reviving the rosy phantoms that have been lulled! I still had in my ear the language of the prison-hulk; I still could see, so to speak, at my feet, the chain barely broken; the soul still quivered from the torments endured... many of them, left untold because I shrink from the posture of the martyr... those agile fingers, which materially ran over the keys, were virtually skimming the hidden fibre within me that ached, as lightly as the fingers of a mesmerist. And I tasted the positive sweetness that is coupled with the cessation of all torment. There were notes, too, which, reverberating in the depths, called up an imprecation, a groan, a blood-stained phantom, the roar of a tempest; but all of it appeared softened and rendered harmonious to me by time and by hope...

I believe I shed tears over it; I cannot recall how many. I felt the mastery of art profoundly, because I too belonged to art... I had opened my mouth wide among the choristers of Drury Lane!

That enchantment was of brief duration. An acquaintance of the doctor's came rushing in to announce that a French warship had moored at the wharf. "Let us go and see her!" was the unanimous cry. And we departed. A few minutes later, in the twilight, the menacing outlines of the *Abeille* loomed gigantic before my eyes.

The tactic adopted by Gousin of steering our dinghy northward had produced its effect. When, compelled by the spoilage of our provisions, we turned back towards the South American continent, the *Abeille* had already passed beyond the tangent we had traced. Not finding us at New Amsterdam, she had pushed on as far as the waters of Venezuela, and the captain, disillusioned, was already returning on his course when, passing again before the Berbice, he had fallen in with the smack and the Portuguese. From them he learnt of our arrival and had come up the river to demand my extradition.

There was no doubt of it. At the rail of the *Abeille* stood, side by side, two odious figures... the warders of my gang. For what reason were they on board, if not to identify me? They caught sight of me and were animated by a sudden ferocity: "There he is, there he is!" exclaimed one of them, pointing at me with his finger. And he added: "Hey, you — the one with the bombs! Before long you will be up here and tasting your medicine again!" This taunt was not enough for him. He came ashore and, advancing towards me with his right hand extended and a diabolical smile on his ugly mouth, congratulated me on the masterliness of our escape. A courtesy which I repaid by turning my back on him.

That same evening, a policeman presented himself to the doctor and announced that the Governor required my immediate presence at Government House. My host consulted with the friends who had remained until that hour, then said: "Let us go to Government House in a body. Some act of weakness... one never knows!"

I traversed the long road with feverish anxiety. Having then climbed the steep stairs, I saw in the ante-chamber — a dismal preamble! — my two warders. A few minutes later I found myself in a great hall in the presence of the Governor.

Beside his writing-desk stood a man in uniform... the captain of the *Abeille*.

The Governor said: "Is this the fugitive you are seeking?"

I protested: I had never sought to conceal my identity!

— I beg you not to speak when you are not being questioned.

And the captain: "I do not know him personally. But I have with me two warders who will be able to reply to Your Excellency in categorical terms."

One of them was shown in. Who, imagining that I had lied about my identity, without waiting for any question, cried out: "It is he! The Count di Rudio in person... himself and no other!"

— You may go.

The second warder enters and the denunciation is renewed.

The Governor rose to his feet: "Count di Rudio, I had doubted your word. I ask your pardon for it." Then, turning to the other: "Captain, England does not accord the extradition of political prisoners. I am sorry... but I am unable to give you any other answer!"

I was free, definitively free! And in the excess of the joy which seemed to engulf me, instead of drawing myself up to the full dignity of a man restored to his rightful place, returned to those he loved, to the sacred struggles on behalf of his ideals, I became suddenly a child again. To the captain as he moved away giving me a stern glance, I directed that mischievous act of derision which consists in placing one's thumb against the tip of one's nose and waggling one's fingers.

The following morning... two important pieces of news. The *Abeille* had weighed anchor but, in departing, had left behind at New Amsterdam a spy charged, certainly, with reporting on my movements. Some precaution was necessary; for if the Napoleonic claws were to seize me again, even illegally, there was no diplomatic note powerful enough to save me.

The other piece of news, though it did not directly concern me, made a greater impression upon me.
