My companions in escape, terrified by the spectre of extradition, had crept back in the night to the dinghy, in order to put out to sea again in secret. It was a grave error... the French authorities, having demanded my person, had taken no account of them whatsoever. According to the doctor, they had beyond all doubt steered their course towards some port of Venezuela. Did they reach it?

I believe that their virile mettle made them worthy of the liberty they had yearned for so ardently. But I never had any further word of them, and for me it was as though the Ocean had swallowed them whole.


### Chapter Twenty-Eight

<!-- pages:240-245 -->



IN the afternoon, I saw the old Governor once more. Inspired by the boundless democracy of the English, who are nevertheless so aristocratic in their bearing, he had thought it his duty to descend to my level, in order to congratulate in person the political prisoner reborn to civil life. And having gathered, from what the doctor himself had told him, that my departure for England by ordinary means was not without its dangers, he offered to secure me a place aboard a man-of-war which would make for the capital of the Empire no later than March.

With this, he took his leave. But before departing he placed in my hands, without a word of comment, a large volume of his, bound in black morocco. It was... a Bible. The good old man did not feel he had done enough by contributing to my material liberation; he would also have wished to cast into my soul the seeds of a moral fermentation. Although I considered, and still consider, that his pious desire, had it taken root and flowered, would have, so to speak, unmanned me, I could not nevertheless refrain from feeling a sense of gratitude for the disinterested nobility of his intention. From the window I watched him until he disappeared. Many times I have wished to see him again, to prove to him that the good he did was not turned to ill. But he was already then some seventy years of age, and who can say how long since the virtues and follies of the world have ceased to move him! He left upon me an indelible impression, not only because with him begins a new period of my existence, but also because, in so brief a time, he knew how to reveal to me many facets of his character. His admiration for bold deeds performed at sea; the pride which led him, while not disowning the merits of others, to proclaim in our presence the superiority of his own race; his humane feelings; his conception of the nobility of labour and of the regenerating influence of parsimony; his faith in the rehabilitation of the fallen; his abhorrence of falsehood; his spontaneous redress of an offence, however slight, given to one who stood in a social condition so far beneath his own; the respect he showed for the rights of the oppressed; even his tendency towards mysticism — all these were the synthesis of the virtues and faults of a People... of a great People.

In my head, amid so great a kaleidoscope of men and things, a certain confusion of names has crept in. I may be mistaken; but I believe he was called Melville...

To wait until March... a plan wholly irreconcilable with my impatience! A careful examination of circumstances led us to frame the following scheme: there was still lying in the Berbice an English brig, the *John Romelly*, Captain Kandel, which had been wishing to sail for London on Christmas Eve, but could not, for want of the prescribed complement of sailors. The doctor would go and parley with the captain, and would persuade him to enter me on the ship's roll. I was to remain, until the departure, shut in my room, feigning illness; and, the better to deceive the watchful eye of the French spy, the doctor would publish in his newspaper that I had joined a caravan of rubber-gatherers which was, on the morrow, to set out on an expedition into the interior.

Events surpassed even the bounds of our desire. The caravan departed. Not many days had passed before there arrived the grim news that it had been annihilated by savages. The doctor seized upon this to publish that I too, bloodless and flayed, had passed into the number of the dead. The French spy vanished from New Amsterdam. But the *Times* of London reproduced the tragic little tale, and for the second time poor Elisa was compelled to make a terrible effort not to believe the plausible, if not the true.

Christmas Eve arrived. At nightfall I embraced my courteous host and, craving her leave, kissed the hand of the gentle creature who, repeatedly, during those days of painful inaction, had soothed my fevers, stirring in my breast a broad wave of poetry. At dawn the *John Romelly* was far away, and around me I could see nothing but the sea.

It was reckoned that the crossing would require from twenty-two to twenty-five days. Fierce storms drove us off our course. Our daily ration was cut by half, and it was good fortune to have been able to signal a steamship which resupplied us with victuals. We came in sight of the Thames after more than two months at sea.

How many tragic events had come to pass since the day I had traversed those same waters in the opposite direction, summoned by the patriotic voice of Felice Orsini! Now, as then, the elements seemed to be seized with a nostalgia for chaos, and with such fury as to mark a memorable date in the history of maritime disasters. Death and destruction had preceded us, and we descried unmistakable traces thereof in the masts rising, in more than one spot, above the surface of the water; in the wreckage that drifted wildly around us.

Towards ten o'clock in the evening of the 29th of February, 1860, I set foot at last upon the docks of that immense capital, where I knew not a single person who believed me yet alive. Of the tumultuous events crowding upon one another at that hour, above all in my own Fatherland, I knew nothing. In my pocket I had a couple of shillings borrowed from the mate of the *John Romelly*; my breeches, those of the convict prison, I had reinforced and adorned. From so humble a beginning was to unfold what I might call my second existence. And in my soul, alongside a confused impulse towards action and an imperious longing to fling myself into beloved arms, there was the bitterness of having arrived alone. My thoughts tore me from the infinite sweetness I cherished in imagination, to carry me back to that fatal land, where two martyrs with severed heads lay dishonoured beneath the earth, enduring the vicissitudes of matter.

An hour later, the miseries of life and political hatreds had reclaimed me.

My first steps were directed to the offices of the *Times*, to pay a debt of gratitude and to announce to friends, who might perhaps still take an interest in me, my resurrection. Elisa, as I supposed, must be at Nottingham, with her mother, and whether I resolved to go there myself, or to write bidding her come to London, a few pounds were indispensable to me. To procure them, I was counting on Doctor Simon Bernard. This thought induced me to enter a café in the West End which I knew to be frequented by Frenchmen.

While I was consuming my refreshment, I perceived that my attire, more than threadbare, was provoking the smiles and unflattering comments of the other patrons. Whose astonishment must have reached its height when a young man, entering noisily, ran to my table, crying: "Oh, Moretto, you here? And I who wept for you as dead!" No sooner said than he was embracing me and kissing me.

It was that Rossi against whom Foschini had aimed the thrust which had passed through my breast. From the pavement, glancing in through the glass panes, between the green curtains, out of simple curiosity, he had seen and recognised me. Pressing and tumultuous were the questions he put to me; but no less pressing those which I put to him. From him I learned that Simon Bernard had stood trial; that my wife had appeared in it as a witness; that the people and the press had protested in terms so energetic against that manifest subjugation of old England to the will of Napoleon III as to provoke not only the acquittal of the accused, but also the fall of the Palmerston Ministry.

Since that time, Bernard had puffed himself up and was noisily posturing as a hero. He attributed to himself the principal rôle in the Orsini conspiracy, which, by his account, would not have failed if...

At this point Rossi broke off, and then, resuming the thread of the discourse: "Bernard no longer frequents this café. If you wish to speak with him, I know where to find him. But I cannot for the life of me understand how he could be of use to you... In short, do you want me to speak plainly? He holds you to blame for having sold yourself to the French Police and for having denounced your accomplices."

— You know where to find him? Take me to him at once.

Doctor Simon Bernard had organised a small club which met in modest premises off Leicester Square. To reach it one had to pass through a tobacconist's shop. The proprietress, of whom I enquired, looked me up and down and refused me passage. She sent however to give notice to Bernard, who did not keep me long waiting. As he was descending the stairs, I noted from his surprise that he had recognised me, and I noted likewise that, in order to keep up, as the saying goes, with the times, he had suffered two enormous moustaches to grow in the style of Vittorio Emanuele II.

— Good evening, Bernard.

— With whom have I the honour of...

— You know full well. At any rate I shall tell you...

And seized by an impulse of indignation, I grasped him with both hands by his long whiskers, crying: "This will convince you that I am not a shadow of myself, but Carlo di Rudio in flesh and blood!"

It is easy to understand that, after such methods of a somewhat unforensic eloquence, the discussion could not proceed in gentle tones. But the place and respect for the lady did not allow me to prolong a discussion which resembled far too closely a brawl. When he had menacingly warned me not to make a scene if I did not wish to run the risk of being expelled from England, and when I had answered him with a flourish of insults, that I had no need whatsoever to make a scene, since I had already charged the *Times* to make one on my behalf, we ended by establishing by mutual agreement that the following evening at eight o'clock we should meet at the corner of Leicester Square, to settle our affair thoroughly — and coward be he who failed to appear!

I passed the night in Rossi's room, seething and writing. In the morning, resolved to seek out Giuseppe Mazzini, I went to Leather Lane to ask Massarenti for information about him.

The latter was unloading a consignment of cheeses when I appeared before him. He had read the *Times* and already knew of my escape and arrival. He lavished great flatteries upon me; but when, cutting him short, I asked for the Maestro's address, I received the reply that he had not seen him for a long time, and did not know in what part of the world he had gone to ground.

The great Italian revolution, which had cast upon London so many of my friends, so many patriots, had by now drawn almost all of them back again, as into a vortex.

Evening came. At the appointed hour I turned the corner into Leicester Square and descried, through the fog, beneath a lamp-post, Simon Bernard in close conversation with two strangers. He saw me and invited me to follow him. "It is a beastly cold! In a public house," he said, "we shall not catch a chill and shall have ample opportunity to come to an understanding."

After walking in silence for a couple of blocks, I suddenly turned round... the two strangers were following us. We walked another couple of blocks; I turned round again and the two were still following us. I stopped short, and the moment the two realised they had been observed, they planted themselves before a shop window and stood gazing at it. I resumed my walk, and the two resumed theirs.

Then I flared up, and flashing a silent weapon which the provident Rossi had slipped into my pocket, I said to Bernard: "What game is this? Who are these gentlemen? Take care not to trifle with an Italian... If they come closer, I shall run you through."

Not satisfied with this, I advanced with sudden resolution towards the two strangers, who had now stopped before a stationer's shop, and placing myself abruptly between them, I stared each of them in the face in turn.

From that moment I was followed no longer, and at the first public house I said to Bernard: "Let us go in here. Now indeed we shall be able to speak freely."

But our conversation turned out very differently from what I had expected. Doctor Bernard, a crafty, wary, double-dealing man, shrinking from acknowledging his own conduct and from assuming responsibility for it, incapable of confronting manfully an adversary and an equivocal situation of his own making — a virtue, this, which adorns the breast even of brigands! — opposed to my impetuosity diplomatic equivocations, protestations of the deepest sympathy, extravagant offers of assistance. I was not to give credence to the foolish tittle-tattle of ill-wishers; I was to go to Nottingham, to hasten to bring an unhoped-for joy to my wife, who certainly had great need of it. He would confer with friends and write to me to communicate some definitive plan in my favour.

And he managed to slip from my grasp. I believe that, even as he extended his right hand to me, he was already meditating the intrigues which, in his design, were to hoodwink me and send me to my ruin.

Oh, let me be permitted to pass for a moment beyond these miseries. There trembles in my soul now a remembrance of a wholly different nature... in the face of it, the baser passions find no room in the heart.

I had written to my good mother-in-law at Nottingham to announce my arrival. In the envelope I had enclosed a letter for Elisa. The morning after my second colloquy with Bernard, scarcely had I thrown back the bedclothes when I descried upon the carpet near the door, where it had been slipped beneath, a letter addressed to me. It was the mother-in-law's reply. Having given vent to her own happiness at my escape, she informed me that Elisa was in London, at the Mancherinis' house. She had forwarded my letter to her, giving her at the same time my address.

I was so moved by this that in dressing, my fingers could not find their way to a single buttonhole. And now, while I was drying my hands, the knocker on the street door beat repeated blows. I distinguished the step of the landlady going to open; then a woman's voice asking whether a young man of such-and-such description was lodging in that house... An harmonious voice... hers!... But why did she not pronounce my name?

Perhaps she doubts, I thought, that I, out of prudence, have given a name not my own. With a single bound I was on the threshold... and I knew that excessive happiness seizes the heart with a rough hand, tyrannically, producing sensations that resemble pain. I saw her... but dimly, and as in a dream; I wished to cry to her: "I promised you... here I am!" but the voice would not come; I made to fling myself forward with outstretched arms, but my arms turned to lead and my feet remained nailed to the floor.

Elisa too stood motionless for some moments... a mortal pallor drained the colour from her face. Suddenly she was before me; but, having taken a few steps, her head fell upon her breast and her knees gave way beneath her... She fell senseless into my arms. And when she came to herself, as I kissed her and wept: "Elisa, Elisa! Honest as I was; more glorious — that they will not grant, and little does it matter. But ever thine; and in every fibre of my heart, a shrine for thee!"

At that moment a thought flashed through my mind. Should I find my Fatherland united, free, great, as I had known how to find again my love?


### Chapter Twenty-Nine

<!-- pages:245-249 -->

<!-- pages:245-249 -->



HOWEVER vast my desires, for the moment I could do nothing other than seek a way to establish myself decently in London. Soon the arts of Bernard put me in such a position that, far from combating the enemies of Italian integrity, I was compelled to provide, with all urgency, for the defence of my own personal integrity.

The calumnies of which I had received, from Rossi, the first inkling, were at once confirmed to me anew, with a greater abundance of particulars, by various other persons. Doctor Bernard not only persisted, in private, in the assertions he had launched when he believed me already beneath the earth; but he attempted to lend them credence by maintaining that I had regained my liberty, not through a premeditated and boldly accomplished escape, but because the French Authorities, in recompense for my denunciations, had, so to speak, thrown wide the doors of the penal colony.

Elisa informed me furthermore that the gentlemen of the Times and others — the very same who had initiated the petition for my pardon — had crowned their work by collecting, in her favour, a small capital of one thousand one hundred pounds sterling. From such generosity, and from the sort of allowance settled upon her on the eve of my departure for Paris, she had drawn no relief whatsoever. During my long absence, Bernard had delivered to her a single, solitary pound sterling.

Upon whom was the blame to be laid? Was I to consider Bernard's conduct towards me as inspired by the terror of a wicked man? Did he slander me because he found himself incapable of defending himself honestly? To what end did his cowardly manoeuvres tend?

I wrote, on this matter, to several conspirators of the English Committee; but not one deigned to answer me — whether because Bernard had forestalled me; or because secret ties induced them to treat the question exclusively in secret assembly; or because human selfishness counselled them to imitate the worthy Pilate, of traditional memory.

I must make a half-exception for Tomaso Allsop, the man who had lent his own name to Felice Orsini. And I say half, because he sent me a letter containing very courteous phrases and — a banknote of five pounds sterling; but not the slightest allusion to the pressing questions I had put to him.

Having weighed my situation thoroughly, I concluded that to insist upon the question of the one thousand one hundred pounds might give the impression that my agitation was intended to satisfy venal instincts. I therefore resolved not to raise it again, and to combat Bernard openly, solely on the other question. As judge between his assertions and mine, I would call upon the English public.

Rossi, who did not cease to take an interest in my fortunes, introduced me to a certain Kinaer, a journalist already reduced to extremity by tuberculosis; but, intellectually, still full of vigour. He, guided by the information I furnished, wrote a succinct account of the Orsini trial and of my escape. A German publisher undertook to send a printed copy to the most widely read English newspapers. This preliminary accomplished, I embarked with Kinaer upon a tour of lectures, beginning with Nottingham, my wife's city, and proceeding thence through Derby, Birmingham, Manchester, Lee, and other manufacturing centres. Kinaer attended, with great dexterity, to the financial side of the enterprise. I had reserved to myself the task of setting forth to the audience the history of my affairs, and of answering whatever questions might be put to me when I had done speaking.

I never failed to make it plain that I had resolved to speak in public, not to make money, but to provoke a discussion from which I had nothing to fear. And I challenged my accusers to come forward and oppose, to my defence, their alleged documents and their arguments.

Did this ever come to pass? A certain Sherman, who had attended one of my lectures, spoke of it at a Club of Freethinkers to which he belonged, observing how contrary it was to the innate sense of justice of the English people to witness the spectacle of a man struggling under the weight of an infamous accusation, and not to lend him a strong hand so that he might find himself face to face with his detractors.

The *Reasoner*, the organ of that club, then directed by Hollyoak, reproduced that sentiment in one of its issues which came under my eyes at Birmingham. Immediately, I wrote to Hollyoak declaring myself most willing to submit to the judgement of whatever Committee he might be pleased to appoint. At the same time, I expressed the wish that Giuseppe Mazzini might be included as a member of that same Committee — he who, knowing thoroughly the inner workings of our revolutionary endeavours, and also, in some measure, my own public career, would greatly have facilitated the rendering of a dispassionate and impartial verdict.

Three events, in rapid succession, led me to consider the affair fully resolved, notwithstanding the well-known proverb: “Calumniate, calumniate, calumniate, etc.”

Among the convicts I had known on the Île Royale du Salut, there was a Le Roux, a Frenchman who, shortly after my escape, had been transferred to the penitentiary of Corù. From that latter place he had contrived to escape and take refuge in England. Having heard of the rumours circulating on my account, he had, of his own accord, set about confuting them, declaring himself an eye-witness of my “coup d’état,” as he called it. Narrating its particulars, he added those of the sequel it had upon the life and the rigours of the penal colony.

Now, however absurd it may appear, the faith that had not been accorded to the words of an Italian patriot was freely given to those of a French convict. It is somewhat humiliating; but I shall not complain of it — the strangeness redounded to my advantage and to that of the truth. And thus the little tale of “the doors thrown wide” collapsed.

The second incident had its origin in Giuseppe Mazzini. The editor of the *Reasoner*, having conferred with him, wrote to me and published that a jury of honour was superfluous. A dispassionate examination of the circumstances, together with the attestations of the Maestro, had placed my innocence beyond all dispute.

The third was brought about, unexpectedly, by Simon Bernard himself. “He who is born of a hen must needs scratch.” The funds entrusted to his care by a certain secret society vanished in a manner considered not in the least mysterious. He was abandoned by his very own supporters, lost his reason, and — *si vera sunt exposita* — died in a madhouse. This reticence is rendered necessary by the fact that I never troubled to verify, in person, the death certificate relating to him...

Let us refresh ourselves in the contemplation of a very different conspirator. During the wanderings to which I have alluded, I made the personal acquaintance of Baron Egassy di Torocfalda, the inventor of the Orsini bombs, the technical mind of our conspiracy. If the martyr, in his trial, attributed the original idea to himself, the reason is to be sought in his honest circumspection. I have already remarked that he never named any person not already compromised.

Baron Egassy was a native of the Carpathian mountains and had been a lieutenant-colonel of Austrian artillery. A great liberal and a man of action, but rough, soldierly to the extreme, he detested all those who pretended to vindicate their rights by clamouring in the public squares, or to foment revolutions by preaching the postulates of philosophy. His muscular strength surpassed the bounds of credibility.

He maintained that the best way of disposing of a despot is to approach him on some plausible pretext; to place, with lightning swiftness, one hand upon his shoulder, the other upon his head, and to snap his vertebrae with a single sharp blow. Unfortunately it is exceedingly difficult to bring Brutus back to life and to endow him, into the bargain, with the muscles of an Egassy di Torocfalda.

He possessed, among his other gifts, that of being a chemist of no small distinction. Not only did he teach Felice Orsini the method of manufacturing mercury fulminate, but he had also invented a mixture which, on exploding, would asphyxiate all those within a circuit of five hundred feet in diameter. I believe it was something akin to modern lyddite. He was unwilling to reveal its secret to Governments, because they would have used it — he said — to perpetuate the oppression of Peoples.

One day, stigmatising with wrathful phrases the sheepishness of those petty politicians who strain so mightily to vindicate the right of “free speech,” while remaining deaf and dumb and blind to the far more vital question of the liberty of action, he ended by promising me that, sooner or later, he would confide to me the formula of his explosive.

Uttered in a moment of expansive agitation, I attached no great weight to that promise. A few days later we parted, and I thought no more of it. To my surprise, in 1862 I received a letter from him. He begged me to hasten without delay to the Hospital at Birmingham. He lay there in a grave condition and wished to keep his promise — to confide to me “the secret.” I departed by the first train; but scarcely had I entered that house of sorrow when my eagerness was unexpectedly arrested by an attendant. “Baron di Torocfalda? He died this morning.”

I wished to see him. His floridness, his portentous strength, more than extinguished and destroyed by the onset of an overpowering malady, seemed rather to have evaporated — the yellowish skin clung to the bones as though glued fast. Observing him, I could discern the slow work of hunger; though the physicians, taking their pretext from the final collapse, had attributed it to an entirely different cause, distinguished by some name drawn from the Greek which I no longer recall.

And yet that man possessed the secret of a formula which might have restored him to his former wealth. To follow the current, to confide — who could have blamed him? For centuries, every great invention has been turned, principally, to profit and to subjugation. Progress the exclusive possession of a few; science a prostitute, like religion, like human Justice. It seems fated!... But not with impunity did the poor Baron enclose within his heart a spark of greatness!

He had preferred to die of want among the destitute; forgotten, without glory — if glory consists in the applause of men!

To tear myself away from that corpse, already well-nigh in dissolution, I was forced to make an effort. His eyes were wide open, as though he had died with his thoughts fixed upon my arrival and were waiting for me still. I wished to close them and was seized with a shudder — it seemed to me that, in drawing down the thin veil of his eyelids, I had laid the last touches upon the handiwork of death; that with my own fingers I had thrust that exceptional man into eternity!


### Chapter Thirty

<!-- pages:249-252 -->



IN THE very year of my arrival in London, the French Police made, as far as I was concerned, their last attempt at corruption. My finances were then turning for the worse. The five pounds from Allsop had vanished; the few savings made during my round of lectures, vanished! Elisa, on the point of becoming a mother a second time, required great care. Fortunately, the help which, out of a sense of pride, I no longer solicited from my compatriots, I received from Le Houx, the former French convict. Having found employment in a marble saw-mill, he had induced his employer to take me on for polishing slabs. But my inexperience thinned my wages. Made at such a moment, the move of the French Police was not wanting in tactics. The Inspector who entered my modest dwelling proceeded to the attack without any beating about the bush, cards on the table, with a rough frankness worthy of a better cause. He dwelt upon my isolation, upon the ingratitude of the persons at whose “instigation” I had faced the scaffold, upon the indigence revealed by my lodgings and my appearance. Was it worth sacrificing myself for people who, in the best of cases, cared nothing for my sacrifices? He understood perfectly well how I might harbour ill feelings against the Government of Napoleon III; but that Government harboured none at all towards me. A single word, and all my misery would be ended, and I could restore my wife to the comfortable station she deserved, that was hers by right.

And he lent greater weight to his words by drawing from his pocket a handful of pounds sterling, which would — he said — be periodically renewed.

But cunning had failed to reckon into its calculations one subtle and imperious coefficient: the honest conscience that changes the nature of the sufferings endured for a worthy cause and converts them into profound satisfactions; that changes the nature of the satisfactions born of wicked deeds and converts them into unspeakable torments.

My Elisa? She would without question have disdained the comfort offered at the price of my honour. What was a fistful of gold, set against her love? Fortunate the man who can, in difficult moments, see himself mirrored in the serene eye of a woman worthy of that name! He passes over the social mire as the Christ of legend upon the waters, with feet uncontaminated and dry!

Once the importunate pressure of the Police had ceased, there came that yet more nauseating pressure of the priest. One day, as I was busy at my writing-desk, I found one underfoot — affable, insinuating, a master at dissembling the state of his soul beneath a smile... that smile irritated me from its very first appearance. Whereupon I, cutting short the opening phrases in which he expressed his pleasure at making my personal acquaintance, asked him, point-blank, in what way I might serve him.

— My parishioners are all, in a way, my children. I have left the ninety-nine sheep safe upon the mountain...

A new interruption on my part, to establish firmly a necessary premise. Of all the quadrupeds to which, with a comparison of no very distinguished kind, he might have seen fit to liken me, the sheep was the one I found most detestable.

— Come now, — he said — let us not quarrel over mere words. The reasons that moved me to come here are grounded in a sense of duty, and in the name of duty I may well ask for a few minutes' attention, even at the cost of proving importunate.

At a sign from me, he sat down, and protesting that he wished to come at once to the heart of the matter, reminded me of my quality as an Italian, which he held to be inseparable from that of a Catholic. Not from any eagerness to meddle in the affairs of others, but for the conscientious discharge of his own ministry, he had come to ask my permission to baptise my child, and he was sustained by the hope that I would not refuse to render that dutiful homage to the religion of my father.

— My father has no faith in priests... that is not, therefore, the key that can lock and unlock my heart. But even if it were... if the obligation fell to children to practise the faith of their forebears, we should still be throwing, in the full light of the nineteenth century, human victims into the fiery belly of Moloch; we should still be worshipping onions!

With that reply I had offered the foothold for a discussion which it was my intention to avoid. The priest seized the opportunity on the wing and, ever smiling, observed to me that our ancient ancestors believed in error and practised falsehood. For their children, then, the obligation was to seek the truth, even if by groping in the dark. But from the moment that truth had been revealed to us by One Who is the very essence of truth, why depart from it, why obstinately close one's eyes to the light? And even if, through a misguided pride, I chose to plunge headlong into evil, by what right did I deny redemption to an innocent creature?

And I: If my son is innocent, why would your God hold him answerable for sins not his own?

— These are mysteries of our holy religion; nor is it given to the human mind to fathom them...

— Not even to men chosen by a "holy" vocation and anointed by the Lord?

— It would be too much to presume from poor clay!

— From which I must infer that you set yourself up as herald and teacher of things you do not know; which, by your own confession, you will never be in a position to know. What would you say of me if I claimed to teach Greek while ignorant of even its alphabet?

— Faith, my dear Count, faith!...

— If it be a virtue to believe the absurd, I fail to see what distinction could be drawn between a bishop and the savage who worships the meteorite fallen from the sky.

And here, through my own foolishness, I let myself be drawn into expounding for him the essence of the Brahmanic legends, the fertile matrix from which so many other religious legends sprang, the Christian among them. Indeed, pressing further back through the succession of centuries, I proved to him — an easy enough undertaking! — that all the religions which cloud the human intellect derive, in the last analysis, from the astronomical knowledge possessed by the ancients, and that upon the 'science of the Sun' is founded precisely every sacred allegory, every pretended sacred mystery. He came to me, therefore, unknowingly perhaps, but indubitably, to propose that I bend the knee, that I bow the soul before the majesty of Apollo, of Bacchus, of Mithras,... of the Sun!

He let me speak, contriving to assume here and there the expression of a sage who listens, out of benevolent condescension, to the fables created by the exuberant and wayward fancy of a child. But at the final phrase, his thin lips acquired the hardness of sarcasm and seemed on the point of stretching, at their very corners, as far as his ears.

Was that sarcasm sincere? Perhaps!... In the Church things proceed as in an army: the General Staff knows the secret plans; but the multitude of subordinates operates in the dark.

He said: My dear Count, permit me to employ, in defence of the truth — which would, besides, have no need of any such defence — the selfsame frankness which you employ in defending heresy. Human fancy has indulged itself in flights far bolder than this one of yours. But in the field of positive facts, the Holy Catholic, Apostolic and Roman Church has nineteen centuries of good to her name which neither you, nor any other unbridled spirit, could destroy.

And I in return: “Good existed in theory and in practice even when mankind had not so much as dreamt that, in a small and picturesque peninsula of the savage West, a Rome would arise, and in Rome, after a long succession of intrigues and outrages, a Catholic coalition of superstitions and cupidity which would take morality for its signboard or, more precisely, for a kind of skeleton-key. And I maintain that good will continue to be practised even when of the Rome of the Popes nothing remains, a pale memory, save the name!

“Individually, notwithstanding the habit he wears, even a priest may practise virtue, for goodness lies in germ in human nature and the day will come when, its obstacles shattered, it will at last triumph upon this Earth. But the evil which the Catholic Church, considered in its exclusive essence, has wrought and persists in working — ah, that is not eclipsed by a handful of phrases! Would you truly have me call it to mind, by evoking nineteen centuries of obscurantism, of deceit, of tyranny, of torture, of the stake, of tears and of blood?”

He lowered his eyes and appeared to reflect. Then came the stroke of the venomous tail! “Let us set aside for a moment,” he said, “opinions which, in any case, can do little or nothing against the fatal course of things... Could one not, with a little goodwill, find a ground upon which two men of honour might reach an understanding, despite the disparity of their views?”

He lowered his eyes again, and in a subdued voice: “Allow me, not as a priest, but as a man of feeling who might, by his age, be your father... Were you to authorise me to baptise your son, I would undertake to procure for you — on my honour! — a lucrative position such as would set you back upon your feet in society. I perceive around me signs of poverty all too manifest. Forgive me!... Let us speak as serious men, as upright consciences, who yet know how to weigh the exigencies of life. Why sacrifice reality to mere abstractions? Does it not seem to you that the greatest among our duties is precisely that of providing decently for those dear to us? And what greater satisfaction for a father?”

It was the noose of need cast about my neck! General Staff or subordinates, the indelible priestly character never belies itself.

I sprang to my feet and, in a voice full of anger: “The greatest of my duties is to leave my son an inheritance of manly example. In any case, I am not a priest, and I do not traffic in my convictions!” And I showed him the door.

That same year, after an existence of but a few brief weeks, my second-born ceased to live. It was the eve of Christmas... the anniversary of the day on which I had embarked to navigate at full sail toward the fellowship of free men... and I had not so much in the house as would allow me to arrange for his burial. I gazed with dry eyes upon his little face, white as the pillow on which it rested, and, endeavouring to dry Elisa's tears, I said:

“Sooner or later, its obstacles shattered, good will triumph upon this Earth. And it will be, in great part, the merit of those who, knowing the truth, do not shrink from practising it. But now... Hush, hush! An upright conscience expects no reward from men. And perhaps this desolation... is our one, true glory!”


### Chapter Thirty-One

<!-- pages:252-258 -->

<!-- pages:252-258 -->



BETWEEN one and another incident of my private life, the History of my Country sends forth gusts laden with renewed hopes and enthusiasm, with bitterness and wrath.

My predictions concerning the fatal consequences of the alliance forged between the King of Piedmont and Napoleon III, though they had come to my mind at so great a distance from the theatre of events and while I found myself as though excluded from the rest of humanity, were in perfect accord with those expressed by Giuseppe Mazzini. Those predictions had come to pass.

Having undertaken the expulsion of the Austrians from the Ticino to the Adriatic in exchange for a compensation which, wounding our national sentiment, placed in his hands the keys to the western gates of Italy, Napoleon had unexpectedly halted his victorious army and concluded the treacherous peace of Villafranca, which left to the enemy the Veneto, the quadrilateral, and part of Lombardy itself.

Thus the pact of alliance was torn asunder in the very face of Vittorio Emanuele II who, as a further insult, was not granted, in the discussion of the armistice and of the peace that followed, so much as a single consultative, *pro forma* vote.
That reversal of front, that broken faith, that sudden manifestation of imperial displeasure were owing to full knowledge of Napoleon’s manœuvres — in part confessed, in part too plainly manifest of themselves. Napoleon had descended into the valley of the Po in order to substitute his own influence for that of Austria. A Piedmont enlarged yet weak, yet defenceless against imperial tyranny; a central remnant governed by Plon-plon and vassal to France; a Murat at Naples; an Italy still in fragments, under the Presidency of Pius IX; the Pope protected and made the instrument of the man to whom he owed his sceptre... this was, in a form more or less veiled yet always substantial, the conquest of the entire Peninsula — the saddest pages of our History revived in the full light of the nineteenth century!

The unitary patriotism of the Tuscans, and the shrewdness of Bettino Ricasoli, who was its supreme embodiment, made a mockery of the trafficking by which the vulgar Plon-plon had been made the King’s son-in-law, and by persuading the latter of the futility of any attempt to deceive the Italians, lifted from the hearts of the patriots that newest oppression.

Following the example of Tuscany, Umbria, Modena, Parma, Piacenza, Bologna and the Romagne had risen, had dismissed their petty tyrants, not to bow before foreign sovereigns, but to attest their solidarity with the rest of the Italian family. The long propaganda of Giuseppe Mazzini was bearing its fruit.

Had the Empire possessed a popular foundation; had the ill-suppressed apotheosis of Felice Orsini, enacted by his very own subjects, not stripped Louis Napoleon of every illusion in the matter; had the general conditions of the age permitted it, he would without doubt have assumed the bearing of absolute master, dictating to the peoples of Italy his own will as supreme law, and the Gallic herds would have drunk the waters of the Po for an indefinite time. He lacked the audacity, and, ill-appreciating our popular forces — destined henceforth to expand “with, without, or against” the higher foreign and domestic influences — and trusting moreover too greatly in the devious arts of his diplomacy, he preferred to watch events, playing for time.

And he announced this plainly with the celebrated phrase: “Now we shall see what the Italians know how to do on their own!” Which was admonition, was threat; which, broken into its component parts and glossed in plain speech, signified: “Engaged in the absorbing concerns of a war, you would slip entirely from my grasp. Nor do I risk my crown for the platonic love of your fair sky. Free from excessive cares, at the head of an army strengthened by the prestige of victory, I shall be better able to dominate the situation. And let the Italians take care not to go too far beyond the limit required by my interests.”

Vittorio Emanuele II — he had the Austrians, thirsting for vengeance and well entrenched on Italian soil; he had the spectre of revolution seething in every corner of the Peninsula; he was fearful of losing even the little he had gained; he was compelled to subordinate the unity of the Fatherland to the fortunes of his own dynasty (he and Cavour regarded it as a dream of exalted minds) — he could no longer flatter himself that he might attempt new ventures, might take a step, without the good pleasure of the “magnanimous ally.”

In other words, the latter had reduced the great King to the condition of a simple vassal of the Empire, perhaps more so, certainly no less so than the Pope himself. And we observe that, precisely from the peace of Villafranca, the Government of the King initiates that policy of phenomenal pliability to the caprices of the man of destiny — a policy which may appear practical, and perhaps even necessary, but which is character-forming, but Italian, never.

And Vittorio Emanuele II exclaimed: “Poor Italy!” But at bottom it was yet another great intellect deceiving itself. Tuscan unitary patriotism not only added the true cornerstone to our national edifice; it also, by compelling Napoleon III to throw off his mask, was to render his much-lamented defection a propitious event in the annals of Italy. The sublime boldness of the party of action, intolerant of delay, redoubled in intensity and precipitated History.

Two months after my arrival in London, there spread, swift as lightning, the news that Giuseppe Garibaldi had landed at Marsala; then, amid universal trepidation, the most contradictory rumours — and even that he had been captured by the Bourbonists and summarily hanged from a tree. To dispel that dire report came the announcement of the victory of Calatafimi and of the rapid triumphs that followed. England remained as though dazzled by those bursts of intermittent light, and such and so intense was her enthusiasm as to leave one almost supposing that the legionaries of Garibaldi, rather than fighting battles for the redemption of Italy, were fighting for the greatness of the English People.

Garibaldi’s entry into Palermo was matched, unhappily, by the sanction of Parliament for the cession of Nice and Savoy — the full price paid for a service only partially rendered. Thus, while the Revolution sacrificed the blood of heroes to add new Provinces to the resurgent Nation, to make it whole, the Savoyard Monarchy worked in reverse and severed from it two of its ancient Provinces.

All at once the superior intellect that governed the fortunes of that Monarchy, with a master stroke, turned from devil to friar. I mean that Camillo Benso di Cavour, while deploring as the supreme affliction of Italy the arts of the revolutionaries, himself became, in his turn, a provoker of revolutions. The Marches, stirred to revolt, were traversed back and forth by his emissaries who encouraged the wavering, gathered together the scattered threads, distributed arms, redoubled boldness and enthusiasm by promising the imminent intervention of the royal army. And General Cialdini, charged with making good that promise, invaded the Marches to vanquish the mercenaries of reaction. This took place on the very day that Garibaldi entered Naples in triumph.

Naturally, for this new enterprise too, the King’s Minister had sought the consent of Napoleon III, to whom he demonstrated the necessity of drawing a cordon of defence around the Pontifical States, threatened by the red-shirts. But the supreme aim was to efface, in part, the shame of the cession of Nice — to which Napoleon himself had shown himself disposed to renounce — to efface that shame, and to neutralise the prestige of the party of action (grown almost irresistible after the conquest of the Two Sicilies), whose republican tendencies he feared to excess.

Monarchist to the point of fanaticism, he could not desire the unity of Italy when the attempt would have compromised the Monarchy; he had become a unitarian when the Monarchy would have been compromised by inaction. The dynamic force of republican thought had thus, indirectly, thrust the Marches too into the bosom of the Italian family.

On the 30th of November of that year, I received from the English newspapers the news that Giuseppe Garibaldi, a new Cincinnatus, had retired, as a private citizen, into the solitude of Caprera. With scanty troops, poorly armed, in a few months and as though by a single bound, he had conquered a Kingdom. Having been proclaimed its Dictator, from that height which allowed him to treat on equal terms with the great King, to dictate conditions, he proffered him — that he might bind it about his brow — the crown of the Bourbons. A gesture wholly new in History! — but not new the bad faith with which he who was the beneficiary repaid him. Vittorio Emanuele II assumed the bearing of a man who had known how to forestall usurpation; who by his own virtue recovered what was his by right. The debt of gratitude he would attend to repaying later.

The glorious enterprise of the Thousand had been organised by Giuseppe Mazzini, by Rosalino Pilo — who gave his life in it — by Agostino Bertani, by Nino Bixio, and by many and many others, with those means and those arts which were so frequently reproached to the Master as a crime. The last in a series of ill-understood and sublime attempts that had created an “Italian question,” that had compelled the Potentates of Europe to occupy themselves with it, it was the most fortunate, not the most overflowing with boldness and spirit of sacrifice. To it the glory of triumph; to the others that of having prepared it and rendered it possible. And I shall note that Francesco Crispi, who had not yet renounced the idea of embodying in himself the spirit of rebellion, brought his contribution, among other things, with a conspicuous supply — of Orsini bombs!

The enterprise itself gave the various tendencies wider scope to define themselves. Napoleon III accentuated ever more his Italophobe designs, and Vittorio Emanuele II appeared ever more in his equivocal light: a champion of the Italian cause, he conspired with the enemies of Italy; a liberator, he opposed liberty. Intent on impeding the triumphal advance of Garibaldi, he did not blush to enter into negotiations with the Bourbon himself. And with the Emperor of the French he came to an open understanding to stretch, along the Strait of Messina, an ambush of iron and lead, to bar to the bold legionaries of redemption the road to the Continent. But the English Cabinet, sounded on the matter by Paris, replied that the Neapolitans had the right to dispose of their own destinies, and imposed upon Napoleon the policy of non-intervention.

The greatness of the republican soul shines all the more brilliantly from these contrasts. To repeat is of use: The monarchical entity never throws itself into the fray without an eye to its own interest and subordinates the unity of Italy to privileges which it proclaims inviolable; the republican entity, having inscribed its own epic in characters of blood, freely sacrifices upon the altar of the Fatherland both itself and its serene convictions.

A true statesman, Vittorio Emanuele II was regarded by Garibaldi at that time as shrewd, yet foreshadowed by Dante as the Prince schooled by the powerful mind of Machiavelli. Giuseppe Mazzini, more sparing in judgement, contented himself with holding him better than his Ministers.

The days of disillusionment succeeded one another like lightning. Cavour, overwhelmed by unrelenting toil, succumbed before his time to the common fate. In England he was sincerely mourned; though, to shake his popularity, something of those secret pacts had already transpired — pacts very unwelcome to English liberalism — which, in the ill-fated “September Convention,” were to lead the first King of Italy to the renunciation of Rome and to the defence, against the Italians themselves, of the greatest among the enemies of our Country. The party of action hoped instead that, with the disappearance of Cavour, it would be given to it to draw the King from the baneful influence of Napoleon.

To the Piedmontese statesman had succeeded a man of the most Italian spirit, endowed with broad vision, who desired unity and independence not as an end, but as a means of ensuring the political regeneration and the greatness of the People — the Baron Bettino Ricasoli.

The ship of State changed course abruptly. The edict perpetuating the exile of Giuseppe Mazzini was undermined; constitutional guarantees were respected; the extreme parties were encouraged to come together, to give concrete form, with their accustomed boldness, to the good of the Fatherland — a renewed current of enthusiasm, a powerful tremor, a feverish activity shook the entire Peninsula. But when, coming to an understanding with Garibaldi, the proud baron set himself to making of the Italian collectivity not an inert mass at the mercy of its own governors, but a formidable armed force capable of imposing upon Privilege respect for popular rights, the retrograde party, already livid with suppressed fury, took fright and resolved to have done with him. One of those parliamentary crises which conceal, beneath the mantle of legality, the odious interest of caste, unexpectedly wrested from him the reins of government. And the antiquated and mendacious Diplomacy, under the auspices of Urbano Rattazzi, sharpened abuse and violence the better to corrupt young Italy.

Rattazzi concealed himself, at first, beneath the skin of the just man; he assumed the bearing of a continuator of his predecessor’s work — he promised, encouraged, and drove the party of action toward the conquest of the Trentino and the Alps. At the tactical moment, news burst forth of the seizure of arms and the arrest of patriots. Brescia, her horrible wounds inflicted by the beast Haynau not yet healed, had to writhe beneath those opened in her by Piedmontese lead. A domestic tyranny had taken the place of the foreign one. And Garibaldi, in a burst of indignation, flung his own sarcasm in the faces of the fierce, proposing a national subscription intended to offer a sword of honour to the Russian Popov who, at Warsaw, had broken his own sword rather than stain it with the patriotic blood of defenceless Poles.

Nor was the measure yet full. The possibility of action in the North having been cut off, Giuseppe Garibaldi assembled in Sicily a band of volunteers, recrossed the strait, and marched up through Calabria, resolved to give to the Fatherland its glorious capital. He had made his own the battle-cry launched by the patriotic population of Marsala: “Rome or death!”

“You shall have death, not Rome!” was the cry at Paris. And Napoleon imposed upon Vittorio Emanuele, who accepted, the part of the modern Cain.

We are arrived at the infamy of Aspromonte. When the Garibaldian Legion and the King’s soldiers met, on the 28th of August 1862, the leader of the Thousand replied to the assault of the Bersaglieri by ordering his men to stand their ground without firing. That alone ought to have sufficed to avert fratricide — had not those men who had passed off the Orsini outrage as execrable aimed in truth at removing forever from the stage of the world the first, the most beloved among the fighters for the cause of Italy. And perhaps it is owing to the conscience of Sergeant Cavalli of Ivrea that the conspiracy, truly execrable, miscarried. Stepping from the ranks and advancing some three hundred paces beyond the line of fire, he — an expert marksman — discharged a shot that went wide. Reprimanded, covered with abuse, he took aim once more —

“Long live Italy!” cries the leader of the Thousand, and falls at the foot of a tree which resurgent Italians ought to regard as sacred as the wood of Calvary. The shot that had wounded Garibaldi in the ankle of his right foot struck the Fatherland to the heart. Vittorio Emanuele II repaid in that kingly fashion the debt of gratitude — and the courtiers had one more reason to hand down his name to posterity with the epithet of Galantuomo.

Major De Villata shot by summary execution, shortly after, at Fantina, the legionaries who, wandering in the vicinity of Messina, had attempted to reach Garibaldi. And the insult of that modern Maramaldo — who presumed to brand their unsullied brows with the mark of the brigand — reached them certainly more bitter than death itself. In that lust for reaction, Alfonso Lamarmora, the narrow-minded Lamarmora — the same man who in 1849, in response to offers of fraternal assistance from the Roman Triumvirs, had counter-proposed, and boasted of it, the cynical response of the bombardment of Genoa; the same man who was to lead us to the humiliation of Custoza — arrested, in defiance of parliamentary guarantees, three Deputies, three representatives of the Nation, and wished to have them shot. But Urbano Rattazzi, frightened by the possible consequences, paralysed by the indignant reprobation that, on the morrow of Aspromonte, rose like a tide in every part of the civilised world, compelled him to set them at liberty and to make his apologies.

“The Monarchy!” exclaimed Giuseppe Mazzini in those days — “rails in Lombardy against the patriots, in favour of Austria; in southern Italy shoots the patriots, in favour of the Pope. Equivocation is no longer possible!” And he broke openly, in full view of all, from the Monarchy.

Too late! No longer the interpreter, even in appearance, of the popular will, Vittorio Emanuele II now felt secure in his own course and declared himself boldly the sole arbiter of the destiny of the annexed Provinces. He cast over the rest of Italy, like a leaden pall, the pedantic administrative systems which were suited with difficulty even to little Piedmont.

And, what is worse, he lavished medals and rewarded with promotion the dishonest and the deluded who had served as his hired assassins — thus inaugurating that policy, continued by his successors, which, aiming almost exclusively at the augmentation of the army and honouring ferocious repressions, made it possible to renew them, and by means of them, to survive. Nor are there ever wanting, at every shameful act, the great poppies of the Ministry to smother the solitary voice of some Deputy, exalting to the skies the spirit of self-abnegation, the profound sense of duty with which the army shows itself animated — as though Italy had undergone and still undergoes so many sacrifices to impose upon the flower of its youth the lofty ideal of incruelling itself against hungry and defenceless brothers, and of being cowardly!


### Chapter Thirty-Two

<!-- pages:258-263 -->



SAD fatality that seems like punishment! From the misdeed of Aspromonte to the present day, in half a century of History, the House of Savoy, deserving of credit from various points of view, has no longer known how to lead Italy to victory — victory that yet smiled repeatedly upon the glorious victim of its fratricidal lead.(1)

In 1866 Venetia was wrested from the hateful yoke of Austria; but only by submitting to mysterious defeats, to humiliations that call to mind all too vividly another dark mystery — the one coupled to the name of Novara. As at Novara, so at Custoza and at Lissa, the enemy, sensibly inferior in forces, marvels at his own victory and is, for a moment, scarcely able to credit it. Meanwhile, a handful of volunteers, deliberately left wanting in arms, redeems, on the crags of the Tyrol, the honour of Italy. Once again, those who arrogated to themselves the monopoly of genius and gravity gave proof of their hollow pomposity, their inferiority before the band of "madmen" who obstinately persisted in demanding an Italy whole and glorious. And the peremptory order issued to Garibaldi to evacuate the redeemed lands fertilised with the blood of his men reveals, all too plainly, the royal displeasure at so glaring a contrast.

In 1870, Rome was wrested from the baneful yoke of the Pope; but the Monarchy did nothing more than gather, reluctantly and almost under the compulsion of an overbearing injunction, the fruit of the audacity of Mentana. Of that Mentana which saw the royal army stand by, motionless, while the dauntless men who sacrificed themselves to clear the way to the Capitol for the great King were cut down. Where were, on that day, the indignation and the generous impulse that make the Italians the most genuine, the most sympathetic among the races of old Europe?

Little by little, as the Fatherland gained each portion of her territory, that part of the Nation which had made itself comfortable under the leaden cloak lost a portion of its fine character. Ideality set below the horizon, and in its place there succeeded a bastard pragmatism distilled from all that smacks of the most vulgar. "We must be practical!" cried, until hoarse with crying it, the wiseacres of Italy, who looked on, with hands at their sides, at the fruitful labours of the "hot-headed." And they did not perceive that for a Nation, merely to live is less than nothing, when it knows not how to live gloriously. I say that the men who pass themselves off as sober and positive are precisely those who perpetuate the ills and the shames that afflict us; for where the effort is wanting that strikes and crumbles the sad reality pressing down upon us; where men do not ceaselessly aspire, in thought and in deed, to the realisation of "dreams," progress becomes a fable; while the utopia of today, nourished by sacrifice and blood, inevitably becomes the reality of tomorrow.

Be that as it may, the conduct of the royal army at Mentana must have cast a lurid light in the breast of the ruler of Italy.

The maladies of the mind reveal themselves through actions. To draw up in battle array, as upon the tiers of an amphitheatre, and to contemplate, motionless, the foreigner who, ensconced in the very sanctuary of Italian identity, bayonets the gallant insurgents to drive them out, as one drives out thieves who have entered the domestic sanctuary with a false key; to count the wounds; to hear the death-rattle of the dying; to see the countryside, all red with our blood, covered over with corpses, and to persist in immobility, neither levelling one's rifle nor striking the aggressors — even from behind... In vain do I search through my knowledge of modern and ancient History... I find therein no more complete an eclipse of every patriotic sentiment; no more abject a renunciation of the rights and duties imposed upon virile souls.

And what Sovereign, even one animated by views as clear as the hardest stretch of sky, would not have exclaimed: "Your bodies and your souls are mine!"

From that servile submission to the vain prestige of a crown, the Italian People, taken as a whole, has never recovered. Italy is no longer a geographical expression, no longer the land of carnival; but neither is she that of Brutus and the Gracchi. "His Majesty... His Holiness"... between the two runs not merely the analogy of the rhyme. The one by grace, the other by the will of God; the one sacred and infallible, the other sacred and inviolable; the one inseparable from Truth, the other from Good; to speak ill of either alike a crime! The heart bleeds to think of it...

We have cast aside one set of dogmas only to fall back into another that is its equal; our Fatherland is now the Peninsula of two Pontiffs!

The Monarchy, having thrown the national programme on the scrap-heap, placed boldly, after Mentana, its veto upon every aspiration of the Italians towards the North. Was it bound to Austria by secret pacts even before it thought to bind her by open alliances? I cannot say... but I know that from that time forward the motto "Italy belongs to the Italians" sets it shivering with something akin to hysteria. It dismisses the old generals who still dare to pronounce it; prepares the manacles for the patriots who refuse to resign themselves to treating it as a mere understood thing. It will then attempt, by repudiating the principles that were the foundation of our *Risorgimento*, to revive its own military glory by conquering in distant Africa the land of the "four brigands." But even with soldiers who are the very incarnation of dash and sacrifice, it will succeed in nothing beyond repeating the ignominy of its own defeat.

And yet, personally, the Savoyard princes possess in the highest degree that military courage which descended to them through a long line of ancestors, and at San Martino, where there was "glory for all," even the great King reaped a rich harvest of it!...

Vittorio Emanuele II knew also how to conceal his own weaknesses by assuming, before the astonished gaze of Europe, attitudes worthy of the bronze. Nevertheless, despite his essentially aristocratic nature, he ran, driven by his exuberant vitality, after pleasures bordering on the vulgar. These provoked a smile of complacency — fetid and indulgent — both among the refined of high society and among the multitude who, accustomed to picturing Kings as they are drawn in tragedies, admired them almost as revelations of a sincerely democratic spirit. The times granted him the spectacular rôle of the triumphator, and his robust individuality seemed fashioned expressly to play it. He took upon himself the mission of guiding, unscathed, through the stormy present, the Institutions that had survived from the past. A fateful mission... he kept himself ready to fulfil it; but at the cost of sacrificing the future. The common people admire him, and will admire him, so long as they are educated in the school that teaches prostration before success. The thinkers who analyse already reproach him for the subtly underhanded policy, almost that of an arriviste banker, which cast upon the treasury of our enthusiasms the seeds of dissolution.

My sympathies go by preference to his predecessor. Carlo Alberto is beautified in my eyes by the merit belonging to those who lead the way, and by his serene dignity in adversity. He did not gather the fruit of his own good works; from the works of others he gathered their bitter fruits. All he offered, and all he lost. Epic and resounding, despite their errors, were his battles; yet they become of small account beside those fought silently within his conscience. He betrayed... perhaps; but he was far more betrayed himself. His weaknesses, his waverings, his defections, even his intermittent cruelty, reveal the profound sufferings of a sick and extremely sensitive soul; so that the instinctive aversion towards the head of a system of privilege and reaction yields before the rising of pity for the man. It yields... No, it changes its object and pours itself, the more intensely, upon the nefarious sect which, sheltering behind things accounted sacred, succeeds in defiling natures as elect as his!

"The Hamlet of Italy"... well defined! His figure emerges not from triumphal parades, but from a tragedy of passion, all shadow and sudden flare. Is there, among my countrymen, one who feels himself equal to attempting it!

It has been written that I have no vision of contemporary Italy, free, united, politically and civilly reborn; advancing with a sure step towards the glorious goal that awaits her... loved and envied anew by the whole world.

It has been written that I have, nonetheless, the vision of the Italy of my own times, enslaved, unhappy, and full of grief; that I am still moved and suffer at the remembrance of the ills that afflicted her.

Whoever wrote this shows himself more a poet than an observer, or at the very least grasps but the smallest part of the truth.

I have the vision of an Italy such as was glimpsed by Giuseppe Mazzini; such as we all dreamed of, somewhat, when between her and us lay so much a Calvary of suffering. Contemporary Italy resembles her too little... that is why I am grieved! Bursts of virile energy are not lacking in the classes that have least reason to congratulate themselves upon the independence that has been gained. But their gaze is set upon a height that the times do not permit us to reach, and passes over — almost as if tracing their outlines — the intermediate vindications that would nonetheless be within our grasp. It is mere idle chatter to speak of universal brotherhood when the sorry plant of bondage takes root, by foreign force, in one's own domestic garden.

And I know that, with the exception of the government of Francesco Crispi — marked by an energy not always well applied, but virile — we have had from a long succession of Ministers nothing but manifestations of a neurotic ferocity that betrays fear; or of a myopic and senile cunning that commands no respect whatsoever, that arouses the envy of no one.

And I know that insolent Austria still stretches its iron hand on this side of the Alps. It could descend, in a single day, into the very heart of the valley of the Po, the most fertile region of Italy. And while her Emperor, in order not to retreat, even in appearance, from the position which from the outset he was pleased to assume, refuses to return to Rome the visit paid him by our Sovereign, our Ministers sign away with easy cheerfulness the alliances that guarantee him the peaceful enjoyment of the usurped lands; that repudiate the integrity and the greatness of the Fatherland. What a contrast!... and how the fibre of those who hold the destinies of the young Nation is shown to be enfeebled before that of the old reactionary and hangman Monarch!

I ask myself: Who will realise the aspirations consecrated by the blood of so many martyrs, if one makes love to the very enemy whose hands are at our throats? So far have we fallen: a special word has been coined — *quarantottate!* ["forty-eightisms!"] — to designate, with derision, the desperate heroism of the fathers!

One thought turned towards him who reigns at present, and I am done. For some years now our Monarchy has given signs of undergoing a partial transformation. The acts of royal humaneness aim less at theatrical and ephemeral show, and more at leaving some trace that may endure. Respect for the individual right is more keenly felt within it, and political liberty is extended. Perhaps the young King has been instructed by the tragic end of his father, violently removed for his frequent returns to antiquated and tyrannical methods that ought to have been the sad exclusive property of bygone times. May 1898, for instance, in its means and its effects, if not precisely in its aim, is too strongly reminiscent of the 2nd of December for the painful doubt not to insinuate itself into more than one heart, that it may be its sad and ill-judged imitation.

And yet, while the Monarchy makes concessions to liberalism and shows itself disposed to proceed alongside it, it draws back in secret, without letting it be seen, as if it aimed at maintaining its balance by blunting, through the increased power of the Church, the ever-growing demands of the labouring multitudes.

A Government that seats itself midway between the parties, moderator of all, superior to all!... This may seem wise to the *praticismo* [spirit of pragmatism] that cannot discern the future, or that, discerning it, fears it wrongly. I see in it, rather, the tactic recommended by the little treatises on political philosophy. A "living" Nation is of necessity in perpetual struggle, and the Rulers of a State, monarchical or republican, may temporarily impede or deflect it, but suppress it — never. In the social struggle that presses upon us, equilibrium is a chimera, and progress, now rapidly completing its fateful course, is the inexorable condemnation alike of economic rapine and of dogma. The labouring multitude will triumph and will annihilate, in the fever of its triumph, not only the hostile elements, but also the Institutions that do not range themselves openly, sincerely, beside it.

Let it not be thought that I harbour resentments. Distance in time and in space have worn down the sharp edges, have stifled many passions. Only love survives, intense. It blazes in the truth. Nor shall I go down into the earth with the remorse of having kept silence upon it.

As a republican I could wish that the Monarchy would commit a thousand errors; for, once the measure were full, it would plunge into the pit dug by its own lack of foresight. But as an Italian educated by his elders to place, above his political ideals, the integrity of the Fatherland, I do not hesitate to rekindle the last flame still alive in me, in order to give warning:

He who leans upon the past sets his foot upon ground that the waves are daily undermining and swallowing. Trust in the future and advance boldly to meet it. It is thus that you will succeed in mastering it. The past evolved out of barbarism and is saturated with violence and crimes; but History tends towards the triumph of Democracy, of equality before rights, before duties and before opportunities; towards the triumph of justice. He who does not second the tendencies of History cannot have a kingdom that is long, blessed, and glorious. In foreign policy the traces of the triumph of the violence suffered by the Italian people are too plain and drip with too much blood for your mission not to stand out clear and precise. The Monarchy exists because every patriotic party lowered its own banner before hers, as the vanquished lower them; but in the act of binding a solemn compact that guaranteed, as the counterpart of the sacrifice, an Italy independent from the Alps to the uttermost southern shores. The Monarchy, now on one pretext, now on another, has evaded the full observance of that compact. In the meantime, Austria, despite the notorious alliance, cannot slumber. She renews and multiplies her arms; sharpens her claws and points openly at the flesh in which she is eager to sink them. It is urgent to act humanely within the borders of the Kingdom, so as to acquire over the multitude the ascendancy that comes from respect; the respect that has its root in gratitude and in love; it is urgent to rekindle the drowsy enthusiasm and the virile spirit intolerant of insults — but not with empty commemorative celebrations, nor with the puerile rhetoric of the banquet table; it is urgent, I say, that the Nation be ready to breathe its own soul into the army. Cold and arid discipline is no longer sufficient for armed bodies that, at the first disaster, fall to pieces unless renewed and sustained by a nobler sentiment.

Upon Vittorio Emanuele III falls the exalted task of completing and correcting Vittorio Emanuele II. One wears a crown ill that has come by the ephemeral right of succession, if, in appropriating its privileges and its honours, one makes light of its burdens. All further delay is now superfluous; to temporise any longer would mean usurping, by virtue of an equivocation, the throne of Italy!

The Count di Rudio closed his eyes before our victories in Libya could dazzle them. What judgment he would have pronounced upon them, the present writer is not in a position to determine precisely. But if one reflects that he disdained material advantages when they were not coupled with a moral and regenerating triumph, one might readily wager that they would have wrung from him another of those cries of his that reveal a bleeding heart.

After more than a year of exploits officially depicted to us as worthy of the ancient Romans... a Treaty of Peace that calls to mind the ignominious "cession" of Venetia!

But in setting forth the reasons why they had not known how to achieve the better course, the Counsellors of the Crown showed themselves truly without equal! C. C.


### Chapter Thirty-Three

<!-- pages:263-268 -->



THE sufferings of the *ergastolo* were by now erased by my long immersion in the free light and in the affections of my family, which had almost reconciled me to inertia, when the news of the crime of Aspromonte came to rouse me.

The conferences and public meetings organised to protest against the Temporal Power of the Popes had become, in London, a daily occurrence, and I threw myself into them, so to speak, headlong.

An incident occurred which I recall with satisfaction. The Irish element, which obscures its fine qualities by subordinating them too readily to the watchword of the tonsured camarilla, took offence at this altogether new agitation. One day when an imposing international meeting was being held in the Park, breaking through the police cordons that protected us, they fell upon us furiously. The demonstrators were swept away as if by a broom, and the banners brought down. Two alone remained upright and flying: that of the French republican delegation, which stood at my side, and the one I gripped in my hands — upon it blazed, in scarlet letters, the motto: “O Roma o Morte.”

The modest triumph was owed to the short blade which had escaped the eye of the French police. Even on that occasion it remained virgin of blood — its menacing flash had sufficed!

Resolved to reconsecrate my activity to greater ends, I set about finding a way to return to my homeland. Giuseppe Mazzini, while lending his support, when they seemed inevitable, to the movements that tended toward the liberation of Rome, preferred, for practical reasons, to effect first the liberation of the Veneto. And toward that end his efforts were directed, after Aspromonte. I hoped, on returning home, to enter fighting into the lands to which so many intimate memories bound me; to crown my modest endeavours beneath the very eyes of the two dear creatures from whom I had drawn, with my blood, my whole soul.
For a moment, it seemed as though the Italian Government was destined to fulfil my wishes. There was in English waters one of our warships — perhaps the *Vittorio Emanuele*. She was to make for Genoa, and a ministerial provision granted free passage to whatever exiles desired to return home. At the Consulate there was a press of individuals of every sort, among whom I recognised more than one who, notoriously, carried both his own honour and the Fatherland in his pocket. The consul Corti, an official who afterwards advanced in his career and with whom I was to find myself face to face, in other lands and in circumstances so greatly altered for me, received me with courteous manners, but concluded by enjoining me to return the following morning — a suspicious delay! Nevertheless, at the appointed hour I appeared before him. He had at his side a certain person who, on seeing me enter, whispered a few words in his ear. The consular mask darkened: “Why did you give a false name yesterday?”

— Not false, but simply incomplete. I said that my name is Carlo Nosadano; my full name is Carlo Camillo di Rudio dei Conti di Nosadano.

— I am sorry, but there is no place for you on the Royal vessel.

— I understand! You do not wish to confound me with the common criminals whom you have nonetheless received with open arms.

— Take it as you please. I must, however, warn you that, wherever the King’s flag flies, the Revolution and the wind no longer blow propitiously for certain methods and for certain persons. I believe, in all conscience, that were you to be discovered within the borders of the Kingdom, you would be imprisoned and handed over to the French authorities, with whom, unless I am mistaken, you have some accounts to settle...

Consul Corti was, on this point, perfectly right. The King of Italy and Napoleon III were bound together, and he who, under the Emperor’s pressure, had not hesitated to fire upon the man to whom he owed a crown, could not remain in doubt between that pressure and the defenceless rights of a simple, obscure citizen.

They did not wish me to have a Fatherland! For some days I remained as though stunned; then I thought: “The despots are all in league, even without need of formal pacts, just as all the peoples of the world are brothers. I shall find bullies to combat and good causes to defend everywhere.”

From this I concluded that what I had attempted for my Country’s benefit, to the extent of my powers, did not exempt me from my obligations toward the rest of mankind. I would have shown, if nothing else, that they were wrong to despise the cooperation of a patriot who, if he was not among the best, was not among the lowest either.

Heroic Poland, swept back and forth by Cossack hordes, was then writhing in the spasms of her agony. I asked to be admitted among the ranks of her defenders — but even this time, circumstances were to prove stronger than my designs. At that very juncture, unexpectedly, a letter from Giuseppe Mazzini invited me to a meeting.
Swept along by the whirlwind of events, he had passed the last years now in Italy, now in Lugano, now in London, but gravely ill and accessible only to the most intimate. And profound emotions and sufferings had so deepened the marks of age upon that noble countenance that, on seeing him, I could not conceal a gesture of pained surprise.

He perceived it, and pressing my hand said: “Everything passes, everything breaks asunder. It grieves me that our ideals have been realised only in part, and even that portion disfigured by a fatal materialism which drives men to renounce the most vital principles for the brief advantage of the moment. Oh, how many, without being vulgar, have acted vulgarly!”

He lowered his great eyes, still beautiful, still serene in the face grown almost ashen, and remained for some seconds absorbed in his thoughts. Then he roused himself and added: “Let us be content with the consciousness of having spent our lives in action, and with the certainty of leaving an Italy better than that which we found.”

I wished to say to him: “To leave it when the hour of rest arrives... may be bitter.
