But to be driven from it like a leper, after having loved it so dearly, and when, young and full of good will, you feel yourself in the fullest right to witness its resurrection, to struggle to secure for it new triumphs... this is a sorrow that knows no comfort!”

He forestalled me by asking for minute particulars concerning the last moments of Felice Orsini. He insisted on knowing whether, in the imminence of martyrdom, Orsini had thought of his old friend, and had recognised that their souls were made not to tear one another apart but to understand and to love one another.

Having been reassured on this point, he exclaimed: “How much it grieved me not to have known in time what you were plotting!”

I then reminded him of our last meeting at Cheapside. It was precisely in order to inform him of our conspiracy that I had sought, but in vain, an interview with him. He appeared troubled by some disquieting thought, and after a brief silence said: “I had not forgotten you, and had I suspected even remotely... But in the welter of engagements... What would you have? There is a fatality in things!”

With an abrupt transition, he launched at full flight into a wholly different subject. He had been informed of my intention to fight for the independence of Poland. It was to dissuade me from this that he had written.
And with regard to that unhappy People, he expressed himself as follows: “The Polish cause is as holy as any; but it is a lost cause. Russia has known how to divide the interests of the labouring multitude from those of the landowners — a tactic fatal to the Revolution. Nothing is more baneful to Liberty than a tyranny that hypocritically waves the banner of liberal reforms. Between their countrymen who urged them to continue a struggle that was uncertain, long, and bloody, and the foreigner who offered them fields made fertile by their own sweat, the agricultural class did not hesitate for a moment. Intellectual Poland finds itself utterly isolated, and the hour of catastrophe is imminent. Wretched the Fatherland that fattens its minority at the expense of those sons who, by ceaseless toil, produce its wealth. The appeal to national solidarity, launched in the moment of danger, rings in the ears of the dispossessed as a mockery, and rapacious egotism ends by reaping, in a brief turning of time, the tempest accumulated across the centuries. And perhaps the lesson will prove of use! Russia has undertaken a dangerous game for her own internal tranquillity; but in the tenacious struggle engaged by the various Powers to overcome one another, that Power might triumph which, laying seriously to hand at home the work of wise social reforms, should find itself able to guarantee their application — in the event of war, and without internal dangers — in the houses of others. The multitudes would receive her as a liberatrix, and her own armies would find themselves, in effect, quintupled.”

He fell silent, and after brief reflection: “Be all that as it may, why should we contribute to a desperate cause an expenditure of energy and blood destined to remain sterile?”

I shrugged my shoulders, and he then, with fervent words, turned to the epic struggle which a portion of the United States of North America was sustaining in order to preserve the integrity of the Union and for the abolition of slavery. “Notwithstanding this detestable anachronism, Liberty has deep roots over there. Great is the ferment of human activity, and the vast expanse of that environment favours great ideas and colossal enterprises. Once the sad present moment is past, once the shame that has driven them one against the other is blotted out, the United States will advance without stumbling and will achieve, in short order, such a progress as will astonish the world. You who will live, will see. I counsel you to sail westward. We of the old party of action must persevere in the old school: to fight, even at the cost of adding a fresh triumph to the enemy; but sustained by the certainty that every drop of blood shed hastens the advent of the ideal — and little does it matter if others, and not we, shall gather the fruit of our sacrifice. I maintain that every sword-stroke is an active coefficient in the titanic war sustained by the American Union, and even if you were to leave your bones there, you would not have laboured in vain.”

He looked at me, as in former times, steadily in the eyes, and exclaimed: “The matter is settled — you depart for the United States!”
Without more ado, he seated himself at his writing-table and set about penning the letters that were to place me in a position to distinguish myself honourably in the task I had silently assumed. His pen ran rapidly, scratching across the paper, while I, standing in the embrasure of a window, watched him with affection, and had thoughts, old and new, which time has been able to amplify, but which were never to be given the lie:

“What nobility, and what force of intellect in that slender frame, which with a slight effort of my arm I could have overpowered!”

“Why ever do the Italians turn their backs upon him, disavow him with such precipitancy?”

“A dreamer incapable of practical vision” — yet he alone, with an accuracy almost mathematical, was able to intuit events whose very possibility the great minds of the era did not even suspect. He alone was able to photograph, as though they were already accomplished, the hidden effects of the vices and virtues of his generation.

Humble, not wealthy, without arms, he eluded for half a century the combined police forces of all Europe; wrested from every Chancellery secrets jealously kept; undermined the pedestal of Metternich, that cynical colossus, ultra-positive, who pressed upon the Peoples more heavily than a nightmare. He devised and applied Laws which, renewed, would form even today the pride of any civilised Nation; raised up armies consecrated to Liberty where nothing swarmed but slaves; added, by his actions, to the modern history of Rome pages worthy of ancient Rome; devised plans of war which the arrogant Generals of the King disdainfully rejected in order to rush headlong to defeat, yet which Bismarck and Moltke vindicated. He treated on equal terms with Kings and stripped every despot of his self-assurance. He cast broadcast upon the multitudes, sick with apathy, a ferment that gave forth flashes of light; set back upon a broader course the fateful march of peoples. Not a year of his youth, his manhood, his old age that is not wholly a poem of fruitful action and daring and sacrifice. How many practical results, for a man incapable of practical vision!
In the writings of Giuseppe Mazzini — who would dare deny it? — we encounter with excessive frequency mystical phrases, ascetic invocations to the empty phantoms created by human illusion and human pride. And that strong and gentle soul does not perceive that the very facts upon which it stumbles are the irrefutable negation of those phantoms. For if a Mind governed all things and aimed at the triumph of the good and the just, it would have simplified its own work by not creating Evil, and would not have permitted the criminals who dwell in palaces to annihilate the unblemished consciences that aspire precisely to the good and the just. If nothing else, by a single simplest act of Its omnipotence, It would have snapped the noose about the necks of the victims.

And what avails it to bow before a Most High who is always on the side of the strongest? Would it not be more logical to set about eliminating, by manful action, the causes of our weakness, so as to compel Him, through the power thus acquired, to declare Himself for us?

In the belief in God there takes root and is nourished every privilege, every spoliation, every political and social injustice. Giuseppe Mazzini, by a singular aberration, consecrated so great a part of his energy to reinforcing the error that served as a picklock in the hands of his most bitter enemies.

But setting aside this weakness, what wholeness in the pages of that great man, what passion, what depth of conception, what an ocean of truth!

Certainly, human thought does not crystallise in some few formulas and volumes, and toward ever more evolved ideals the human race tends. But I affirm that the writings of Giuseppe Mazzini will have the power to educate to high purpose even when our political institutions and our social order shall have attained all the perfection of which they are capable. Yet if the rising generation does not nourish its spirit on such fare, let it not claim to have so far progressed that it can disdain it with impunity; let it say rather that for its all-encroaching *arrivismo* the school of sacrifice is repugnant; let it say that from the Master’s pages there breathes, upon this head, a rebuke which, for being silent, proves no less irksome on that account.
Then he rose and came toward me, smiling a smile that revealed all the melancholy of a farewell. He held out to me, doling out a few instructions, several letters addressed to American political figures. In his right hand he retained a small sheet of the thinnest paper, such as he was accustomed to use in his correspondence at the time of his classic conspiracies: “This you will keep in your pocketbook. It will serve you as a good viaticum.” And he wished to read it aloud. It said:

*“Mi fo lecito di raccomandare a tutti quei cittadini americani che simpatizzano con me, Carlo Rudio, valoroso, attivo, risoluto e disposto ad unirsi ai difensori della causa dell’Emancipazione. Egli merita d’essere aiutato. Giuseppe Mazzini.”*

“I take the liberty of commending to all those American citizens who sympathise with me, Carlo Rudio, valorous, active, resolute, and disposed to join the defenders of the cause of Emancipation. He deserves to be assisted. Giuseppe Mazzini.”

That spontaneous attestation of esteem from the most venerable figure of our *Risorgimento* I preserve to this day; and if I have ever acted and suffered for my Country, in that one but inestimable recompense I had my reward — and it suffices me!

I turned my last gaze upon the Master from the foot of the short staircase. He stood leaning with both hands upon the banister. He too looked at me intently. I understood his thought, and I divined that he understood mine: “Everything passes, everything breaks asunder!...”

On the 8th of February 1864, when in London the preparations were already being laid for the triumphal reception of Giuseppe Garibaldi, I, having torn myself once more from Elisa and my little child, embarked at Liverpool on the steamship *Virginia*, bound for New York. I left behind me peoples torn by grave upheavals and fierce wars... before me, beyond the vast Ocean, other Peoples were tearing one another no less fiercely apart in a titanic fratricidal war. In antiquity and in modern times, in the old world and the new, the road to Liberty is slippery with blood.

From my arrival in New York, half a century dense with events has now gone by... yet even in the moments of greatest fortune, even in the hours of triumph, a secret sorrow persisted in the depths of my soul... my Fatherland I have never seen again!

THE END
